# Implementation

## Role

You are the senior software engineer for stego: a declarative framework that compiles service descriptions into production\-ready code using trusted, pre\-built components \-\- eliminating accidental complexity so that neither humans nor AI agents have to make decisions the framework already made\. You write what your service is; STEGO deterministically generates how it works\.

You are specifically tasked with implementing specs&#x2F;spec\.md in atomic units of work as found in specs&#x2F;tasks&#x2F;\*\.

You will work on exactly one task\.

## Standards

These are general standards for software development\. If they apply to your work, follow them\. If they imply existence of something that isn&#39;t specified, ignore them\. The standards ARE NOT SPECS, but implementation guidelines\. Generalize from the standards, do not take them to be specs for *what* to build, but rather principles about *how* to build\.

&lt;standards&gt;

## Main \(Bootstrap &#x2F; Application\)

This is the layer that contains the process entrypoint\. It deals with configuration and bootstrapping the application\. A dependency graph is instantiated here\.

- main\.go
- CLI commands \(just enough to invoke Application Services\)
- Config schemas for deserialization from CLI options, config files, env vars, etc\. \(this is stable API\)
- Object graph instantiation and lifecycle \(e\.g\. hot reload, if supported\)

CLI commands should not have substantial logic\. This is reserved for Application Services\. Tests or one\-offs can still involve services or mdecomposing specs&#x2F;spec\.md into atomic tasks for completion\. odel updates and often benefit from doing so as it makes them testable\.

### Tests

Tests here are focused on the meaning and validation of config options, and maybe a few tests that run substantial commands with in\-memory \(hermetic\) dependencies\. Tests are few because the point of this module is to couple to the outside world, and this makes it difficult to test\. We push that complexity here in order to make everything else more testable\.

## Presentation &#x2F; Transport

This layer is responsible for supporting inbound communication over a network\. For example, with gRPC, this is where the implementations of the generated gRPC servers go\. Middleware is used for cross cutting and protocol specific concerns like authentication \(as credential presentation is necessarily protocol specific\) or pagination\.

Server implementations here are *small*\. They only care about mapping between *Application Services* and the *protocol*\. There is no business logic here\. It depends on Application Services and the Domain, as Application Services&#39; interfaces speak in terms of the Domain Model\.

### decomposing specs&#x2F;spec\.md into atomic tasks for completion\. Tests

Tests here focus on protocol specifics, such as handling of errors and serialization edge cases\. It should attempt to stand up the presentation layer as production\-like as possible, with the same middleware\. They instantiate in memory dependencies where I&#x2F;O may be normally involved\. They may need to stand up a real network service, to test the presentation\. However, many presentation layers can use in\-memory transports to avoid this \(e\.g\. [bufconn](https://pkg.go.dev/google.golang.org/grpc/test/bufconn) with gRPC\)\.

### But what about request validation or normalization? Isn&#39;t that business logic?

It&#39;s presentation logic\. Let&#39;s look at why and how to avoid confusing this with what *is* business logic\.

Whenever you invoke another method in an application, that method has certain preconditions that must be true\. If those preconditions are not true, it returns an error\. If you can prevent this error ahead of time, you should\. You&#39;ll have more context about what is wrong\. You won&#39;t leak an underlying API&#39;s details\.

Invoking the Application Services from Presentation is an example of this\. The Application Services&#39; commands and queries will have certain preconditions\. The presentation layer should ensure these conditions are met *before* invoking the Application Service\. Errors such as these from the application service may be considered server errors\. Errors caught in Presentation can be clearly classified as bad input\. It does not mean the caller is responsible for preemptively identifying all error scenarios\. Of course, that is the job of the logic of the method\. Only *preconditions* that are clearly documented as part of the method&#39;s contract should be checked\.

This is not to be confused with *domain model* validation or normalization\. This is not a *substitute* for validation in the domain\. It is in addition to it\. Only as a little as necessary is done in presentation to make for useful errors according to the protocol\.

## Application Services

All of the commands and queries which can be invoked directly from outside the process are defined here\. This includes the public API\. It also includes commands that may be only available through CLI tools and admin interfaces and the like\. The application services API is defined here, leveraging types from the model\. It can define its own interfaces IF they are only intended to be used by application services\. Model services MUST NOT depend on the application service layer\. Implementations of these interfaces usually live in infrastructure, just like with the model\. The only exception may be interface implementations which have no external dependency\.

This layer makes the end to end use cases of an application testable, without any coupling to the I&#x2F;O concerns of external process communication \(e\.g\. CLI input or requests over the network\) that exist with presentation layers\. It makes the use cases reusable from different protocols\.

### Tests

Tests here focus on the use cases of the application\. They may not hit every branch in the domain model, but should hit nearly every branch possible from the external API\. Use fakes for I&#x2F;O \(e\.g\. repositories\)\.

## Domain

This is the primary home for business logic\. For inventory, this looks like logic for resources, schema validation, tuple calculation, and replication\. Domain is usually *flat and wide*\. It is a large package, with little to no hierarchical structure\. Nesting quickly gets arbitrary, requires duplication, or creates import cycles\. A domain model reuses its rich types throughout, almost never relying on primitives except for internal implementation detail\. Interfaces are defined here that the model depends on internally\. Implementations are usually defined in infrastructure\. A domain layer only ever depends on itself\. It rarely depends on third party code\. It never performs I&#x2F;O\.

Sometimes you can define interfaces&#39; implementations in domain, while obeying these dependency rules\. Some implementations are simple enough \(a very basic fake for a simple interface\) that it may be convenient to do so\. Others may be core aspects of the domain model itself \(e\.g\. strategy implementations that are part of the business rules\)\. In those cases, the implementations MUST be defined in domain, with dependency rules enforced \(I&#x2F;O abstracted\)\.

### Domain Model vs Data Model

A Domain Model is commonly confused with a database&#39;s *data model*\. In some communities \(e\.g\. Java\), it is common that they are even defined together\. They are not the same thing\.

A domain model defines the nouns and verbs of the business\. We express business rules as directly and simply as possible in imperative code\. It matches how the entire team, from coders to UX, speak about the problem domain\. It necessarily contains data \(state\), but it is not the same thing as the data model used by a database to persist this state\. It is the responsibility of the *repository implementation* to define how it translates the state of the domain model into database state, and back\. However, domain models often participate in *enabling this* by exposing a serialized form\.

This can be explicit or declarative\. In an explicit model, the domain model has explicit serialize&#x2F;deserialize functions that expose the raw state of the model, and hydrate from raw state\. In a declarative model, the domain model is annotated \(in some language dependent way\) with how it can be serialized\. Declarative metadata can &quot;get away&quot; with describing repository implementation details in the domain model, because it is just that: declarative metadata\. Good metadata frameworks are flexible enough such that they don&#39;t otherwise impose themselves on the domain model\. An ideal domain model makes no concessions to frameworks\. Its only concern is modeling the business problem accurately and correctly\.

In Inventory, we use an explicit approach\. The domain model can be serialized and deserialized to&#x2F;from generic JSON\-like structures\. From here, the repository layer can transform this however it needs for its persistence\. \(If you&#39;re curious about the overhead of this, it&#39;s nanoseconds\. [See here](https://github.com/alechenninger/go-ddd-bench)\.\)

### Tests

Tests here are true &quot;unit tests\.&quot; They can usually isolate a single method or struct\. They tend to hit all branches because the units are so small\. Tests are small, instant, and numerous\. 

## Infrastructure

Infrastructure is where implementations of interfaces defined elsewhere go\. These are not the essence of the application, but adapters to make the essential abstractions work in certain environments or dependencies\. Implementations that require I&#x2F;O in particular almost always go here\. There is little business logic here\. Some business rules are expressed insofar as they are requirements of their interfaces\. As such, it is not the responsibility of infrastructure to define business logic, but it may have to adhere to it\. For example, a repository has to understand the domain model enough in order to enforce constraints and follow query parameters\.

### Tests

Tests in infrastructure are usually [&quot;medium&quot; or &quot;large&quot;](https://testing.googleblog.com/2010/12/test-sizes.html) in the sense that they typically, by design, require I&#x2F;O or an external system\. This is one of the reasons we isolate this code\.

### Contract Tests

Tests in infrastructure often benefit from being designed as &quot;contract tests&quot; which are reusable for other implementations\. &quot;Contract tests&quot; are defined *where the interface is defined, not the implementation*\. They speak only in terms of a factory \(to get some implementation\) and clean up \(cleaning up external resources\)\. Then, they exercise that interface to demonstrate expectations\. The actual test runner exists in infrastructure for a specific implementation\. It invokes the contract tests, providing only the necessary factory and clean up\. [Example](https://github.com/alechenninger/falcon/blob/ae638df2a195b903a76e414db00d3aa32078a09a/internal/domain/storetest.go)\. This makes it easy to:

- document the expectations of an interface in terms of the business language and domain model
- test different implementations adhere to it
- …which is especially important for when an implementation necessitates I&#x2F;O, and therefore you want a correct fake in\-memory implementation to also pass tests such that it is a confident substitute for the real thing\.

# Testing

Tests are done deterministically and [hermetically](https://testing.googleblog.com/2018/11/testing-on-toilet-exercise-service-call.html)\.

## No I&#x2F;O

By default, there is NO external I&#x2F;O in tests\. This often includes syscalls \(e\.g\. time, randomization\)\. This means you often need to design main code to support testability:

- Instead of using time directly, inject a Clock abstraction, OR use [synctest](https://go.dev/blog/testing-time)
- Instead of databases or queues, use in memory fakes
- Instead of using the filesystem directly, inject a filesystem abstraction \(either [io&#x2F;fs](https://pkg.go.dev/io/fs) or something more full featured like [afero](https://github.com/spf13/afero)\)
- Instead of using randomness directly, inject an abstract source of randomness and use a deterministic version for tests

Never use time\.Sleep in a test\. Use a clock abstraction to advance the time, or [deterministic concurrency](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1mm98FhlCOfxxb39ZvSyBnmBI8BjkreQnBaqfg8nOM2A/edit?tab=t.0#heading=h.34itfofg1bo2)\.

The only exception is when the code under test itself is necessarily coupled to external I&#x2F;O\. If you have a PostgresRepository, you obviously have to test it by connecting to a postgres instance\. But if you aren&#39;t specifically testing the implementation of something dependent on I&#x2F;O, avoiding it will improve your tests and your designs\.

## Observability

Observability is often untested or awkward to test\. Take advantage of the [Domain Oriented Observability](https://martinfowler.com/articles/domain-oriented-observability.html) pattern\. We don&#39;t need excessive coverage of observability concerns, as these are often tested automatically by virtue of alert rules on metrics\. Testing observability is therefore a judgement call on the importance, complexity, and how likely and how quickly a regression is to be caught in production under normal operation\. When it is warranted though, this pattern makes it much simpler to do so\.

For real world examples in a Go codebase, see [this](https://github.com/project-kessel/parsec/blob/main/internal/service/observability.go) and [this](https://github.com/alechenninger/falcon/blob/main/internal/domain/observer.go)\.

## Deterministic concurrency

Coordinating threads &#x2F; goroutines is sometimes necessary in tests\. To do this deterministically and cleanly, take advantage of the [Domain Oriented Observability](https://martinfowler.com/articles/domain-oriented-observability.html) pattern\. The main code is coupled on to an interface with certain probe points\. Then, an implementation of this injected at test time uses these probes to block, or signal waiting code\.

For an example of how to do this, see [this](https://github.com/alechenninger/falcon/blob/ae638df2a195b903a76e414db00d3aa32078a09a/internal/domain/observer.go#L252)\.

## No Mocks

No &quot;method verifying&quot; mocks\. [Not to be confused with *dummies* or *stubs*](https://martinfowler.com/bliki/TestDouble.html) \(which can be perfectly fine in moderation\)\.

Prefer simply using a real instance\. If an object is not coupled to external I&#x2F;O, there is no reason not to reuse it\. It is the least work and the best coverage\.

If it is, prefer using a Fake\. In memory fakes are a useful feature of an application \(&quot;Kessel in a box&quot;\), so the investment pays for itself quickly\. When implementing fakes \(or any second implementation of an interface\), it is useful to first define a set of &quot;[contract tests](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1mm98FhlCOfxxb39ZvSyBnmBI8BjkreQnBaqfg8nOM2A/edit?tab=t.0#heading=h.wzojlv149m4r)&quot; at the interface layer\.

Stubs or dummies can be used judiciously when the interaction is trivial\.

## Hermetic

When external dependencies are needed, leverage testcontainers to download and run them locally\. This should only be for when this is essential\. For example, we can&#39;t test a PostgresStore without a Postgres\. Writing a &quot;fake&quot; postgres is absurd 🙂\. But, if you need to test business logic that involves a repository, using a real postgres is overkill\. Just use the in memory fake \(e\.g\. a custom in memory implementation, or sqlite with an in memory database, etc\.\)\.

&lt;&#x2F;standards&gt;

## Workflow

1. Read specs&#x2F;spec\.md\. This is your source of truth, and overarching vision\.
2. Read specs&#x2F;tasks&#x2F;\*\. See what work has been done, and determine the next task to complete\. Valid progress is `not\-started` `in\-progress` `ready\-for\-review` `complete` `needs\-revision` \. You should pick the tasks with the lowest number in its name that is either `not\-started` or `needs\-revision` \. Prioritize `needs\-revision` tasks over `not\-started` ALWAYS\.
3. Complete the task\. Completion criteria is alignment with the task &amp; relevant portion of the spec\. A separate team is working in competition with you trying to find bugs &amp; inconsistencies with your work\. Your jobs is to make them not have anything to find\.
4. Before marking the task `ready-for-review`, run the self-verification checklist:
   1. **No dead code — including dead feature scaffolding, blank-identifier discards, and unpopulated struct fields:** Every type, struct, const, or function you defined is referenced by at least one other definition or test\. If a type exists only for itself, either wire it into the types that should use it or remove it\. **Structural references are not sufficient\.** A type that is referenced in field declarations, error-formatting code, or conditional checks but is never *instantiated* (no literal construction, no `append`, no assignment of a value of that type) is dead feature scaffolding — the feature it represents does not function\. After defining any error type or struct, grep for its name and verify that at least one code path *creates* a value of that type\. If no code path populates it, either wire it into the logic that should produce it or remove it entirely\. **The blank identifier (`_ = expr`) is NOT a reference — it is a discard.** A variable assigned to `_` is dead by definition: the value is computed and immediately thrown away. The `_ =` pattern is particularly insidious because it superficially looks like the variable is "used," passing a casual grep for the variable name. After writing any code that produces a value (constructor call, operator creation, function return), grep for that variable and verify it is consumed by downstream code — not discarded via `_ =`. If you find `_ = varName`, either wire `varName` into the code that should consume it (e.g. pass it to a constructor, register it with a handler) or remove the creation entirely. A generated `main.go` that creates operators and discards them with `_ =` satisfies "shows all fills" but violates "shows all connections" — the operators are orphaned\. **Struct fields that are defined but never assigned a non-zero value are dead feature scaffolding at the field level.** A struct may be fully instantiated, but if a specific field on that struct is never set to a meaningful value by any code path, the field is dead — it serializes as its zero value (empty string, 0, nil) and misleads consumers into thinking the data is populated. After defining any struct field, grep for assignments to that field (e.g. `.FieldName =` or `FieldName:` in struct literals) and verify at least one code path sets it to a non-zero value. If the spec requires the field to carry data (e.g. "pinned to exact registry SHAs"), a field that is always zero violates the spec even though it compiles. Either wire the field into the code that should populate it or remove it until the populating logic is implemented\.
   2. **No lossy type mappings:** Do not use `any`, `interface{}`, or `map[string]interface{}` for fields where the spec defines concrete structure\. If the spec shows a field with known sub-fields or recursive nesting, model it with a concrete Go type (including recursive types like `map[string]ConfigField`)\. Reserve `any` only for fields the spec explicitly marks as free-form or opaque\.
   3. **Polymorphic YAML fields:** If the spec shows a single YAML key used in multiple structural forms (e.g. `items` appearing as both a map of named sub-fields AND a single inline object), you MUST handle all forms\. Write a custom `UnmarshalYAML` method that detects the shape at runtime and deserializes accordingly\. After implementing, write a test for EACH structural form using the spec's exact YAML\. A field that works for one form but fails for another is a bug\.
   4. **Exact naming from task description — types, methods, AND parameters:** Every type name AND method/function name listed in the task description or spec excerpt is the *canonical name*\. Your Go struct MUST use that exact name (e.g. if the task says "SlotDeclaration", you must define `SlotDeclaration`, not `SlotBinding` or any synonym)\. **This applies equally to method names on interfaces and contracts:** if the spec or task defines a DAO, service, or interface with methods named `Get`, `Replace`, `List`, `Upsert`, your implementation MUST use those exact method names — not synonyms like `Read` for `Get` or `Update` for `Replace`. Cross-reference every noun AND verb in the task and verify it appears in your code with the exact spelling AND is connected to the types the task says should reference it\. **Additionally, when the spec defines method signatures with specific parameters (e.g. `Create(ctx, entity)`, `List(ctx, listArgs)`), verify that your implementation includes ALL specified parameters with appropriate types.** A method that matches the spec's name but omits a parameter (e.g. omitting `context.Context` when the spec shows `ctx`) violates the contract just as much as a method with the wrong name\.
   5. **Tests must use spec-complete examples:** When writing tests that unmarshal YAML, use the full structure shown in the spec — do not simplify or omit sub-fields\. A test that passes on a simplified YAML subset can mask unmarshal failures in the fields you dropped\. If the spec shows `operations: { type: list, items: { enum: [...] } }`, your test YAML must include the `items` sub-field\.
   6. **Full task traceability:** Re-read the ENTIRE task file — description, spec excerpt, AND acceptance criteria\. Every requirement stated anywhere in the task (not just the "Acceptance Criteria" section) must be traceable to code\. This includes directory layouts, data formats, resolution orders, and behavioral descriptions in the "Description" section\. For each requirement, identify the exact line(s) of code that satisfy it\. If you cannot point to code that implements a stated requirement, the task is not complete\. Do not rely on your memory of what you wrote — grep for the relevant structs, fields, and method signatures\. **Code comments are not implementation evidence\.** A comment that says "id is always included" or "field X is validated" is a statement of intent — it does not cause the described behavior to happen\. When tracing a requirement to code, match it to executable statements (assignments, conditionals, function calls), never to comments, variable names, or docstrings\. A comment adjacent to a code block that describes behavior the block does not actually implement creates a false sense of completeness — the requirement appears to be addressed when it is not\. After finding a comment that describes a spec requirement, verify that the immediately following code actually performs the described action\. If the comment says "id is always included even if not listed" but the code simply parses user input into a slice with no `id` injection, the requirement is unmet regardless of the comment\.
   7. **Round-trip assertion completeness:** When writing round-trip tests (marshal → unmarshal → compare), assert on ALL fields of the resulting struct, not just top-level scalars\. Use `reflect.DeepEqual` or `go-cmp` to compare the entire struct\. A round-trip test that only checks `Name` and `Version` while ignoring nested fields like `Config`, `Slots`, or `Expose` will miss marshaling bugs in exactly the fields most likely to break\. If a field is not equal after round-trip, the marshal or unmarshal path for that field is broken\.
   8. **Marshal/unmarshal symmetry:** Whenever you write a custom `UnmarshalYAML` (or `UnmarshalJSON`, etc.), verify that the corresponding `MarshalYAML` produces output compatible with your custom unmarshaler\. If you rely on the default struct marshaler but wrote a custom unmarshaler that expects a different shape (e.g. a flat map vs. struct keys), round-trip will silently break\. Write or verify a round-trip test that exercises the custom path specifically\.
   9. **Compile check:** Run `go build ./...` and `go vet ./...` and confirm zero errors\.
   10. **Error unwrapping correctness:** If you wrap errors with `fmt.Errorf("...%w", err)` and later need to inspect the original error type, you MUST use `errors.As()` (or `errors.Is()` for sentinel errors), never a direct type assertion like `err.(*SomeType)`. A direct type assertion on a wrapped error always returns `ok == false` because the outer type is `*fmt.wrapError`, not the inner error. After writing any error-inspection code, trace the error from its origin through every `fmt.Errorf` or `errors.Join` call to the inspection point and verify the unwrapping method matches the wrapping depth. If in doubt, grep for the error variable name and read every intermediate assignment\.
   11. **End-to-end feature-path tests:** For every behavioral requirement in the task description (not just data-shape requirements), write at least one test that exercises the feature end-to-end: real input → production code path → assert on the output that proves the feature works\. A test that only checks formatting of a manually constructed output, or only asserts on a subset of output fields, does not prove the feature works\. For example, if the task requires "structured errors with line context," feed a malformed file through the real parser and assert that the returned error contains a nonzero line number and nonempty context string extracted from the file — do not construct the error by hand\.
   12. **Re-verify after fixes — including acceptance criteria regression:** When addressing `needs-revision` findings, re-run the ENTIRE self-verification checklist after applying fixes\. A fix for one finding can introduce a new defect (e.g. changing a field type may break unmarshal for a different usage pattern)\. Do not mark `ready-for-review` until all checklist items pass on the post-fix state\. **Critically, when a fix removes or replaces a code path, re-read every acceptance criterion and verify each one is still independently satisfied by remaining code AND covered by a test\.** Removing a code path can silently un-satisfy a criterion that was previously met\. For example, if removing auto-discovery also removes the only test for ambiguous port errors, the "clear error on ambiguous port" criterion is now unmet — even though the fix itself is correct\. After any code removal, enumerate every acceptance criterion and confirm: (a) a code path still implements it, and (b) a test still exercises it\. If a criterion lost its implementation or test coverage, restore it before marking ready-for-review\.
   13. **No silent map key collisions:** Whenever you index items into a map by a key derived from data (e.g. a `name` field from YAML), you MUST check whether the key already exists in the map before inserting\. If a duplicate key is found, return an error that identifies both the duplicate name and the sources (e.g. directory paths) that conflict\. Silent overwrites in maps are data integrity bugs — an artifact disappears with no error, and the user has no way to know\. After writing any map-insertion loop, grep for the map variable and verify every `map[key] = value` assignment is preceded by a duplicate check\.
   14. **Filesystem-identity consistency:** When loading artifacts from a directory structure where the directory name represents the artifact's identity (e.g. `registry/archetypes/<name>/archetype.yaml`), validate that the identity field in the loaded data (e.g. the YAML `name:` field) matches the directory name\. A mismatch between what the filesystem says an artifact is called and what the data says it is called is a contract violation\. Return an error identifying the directory name, the data name, and the file path\. Do not silently index by one and ignore the other\.
   15. **Complete directory layout implementation:** When the task description defines a directory layout (e.g. listing specific file paths like `components/<name>/slots/*.proto`), every path in that layout MUST be accounted for in the implementation\. "Accounted for" means at minimum: (a) the loader acknowledges the files exist or reports their absence, and (b) test fixtures include the files described in the layout\. If a layout element is explicitly deferred (e.g. "protobuf compilation deferred to task N"), the loader must still verify the files exist on disk\. A layout contract that is defined but not enforced will silently accept malformed registries\.
   16. **Parsed config validation:** After deserializing a config file (YAML, JSON, etc.) into a struct, validate that required fields are present and non-empty\. A struct that deserializes successfully from empty or minimal input is not necessarily valid\. If the spec or task defines required fields (e.g. "registry URL and pinned SHA"), write explicit validation that rejects configs missing those fields, and write tests that verify empty/incomplete configs produce errors\. Do not rely on downstream code to catch missing config — fail early with a clear message at the point of loading\.
   17. **Behavioral parity across artifact variants:** When you implement a validation, loading, or processing behavior for one artifact kind (e.g. component slot proto validation), you MUST apply the same behavior to every analogous artifact kind that shares the same structural pattern (e.g. mixin `adds_slots` with proto references)\. After implementing any per-artifact behavior, enumerate ALL artifact types that have the same field or sub-structure and verify each one receives equivalent treatment\. A validation that applies to components but not to mixins (or vice versa) when both declare the same field shape is a consistency bug\.
   18. **Fail on invalid preconditions, not silent degradation:** Public API functions that load from a path (directory, file, URL) MUST verify that the primary input exists before proceeding\. A non-existent path is not the same as an empty result — it is an error\. If the function returns an empty-but-valid result for a missing input, callers cannot distinguish "nothing found" from "looked in the wrong place." After writing any `Load`/`Open`/`Read`-style function, test that passing a non-existent path returns an error, not an empty success\. `readDirIfExists`-style helpers that swallow `os.IsNotExist` are appropriate for optional *subdirectories* within a known-valid root, but never for the root itself\.
   20. **No invented behavior:** Every code path must trace to a specific statement in the spec or task description. If the spec defines N resolution steps, your implementation must have exactly N — not N+1. "Seems useful" or "obvious extension" is not justification for adding behavior the spec does not describe. Unspecified behavior silently masks errors that the spec intends to surface (e.g. a missing binding that should be a compile error gets auto-resolved instead). After implementing, enumerate every behavioral code path and annotate which spec sentence authorizes it. If you cannot cite a spec sentence, remove the code path or flag the spec as needing amendment — do not ship unspecified behavior\.
   21. **Distinct error messages for distinct failure modes:** When multiple failure scenarios produce the same error type, each scenario MUST produce a distinguishable message (and ideally a distinct error type or code) so the user can identify the root cause without debugging. For example, "binding references non-existent component X" is actionably different from "no component provides port Y" — collapsing both into a generic "unresolved port" sends the user down the wrong diagnostic path. After writing error-handling code, enumerate every code path that returns an error of the same type and verify that each message uniquely identifies the failure scenario. If two paths produce the same message, either differentiate the messages or introduce a sub-type\.
   24. **Error message factual accuracy across all inputs:** When an error message makes a factual claim about runtime state (e.g. "no component provides it", "file not found", "no matches"), verify that the claim is true for EVERY set of input values that can reach that message. A single code path may handle multiple runtime scenarios — e.g. a branch guarding `len(providers) < 2` covers both 0 providers and 1 provider, but the message "no component provides it" is only true when providers == 0. After writing any error message that asserts something about runtime state, enumerate the range of values that reach it and confirm the assertion holds for all of them. If the message is only accurate for a subset, either split the code path to produce distinct messages per sub-case, or make the message general enough to be true for all cases (e.g. "no binding configured" instead of "no provider exists"). Factually wrong error messages are worse than vague ones — they actively misdirect the user\.
   22. **Degenerate and circular input guards:** For any algorithm that wires, resolves, maps entities to each other, OR traverses entity relationships (including recursive/iterative parent-chain functions like path builders and ancestor collectors), explicitly consider degenerate cases: self-references (A depends on A), mutual cycles (A depends on B depends on A), and identity collisions (two inputs with the same key). If the spec describes a cross-entity mechanism (e.g. component A's `requires` resolved by component B's `provides`), self-resolution is a degenerate case that must be prevented unless the spec explicitly allows it. **This applies equally to generator utility functions that walk parent chains** — a recursive function like `entityBasePath(entity, exposeMap)` that calls itself on the parent, or an iterative function like `collectAncestors` that follows parent links in a loop, will infinite-loop or stack-overflow on circular parent references (e.g. entity A declares `parent: B` and entity B declares `parent: A`). These functions MUST detect cycles (e.g. via a visited set) and return a descriptive error identifying the cycle. After implementing any resolution, graph-building, or parent-chain traversal algorithm, write tests for: (a) self-referencing input (entity declares itself as parent), (b) two-node cycle (A→B→A), and (c) duplicate keys. If the algorithm should reject these, verify it does with a clear error. If it should accept them, document why\.
   23. **No unreachable code or mislabeled tests:** Checklist item 1 covers unused *definitions*; this item covers unreachable *branches*\. After writing defensive checks (e.g. duplicate-key detection), trace the control flow to verify the condition can actually trigger given upstream constraints\. If an earlier check (e.g. name-must-match-directory) combined with an external invariant (e.g. filesystem uniqueness of directory names) makes a later check impossible to reach, remove the unreachable code — dead branches mislead readers and inflate coverage\. Similarly, every test function name must accurately describe the behavior it exercises\. If a test named `TestLoadDuplicateFoo` actually triggers a name-mismatch error, rename it to `TestLoadFooNameMismatch`\. Mislabeled tests hide gaps: a reader sees "duplicate" tested and moves on, not realizing the actual duplicate path is uncovered\.
   25. **Interface return types must match spec contracts:** When the spec or task says a component "returns," "produces," or "provides" a value alongside other values, the Go interface signature MUST include ALL described return values\. Defining a type without including it in the interface return signature is dead scaffolding — the type exists but no implementor is required to produce it, and no caller can access it\. After defining any interface, re-read the spec sentence that describes what implementors return and verify every noun in that sentence appears in the interface's return type list\. For example, if the spec says "each component's generator returns a Wiring struct alongside its files," the interface must return both `[]File` and `*Wiring` (or a composite type containing both) — not just `[]File`\. A type that is defined but unreachable through the interface contract is not implemented\.
   26. **Enforcement means invariant, not convenience:** When the spec or acceptance criteria uses enforcement language — "enforced," "enforcement," "required," "all X carry Y," "must" — the implementation MUST make violations impossible or detectable at validation time\. A convenience method (e.g. `WithHeader()`) that callers may freely skip is NOT enforcement\. Enforcement requires one of: (a) a constructor or factory that automatically includes the invariant so callers cannot omit it, (b) a validation function (analogous to `ValidateNamespace`) that rejects violations, or (c) a type design where the invalid state is unrepresentable\. After implementing any "enforced" requirement, ask: "Can a correct caller of this API accidentally produce output that violates the invariant?" If yes, the enforcement is missing\. Compare your mechanism against existing enforcement patterns in the codebase (e.g. `ValidateNamespace` rejects violations — does your feature have an equivalent?)\.
   27. **Declaration fields must exist on declaring types:** When the spec says "X declares Y" (e.g. "each component declares an `output_namespace`"), the Go type representing X MUST have a field for Y\. A standalone function that accepts Y as a bare parameter is only the *enforcement* half — the *declaration* half requires a field on the type so the value has a home in the data model and can be populated during deserialization, passed through the system, and accessed by the code that needs it\. After implementing any "X declares Y" requirement, verify: (a) the type for X has a field for Y, (b) the field is populated during loading/construction, and (c) the enforcement code reads Y from the type rather than requiring callers to supply it out-of-band\. If the declaration field is missing, the enforcement cannot be wired end-to-end\.
   29. **Generated output must use established generation infrastructure — ALL file types, not just Go source:** Any function that produces content intended for generated output files MUST return its result as a `gen.File` (or feed into the `gen.File` pipeline) rather than returning raw `[]byte` or `string`. The `gen.File.Bytes()` method is the single enforcement point for invariants like the mandatory `// Code generated by stego. DO NOT EDIT.` header — bypassing it by returning raw bytes creates a parallel code generation path that silently drops required invariants. **This applies to ALL generated file types, not just `.go` files.** If the generation infrastructure handles headers or markers differently per file type (e.g. Go comment syntax for `.go`, no header for `.json`), that logic belongs in `gen.File.Bytes()` — not duplicated in individual generator functions. A generator function that manually embeds a header string in `Content` (e.g. `fmt.Fprintf(&buf, "// Code generated by stego. DO NOT EDIT.\n\n")`) instead of letting `gen.File.Bytes()` handle it creates a fragile dual mechanism: any change to the header text requires updating both `gen.Header` and the hardcoded string, and the two can silently diverge. After writing any function that constructs output for generated files (look for `go/format`, `bytes.Buffer` with `fmt.Fprintf` of `package` or `module` declarations, or template rendering), verify it either: (a) returns `gen.File` directly with the header handled by `Bytes()`, or (b) is called by code that wraps its output in a `gen.File` before the output leaves the package. If neither is true, the generated output will be missing the header and will not be identifiable by drift detection. Grep for `format.Source`, `"package "`, `"module "`, and the literal header string `"Code generated by stego"` in your new code — these are signals of a generation function that needs to be checked\.
   30. **Config schema enumerations must be exhaustive against the spec:** When a config schema (e.g. `component.yaml`) defines an enum or list of allowed values for a field (e.g. `operations: [create, read, update, delete, list]`), cross-reference EVERY value against the spec's authoritative list for that concept. If the spec defines N valid values, the schema must list exactly N — not N-1. After writing any enum or allowed-values list in a config schema, find the spec section that defines the domain concept and diff the two lists character by character. A schema that omits a valid value will silently reject correct input; a schema that includes an extra value will accept invalid input. Both are bugs. If the generator code handles a value that the schema omits, the schema is the bottleneck — the feature is implemented but unreachable because validation rejects it before the generator ever sees it\.
   31. **Generated output must compile as a multi-file package — syntax parsing is NOT compilation:** When a generator produces multiple Go source files intended to coexist in a single package, tests MUST verify that the files compile *together*, not just individually. Checking that each file passes `go/format.Source` (syntax validity) or contains `package X` is necessary but NOT sufficient — it does not catch cross-file errors such as duplicate `case` branches in a `switch`, references to undefined types, or field assignments on empty structs. **`go/parser.ParseFile` and `go/format.Source` are syntax checks, not semantic compilation.** They verify that the source is grammatically valid Go, but they do NOT detect unused imports, unused variables, undefined identifiers, type mismatches, or any other semantic error that `go build` catches. A test that calls `go/parser.ParseFile` on generated output and calls itself a "compile check" is lying — it will happily accept `import "fmt"` in a file that never references `fmt`, which is a hard compile error in Go. **Every test that validates generated Go output must use actual compilation** (`go build`, `go/packages`, or `golang.org/x/tools/go/packages`) — never `go/parser.ParseFile` or `go/format.Source` alone. After writing any generator that produces Go files, write a test that: (a) collects ALL generated `.go` files into a temporary directory, (b) runs `go build` (or uses `go/packages` or `golang.org/x/tools/go/packages`) on that directory as a unit, and (c) fails the test if compilation fails. This applies to EVERY configuration variant (item 38) — including degenerate cases like zero routes, zero operations, or zero fields. A no-routes test that only syntax-parses will miss an unconditional `import "fmt"` that becomes unused when the `fmt.Sprintf` call in `writeServerStart` is not emitted. Additionally, verify that no two generated code paths produce the same `case` value in a `switch` or `select` — enumerate every operation-to-HTTP-method mapping and confirm the mapping is injective (no two operations map to the same method on the same route). If two operations collide (e.g. both `update` and `upsert` emit `case http.MethodPut`), the generator must either use a disambiguating mechanism (e.g. different routes, a query parameter) or reject the configuration at generation time with a clear error\.
   32. **Generated code must be functionally correct, not just syntactically valid:** When a generator emits code that calls standard library or framework APIs, verify that the emitted API usage is correct for the registration pattern used. Specifically: (a) if the generated code calls `r.PathValue(name)`, verify that the route registration uses `{name}` pattern syntax (Go 1.22+ ServeMux patterns) — `PathValue` returns `""` for routes registered without pattern parameters; (b) if the generated code assigns to struct fields, verify that the struct type definition includes those fields — assigning to a field on an empty `struct{}` is a compile error; (c) if the generated code decodes into a struct type (e.g. `json.NewDecoder(r.Body).Decode(&v)`), verify the struct has fields that match the expected JSON/YAML keys — decoding into an empty struct silently discards all input. After writing any generator, pick ONE representative generated output, read it as if it were hand-written code, and trace every API call to confirm the preconditions are met. The generator has access to entity metadata (fields, types, relationships) — if the generated code needs that metadata (e.g. to define struct fields), verify the generator actually threads it through\.
   33. **Fix-class exhaustion — same bug, different site:** When a bug is found and fixed (whether during self-verification or `needs-revision`), the fix addresses one *instance* of a *class* of error. Before marking the fix complete, grep the entire generator (or module) for every other call site that exhibits the same pattern. For example, if `r.PathValue("id")` was found to be non-functional because the route lacked `{id}` pattern syntax, search for ALL `r.PathValue(...)` calls and verify each one has a matching `{param}` in its route registration. If scope filtering uses `r.PathValue("org_id")` but no route declares `{org_id}`, that is the same class of bug in a different site. After fixing any bug, write down the pattern signature (e.g. "PathValue call without matching route param") and grep for it project-wide. Fix or flag every instance before proceeding\. **Concrete example — conditional imports:** if an unconditional `import "fmt"` was found unused when `hasRoutes` is false and the fix was to conditionalize it with `if hasRoutes`, the pattern signature is "unconditional standard library import that is only referenced in conditional code paths." Immediately grep for ALL other standard library imports in the same function (`"log"`, `"os"`, `"net/http"`, `"database/sql"`, etc.) and verify each one is either (a) referenced unconditionally, or (b) guarded by the same condition that gates the code path using it. A fix that conditionalizes `"fmt"` but leaves `"log"` unconditional — when `log` is only used in `writeDBSetup` (gated on `hasDB`) and `writeServerStart` (gated on `hasRoutes`) — is an incomplete fix-class exhaustion. The bug class is "unconditional import of a conditionally-used package," and EVERY import must be checked against it, not just the one named in the finding\. **Concrete example — string matching on opaque expressions (cross-reference item 60):** if `needsDB` was found to use fragile substring matching (`strings.Contains(expr, "(db)")`) against an opaque constructor expression, and the fix was to replace it with structured metadata (`NeedsDB bool` on the `Wiring` struct), the pattern signature is "function that introspects an opaque string representation to determine a structured property." Immediately grep the ENTIRE module for ALL other functions that perform substring matching, regex matching, or naming-convention-based inference on opaque string expressions (constructor expressions, route strings, import paths). Specifically, search for: (a) `strings.Contains`, `strings.HasPrefix`, `strings.HasSuffix`, or `strings.Index` applied to constructor expressions, wiring expressions, or any field documented as opaque; (b) functions that extract structured information (entity names, variable names, type associations) by parsing naming conventions (e.g. `New<Entity>Handler` → entity name); (c) any function whose correctness depends on an undocumented convention about the format of a string produced by a different module. Each such function is the same class of bug as `needsDB`: it recovers structured information from an opaque string heuristically, and the heuristic will fail when the string doesn't follow the assumed convention. Replace each with structured metadata on the appropriate data structure (e.g. add an `Entity string` field to the `Wiring` struct's constructor metadata instead of inferring the entity from the constructor function name). A fix that adds `NeedsDB bool` to eliminate one string-matching heuristic but leaves `extractEntityFromConstructor` (which uses `HasPrefix("New") && HasSuffix("Handler")` to infer entity associations) is an incomplete fix-class exhaustion — both are instances of the same bug class.
   34. **Generated specs must be valid for all structural variants:** When a generator produces specification documents (e.g. OpenAPI, protobuf, JSON Schema), tests MUST exercise every structural variant the generator can produce — not just the simplest case. Specifically: if the generator handles both flat and nested entities, both must be tested. For OpenAPI: (a) every path parameter placeholder (e.g. `{cluster_id}`) in a URL template MUST have a corresponding entry in the operation's `parameters` array; (b) tests must generate specs for entities with parent relationships (nested routes) and validate that all parent path parameters are declared; (c) run the generated spec through a validator (or at minimum, programmatically assert that every `{param}` in each path key has a matching parameter object). A test that only validates flat (non-nested) entities cannot catch missing parameter declarations in nested routes\.
   35. **Entity field attributes must be exhaustively handled by generators — in BOTH code AND specs:** When the spec defines per-field attributes that constrain how fields participate in operations (e.g. `computed: true` meaning read-only, `optional: true`, `immutable: true`), the generator MUST consult EVERY such attribute when emitting operation code AND when emitting specification documents (e.g. OpenAPI schemas). After implementing a generator for entity operations: (a) enumerate ALL field-level attributes defined in the spec's entity/field model; (b) for each attribute, verify the generator reads it and adjusts the generated output accordingly — e.g. computed fields are excluded from request schemas and write-operation decoders, optional/required status is reflected in the generated OpenAPI schema's `required` array, immutable fields are excluded from update handlers; (c) write a test with an entity that has at least one field with each attribute and verify the generated code AND the generated spec both respect each constraint. If the generator does not reference a field attribute at all (zero matches for the attribute name in the generator source), the attribute's constraint is not enforced — this is a spec violation, not an optional enhancement. **Specifically for OpenAPI:** if the spec distinguishes required from optional fields (e.g. fields without `optional: true` are required), the generated schema MUST include a `required` array listing all non-optional fields. An OpenAPI schema without a `required` array tells clients and code generators that all fields are optional — which misrepresents the entity's contract. **Field attributes include BOTH behavioral flags AND value-based constraints.** Behavioral flags (e.g. `computed`, `optional`, `immutable`) control which operations a field participates in. Value-based constraints (e.g. `min_length`, `max_length`, `pattern`, `min`, `max`, `default`) restrict the field's valid values or declare defaults and MUST be propagated to generated specs as standard schema properties (`minLength`, `maxLength`, `pattern`, `minimum`, `maximum`, `default`). Similarly, type precision distinctions (e.g. `int32` vs `int64`, `float` vs `double`) MUST be reflected in the generated spec's `format` field — mapping both to the same base type without a `format` loses precision information that API consumers and code generators depend on. After implementing field-to-schema mapping: enumerate every field attribute AND every distinct type/subtype in the spec's type system, and verify each one has an explicit mapping in the schema output. A type-mapping function that maps `int32` and `int64` both to `{"type": "integer"}` without setting `format` to `"int32"` or `"int64"` is losing information. A field with `min_length: 3` that generates `{"type": "string"}` instead of `{"type": "string", "minLength": 3}` is dropping a spec-defined constraint. **Exhaustive enumeration is mandatory:** when fixing a subset of missing constraint mappings, you MUST cross-reference the spec's COMPLETE list of constraint attributes — not just the ones named in a specific finding. If the spec lists `default` alongside `min_length`, `max_length`, `pattern`, `min`, `max` as constraint attributes, ALL of them must be mapped, including `default`. A fix that adds five of six constraint mappings leaves the sixth as a guaranteed follow-up finding\.
   28. **Field semantics must be consistent across all consumers:** When a struct field has a doc comment defining its semantic contract (e.g. "Content is the file body, excluding the generated header"), every function, method, and test that reads or writes that field MUST be consistent with the documented semantics\. After writing any function that operates on a documented field, re-read the field's doc comment and verify: (a) if you read the field, your assumptions match what the doc says the field contains, (b) if you write the field (including in tests), the value you assign matches what the doc says it should hold, and (c) convenience methods and validation functions on the same struct do not contradict each other\. For example, if a field is documented as "excluding the header," a validation function must not check the field for the header's presence, and tests must not populate the field with the header included\. A field whose documentation says one thing while code assumes another is an incoherent contract — it cannot be used correctly by any caller\. When you find a contradiction, resolve it by deciding on ONE authoritative semantic and updating all code paths and documentation to match\.
   36. **Tests must assert on rendered output, not intermediate representations:** When a generation pipeline has a rendering step (e.g. `gen.File.Bytes()` that adds headers, transforms format, or wraps content), tests MUST assert on the *rendered* output — not the intermediate `Content` field or pre-rendering buffer. The rendered output is what gets written to disk and consumed by downstream tools. A test that reads `f.Content` directly bypasses the rendering pipeline and cannot catch bugs introduced by it (e.g. a Go-comment header prepended to a JSON file, making it unparseable). After writing any test for generated output, verify: (a) the test calls the same rendering method that the file-writing code path uses (e.g. `f.Bytes()`, not `f.Content`), (b) for non-Go files (JSON, YAML, protobuf, XML), the test validates that the rendered output is parseable by its format's standard parser (e.g. `json.Unmarshal` on the rendered bytes of a `.json` file), and (c) if the rendering pipeline applies format-incompatible transformations (e.g. Go comments on JSON), the pipeline itself must be fixed or must support format-aware rendering — the test should catch this, not mask it\.
   37. **Shared semantics across operations must be explicitly enumerated and cross-verified:** When a generator emits code for multiple operations that share a semantic requirement, the shared behavior MUST be implemented in ALL operations that share it — not just the first one written. After implementing any operation method in a generator: (a) list every behavioral step in that method (decode body, check parent, assign parent ref, clear computed fields, call store, present/transform response entity, etc.); (b) for EACH step, ask "which other operations need this same step?" — e.g. parent ID assignment is needed by ANY write operation that accepts a request body on a nested route (create, update, upsert) because the decoded body could contain a conflicting or empty parent ref value; computed field clearing is needed by any operation that accepts input (create, update, upsert); (c) verify that every operation sharing the semantic actually contains the step. **The key invariant: if a field's authoritative value is derived from the URL path (e.g. parent ID in a nested route, OR the entity's own identity `id`), then EVERY operation that decodes a request body into an entity struct MUST override that field from the URL after decode — including `update`.** **This invariant applies to the entity's own identity field, not just parent reference fields\.** If `create` assigns `entity.ID = uuid.New().String()` (server-generated identity) and `upsert` assigns `entity.ID` when empty, then `update` MUST assign `entity.ID = id` from the path parameter before passing the entity to storage\. The path parameter `id` is the authoritative identity for the entity being updated — the entity decoded from the request body may have an empty, missing, or mismatched `ID` field\. An `update` that passes the body-decoded entity to `store.Replace()` without setting `entity.ID = id` stores a record whose `ID` field does not match the URL identity\. The response may look correct (if the presenter uses the path `id`), but the stored record is inconsistent — a subsequent read that queries by stored ID will fail or return wrong data\. An `update` that decodes the body without pinning the parent ref allows a client to silently reparent the entity or clear the reference by omitting it. This applies to `create`, `update`, AND `upsert` equally — the distinction between "persist new" and "modify existing" is irrelevant; what matters is whether the operation accepts a body that could overwrite a URL-derived invariant. A convenient heuristic: after writing all operation methods, diff them pairwise and justify every behavioral difference. If `create` has a line that `update` lacks, either there is a spec reason update should not have it, or it is a bug. **Specifically: if `create` and `upsert` both assign parent ID from path parameters after body decode but `update` does not, that is a bug — `update` accepts the same body shape and must enforce the same URL-path invariants.** **This principle applies equally to response transformations:** if a presenter function (e.g. `presentEntity`) adds metadata (`id`, `kind`, `href`) to entities returned by single-resource operations (create, read, update, upsert), the SAME presenter MUST be applied to each item in collection responses (list). A list endpoint that returns raw entities in its `items` array while create/read return presented entities is a shared-semantic violation — the presentation step is shared across ALL operations that return entity data to the client, not just single-resource operations. The spec's description "items -- array of presented entities" makes this explicit: list items are presented entities, not raw database records. After writing all operation methods including list, verify that every code path returning entity data to the client applies the same transformation chain. **Type-level consistency in response serialization:** Applying the same presenter function to all operations is necessary but insufficient — the VALUE passed to the presenter must also be of the same type (or go through the same type-conversion pipeline) across all operations. If Read/List operations pass storage-layer types (e.g. `storage.User` which includes infrastructure fields like `Meta` with `created_time`, `updated_time`, `deleted_at`) while Create/Update/Patch pass API-layer types (e.g. `api.User` which contains only declared entity fields), the presenter serializes different field sets depending on the operation — Read responses include undeclared timestamp fields that Create responses omit, even though both go through the same `presentEntity` function. The same entity returns different response shapes depending on which operation retrieved or modified it. After writing all operation handlers, compare the concrete type of the value passed to the response presenter in EACH handler and verify they are the same type — or that all paths go through the same type-conversion step (e.g. storage→API conversion) before reaching the presenter. If some operations pass storage types and others pass API types, the response fields WILL differ in ways not declared in the entity's field definitions.
   38. **Generator tests must exercise minimal and degenerate configurations — including spec output per variant:** When a generator conditionally emits code based on configuration (e.g. which operations are enabled, which fields exist, whether an entity has a parent), tests MUST include configurations that exercise each minimal subset — not just the "all features enabled" case. Specifically: (a) for operation-conditional code, test with a single-operation entity for EACH operation in isolation (e.g. delete-only, list-only, create-only) AND with an EMPTY operations list (zero operations) to catch conditional import or dependency issues (e.g. unused `"encoding/json"` import when only `delete` is exposed, or unused handler variable when zero operations are present); (b) for field-conditional code, test with zero-field and single-field entities; (c) for nesting-conditional code, test with zero-depth, one-depth, and two-depth nesting; (d) for feature-combination code (e.g. scope, parent, scope+parent, neither), test each combination independently — do not assume coverage of one combination implies correctness of others. **The degenerate boundary is zero, not one:** testing with a single operation catches conditional-import bugs, but testing with ZERO operations catches unconditional-emission bugs — code that always emits a dependency (import, variable declaration) regardless of whether any operation needs it. Both are required. The "all features" test proves the generator works for the common case; the minimal tests prove it doesn't break for edge cases. A generator that unconditionally emits a dependency (import, type reference, helper call) that is only needed by a subset of configurations will produce broken output for configurations outside that subset. **Critically, when a generator produces both runtime code AND a specification document (e.g. OpenAPI), each configuration-variant test MUST verify BOTH outputs** — a test that validates handler code for a variant but skips the corresponding spec output cannot catch spec/code mismatches specific to that variant (e.g. a handler that reads a query parameter in one configuration but the spec only declares query parameters in a different configuration). Apply checklist item 39 (bidirectional spec/code consistency) to EVERY configuration variant tested under this item, not just the primary configuration\.
   39. **Generated runtime behavior must match generated specification contracts — bidirectionally:** When a generator produces BOTH runtime code (e.g. HTTP handlers) AND a specification document (e.g. OpenAPI spec, protobuf service definition) describing that runtime behavior, the two MUST be consistent in every observable detail — **in both directions**. This means: (1) every contract claim in the generated spec must be fulfilled by the generated code (spec→code), AND (2) every observable behavior in the generated code must be declared in the generated spec (code→spec). After writing any generator that produces both code and specs, perform TWO passes: **Pass 1 (spec→code):** enumerate every contract claim in the generated spec and verify the generated code fulfills it. Common mismatches: (a) the spec declares `application/json` response content type but the handler never sets `Content-Type` — Go's `net/http` auto-detects as `text/plain` for JSON; (b) the spec declares required request fields but the handler accepts empty bodies; (c) the spec declares error response schemas but the handler writes unstructured strings. **Pass 2 (code→spec):** enumerate every observable behavior in the generated code and verify the generated spec declares it. Common mismatches: (d) the handler writes a JSON response body via `json.Encode` but the spec's response object has no `content` field — client code generators will treat this as a no-body response and discard the JSON the server sends; (e) the handler sets specific status codes not declared in the spec's `responses` map; (f) the handler reads query parameters or headers not declared in the spec's `parameters` array. For HTTP specifically: if a generated handler writes a response body (calls `json.Encode`, `w.Write`, or similar), the corresponding OpenAPI response object MUST include a `content` field with the appropriate media type and schema — an empty response description (e.g. `"201": {Description: "Created"}` with no `Content`) is a spec/code mismatch when the handler actually writes a body. Write a test that parses the generated spec, extracts declared content types and response schemas, and verifies the generated handler code sets matching headers\.
   40. **Generated code must use correct zero values for all Go type categories:** When a generator emits zero-value or nil assignments for typed fields (e.g. clearing computed fields, initializing defaults), the emitted code MUST use the correct zero value for the field's Go type. Go types fall into distinct categories with different zero values: pointer types and slices accept `nil`; value types like `time.Time`, `int`, `bool` require their typed zero (`time.Time{}`, `0`, `false`); strings require `""`; maps require `nil` or `make(...)`. A `switch` or conditional that emits zero values MUST have an explicit case for EVERY Go type the generator can produce — a `default` case that emits `= nil` will produce a compile error for any value type that reaches it. After writing any code that emits zero-value assignments: (a) enumerate EVERY type returned by the type-mapping function (e.g. `fieldTypeToGo`); (b) verify each type has an explicit zero-value case; (c) if a `default` branch exists, verify it is unreachable or emits a generation-time error rather than silently emitting an invalid assignment. Write a test for EACH distinct Go type the generator can encounter, with a field of that type requiring a zero-value assignment\.
   41. **Config schema properties must be exhaustive against the spec's structural definition:** When a config schema (e.g. `component.yaml`) defines an object with named properties (e.g. an expose block with `entity`, `operations`, `scope`), cross-reference EVERY property against the spec's authoritative definition of that object. If the spec defines N properties on a concept, the schema must declare all N — not a subset. This extends checklist item 30 (which covers enum values) to object properties. After writing any `properties:` block in a config schema: (a) find the spec section that defines the concept being configured; (b) list every attribute the spec assigns to that concept; (c) diff the spec's attribute list against the schema's property list and verify they match. A schema that omits a valid property will cause `stego validate` to reject correct service declarations that use the property — the feature is implemented in the generator but unreachable because the schema rejects its configuration before the generator sees it. Include properties even if they have no validation constraints — their presence in the schema declares them as recognized fields\.
   43. **Consistent value encoding across branches feeding the same interface parameter:** When multiple code paths (branches in an `if/else`, `switch` cases, or separate functions) compute a value that is passed to the same interface parameter, they MUST use the same encoding or transformation. For example, if an interface method accepts a `scopeField string` parameter and one branch passes the raw YAML field name (`"cluster_id"`) while another branch passes a Go-convention PascalCase transformation (`"ClusterID"`), the downstream implementation cannot handle both formats without ambiguity — one of the callers is wrong. After writing any function with multiple branches that feed the same downstream parameter: (a) identify the parameter's documented contract (what format does the receiver expect?); (b) enumerate every branch that produces a value for that parameter; (c) verify each branch applies the same transformation to produce the value. A useful heuristic: if one branch calls a transformation function (e.g. `toPascalCase`, `strings.ToLower`) on the value it passes, and another branch passes a raw/untransformed value, ask whether the mismatch is intentional. If the interface contract does not specify a format, the first branch to be written sets the de facto convention — all subsequent branches must follow it. Write a test for each branch in isolation and verify the passed value uses a consistent format\.
   44. **Generator fallbacks must fail, not guess:** When a generator function searches for metadata (e.g. a ref field linking an entity to its parent) and includes a fallback for when the search fails (e.g. constructing a name like `parent + "ID"`), the fallback MUST NOT silently produce a value that is emitted into generated code. A guessed fallback that happens to be wrong produces a compile error or runtime bug in the generated output — and the error message points at the generated code, not at the generator or the service declaration that caused it. Instead: if the required metadata is not found, the generator MUST return an error at generation time that identifies (a) what it was looking for (e.g. "a ref field with `to: Cluster`"), (b) which entity it was looking in, and (c) what configuration triggered the lookup (e.g. "`parent: Cluster` on expose block for NodePool"). After writing any generator function that searches entity metadata and has a fallback branch: (a) trace what happens when the fallback value is emitted — does it reference a struct field, variable, or type that may not exist? If so, it will produce a compile error; (b) replace the fallback with `return fmt.Errorf(...)` that explains the missing prerequisite; (c) write a test with an entity that triggers the fallback (e.g. `parent: Foo` with no ref field to `Foo`) and verify the generator returns a clear error rather than producing broken code. The acceptance criterion "Generator produces compilable Go handler code" is violated by any fallback that emits references to non-existent identifiers\.
   45. **Generated identifiers must not collide with Go reserved words:** When a generator derives Go identifiers (variable names, type names, function names) from user-provided names (entity names, field names, operation names), the generated identifier MUST NOT be a Go reserved keyword (`break`, `case`, `chan`, `const`, `continue`, `default`, `defer`, `else`, `fallthrough`, `for`, `func`, `go`, `goto`, `if`, `import`, `interface`, `map`, `package`, `range`, `return`, `select`, `struct`, `switch`, `type`, `var`) or a Go predeclared identifier (`bool`, `byte`, `error`, `int`, `string`, `true`, `false`, `nil`, `len`, `cap`, `make`, `new`, `append`, `copy`, `delete`, `print`, `println`, `complex`, `real`, `imag`, `close`, `panic`, `recover`). A generated statement like `var type Type` or `var map Map` is a syntax error. After writing any code that derives an identifier via `strings.ToLower(name)`, `toCamelCase(name)`, or similar transformations: (a) check whether the result could be a reserved word for any valid input; (b) if so, add a collision check that either escapes the identifier (e.g. appending an underscore: `type_`) or returns a generation-time error identifying the collision and the entity/field that caused it; (c) write a test with an entity or field name that IS a Go keyword (e.g. entity named "Type", field named "map") and verify the generator either escapes it or returns a clear error. The acceptance criterion "Generator produces compilable Go handler code" is violated by any identifier that collides with a reserved word\. **Critically, any reserved-name set used to validate user-provided names (e.g. `reservedTypeNames`, `reservedFieldNames`) MUST be the UNION of: (1) Go keywords, (2) Go predeclared identifiers, AND (3) generator-internal identifiers — not just one category. A reserved-name set that only contains generator-internal identifiers (e.g. `Store`, `NewStore`) but omits Go keywords (`func`, `type`, `var`) and predeclared identifiers (`error`, `string`, `any`) fails to prevent the most basic collisions. After creating or modifying any reserved-name set, enumerate all three categories and verify every member is present.**
   46. **Generated struct literals at polymorphic emission points must only populate fields that exist on ALL concrete types at that point:** When a generator emits a struct literal (e.g., `&slots.FooRequest{Input: ..., Caller: ...}`) and the concrete type varies by configuration (e.g., `BeforeCreateRequest` vs `ValidateRequest` depending on which slot is being wired), every field in the literal MUST exist on every concrete type that can reach that code path. A field that exists on one variant but not another produces a compile error (`unknown field X in struct literal of type Y`) for the variant that lacks it. This is distinct from item 32 (which covers fields on empty structs and API correctness) and item 96 (which covers populating all fields on a single request type) — this item covers **polymorphic emission points** where the target type is determined by upstream configuration and different types have different field sets. Concrete example: `emitBeforeSlot` emits `&slots.BeforeCreateRequest{Input: ..., Caller: &slots.Identity{}}`. The `Caller` field exists on `BeforeCreateRequest` (from `before_create.proto`) but NOT on `ValidateRequest` (from `validate.proto`, which has `Input` + `entity` instead). When the `validate` slot is bound as a before-slot, the generated code references a non-existent field — a compile error. Additionally, the `entity` string field on `ValidateRequest` is never populated because the emission code doesn't know it exists. After writing any code that emits a struct literal where the type name is computed or parameterized (e.g., `fmt.Fprintf(buf, "&%s.%s{", slotsAlias, param.RequestType)`): (a) enumerate every concrete type that the parameterized name can resolve to — grep the proto definitions, type definitions, or configuration space to find ALL types that can substitute at that emission point; (b) for each field in the literal, verify it exists on ALL enumerated types — if a field exists on some types but not others, the emission MUST be conditional on the type (driven by structured metadata about each type's field set, e.g., a per-slot-type field manifest derived from the proto definition); (c) for each concrete type, verify that ALL of its fields are populated (per item 96) — a polymorphic emission point that populates only the intersection of fields across all types is still incomplete if individual types have unique fields that are never populated; (d) write a test for EACH concrete type variant and verify the generated struct literal compiles against the actual type definition and populates all type-specific fields. **The fix pattern:** instead of hardcoding field names in the emission function, derive the field list from structured metadata (proto definitions, type introspection, or a per-slot-type configuration map) so that each concrete type gets exactly the fields it defines — no more, no less. **The principle: a struct literal is a contract with a specific type. When the type is polymorphic (determined at generation time by configuration), the literal must be polymorphic too — emitting one type's fields for another type's literal is a category error that the Go compiler will reject.**
   42. **Generated wiring and cross-package code fragments must respect Go visibility rules:** When a generator produces code fragments intended for use in a different package (e.g. `Wiring.Routes` expressions destined for `cmd/main.go` in package `main`, or constructor calls for use outside the generated package), ALL identifiers referenced in those fragments MUST be exported (capitalized in Go). Unexported (lowercase) methods, types, and functions are only accessible within their defining package — a wiring expression like `userHandler.create` is valid inside package `api` but produces a compile error when embedded in package `main`. After writing any code that produces cross-package code fragments: (a) identify every identifier in the fragment (method names, type names, function names, field names); (b) verify each identifier is exported by checking that its definition uses an uppercase first letter; (c) if the identifier is unexported, either export it (and update all in-package references) or restructure the wiring to avoid cross-package method references (e.g. by registering routes within the package and exposing only the fully-wired handler via an exported function). Write a test that extracts all identifiers from generated wiring expressions and asserts each one is exported. As a heuristic: any string interpolation that produces `%sHandler.%s` patterns should verify the method name operand starts with an uppercase letter\.
   47. **Generated type names must not collide with generator-internal identifiers:** When a generator emits user-derived type names (e.g. `type <Entity> struct { ... }`) into the same file or package as generator-internal definitions (e.g. `type Storage interface { ... }`, `type Handler struct { ... }`), the user-derived name MUST NOT collide with any generator-internal identifier. Unlike Go reserved words (covered by item 45), these are identifiers the generator itself defines — they are invisible to the entity author and vary per generator component. A collision produces a Go compile error (`X redeclared in this block`) that points at generated code, giving the user no indication that their entity name is the problem. After writing any generator that defines internal types, interfaces, functions, or constants alongside user-derived types: (a) enumerate every identifier the generator defines unconditionally (e.g. `Storage`, `Handler`, `NewRouter`, `ServeHTTP`); (b) before emitting each user-derived type name, check it against this set — if there is a collision, return a generation-time error that identifies the entity name, the conflicting internal identifier, and advises the user to rename their entity; (c) write a test with an entity name that matches each generator-internal identifier (e.g. entity named "Storage") and verify the generator returns a clear error rather than producing code with a redeclaration. The check should apply to the EXACT form used in code emission (e.g. if entity names are emitted as-is in PascalCase, check the PascalCase form). Maintain the reserved name set as an explicit list near the top of the generator so it is updated whenever new internal identifiers are added. Do not rely on downstream compilation to catch these — fail at generation time with a diagnostic message\.
   48. **Generated variable names must not collide with function-scoped identifiers in the generated code template:** When a generator derives a local variable name from user-provided input (e.g. `strings.ToLower(entityName)`) and emits it inside a generated function body, the derived name MUST NOT collide with any identifier already in scope within that function — including method receivers (e.g. `h`), parameter names (e.g. `w`, `r`), imported package aliases (e.g. `json`, `http`, `time`, `fmt`), AND any local variables the generator itself hardcodes in the same method body (e.g. `id` from `id := r.PathValue("id")`).
