Item 45 covers Go reserved words and predeclared identifiers; item 47 covers generator-internal type names at package scope; this item covers **function-level scope collisions** that neither of those catch. A generated statement like `var w W` inside `func (h *WHandler) Create(w http.ResponseWriter, r *http.Request)` redeclares the parameter `w` — a compile error. Similarly, `var json Json` shadows the `encoding/json` import alias, causing `json.NewDecoder(...)` to resolve to the local struct instead of the package — also a compile error. **Critically, the collision set must include generator-emitted local variables, not just externally-defined identifiers.** If the generator hardcodes `id := r.PathValue("id")` in Read/Update/Delete methods, then `"id"` is an in-scope identifier — an entity named "Id" or "ID" (whose `strings.ToLower` produces `"id"`) will produce `var id Id` in the same method body, causing a redeclaration compile error. These template-local variables are easy to overlook because they are not language-level reserved words, not import aliases, and not parameters — they are identifiers the generator itself introduces via hardcoded `fmt.Fprintf` calls. After writing any generator function that derives local variable names from user input: (a) enumerate ALL identifiers that are in scope in the generated function — receivers, parameters, every import alias used in the function body, AND every local variable the generator hardcodes via `:=` or `var` in the same method body; (b) add ALL of these identifiers to the collision-check set used by the name-escaping function (e.g. `safeVarName`); (c) write tests with entity names that produce collisions for each category: single-letter names matching receiver/params (e.g. entity "H", "W", "R"), names matching import aliases (e.g. entity "Json", "Http", "Time", "Fmt"), AND names matching generator-emitted locals (e.g. entity "Id", "ID" where the generator emits `id := ...`, AND entity "Err", "ERR" where the generator emits `entity, err := ...` in a dual-assignment), and verify the generator either escapes the name or returns a clear error. The collision set must be maintained alongside the reserved-words set and updated whenever the generated code template introduces new in-scope identifiers — whether those identifiers come from function signatures, imports, OR the generator's own hardcoded local variable declarations. **Key principle:** the collision domain for a generated variable name is not just the language's reserved words — it is the union of reserved words, predeclared identifiers, generator-internal type names, AND every identifier the generator's own code template places in scope at the point where the variable is emitted, including identifiers the generator itself introduces via hardcoded statements in the method body. **Mandatory audit step after modifying the collision set:** after adding any identifier to the set (e.g. when fixing a finding), you MUST grep the generator source for ALL `Fprintf` calls that emit `:=` or `var` declarations (e.g. `grep -n ':=' generator.go` filtered to `Fprintf` lines), extract EVERY left-hand-side identifier from those declarations, and confirm each one is present in the collision set. Do NOT stop at the specific identifier named in the finding — enumerate exhaustively. Dual-assignment patterns like `%s, err := h.store.Read(...)` introduce TWO identifiers: the user-derived `%s` AND the hardcoded `err`. Both must be in the set. A fix that adds one generator-emitted local but misses another in the same method body is an incomplete fix-class exhaustion (item 33). **See also item 188:** when adding a NEW `generate*Method` function, the trigger for updating `handlerScopeIdentifiers` is the creation of the method itself — not a modification of the existing collision set. Item 188 provides the specific audit protocol that must be followed whenever a new handler method template is introduced.
   49. **Configurable parameters must be used everywhere — no partial parameterization:** When a function, generator, or module accepts a configurable parameter (e.g. `ctx.OutputNamespace`, `ctx.BaseURL`), every reference to the concept that parameter represents MUST derive from the parameter — never from a hardcoded default value. A common error pattern: the parameter is threaded correctly into some code paths (e.g. file path construction uses `path.Join(ns, ...)`) but its default value is hardcoded in others (e.g. `"package api"` instead of `"package " + filepath.Base(ns)`, or `[]string{"internal/api"}` instead of `[]string{ns}`). This creates a latent bug: the code works as long as the parameter equals its default, but breaks silently when the parameter is overridden. After writing any function that accepts a configurable parameter: (a) identify EVERY place in the output (generated code, wiring metadata, file content, import paths, package declarations) where the concept appears; (b) grep the generator for hardcoded occurrences of the parameter's default value (e.g. if the default namespace is `"internal/api"`, grep for `"internal/api"` and `"package api"` and `"api."`); (c) replace every hardcoded occurrence with an expression derived from the parameter; (d) write a test with a NON-DEFAULT parameter value (e.g. `OutputNamespace: "pkg/http"`) and verify all generated output — file paths, package declarations, import paths, wiring constructor qualifiers — consistently reflects the non-default value. If any output still contains the default, the parameterization is incomplete\.
   50. **Parallel generation loops must iterate the same scoped collection:** When a generator produces multiple output artifacts (e.g. Go struct definitions AND OpenAPI schemas) from the same conceptual input (e.g. "entities exposed by this component"), every generation loop MUST iterate over the same scoped collection — not different collections that happen to overlap. A common error pattern: one loop correctly iterates over the scoped subset (e.g. `ctx.Expose` for router struct generation) while a parallel loop iterates over the broader superset (e.g. `ctx.Entities` for OpenAPI schema generation). This produces orphaned output: schemas for entities that have no corresponding API paths, struct definitions for entities that are not exposed, etc. After writing any generator that produces multiple output artifacts from entity data: (a) list every `for ... range` loop that iterates over entities or entity-like collections; (b) for each loop, determine whether the loop's output should cover ALL entities in the context or only the EXPOSED/CONFIGURED subset; (c) verify that loops producing output for the same scope use the same collection variable — if one uses `ctx.Expose` and another uses `ctx.Entities`, ask whether the difference is intentional and justified; (d) write a test where `ctx.Entities` contains MORE entities than `ctx.Expose` (e.g. a ref-target entity that is not itself exposed) and verify that the unexposed entity does NOT appear in any output artifact where it doesn't belong (no orphaned OpenAPI schema, no orphaned struct definition, no orphaned route). The broader collection (e.g. `ctx.Entities`) is appropriate for building lookup maps used to resolve references, but NOT for driving output generation\.
   51. **Cross-references within code generation input must resolve within the configured scope — across ALL generation stages:** When ANY code generation stage (generator, assembler, OR reconciler) processes input configuration containing named references between configured entities, the referenced entity MUST be present in the scoped collection that drives the downstream behavior depending on it. A reference to an entity outside the scope is an invalid precondition — the generation stage cannot produce correct output because the referenced entity has no corresponding infrastructure (no route, no handler constructor, no path segment, no wiring target). **This applies to ALL code generation stages, not just generators.** Generators resolve cross-references within their `Generate` function (e.g. `parent: Cluster` on an expose block must resolve within `ctx.Expose`). Assemblers resolve cross-references when wiring independently-generated artifacts together (e.g. slot binding entities must resolve within the expose list that drives constructor generation). Reconcilers resolve cross-references when validating service declarations against registry constraints. A common failure pattern in generators: the path-building function checks `exposeMap[parent]` and silently falls through when the lookup fails, producing a URL without the parent's path segment. A common failure pattern in assemblers: slot wiring builds a decision set (e.g. which variables to inject vs. suppress) from ALL entity-associated slot bindings, assuming every entity has a matching constructor — but constructors only exist for exposed entities. When a slot binding declares an entity not in the expose list, the slot variable is created without `_ =` suppression, never injected into any constructor, and produces a Go compile error (`declared and not used`). After writing any code generation logic that resolves named references between configured entities — whether in a generator's `Generate` function, an assembler's wiring logic, or a reconciler's validation: (a) before emitting any code or making decisions based on the reference, validate that every named reference resolves within the appropriate scoped collection — expose block parents resolve within the expose list, slot binding entities resolve within the expose list (which drives constructor generation), ref targets resolve within the entity collection; (b) if a reference does not resolve, return an error that identifies the referencing configuration, the referenced name, the field that contains the reference, and the collection it should resolve within (e.g. "slot binding declares entity 'Gadget' but Gadget is not in the expose list — slot operators can only be wired to entities that have handler constructors"); (c) write a test for each type of cross-reference with an input where the reference does not resolve and verify a clear error is returned at generation time — not silently broken output; (d) the validation should run early (before any code emission or decision-set derivation) so that all cross-reference errors are reported together, not discovered one at a time during generation or compilation.
   52. **User-provided path templates are the authoritative source of parameter names:** When a generator supports user-overridable path templates (e.g. `path_prefix` on an expose block), all code that extracts values from the HTTP request path MUST derive parameter names from the actual path template being used — not from entity or type names via a naming convention. When the path is auto-derived, the convention and the template agree by construction. But when the path is user-provided, the user's parameter names become authoritative — a `path_prefix: /orgs/{org_id}/users` means the parameter is `{org_id}`, regardless of whether the parent entity is named `Organization` (which would auto-derive `{organization_id}`). A generator that uses `r.PathValue(strings.ToLower(parent) + "_id")` while the route template contains `{org_id}` will silently return `""` for every request — the code compiles but is semantically broken. After writing any generator that supports user-overridable path templates alongside auto-derived paths: (a) identify EVERY `r.PathValue(...)` call (or equivalent parameter extraction) in the generated code; (b) trace where the parameter name argument originates — if it is derived from an entity/type name via convention, verify that the route template also uses the same convention-derived name; (c) when `path_prefix` is set, the generator MUST either: (i) extract parameter names from the prefix template string (e.g. parse `{org_id}` from the path) and use those extracted names in `PathValue` calls, OR (ii) validate at generation time that the prefix contains parameters matching the expected convention and return an error identifying the mismatch (e.g. "path_prefix contains `{org_id}` but parent Organization expects `{organization_id}`"); (d) write a test with a `path_prefix` whose parameter names diverge from the auto-derived convention (e.g. `path_prefix: /orgs/{org_id}/users` with `parent: Organization`) and verify the generator either adapts the `PathValue` calls to use `org_id` or returns a clear error at generation time. **The invariant: route template parameter names and `PathValue` argument names must always agree — if one is overridden by user input, the other must follow or the mismatch must be detected.**
   53. **Configuration field-name references must resolve to actual entity fields:** When a generator's configuration contains string-valued fields that name entity fields (e.g. `scope: org_id`, `upsert_key: [resource_type, resource_id]`), the generator MUST validate at generation time that each referenced field name exists in the entity's field definitions. Item 51 covers cross-references between *entities* in the expose list; item 44 covers fallback guessing for missing metadata within an entity. This item covers the gap between them: *string-valued configuration that names specific fields on a known entity*. A scope or upsert_key that references a non-existent field produces code that compiles but is semantically broken — the storage layer receives a field name that doesn't correspond to any data, resulting in silent query failures or wrong results at runtime. The generator already has access to both the configuration (expose block) and the entity's field list — it is the first consumer that knows both. After writing any generator that accepts field-name references in its configuration: (a) at generation time, resolve each field name against the entity's `Fields` slice; (b) if a field name does not match any `Field.Name`, return an error identifying the configuration field (e.g. "scope"), the unresolved name (e.g. "nonexistent_field"), and the entity being configured; (c) apply this validation consistently — if `parent` ref fields are validated (as `parentRefFieldName` does), then `scope` and `upsert_key` must receive equivalent validation; (d) write tests with invalid field-name references for EACH configuration field that accepts field names, and verify the generator returns a clear error rather than producing silently broken code. **The principle: if the generator validates one category of field-name reference, it must validate ALL categories — inconsistent validation rigor means the unvalidated categories are guaranteed future findings.**
   54. **Generated identifier collision checks must cover dynamically-derived names from other entities, not just static sets:** Item 47 requires checking user-derived type names against a static set of generator-internal identifiers (e.g. `reservedTypeNames = {"Storage", "NewRouter"}`). This is necessary but insufficient when the generator derives ADDITIONAL type names from user input — specifically, when one entity's name is used to construct identifiers (e.g. `entity.Name + "Handler"` → `UserHandler`, `"New" + entity.Name + "Handler"` → `NewUserHandler`) that coexist in the same package with other entities' direct type names. If entity A is named "User" and entity B is named "UserHandler", entity A's handler type (`UserHandler`) collides with entity B's struct type (`UserHandler`) — a redeclaration compile error that no static set can catch because both names are user-provided. After writing any generator that derives type names, function names, or constant names from user input using a formula (e.g. `Name + "Handler"`, `"New" + Name + "Handler"`): (a) enumerate every name-derivation formula the generator uses; (b) before emitting any code, compute ALL derived names for ALL entities being generated together; (c) check for collisions BETWEEN entities — does entity A's derived name `X + "Handler"` equal entity B's direct name? Does entity A's constructor `"New" + X + "Handler"` equal entity B's constructor? (d) return a generation-time error identifying both entities and the colliding identifier; (e) write a test with two entities where one entity's name equals another entity's derived identifier (e.g. entities "User" and "UserHandler") and verify the generator returns a clear error. **The key insight: item 47's static `reservedTypeNames` set cannot grow to cover this because the conflicting names are computed from other user input in the same generation run. The check must be dynamic — computed after all entity names are known.**
   55. **Input collections must be validated for uniqueness before processing:** When a generator receives a list of configuration items that are indexed by a key (e.g. `ctx.Expose` indexed by `eb.Entity`), the generator MUST validate that the list contains no duplicate keys before building any lookup maps or iterating for code emission. Item 13 ("no silent map key collisions") requires checking before map insertion, but the deeper issue is that the input list itself may contain duplicates — and different consumers of the list handle this differently: a map silently overwrites (keeping the last entry), while a slice iteration emits duplicate declarations. This creates an inconsistency: the map sees one definition while the iteration sees two, producing symptoms like duplicate `type` declarations alongside a single handler (or vice versa). After writing any generator or processor that receives a list and indexes it by key: (a) before ANY processing, scan the list for duplicate keys and return an error identifying the duplicates (e.g. "entity 'User' appears in 2 expose blocks"); (b) do NOT rely on the map-insertion check from item 13 as the sole guard — by the time you're inserting into a map, other code paths may have already iterated the raw list and emitted duplicate output; (c) place the deduplication check at the TOP of the `Generate` function, before the `exposeMap` is built, so it is impossible to reach any code-emission logic with duplicate input; (d) write a test with duplicate entries in the input list and verify the generator returns a clear error. **The principle: validate input invariants once at entry, not piecemeal at each consumer. A list that "should not" contain duplicates WILL contain duplicates — the question is whether the generator catches it with a clear error or produces a cascade of confusing downstream failures.**
   56. **Required configuration collections must be validated as non-empty:** When a generator processes configuration items that contain lists (e.g. `Operations` on an expose block), the generator MUST validate that lists required for meaningful output are non-empty. An empty operations list means no handler methods are generated, but the generator may still unconditionally emit scaffolding that depends on those methods — imports (e.g. `"net/http"`) that are only referenced by handler methods, handler constructor calls that produce unused variables, or type definitions that are never instantiated. In Go specifically, unused imports and unused variables are compile errors. The `len(collection) == 0` early return that guards the top-level input (e.g. "zero expose blocks → return empty output") is necessary but insufficient — it does not guard against individual items within the collection having empty sub-collections. After writing any generator that iterates over a configuration list and conditionally emits code for each item: (a) identify which sub-fields of each item drive code emission (e.g. `Operations` drives handler method generation, `Fields` drives struct field generation); (b) if zero elements in that sub-field means the emitted scaffolding has no consumers (unused import, unused variable), either skip the scaffolding when the sub-field is empty, or validate at generation time that the sub-field is non-empty and return a clear error (e.g. "expose block for entity 'User' has no operations — at least one operation is required"); (c) write a test with each required sub-field set to an empty list and verify the generator either produces valid output (no unused scaffolding) or returns a clear error. **The principle: validate each item's preconditions, not just the collection's. A list of one item with zero sub-items is not the same as a list of zero items.**
   57. **User-declared names must be validated against generator-injected implicit identifiers:** When a generator unconditionally injects implicit identifiers into the output (e.g. an `id` primary key column, an `ID` struct field, a `created_at` timestamp column), user-declared names MUST be validated against these implicit identifiers at generation time. Items 45/47/48 cover collisions with language reserved words, generator-internal type names, and function-scoped variables — but none covers identifiers the generator injects implicitly into every entity. These implicit identifiers are invisible to the user (they appear nowhere in the entity definition) and collision produces failures that point at generated code rather than at the user's input. After writing any generator that unconditionally adds fields, columns, variables, or type members to its output: (a) enumerate every implicit identifier the generator injects — check every `Fprintf` or string literal that emits a field, column, or variable NOT derived from the entity's declared fields (e.g. a hardcoded `ID string` struct field, a hardcoded `"id" TEXT PRIMARY KEY` column); (b) during the validation phase, check each user-declared field/entity name (and all case-transformed variants that would collide — e.g. `toPascalCase("id")` → `"ID"`, `toPascalCase("Id")` → `"ID"`) against the implicit identifier set; (c) return a generation-time error identifying the user-declared name, the implicit identifier it collides with, and why the collision is invalid (e.g. "field 'id' collides with the implicit primary key column — entities cannot declare a field named 'id'"); (d) write tests with user-declared names that match each implicit identifier (including case variants) and verify the generator returns a clear error. **The principle: if the generator adds it implicitly, the user cannot also declare it explicitly — and the generator must say so at generation time, not at compile time or migration time.**
   59. **Assembler-level identifier deduplication — collisions across components, not just within a generator:** Items 45–48 and 54 cover identifier collisions within a single generator's output (entity names vs. reserved words, generator-internal types, function-scoped variables). But the assembler (or any code that combines outputs from multiple generators/components into a single file like `main.go`) operates at a HIGHER scope: it merges identifiers from DIFFERENT components, fills, and import paths into one compilation unit. Collisions at this level are invisible to individual generators because each generator only sees its own output. Four specific collision classes arise at the assembler level: **(a) Import alias collisions:** When deriving import aliases from `path.Base(importPath)`, two distinct import paths with the same base (e.g. `internal/api/models` and `internal/storage/models`) produce duplicate `models` aliases. Similarly, when deriving fill import aliases by stripping separators and lowercasing (e.g. fill names `"a-b"` and `"ab"` both produce `"ab"`), duplicate aliases result. Both are Go compile errors. **(b) Constructor variable name collisions:** When deriving local variable names from constructor expressions (e.g. `a.NewStore(db)` and `b.NewStore(db)` both produce variable `store`), two `:=` assignments to the same variable in the same function scope is a Go compile error. **(c) Slot operator variable name collisions:** When deriving slot operator variable names from slot binding metadata (e.g. `snakeToCamel(sb.Slot) + "Gate"`), the derivation formula MUST include ALL distinguishing dimensions — not just the slot name. If the formula omits the entity dimension, two bindings with the same slot name but different entities (e.g. `{Slot: "before_create", Entity: "User"}` and `{Slot: "before_create", Entity: "Organization"}`) both produce `beforeCreateGate` — a duplicate short variable declaration in the same function scope. Every derivation formula must incorporate every dimension that can vary independently in the input. If slot bindings can vary by entity, the variable name must include the entity. If two entities' variable names still collide after including the entity dimension, the disambiguation map must catch it. **(d) Any identifier derived from component/fill metadata by a lossy transformation** (lowercasing, stripping characters, taking path basename) can collide when two inputs differ only in the information the transformation discards. **Mandatory audit step for slot operator variables:** after writing any code that derives slot operator variable names, enumerate every dimension of the `SlotDeclaration` type (Slot, Entity, operator type) and verify each dimension that can vary independently is incorporated into the variable name derivation. A derivation that uses `slot + operatorType` but omits `entity` will collide whenever the same slot is bound to multiple entities — and the lookup map that connects variables to handler constructors (e.g. `buildSlotVarsByEntity`) will map different entities to the same variable name, compounding the collision into a semantic error even if the declaration-level collision is somehow resolved. After writing any assembler or combiner that derives identifiers from component/fill/import metadata: (a) enumerate every derivation function (e.g. `constructorVarName`, `fillImportAlias`, `path.Base` for import aliases, slot operator variable name derivation); (b) for each function, identify what information it discards (case, separators, path prefix, entity name) and construct two inputs that differ ONLY in that discarded information; (c) implement collision detection: after computing ALL derived identifiers for a given scope (all import aliases in an import block, all variable names in a function body), check for duplicates and either disambiguate automatically (e.g. append a numeric suffix: `models`, `models2`) or return a generation-time error identifying the colliding inputs; (d) write a test for each derivation function with two inputs that produce the same derived identifier and verify the assembler either disambiguates or returns a clear error. **The principle: any lossy transformation applied to user-controlled or component-controlled input WILL eventually produce a collision. The assembler cannot assume inputs are globally unique after transformation — it must detect and handle the collision explicitly. When a derivation formula omits a dimension that varies in the input data, the formula is lossy by construction — it is not a question of whether collisions will occur, but when.**
   60. **No string matching on structured or opaque expressions — use structured metadata:** When code needs to introspect the properties of a value that has structured metadata available (e.g. constructor expressions, type signatures, configuration objects), it MUST use the structured metadata — never substring matching against an opaque string representation. A function like `needsDB` that checks for literal substrings `(db)` and `(db,` in a constructor expression string is fragile in three ways: (1) whitespace variations (`NewStore( db )`) break the match; (2) superset names (`dbs`, `dbConn`) false-positive; (3) the convention that constructor expressions must emit exactly `(db)` or `(db,` is undocumented and unenforceable. After writing any function that inspects a string representation to determine a property of the underlying value: (a) ask whether the property is available in structured form — e.g. does the `Wiring` struct, component config, or generator context already carry a `NeedsDB bool` or `Dependencies []string` field? If so, use it; (b) if the structured metadata does not exist, add it to the appropriate data structure at the point where the information IS known (the generator knows whether it emitted a `db` parameter) and thread it through to the consumer; (c) if you MUST match against a string (e.g. parsing external input with no schema), document the exact format contract, validate the input conforms to it, and write tests for edge cases (whitespace, substrings, supersets). **The principle: string matching on structured data is a lossy projection — it discards structure and recovers it heuristically. Every heuristic has failure modes. Use the structure directly.**
   58. **Identifier quoting/escaping functions must fully implement their target system's specification:** When a generator introduces a helper function to quote or escape identifiers for a target system (e.g. SQL double-quoting via `sqlQ`, Go backtick-escaping, JSON string escaping), the function MUST implement the COMPLETE escaping rules defined by the target system's specification — not just the wrapping. Common incomplete patterns: (a) SQL double-quoted identifiers (`"my_col"`) — PostgreSQL §4.1.1 requires doubling embedded double quotes (`"my""col"` for identifier `my"col`), but a naive implementation only wraps without escaping; (b) SQL single-quoted string literals — require doubling embedded single quotes (`'it''s'`); (c) Go string literals — require escaping backslashes, quotes, and control characters. A quoting function that only wraps produces syntactically broken output when the input contains the quote character itself. After writing or reviewing any identifier quoting/escaping helper: (a) identify the target system's specification for quoted identifiers (e.g. PostgreSQL §4.1.1, SQL standard §5.2, Go spec §String literals); (b) verify the function handles the escape-within-quote case (e.g. embedded double quotes in a double-quoted identifier); (c) write a test with an input that contains the quote character itself (e.g. field name `col"x` for SQL double-quoting, value `it's` for SQL single-quoting) and verify the output is valid per the target system's specification; (d) apply item 33 (fix-class exhaustion) — if the generator has multiple quoting/escaping functions (e.g. `sqlQ` for identifiers and `sqlStringLiteral` for values), verify ALL of them handle their respective escape cases. **The principle: a quoting function's contract is not "wrap in quotes" — it is "produce a valid quoted identifier per the target system's specification." The escaping IS the function's core responsibility, not an edge case.**
   59. **User-provided names must be validated against a safe character set for all target identifier systems:** When a generator accepts user-provided names (entity names, field names, operation names) and transforms them into identifiers for one or more target systems (e.g. Go struct fields via `toPascalCase`, SQL columns via `toSnakeCase`, URL path segments), the generator MUST validate that each name consists only of characters that can produce valid identifiers in ALL target systems. Items 45/47/48 catch collisions with specific reserved words; item 58 ensures quoting functions handle special characters correctly; this item prevents invalid characters from reaching those functions in the first place. A field name containing hyphens (`foo-bar`), leading digits (`123`), spaces, or non-ASCII punctuation will produce invalid Go identifiers (`Foo-bar` is not a valid Go identifier), and the failure surfaces only as an opaque `format.Source` error with no indication of which field name is problematic. The generator should validate names early — during the validation phase, before any code emission — and return a clear error identifying the offending name, the invalid character(s), and the expected pattern. After writing any generator that accepts user-provided names: (a) identify every target system the names are transformed into (Go identifiers, SQL identifiers, URL segments, file paths); (b) determine the intersection of valid character sets across all targets — for Go + SQL + URLs, a safe pattern is `^[a-zA-Z_][a-zA-Z0-9_]*$`; (c) validate each user-provided name against this pattern during the validation phase; (d) return an error that names the specific field/entity, shows the invalid character, and states the allowed pattern; (e) write tests with names containing hyphens, leading digits, spaces, embedded quotes, and unicode characters, and verify the generator returns clear errors for each. **The principle: validate the character set of user input at entry, not at the point where a downstream transformation produces an opaque error. The generator knows which target systems it emits for — the user does not.**
   78. **Generated route patterns must be unique across all entities — detect path collisions at generation time:** When a generator registers multiple route patterns (e.g. `mux.HandleFunc("GET /items", ...)`) or writes multiple path entries into a specification document (e.g. OpenAPI `paths`), it MUST validate that no two entities produce the same effective route pattern. Collisions can arise from: (a) two entities with explicit `path_prefix` values that resolve to the same path; (b) two entity names that are case-insensitively equivalent (e.g. "Item" and "ITEM" both producing `/items`); (c) an auto-derived path that matches another entity's explicit `path_prefix`. The consequences of undetected collisions are severe and vary by output type: Go 1.22's `http.NewServeMux` panics at runtime on duplicate pattern registrations; OpenAPI path map assignments silently overwrite, causing the first entity's operations to disappear; and handler variable names derived via `strings.ToLower(entity)` collide, producing a compile error. After writing any generator that produces route registrations or path-keyed output: (a) after computing all base paths (before emitting any code), collect them into a map keyed by the effective path pattern; (b) if two entities map to the same path, return a generation-time error identifying both entities and the colliding path (e.g. "entities 'Item' and 'ITEM' both resolve to route path '/items'"); (c) include derived paths — both collection paths (`/items`) and item paths (`/items/{id}`) — in the collision check, since either can collide independently; (d) write tests with: (i) two entities with identical `path_prefix`, (ii) two entity names that are case-insensitively equivalent, and (iii) an auto-derived path matching another entity's explicit prefix — and verify the generator returns a clear error for each case. **The invariant: every route pattern the generator emits must be unique. If two entities need the same path, the service declaration is invalid and must be rejected at generation time — not discovered via runtime panic or silent spec corruption.**
   79. **Interacting configuration fields must be semantically consistent — validate coupling constraints at generation time:** When two configuration fields on the same item create an implicit semantic coupling (e.g. `scope` and `parent` on an expose block both influence how the list operation extracts filter values), the generator MUST validate that the coupling is coherent — not just that each field individually references valid data. Item 53 validates that field-name references resolve to actual entity fields; item 51 validates that cross-entity references resolve within scope. This item covers the gap: two fields that are individually valid but semantically contradictory when combined. A concrete example: when `scope` and `parent` are both set on an expose block, the scope+parent code branch extracts the scope value from the parent's path parameter (`r.PathValue(parentParam)`) and passes it as the filter for `eb.Scope`. This only produces correct results when `eb.Scope` IS the entity's ref field to the parent (e.g. `scope: org_id` where `org_id` has `type: ref, to: Organization` and `parent: Organization`). If `scope: department` with `parent: Organization`, the generated code extracts the organization ID from the URL and passes it as the "department" filter — semantically wrong, silently returning incorrect results at runtime. After writing any generator where two or more configuration fields jointly determine generated behavior: (a) identify every pair of fields that interact (both influence the same generated code path); (b) for each pair, determine the implicit contract between them — what must be true for their combined use to produce correct output; (c) validate that contract at generation time and return a clear error when it is violated (e.g. "expose block for 'User' sets scope: 'department' with parent: 'Organization', but 'department' is not the ref field to 'Organization' — when both scope and parent are set, scope must name the entity's ref field to the parent"); (d) write a test where both fields are individually valid but their combination is semantically incoherent, and verify the generator returns a clear error rather than producing silently wrong code. **The principle: individual field validation is necessary but insufficient when fields interact. If field A determines HOW a value is extracted and field B determines WHERE that value is used, the generator must verify that A and B agree on what value they are talking about.**
   80. **Metadata lookups must reject ambiguous matches, not silently select the first:** When a generator function searches entity metadata for a match (e.g. scanning fields for a ref with `To == parent`), and the function assumes exactly one match exists, it MUST detect and reject the case where multiple matches are found — not silently return the first. A first-match return is a form of implicit disambiguation that produces three classes of bug: (a) **incorrect assignment** — the wrong struct field is populated from a path parameter when the first ref field is not the intended parent reference; (b) **incorrect filtering** — list operations filter on the wrong field; (c) **false negative validation** — consistency checks (e.g. "scope must equal the parent ref field") compare against the first match, rejecting valid configurations that reference a different (later) match. The fix is straightforward: after finding the first match, continue scanning for additional matches. If a second match is found, return an error identifying the entity, the search criteria, and all matching fields — requiring the user to resolve the ambiguity at the declaration level. After writing any function that searches a list for a matching element and returns the first: (a) determine whether the match is guaranteed unique by construction (e.g. searching by a primary key) — if so, document why; (b) if uniqueness is NOT guaranteed (e.g. multiple ref fields can point to the same entity), add a continuation scan that detects and rejects duplicates; (c) write a test with an entity that has multiple matching elements (e.g. two ref fields to the same parent) and verify the function returns a clear error rather than silently selecting one. **The principle: "find first match" is only correct when "at most one match exists" is an invariant. When the data model permits multiple matches, the lookup must either accept a disambiguator or reject the ambiguity.**
   60. **Structural invariants implied by declarations must be validated eagerly, not lazily through code-emission paths:** When a configuration declaration implies a structural relationship (e.g. `parent: Cluster` implies the entity has a ref field to Cluster), the generator MUST validate that relationship at generation time in the upfront validation phase — not lazily inside operation-specific code-emission methods. Lazy validation creates a coverage gap: the invariant is only checked when a code path that needs the metadata is reached. If the set of operations exposed by the entity does not include any operation that triggers the lazy check (e.g. only `read` and `delete`, which don't call `parentRefFieldName`), the invariant is never validated. The entity is accepted, and the generated code compiles, but the nested routing is semantically hollow — ancestor existence is verified via `checkAncestors`, but parent-child ownership is never enforced because no ref field links them. Worse, adding an operation later (e.g. `create`) causes the generator to suddenly error on a previously-accepted configuration, which is surprising and breaks incremental development. After writing any generator that validates a structural relationship inside an operation-specific method: (a) identify the invariant being checked (e.g. "entity with parent must have a ref field to that parent"); (b) ask whether the invariant is inherent to the *declaration* (the `parent` field itself) or specific to the *operation* (only relevant for create/update); (c) if the invariant is inherent to the declaration — i.e., NO valid configuration exists where the declaration is set but the invariant is not needed — move the validation to the upfront phase at the top of `Generate`, alongside other declaration-level validations like `validateParentReferences` and `validateFieldReferences`; (d) write a test for every combination of operations (including read-only and delete-only) with the invariant-violating input and verify the generator returns an error regardless of which operations are exposed. **The heuristic: if a declaration field creates a relationship between two data-model elements (e.g. entity and parent), the relationship's prerequisites must be validated when the declaration is processed, not when a specific consumer of the relationship is emitted. The declaration is the contract; the operations are consumers of that contract.**
   61. **Name-normalizing transformations must produce unique outputs across all entities:** When a generator applies a normalizing transformation (e.g. `strings.ToLower`, `toCamelCase`, `toSnakeCase`) to entity names to derive identifiers (handler variable names, file paths, route paths), it MUST validate that the transformation produces unique outputs for all entities in the generation run — not just that the original names are unique. Item 55 validates exact-match uniqueness of the input collection; item 57 validates route path uniqueness. This item covers the gap: two entities whose original names are unique (e.g. "Item" and "ITEM") but whose normalized forms collide (both produce `"item"` via `strings.ToLower`). The consequences are severe and format-dependent: (a) handler variable names derived via `strings.ToLower(eb.Entity)` produce `itemHandler` for both entities — a duplicate variable declaration compile error in `router.go`; (b) handler file paths derived via `strings.ToLower(entity.Name)` produce `handler_item.go` for both — the second file silently overwrites the first in the output slice, losing an entire entity's handler code; (c) even when route paths don't collide (because of explicit `PathPrefix` values), the derived identifiers still collide because they go through the same normalizing transformation. Route collision detection (item 57) does NOT catch this when entities have different `PathPrefix` values — the routes are `/items` and `/things` (unique), but the handler variables are both `itemHandler` (collision). After writing any generator that derives identifiers from entity names via a normalizing transformation: (a) enumerate every transformation applied to entity names (lowercase for variables, lowercase for file paths, pluralization for routes, etc.); (b) for each transformation, compute the result for ALL entities and check for collisions; (c) if two entities produce the same normalized identifier, return a generation-time error identifying both entities and the colliding identifier (e.g. "entities 'Item' and 'ITEM' both produce handler variable name 'itemHandler'"); (d) write a test with entities whose names differ only in case (e.g. "Item" and "ITEM") with different `PathPrefix` values (so route collision detection does not catch it) and verify the generator returns a clear error. **The principle: uniqueness validation must be applied at the level of the derived identifier, not the source name. If a transformation is lossy (maps multiple inputs to the same output), the validation must detect collisions in the output space, not just the input space.**
   62. **Sub-collection elements must be validated for uniqueness before iteration:** Item 55 covers uniqueness of top-level collection items (e.g. no duplicate expose blocks by entity name). Item 56 covers empty sub-collections (e.g. zero operations). This item covers the gap between them: **duplicate elements within a sub-collection** of a configuration item (e.g. duplicate operations within a single expose block's `Operations` slice). When a generator iterates over a sub-collection and emits one output artifact per element (e.g. one handler method per operation, one route registration per operation, one OpenAPI operation per operation), duplicate elements produce duplicate artifacts — duplicate method declarations (compile error), duplicate route registrations (runtime panic with Go 1.22's `http.NewServeMux`), and duplicate spec entries (silent overwrite). The symptoms vary by output format, making the root cause hard to diagnose. A map-based consumer silently overwrites (keeping the last entry), while a code-emission consumer emits duplicates — the inconsistency between these behaviors is itself a bug. After writing any generator that iterates over a sub-collection within a configuration item and emits per-element output: (a) before iterating, scan the sub-collection for duplicate elements and return an error identifying the duplicates and the parent item (e.g. "expose block for entity 'User' has duplicate operation 'create'"); (b) place this check alongside the existing sub-collection validation (e.g. next to the empty-list check from item 56), so all sub-collection invariants are validated together; (c) write a test with a configuration item containing duplicate sub-collection elements (e.g. `operations: [create, create, read]`) and verify the generator returns a clear error rather than producing broken output. **The principle: item 55's uniqueness guarantee applies at every level of the configuration hierarchy, not just the top level. If a top-level list must not contain duplicate keys, a sub-list within each item must not contain duplicate elements — the same structural invariant, applied recursively.**
   63. **Derived names must be linguistically correct, not naively constructed:** When a generator derives user-visible names from entity or field names via string transformation (e.g. pluralization for table names, slug generation for URLs, label construction for UI), the transformation MUST produce correct results for ALL valid inputs — not just the common case. Naive transformations like `name + "s"` for pluralization produce incorrect output for inputs ending in 's', 'x', 'z', 'sh', 'ch' (e.g. `Address` → `addresss`, `Status` → `statuss`, `Box` → `boxs`). The generated output (SQL table names, API paths, labels) is user-visible and often part of a stable contract — a malformed name cannot be corrected later without a breaking migration. After writing any string transformation that produces user-visible output: (a) enumerate the linguistic rules the transformation must follow (e.g. English pluralization rules: -s → -ses, -x → -xes, -y → -ies for consonant+y, etc.); (b) either use a well-tested library (e.g. `jinzhu/inflection` for Go) or implement the rules exhaustively with tests for each rule; (c) write tests for at least: regular plurals (`User` → `users`), words ending in 's' (`Status` → `statuses`), words ending in 'x' (`Index` → `indexes`), words ending in 'y' after a consonant (`Entity` → `entities`), and irregular forms if supported. A test that only validates the common `-s` case will not catch the bug. **The principle: string transformations that produce stable, user-visible output are not string concatenation — they are linguistic functions with edge cases that must be enumerated and tested.**
   64. **Generated non-Go output must be syntactically valid for the target language:** When a generator emits output in a language other than Go (SQL, JSON, YAML, protobuf, shell scripts, etc.), the generated output MUST be syntactically valid according to the target language's grammar — not just "looks right" to a string-concatenation review. Checklist items 31 and 32 cover Go compilation and functional correctness; this item extends that rigor to ALL generated output formats. Specifically for SQL: (a) conditional clauses must be attached to the correct statement type — e.g. PostgreSQL's `ON CONFLICT ... DO NOTHING` does NOT support a `WHERE` clause; `WHERE` is only valid after `DO UPDATE SET`; (b) when a generator conditionally emits different SQL branches (e.g. `DO UPDATE SET` vs `DO NOTHING`), verify that post-branch modifiers (e.g. `WHERE`) are only appended to the branches where they are syntactically valid; (c) after writing any generator that emits SQL, read the generated SQL as if writing it by hand and verify each statement against the database's documented grammar. For other formats: JSON must have no trailing commas, YAML must respect indentation, protobuf must have valid field numbers. After writing any generator that produces non-Go output: (a) identify the target language's grammar rules that apply to the generated output; (b) write a test that feeds the generated output through the target language's parser or validator (e.g. `pg_query_go` for SQL, `json.Unmarshal` for JSON, `yaml.Unmarshal` for YAML); (c) if no parser is available, manually trace through the grammar rules for each conditional branch. **The principle: "it compiles" for Go code has an equivalent for every output format — "it parses." A generator that produces unparseable output in any format has a bug, regardless of whether Go compilation succeeds.**
   65. **User-provided string values interpolated into generated output must be escaped for the target format:** When a generator interpolates user-provided string values (field defaults, patterns, enum values, descriptions, comments) into generated output, the interpolation MUST escape the value according to the target format's escaping rules. Unescaped interpolation is an injection vulnerability in generated output — it produces syntactically broken or semantically wrong output when the value contains characters that are special in the target format. Common cases: (a) **SQL:** single quotes in string literals must be doubled (`'` → `''`) — a pattern like `^[a-z']` or a default like `it's` produces broken SQL without escaping; (b) **Go strings:** backslashes and double quotes must be escaped; (c) **JSON:** backslashes, double quotes, and control characters must be escaped; (d) **YAML:** values containing special characters must be quoted appropriately; (e) **regex patterns in SQL CHECK constraints:** the pattern itself may contain single quotes that break the enclosing SQL string literal. After writing any code that interpolates a user-provided value into generated output: (a) identify EVERY `fmt.Sprintf` or `fmt.Fprintf` call where a user-provided variable is inserted inside a target-language string literal (look for patterns like `'%s'`, `"%s"`, or similar); (b) for each such call, determine the target format's escaping rules and apply them to the variable before interpolation; (c) write a test with a value that contains the target format's special character (e.g. a string default of `it's`, an enum value of `foo'bar`, a pattern of `^[a-z']`) and verify the generated output is syntactically valid. **The principle: interpolation without escaping is always a bug. The question is not "will users put special characters here?" but "does the code handle it when they do?" If the answer is no, the generator has a latent injection/syntax bug.**
   66. **Every populated data model field must be consumed by its intended consumer:** When a data model type (struct) has a field that is populated during deserialization or construction (e.g. `UniqueComposite []string` populated from YAML), a downstream consumer (e.g. a generator) that processes instances of that type MUST read and act on the field if the field's semantics fall within the consumer's responsibility. Checklist item 1 covers unused *definitions* (types and functions that are never referenced). Checklist item 35 covers field *attributes* in generated specs. This item covers the gap: a field that is *defined and populated* but *never read* by the code that should use it — the feature the field represents is silently dropped. After writing any generator or processor that operates on a data model type: (a) enumerate ALL fields on the input type (e.g. `types.Field` or `types.Entity`); (b) for each field, determine whether it falls within the generator's responsibility — e.g. a storage generator is responsible for `UniqueComposite` (table-level constraints), `Computed` (read-only enforcement), `Default` (column defaults), etc.; (c) grep the generator source for each responsible field name — if a field is not referenced at all, the feature it represents is silently dropped; (d) for each consumed field, verify the generator produces the correct output (e.g. `UniqueComposite` → `UNIQUE (field1, field2)` constraint in migration DDL); (e) write a test with an entity that populates the field and verify the generated output reflects it. **The principle: a struct field that is populated but never read is worse than dead code — it is a silent feature gap. The data model promises the feature exists; the generator silently breaks that promise. After writing any generator, diff the input type's field list against the generator's grep hits and justify every field that is not referenced.**
   67. **Generated Go types must reflect column nullability from schema attributes:** When a generator maps schema field definitions to Go struct fields that will be used with `database/sql` scanning (e.g. `Rows.Scan`), the Go type MUST reflect whether the corresponding database column is nullable. A column is nullable when the field has `optional: true`, `computed: true`, or any other attribute that causes the migration DDL to omit `NOT NULL`. Non-pointer Go types (`string`, `int32`, `int64`, `float32`, `float64`, `bool`, `time.Time`) cannot receive SQL NULL values — `Scan` returns a runtime error like `"converting NULL to string is unsupported"`. The generator MUST use pointer types (`*string`, `*int32`, `*time.Time`) or `sql.Null*` wrappers for nullable columns. After writing any generator that maps field definitions to Go types for database scanning: (a) enumerate ALL field attributes that affect nullability (`optional`, `computed`, default presence, etc.); (b) verify the type-mapping function checks these attributes and selects pointer/nullable types accordingly; (c) write a test with an entity containing optional and computed fields of EVERY supported type (`string`, `int32`, `int64`, `float32`, `float64`, `bool`, `time.Time`) and verify the generated struct uses pointer or nullable types for those fields; (d) verify that the generated Scan calls work correctly with NULL values by tracing the scan target types. **The principle: the Go type system and `database/sql` enforce nullability at runtime — a type mismatch between the Go struct and the column's nullability is not a warning, it is a guaranteed runtime crash on the first NULL value.**
   69. **Disambiguated import aliases must be propagated to all expressions that reference the original package name:** When the assembler (or any combiner) disambiguates an import alias (e.g. `models` → `models2` because two import paths share the same `path.Base`), ALL opaque string expressions (constructor expressions, route expressions) that reference the original package name MUST be updated to use the disambiguated alias. Constructor expressions like `models.NewBar()` are opaque strings — they hardcode the package name as it was known to the generator that produced them. The assembler cannot assume the generator's chosen package name will survive import alias disambiguation. If `internal/storage/models` gets alias `models2`, then `models.NewBar()` must become `models2.NewBar()`. Without this propagation, the generated code references alias `models` (which resolves to a DIFFERENT package), producing either a compile error (if `NewBar` doesn't exist in that package) or silently calling the wrong function (if it does). This is an extension of item 60 ("no string matching on opaque expressions"): the opaque expression contains a package reference that the assembler must update, but the expression's opacity makes this non-trivial. The implementation must either: (a) rewrite the package-qualified prefix in constructor and route expressions when a component's import alias is disambiguated (e.g. track which component produced each constructor, look up the alias assigned to that component's import, and substitute the package reference); or (b) restructure wiring metadata so that constructor expressions use a placeholder for the package qualifier that is resolved at assembly time rather than hardcoded by the generator. After writing any assembler that disambiguates import aliases: (i) for each component whose import alias was changed, enumerate ALL constructor expressions and route expressions from that component; (ii) verify each expression uses the disambiguated alias, not the original; (iii) write a test with two components whose import paths share the same `path.Base`, verify the generated `main.go` uses the correct disambiguated alias in constructor calls, and verify the generated code compiles (not just parses). **The invariant: the package qualifier in every constructor and route expression must match the import alias assigned to the package that expression references. If the alias changes, the expression must change.**
   70. **String replacement operations on identifier references must be word-boundary-safe:** When code replaces identifier references within opaque string expressions (e.g. renaming `store` to `store2` in route expressions), the replacement MUST match only complete identifiers, not substrings of longer identifiers. A naive `strings.ReplaceAll(expr, oldName+".", newName+".")` matches `store.` anywhere in the string — including within `datastore.`, producing `datastore2.` and corrupting a reference to a completely different variable. This is the *modification* counterpart to item 60 (which covers *detection* via string matching): both are instances of treating opaque strings as structured data without respecting identifier boundaries. The fix must use word-boundary-aware replacement — for Go identifiers, a reference to variable `X` is `X.` where `X` is preceded by a non-identifier character (start of string, whitespace, comma, open paren) or is the entire token. After writing any function that performs find-and-replace on identifier names within code expressions: (a) construct a test case where the old name is a suffix of a longer identifier (e.g. `store` within `datastore`, `handler` within `userHandler`); (b) verify the replacement does NOT corrupt the longer identifier; (c) use a word-boundary-aware mechanism — e.g. regex `\boldName\.` or manual boundary checking before each match; (d) test with the old name appearing at string start, mid-expression after a space, after a comma, and as a suffix of a longer identifier. **The principle: `strings.ReplaceAll` on identifier names is never safe without boundary checking. Identifier replacement must be token-aware.**
   71. **Scope-collision audits must cover ALL identifier categories in the shared scope — including import aliases:** When reserving identifiers to prevent collisions in a shared scope (e.g. a Go function body), the reservation set must include EVERY category of identifier that occupies that scope — not just one category. In Go, a function body's scope includes: (a) explicit local variable declarations (`:=`, `var`); (b) import aliases that are used within the function body (e.g. `log`, `fmt`, `http`, `os`, `sql` — these are package-level but a local variable of the same name shadows them from the point of declaration onward); (c) function parameters and receivers. When a previous fix adds one category to the reservation set (e.g. finding 13 added assembler-internal variables like `mux`, `addr`, `db`), the fix-class exhaustion audit (item 33) MUST enumerate ALL remaining categories and verify each is covered. Specifically: if the assembler generates code that references standard library imports `log.Printf(...)`, `log.Fatal(...)`, `fmt.Sprintf(...)`, `http.NewServeMux()`, and `os.Getenv(...)` AFTER the constructor variable declarations, then the import aliases `log`, `fmt`, `http`, `os`, and `sql` are all shadowable by constructor variables with the same name. A constructor like `logger.NewLog()` produces variable `log`, which shadows the `log` import — any subsequent `log.Printf(...)` call resolves to the constructor variable instead of the package, producing a type error. After adding any identifiers to a scope-collision reservation set: (a) enumerate ALL categories of identifiers in the shared scope (local vars, import aliases, parameters, receivers); (b) for each category, list every member that is used AFTER the point where user-derived identifiers are declared; (c) verify every such member is in the reservation set; (d) write a test for each import alias that could be shadowed — this includes BOTH stdlib aliases (e.g. constructor producing variable `log`, `fmt`, `http`, `os`, `sql`) AND non-stdlib aliases (e.g. constructor producing variable `storage` that shadows a component import alias `storage "github.com/myorg/svc/internal/storage"`, or a constructor producing variable `cache` that shadows a fill import alias `cache`). **Non-stdlib import aliases are assigned dynamically during import block construction (e.g. `writeMainImports`) and vary with each assembly input — they cannot be enumerated statically like stdlib aliases. The reservation set must be populated from the actual aliases assigned during import construction, not from a hardcoded list.** **The principle: a scope-collision reservation set that covers variables but not import aliases is only half-complete — it prevents redeclaration errors but not shadowing errors. Both are bugs; the reservation set must cover both. This applies to ALL import aliases — stdlib and non-stdlib alike. A fix that reserves `log` and `fmt` but not `storage` and `cache` (when those are assigned as component/fill import aliases) is an incomplete scope audit.**
   68. **SQL clause generation must handle empty fragment collections gracefully:** When a generator constructs SQL statements by joining collections of clause fragments (e.g. `SET col1 = $1, col2 = $2` from a `setClauses` slice, or `(col1, col2) VALUES ($1, $2)` from a columns slice), it MUST handle the case where the collection is empty after filtering. An empty collection produces syntactically broken SQL — e.g. `UPDATE table SET  WHERE id = $1` (empty SET), `INSERT INTO table () VALUES ()` (empty column/value lists). This occurs when attribute-based filters exclude all fields (e.g. all fields are `computed: true`, leaving no writable columns; or all non-key columns are computed, leaving no SET targets for upsert). After writing any SQL-generating code that filters a field list and joins the results into a SQL clause: (a) identify every filter that reduces the collection (computed exclusion, key exclusion, immutable exclusion, etc.); (b) for each SQL statement type (INSERT, UPDATE, UPSERT), determine whether the filtered collection can be empty for valid input configurations; (c) if yes, either skip the statement entirely (e.g. don't emit an UPDATE method when there are no writable columns) or return a generation-time error explaining why the operation is inapplicable for that entity; (d) write a test with an entity where ALL non-key fields are computed (or otherwise excluded) and verify each SQL-emitting method either produces valid SQL or is correctly skipped. **The principle: `strings.Join([]string{}, ", ")` returns `""` — embedded in SQL, this is always a syntax error. Every SQL clause built from a filtered collection must guard against the empty case.**
   69. **Case-conversion functions must handle consecutive uppercase letters (acronyms) correctly:** When a generator uses a case-conversion function (e.g. `toSnakeCase`, `toCamelCase`, `toPascalCase`) to derive identifiers from user-provided names, the function MUST correctly handle consecutive uppercase letters (acronyms) such as `HTTP`, `API`, `ID`, `URL`, `UUID`, `SQL`. A naive implementation that inserts a separator before every uppercase letter produces `h_t_t_p_server`, `a_p_i_key`, `user_i_d` instead of the correct `http_server`, `api_key`, `user_id`. The correct rule for camelCase-to-snake_case is: insert an underscore before an uppercase letter when (a) it is preceded by a lowercase letter, OR (b) it is followed by a lowercase letter AND preceded by an uppercase letter (end of an acronym run). After writing any case-conversion function: (a) test with acronyms at the start of the name (`HTTPServer` → `http_server`), middle (`getAPIKey` → `get_api_key`), and end (`userID` → `user_id`); (b) test with single uppercase letters to verify basic functionality (`UserName` → `user_name`); (c) test with all-uppercase input (`HTTP` → `http`); (d) test with adjacent acronyms (`HTTPAPI` → `http_api` or `httpapi` depending on convention — pick one and document it); (e) either use a well-tested library or implement the complete boundary-detection rules with tests for each case. **The principle: case conversion is not character-by-character — it requires context-aware word boundary detection. Acronyms are the primary edge case, and they appear frequently in API and infrastructure naming (HTTP, API, ID, URL, SQL, UUID).**
   70. **Entity-level field attributes that reference sibling fields must be validated and deduplicated at generation time:** When a field-level attribute contains references to other fields in the same entity (e.g. `unique_composite: [field1, field2]`), the generator MUST validate that each referenced field name exists in the entity's field definitions AND deduplicate constraints declared across multiple fields. Item 53 covers field-name references in generator *configuration* (expose blocks, scope, upsert_key); this item extends that principle to field-level *attributes* within the entity data model itself, where the references are between sibling fields rather than between configuration and fields. Two specific problems must be prevented: (a) **unresolved references** — a field declares `unique_composite: [usr_id, org_id]` but `usr_id` does not exist in the entity, producing a migration with a `UNIQUE` constraint on a non-existent column that fails at migration time with no stego-level error; (b) **duplicate constraints** — if two fields both declare `unique_composite: [field_a, field_b]`, the same `UNIQUE (field_a, field_b)` constraint is emitted twice, which PostgreSQL rejects as a duplicate constraint error. After writing any generator that processes field attributes containing field-name references: (a) resolve each referenced field name against the entity's field list and return a generation-time error identifying the unresolved name, the declaring field, and the entity; (b) collect all constraint declarations (normalized — e.g. sorted field lists) across all fields in the entity and deduplicate before emitting; (c) write tests with: (i) a `unique_composite` referencing a non-existent field name, (ii) two fields declaring identical composite constraints, and verify the generator returns an error for (i) and emits exactly one constraint for (ii). **The principle: field-to-field references within an entity are just as prone to typos and inconsistencies as configuration-to-field references — both must be validated at generation time.**
   71. **Relational type semantics must be fully enforced AND cross-entity references validated at generation time:** When the spec defines a type with relational semantics (e.g. `ref` with a `to` attribute pointing to another entity), the generator MUST implement BOTH aspects: (a) the **storage representation** (e.g. `ref` → `TEXT` column type), AND (b) the **relational constraint** (e.g. `FOREIGN KEY ... REFERENCES` for a PostgreSQL adapter). The spec states: "The component decides HOW to enforce them (SQL constraints, middleware, ORM annotations)." For a SQL-based storage adapter, the standard enforcement of a `ref` type is a `REFERENCES` clause — mapping `ref` to plain `TEXT` without a foreign key constraint means referential integrity is not enforced at the database level, and the `to` attribute is dead metadata. Additionally, cross-entity references embedded in field-level attributes (e.g. `to: Organization` on a `ref` field) MUST be validated at generation time — the referenced entity must exist in the entity list passed to the generator. This is distinct from item 53 (which covers field-name references in generator *configuration* like expose blocks) and item 51 (which covers cross-entity references between configured scope items) — this item covers cross-entity references embedded in the *data model itself*. After writing any generator that handles spec-defined types with relational semantics: (a) enumerate every type in the spec's type system that implies a relationship to another entity or external concept (e.g. `ref`, `entity-ref`); (b) for each such type, verify the generator produces the appropriate constraint for the target format — not just the column/field type; (c) verify the generator validates that the referenced entity exists, returning an error for typos like `to: Organziation`; (d) write tests with: (i) a `ref` field with a valid `to` referencing an existing entity, verifying the constraint is emitted; (ii) a `ref` field with `to` referencing a non-existent entity, verifying a generation-time error is returned. **The principle: a type with relational semantics has TWO obligations — representation (what the column IS) and constraint (what the column ENFORCES). Implementing only the representation makes the type's relationship attribute dead metadata, silently degrading data integrity.**
   72. **Spec-mandated generated-file invariants must apply to ALL output formats, not just Go:** When the spec defines an invariant that applies to "all generated files" (e.g. "All generated files carry a `// Code generated by stego. DO NOT EDIT.` header"), the implementation MUST enforce that invariant for EVERY file format the generator produces — not just Go files. Item 29 covers the header requirement for Go files via `gen.File.Bytes()`, but generators that produce non-Go output (SQL migrations, JSON, YAML, protobuf, shell scripts) often bypass `gen.File` and write their own headers manually. When a generator manually writes a header for a non-Go file, it MUST use the spec-mandated text adapted to the file format's comment syntax (e.g. `-- Code generated by stego. DO NOT EDIT.` for SQL, `# Code generated by stego. DO NOT EDIT.` for YAML/shell). After writing any generator that produces non-Go output files: (a) identify every file the generator produces (not just `.go` files — check for `.sql`, `.json`, `.yaml`, `.proto`, etc.); (b) for each non-Go file, verify the file includes the spec-mandated header text using the appropriate comment syntax for that format; (c) if the generator uses `gen.File` for Go files but constructs non-Go output via `bytes.Buffer` or string concatenation, verify the non-Go path includes the header — `gen.File.Bytes()` only adds Go-format headers for `.go` files; (d) write a test for each non-Go output file that asserts the first line contains the spec-mandated header text in the correct comment syntax. **The principle: "all generated files" means ALL — not "all Go files." When the spec defines a universal invariant, every code path that produces output must enforce it, regardless of format. A generator that produces correct Go headers but wrong SQL headers is violating the same spec rule — just in a different file.**
   73. **DDL emission must respect inter-entity dependency order (topological sort):** When a generator emits DDL statements (e.g. `CREATE TABLE`) for multiple entities, and those entities reference each other (e.g. via `ref` fields with `REFERENCES` clauses, or foreign key constraints), the emission order MUST respect the dependency graph — referenced tables must be created before referencing tables. Iterating entities in input order (e.g. `for _, e := range ctx.Entities`) produces correct output only when the input happens to be dependency-sorted, which is not guaranteed. When entity A has a `ref` field with `to: B`, the generated `CREATE TABLE A (... REFERENCES B(id))` will fail at migration time if B's `CREATE TABLE` has not yet been emitted. The fix is to topologically sort entities by their ref dependencies before emitting migration DDL. After writing any generator that emits DDL for multiple interdependent entities: (a) build a dependency graph from ref fields (entity A depends on entity B if A has a `ref` field with `to: B`); (b) topologically sort entities so that dependencies are emitted first; (c) detect cycles in the dependency graph and return a generation-time error identifying the cycle (e.g. "circular ref dependency: A → B → A") — cycles cannot be resolved by ordering alone and require deferred constraints or restructuring; (d) write tests with: (i) entity A referencing entity B where A appears before B in the input list, verifying the generated DDL emits B's CREATE TABLE first; (ii) a circular dependency between two entities, verifying the generator returns a clear error; (iii) a diamond dependency (A→B, A→C, B→D, C→D), verifying D is emitted before B and C, and both before A. **The principle: when output artifacts have ordering dependencies, the generator must compute and enforce that order — relying on input order is a latent bug that surfaces only when the input happens to be unfavorably ordered. Test fixtures that define entities in dependency-satisfying order mask this class of bug; always include at least one test where the input order violates the dependency order.**
   74. **Type-specific required attributes must be validated as non-empty — empty is not absent:** When a spec-defined type has a defining attribute (e.g. `ref` type's `to` attribute, `enum` type's `values` attribute), a guard like `if f.To != ""` that silently skips processing when the attribute is empty is NOT validation — it is silent degradation. The attribute's absence and its emptiness are the same failure mode: the type's defining characteristic is missing, and the field degrades to a bare representation (e.g. `ref` without `to` becomes plain `TEXT` with no foreign key constraint). Item 16 covers required fields in parsed config files; item 18 covers non-existent paths being treated as empty results. This item covers the specific pattern where a type's required attribute is checked with a non-empty guard that branches into "process" vs "skip" rather than "process" vs "error." After writing any validation or processing code that guards on a field attribute being non-empty: (a) ask whether the empty case is valid — does the spec define behavior for a `ref` with no `to`, an `enum` with no `values`, a `pattern` with no regex? If not, the empty case is an error, not a skip; (b) replace `if f.Attr != "" { process }` with `if f.Attr == "" { return error } else { process }` — make the validation explicit; (c) apply this consistently across ALL type-specific required attributes — if `ref` requires `to`, verify that `enum` requires `values`, `pattern` requires the regex string, etc.; (d) write a test for EACH type with its defining attribute set to empty/omitted, verifying the generator returns a clear error (e.g. "entity User: field org_id has type ref but no 'to' attribute — ref fields must specify the target entity"). **Mandatory audit step:** after implementing or modifying ANY validation phase, enumerate EVERY type in the spec's type system that has a defining attribute (at minimum: `ref` requires `to`, `enum` requires non-empty `values`, `pattern` requires a regex string) and grep the validation code for an explicit empty-check for EACH one. A validation phase that checks `ref.To` but does not check `enum.Values` is incomplete — the same silent-degradation bug exists for every unchecked type. Do NOT rely on downstream code (e.g. `columnDefinition`'s `len(f.Values) > 0` guard) as validation — a conditional guard that silently skips emission is the OPPOSITE of validation; it is the mechanism by which the bug hides. The validation must be in the upfront `validate*` functions, producing an explicit error, not in the emission code producing silent omission. **The principle: a non-empty guard that silently skips is validation theater — it looks like it handles the case but actually hides the bug. The user gets no error, the output is silently wrong, and the type's relational semantics are lost. Every type-specific required attribute deserves an explicit "missing attribute" error, not a silent fallthrough.**
   46. **Hierarchical spec requirements must be implemented at ALL ancestor levels, not just the immediate level:** When the spec uses language like "at each level," "every level," "all ancestors," or describes recursive verification (e.g. "parent existence verification at each level"), the implementation MUST recurse or iterate through the ENTIRE ancestor chain — not just the immediate parent. A common error pattern: the generator correctly builds a recursive structure (e.g. `entityBasePath` recursing up the parent chain to produce `/clusters/{cluster_id}/nodepools/{nodepool_id}/adapterstatuss`) but only generates verification logic for the immediate parent (e.g. `checkParent` only verifies NodePool exists, ignoring the `{cluster_id}` parameter entirely). This means a request like `GET /clusters/nonexistent/nodepools/1/adapterstatuss` succeeds if NodePool 1 exists — the Cluster is never verified. After implementing any feature that handles hierarchical relationships: (a) identify every place where the full hierarchy is built (URL paths, breadcrumbs, navigation); (b) identify every place where per-level logic is applied (verification, authorization, scoping); (c) verify that (b) covers ALL levels that (a) covers — if the URL contains `{cluster_id}` and `{nodepool_id}`, then verification must check both Cluster and NodePool existence, not just NodePool. Write a test with at least THREE levels of nesting (e.g. Cluster → NodePool → AdapterStatus) and verify that an invalid ancestor ID at each level produces an error — test with invalid grandparent, invalid parent, and invalid entity separately\.
   75. **Generator-internal field name assumptions must be validated against the entity's actual fields:** When a generator hardcodes a reference to a specific field name as part of a feature's implementation (e.g. emitting `WHERE EXCLUDED.generation > table.generation` for optimistic concurrency), it MUST validate at generation time that the entity actually contains a field with that name. Item 53 covers field-name references in generator *configuration* (e.g. `scope: org_id`); this item covers field names that the generator itself assumes exist based on a feature flag or behavioral mode.
