A hardcoded field reference that is not validated produces SQL or code referencing a non-existent column/field, which fails at runtime with an opaque database or compilation error rather than a clear generation-time diagnostic. After writing any generator code that references a specific field name unconditionally (not derived from configuration): (a) grep the generator source for string literals that match field or column names (e.g. `"generation"`, `"version"`, `"updated_at"`); (b) for each hardcoded field name, verify that the code path checks whether the entity's field list contains that field before emitting code that references it; (c) if the field is absent, either skip the feature with a documented reason, or return a generation-time error explaining that the feature (e.g. "optimistic concurrency") requires a specific field (e.g. "generation") that the entity does not define; (d) write tests with: (i) an entity that HAS the expected field, verifying the feature works correctly; (ii) an entity that LACKS the field, verifying the generator either skips the feature gracefully or returns a clear error — not broken SQL. **The principle: a generator has full knowledge of the entity's field list at generation time. Any field name assumption that is not validated against that list is a latent runtime failure — the generator is emitting code it cannot guarantee is correct.**
   76. **Sub-element uniqueness must be validated within parent definitions — for BOTH raw AND derived names:** When a parent structure contains a collection of named sub-elements (e.g. fields within an entity, parameters within a function, columns within a table, properties within a schema), the generator MUST validate that sub-element names are unique within their parent scope. Item 13 covers map key collisions during data processing; item 70 covers cross-field attribute references (e.g. `unique_composite`). This item covers the fundamental validation that the sub-elements themselves are not duplicated. Duplicate sub-element names produce downstream failures that vary by target: duplicate Go struct fields cause compilation errors, duplicate SQL column definitions cause migration errors, duplicate YAML keys cause silent overwrites. All of these are preventable at generation time with a simple uniqueness check. After writing any generator that iterates over a collection of named sub-elements to produce output: (a) before emitting any output, collect all sub-element names into a set and check for duplicates; (b) if a duplicate is found, return a generation-time error identifying the parent (e.g. entity name), the duplicate sub-element name, and the positions or indices of the conflicting definitions; (c) apply this check to EVERY level of named collections — not just the top level (entities) but also nested levels (fields within entities, parameters within operations, properties within config objects); (d) write a test with duplicate sub-element names and verify the generator returns a clear error. **Critically, uniqueness must be validated for BOTH raw names AND all derived/transformed forms.** Item 82 requires collision detection on derived identifiers at the entity level; this same principle applies at the sub-element level. When sub-element names undergo a transformation before being emitted (e.g. `toPascalCase(field.Name)` for Go struct fields, `toSnakeCase(field.Name)` for SQL columns), the uniqueness check MUST also validate that the transformed outputs are unique within the parent scope. Two raw field names that are unique (e.g. `_name` and `name`, or `foo_bar` and `fooBar`) but produce the same derived identifier (both → `Name` via `toPascalCase`, both → `FooBar` via `toPascalCase`) are a collision — the generated Go struct has two fields with the same name, causing a compilation error. After writing any sub-element uniqueness validation: (e) enumerate every transformation applied to sub-element names (PascalCase for Go struct fields, snake_case for SQL columns, camelCase for JSON keys); (f) for each transformation, compute the result for ALL sub-elements within each parent and check for collisions; (g) write tests with sub-element names that are unique in raw form but collide after transformation (e.g. fields `_name` and `name`, or `foo_bar` and `fooBar`) and verify the generator returns a clear error identifying both raw names and the colliding derived identifier. **The principle: uniqueness validation must be applied at every level of a name hierarchy, AND at every level of the transformation pipeline. Raw uniqueness is necessary but insufficient when a lossy transformation maps multiple raw names to the same output.**
   77. **Generated SQL identifiers must be quoted to prevent reserved-word collisions:** When a generator emits SQL statements (DDL or DML) with identifiers derived from user-provided names (entity names, field names, attribute names), ALL identifiers MUST be double-quoted in the generated SQL to prevent collisions with SQL reserved words. Item 45 covers Go reserved word collisions; this item extends the same principle to SQL. PostgreSQL has over 100 reserved words (e.g. `order`, `check`, `default`, `group`, `table`, `select`, `primary`, `user`, `type`, `comment`, `column`, `index`, `key`, `level`, `name`, `role`, `value`, `zone`), and user-provided entity or field names will inevitably collide. Unquoted identifiers that match reserved words produce syntax errors at migration or query time — the error points at the generated SQL, not at the entity definition that caused it. Double-quoting ALL identifiers (`"order" INTEGER NOT NULL`, `INSERT INTO "users" ("order", "group") VALUES ($1, $2)`) is safe and idempotent — quoted non-reserved words work identically to unquoted ones. After writing any generator that emits SQL: (a) audit EVERY `fmt.Fprintf` or string interpolation that produces a SQL identifier (table names, column names, constraint names) and wrap each one in double quotes; (b) this applies to DDL (`CREATE TABLE`, `ALTER TABLE`, column definitions, constraint clauses) AND DML (`INSERT`, `SELECT`, `UPDATE`, `DELETE` — both column lists and `WHERE` clauses); (c) write tests with entity and field names that ARE PostgreSQL reserved words (e.g. entity named `Order` with a field named `group`) and verify the generated SQL is syntactically valid; (d) do NOT attempt to maintain a list of reserved words and validate against it — the list varies across PostgreSQL versions and is incomplete by nature. Universal quoting is simpler, safer, and forward-compatible. **The principle: the reserved-word collision protection in item 45 must be applied to EVERY target language the generator emits code for. Go reserved words are checked because Go identifiers are derived from user input; SQL identifiers are derived from the same user input and need the same protection. The SQL solution (universal double-quoting) is simpler than the Go solution (collision checking) because SQL's quoting mechanism is idempotent — use it unconditionally.**
   81. **Validation functions must cover ALL user-provided name categories symmetrically:** When the generator adds or modifies a validation function that applies to one category of user-provided name (e.g., field name charset validation via `validateFieldNameCharset`), the SAME validation MUST be applied to ALL other name categories that undergo similar transformations and face the same risks (e.g., entity names that are also transformed into Go identifiers via `toPascalCase` and SQL identifiers via `toSnakeCase`). Item 33 (fix-class exhaustion) applies within a single name category; this item extends it ACROSS categories. A `validateFieldNameCharset` without a corresponding `validateEntityNameCharset` is an asymmetric gap — the same invalid characters that break field names also break entity names, because both go through the same transformations. After adding any `validate*` function for names: (a) enumerate ALL categories of user-provided names the generator accepts (entity names, field names, operation names, attribute values); (b) for each existing validation function, verify it applies to every applicable category — not just the one that prompted its creation; (c) specifically, if a charset validation exists for field names, verify entity names are validated against the same pattern; if a reserved-word check exists for entity names, verify field names are checked equivalently; (d) write tests for the validation applied to EACH name category, not just the one being fixed. **The audit step: after modifying any `validate*` function, grep for ALL `validate*` functions and list which name categories each covers. If any validation applies to fields but not entities (or vice versa), that is a gap. The pattern `validateFieldX` without `validateEntityX` is always suspicious — justify the asymmetry or close the gap.**
   83. **Set-semantic list attributes must be validated for intra-list uniqueness:** When a spec-defined field attribute uses list/array syntax but represents a set (i.e., duplicate elements are meaningless or harmful), the generator MUST validate that the list contains no duplicate elements. Item 55 covers uniqueness of top-level input collections; item 62 covers duplicate elements in sub-collections like operation lists; item 70 covers deduplication of constraints declared across multiple fields. This item covers the gap: **duplicate elements within a single list-valued attribute on a single field** — e.g., `unique_composite: [user_id, user_id]` or `values: [admin, admin, member]`. In both cases, the spec defines these as finite sets (a unique constraint over a set of columns; an enum over a set of allowed values), but the YAML deserialization produces a Go slice that preserves duplicates. The consequences vary by attribute: duplicate columns in a `UNIQUE` constraint produce a PostgreSQL error (`column "x" appears twice in unique constraint`); duplicate enum values produce a semantically invalid `CHECK` constraint that implies a configuration error. The general principle: if the spec's definition of an attribute uses set language ("a set of columns", "allowed values", "finite set"), the generator must enforce set semantics by validating uniqueness at generation time. After writing any validation for a list-valued field attribute: (a) ask whether the spec's definition implies set semantics — can a duplicate element ever be meaningful? If not, duplicates are an error; (b) scan the list for duplicates and return a generation-time error identifying the entity, the field, the attribute name, and the duplicate element(s) (e.g. "entity User: field role has duplicate enum value 'admin'"); (c) apply this consistently across ALL list-valued attributes with set semantics — if `unique_composite` is deduplicated, `values` must be too, and vice versa; (d) write tests with duplicate elements in each set-semantic list attribute and verify the generator returns a clear error. **Mandatory audit step:** after implementing intra-list uniqueness validation for any one attribute, enumerate ALL list-valued attributes in the spec's type system (at minimum: `unique_composite`, `values`, `upsert_key`) and verify EACH one has equivalent intra-list uniqueness validation. A validation that checks one set-semantic attribute but not another of the same kind is an incomplete fix-class exhaustion (item 33). **The principle: YAML lists and Go slices are ordered and allow duplicates; spec-defined sets do not. The generator must bridge this gap by validating set semantics at generation time, not by relying on downstream consumers (PostgreSQL, Go compiler) to reject the duplicates with opaque errors.**
   84. **Post-transformation identifier validity — transformed names must be valid in the target language:** When a generator applies a name transformation function (e.g. `toPascalCase`, `toSnakeCase`, `toCamelCase`) to a user-provided name to derive an identifier for a target language, the generator MUST validate that the TRANSFORMED OUTPUT is a valid identifier in the target language — not just that the input passes a charset regex. **This applies to ALL categories of user-provided names — entity names AND field names equally (per item 81's symmetry requirement).** Item 59 validates input character sets; item 76 validates derived-name uniqueness; this item covers the gap: **a name that passes input validation but produces an invalid identifier after transformation**. The root cause is that transformations like `toPascalCase` are lossy — they strip characters (underscores), split and rejoin segments, and can produce outputs that violate target-language identifier rules even when the input is "well-formed." Specific failure patterns: (a) **underscore-only names** (e.g. `_`, `__`) — `toPascalCase` splits on underscores, skips empty segments, and produces an empty string `""`, which is not a valid Go identifier; for field names, the generated struct has an anonymous/embedded field declaration, and store methods produce `"v."` (syntax error); for entity names, `type _ struct { ... }` uses the blank identifier, and `var v _` in store methods cannot reference the type — a compilation error; (b) **underscore-prefixed digit-only names** (e.g. `_123`, `_42`) — `toPascalCase` strips the underscore prefix and produces a digit-leading string (`"123"`), which is not a valid Go identifier; (c) both failure modes surface only at `format.Source` as opaque formatting errors with no indication of which name is problematic. The input charset regex `^[a-zA-Z_][a-zA-Z0-9_]*$` (from item 59) accepts all of these names because they ARE valid per the pattern — the issue is downstream. After writing or modifying any name-transformation function: (a) enumerate ALL inputs that pass the upstream charset validation; (b) identify which subset of those inputs produces invalid target-language identifiers after transformation — specifically, empty strings, digit-leading strings, the blank identifier `_`, or strings containing characters invalid in the target language; (c) add a post-transformation validation that checks the output against the target language's identifier rules (for Go: non-empty, starts with a letter or underscore, contains only letters/digits/underscores, is not the blank identifier `_`); (d) return a generation-time error identifying the original user-provided name, the transformation applied, the invalid result, and why it is invalid (e.g. "entity '_' is the Go blank identifier and cannot be used as a type name"); (e) alternatively, tighten the input charset regex to make problematic inputs unreachable — e.g., requiring at least one alphabetic character (`^[a-zA-Z_][a-zA-Z0-9_]*$` AND must contain at least one `[a-zA-Z]`); (f) write tests with inputs from EACH failure pattern for BOTH entity names and field names (underscore-only, underscore-prefixed digits, and any other input that the transformation renders invalid) and verify the generator returns a clear error for each. **Mandatory audit step per item 81:** after adding post-transformation validation for any one name category, verify the SAME validation exists for ALL other name categories that undergo the same transformation. If `validateDerivedFieldValidity` checks post-transformation field names, an equivalent `validateDerivedEntityValidity` (or equivalent logic within entity name validation) MUST check post-transformation entity names. **The principle: input validation (charset regex) and output validation (post-transformation identifier check) are complementary, not substitutes. A charset regex defines what characters are allowed; a post-transformation check ensures the transformation pipeline produces usable output. When the transformation is lossy (drops characters, changes structure), the output MUST be independently validated — the input passing validation does not guarantee the output is valid.**
   85. **Reserved-name sets must include import aliases used in the generated package:** When a generator unconditionally imports packages (e.g. `database/sql`, `encoding/json`, `fmt`, `strings`, `time`) in generated source files, the import aliases (the last path element — `sql`, `json`, `fmt`, `strings`, `time`) become package-level identifiers in the generated code. A user-provided entity name that matches an import alias (e.g. entity named `sql`) produces `type sql struct { ... }`, which shadows the `database/sql` import — causing `*sql.DB` to resolve to the entity struct instead of the package, breaking every reference to the imported package in that file. Items 45 and 47 cover Go keywords, predeclared identifiers, and generator-internal type names, but import aliases are a DISTINCT category — they are neither language-level reserved words nor generator-defined types, they are identifiers introduced by `import` statements that the generator emits. After writing any generator that produces `import` blocks: (a) enumerate EVERY import alias used in each generated file (the last path element of each import — e.g. `"database/sql"` → `sql`, `"encoding/json"` → `json`, `"fmt"` → `fmt`); (b) include ALL of these aliases in the reserved-name set used to validate user-provided names; (c) include BOTH unconditional imports (always present) AND conditional imports (present when certain field types exist — e.g. `"time"` when timestamp fields exist) — a conditional import is still a collision when the condition is met; (d) write tests with entity names matching each import alias (e.g. entities named `sql`, `json`, `fmt`, `strings`, `time`) and verify the generator returns a clear error rather than producing code that shadows the import. **Mandatory audit step after modifying import blocks:** whenever an import is added to or removed from a generated file, update the reserved-name set to reflect the change. Grep for all `import` blocks in the generator's output templates and cross-reference against the reserved-name set. An import alias not in the reserved-name set is a guaranteed future finding. **The principle: items 45 and 47 establish that the reserved-name set is the UNION of multiple identifier categories. Import aliases are an additional category that must be included — the set is Go keywords ∪ predeclared identifiers ∪ generator-internal types ∪ import aliases.**
   86. **Generated methods must propagate errors, not collapse them into boolean or zero-value returns:** When a generator emits a method that performs I/O (database queries, HTTP calls, file reads) and can encounter infrastructure failures (connection errors, timeouts, permission denied), the method signature MUST return an `error` value so callers can distinguish between a legitimate domain result (e.g. "entity not found") and an infrastructure failure (e.g. "database unreachable"). A method that returns only `bool` (e.g. `Exists(entity, id string) bool`) or only a zero-value result collapses these two distinct outcomes: `false` could mean "entity does not exist" OR "the database query failed." This has three consequences: (a) callers cannot implement correct error handling — a transient database failure is reported as "not found" instead of "internal error," causing retry-hostile status codes (404 vs 500); (b) operational observability is lost — the error is swallowed inside the method, never logged or propagated, making infrastructure failures invisible; (c) downstream behavior is incorrect — e.g., a parent existence check that returns `false` on database timeout causes the handler to report "parent not found" when the parent may exist. After writing any generated method that performs I/O: (a) verify the return signature includes `error` — for query methods, this means `(ResultType, error)`, for existence checks `(bool, error)`, for void operations `error`; (b) verify that I/O errors from the underlying call (e.g. `db.QueryRow(...).Scan(...)`) are returned to the caller, not collapsed into a default return value; (c) specifically, audit any pattern like `return err == nil` or `return err == nil && result` — this is the canonical error-swallowing pattern; the correct alternative is to check `err == sql.ErrNoRows` for "not found" and return all other errors to the caller; (d) write a test (or review the generated code) for EACH generated method that performs I/O and verify the error path is propagated, not swallowed. **The principle: collapsing an error into a boolean is information destruction. The method knows WHY it failed but discards that knowledge. Every I/O method must give its caller the ability to distinguish "the answer is no" from "I could not determine the answer." If a method can fail for infrastructure reasons, `error` must be in the return signature.**
   87. **Method signature changes must be propagated to all interface consumers across all components:** When a fix changes a method signature on a concrete type (e.g. changing `Exists(entity, id string) bool` to `Exists(entity, id string) (bool, error)` on `*storage.Store`), the implementer MUST trace every interface that the concrete type satisfies — across ALL components in the project, not just the component being modified — and update those interfaces and their call sites to match the new signature. Go requires exact signature matches for interface satisfaction; a concrete type that changes a return type from `bool` to `(bool, error)` will fail to satisfy any interface that still declares the old `bool`-only signature, producing a compile error like `*storage.Store does not implement api.Storage (wrong type for method Exists)`. This is distinct from item 33 (fix-class exhaustion within the same pattern) because the propagation crosses package and component boundaries — the implementation being fixed and the interface being broken live in different generator packages (e.g. `postgresadapter/generator.go` vs `restapi/generator.go`). After changing any method signature on a concrete type: (a) grep the ENTIRE project for the method name to find every interface declaration and call site that references it — use `grep -rn 'MethodName' --include='*.go'` across ALL generator packages, not just the one being modified; (b) for each interface found, verify its method signature matches the concrete type's new signature — if not, update the interface; (c) for each call site of the interface method, update the calling code to handle any new return values (e.g. if `error` was added, every `if !h.store.Exists(...)` must become `exists, err := h.store.Exists(...); if err != nil { ... }; if !exists { ... }`); (d) run `go build ./...` across the ENTIRE project (not just the modified package) to verify interface satisfaction; (e) write or update tests that verify the concrete type satisfies the interface (e.g. `var _ api.Storage = (*storage.Store)(nil)`). **The principle: a method signature is not local to its defining type — it is a contract shared with every interface it satisfies. Changing the signature without updating the interfaces is a half-applied fix that converts a runtime bug (error swallowing) into a compile error (interface mismatch). Item 33 says "grep for the same pattern"; this item says "grep for every consumer of the changed contract." Both are mandatory — item 33 finds other sites with the same bug, this item finds other sites affected by the fix.**
   88. **Identifier categories sharing an output scope must use a unified disambiguation namespace:** When multiple categories of identifiers (e.g., component import aliases, fill import aliases, slots package aliases) are emitted into the same syntactic scope (e.g., a single Go import block, a single function body), disambiguation MUST use a SINGLE shared namespace — not separate per-category maps. Separate maps (`aliases`/`aliasUsed` for components, `fillAliases`/`fillAliasUsed` for fills) each ensure uniqueness within their own category but cannot detect cross-category collisions. A component import `internal/storage` producing alias `storage` and a fill named `storage` producing alias `storage` are each unique within their respective maps, but produce a duplicate alias in the shared import block — a Go compile error. Similarly, a hardcoded alias (e.g., `slots`) that is added without consulting any disambiguation map will collide with any component or fill that derives the same alias. **This principle applies to EVERY syntactic scope, not just import blocks.** When multiple emitter functions (e.g. `writeSlotWiring`, `writeConstructors`) each produce local variable declarations in the same Go function body (e.g. `func main()`), they share a single function scope. Each emitter function MUST either: (i) receive and update a shared variable-name set passed between all emitters writing to the same function, or (ii) emit all variables from a single function that maintains one cumulative set. Independent per-emitter collision maps (`varNames` in `writeConstructors` that is unaware of variables emitted by `writeSlotWiring`) guarantee cross-emitter collisions go undetected. A slot operator variable named `beforeCreateGate` emitted by `writeSlotWiring` and a constructor variable also named `beforeCreateGate` emitted by `writeConstructors` are each unique within their respective emitters but produce a duplicate `:=` declaration in the shared function body — a Go compile error. After writing any code that emits identifiers from multiple categories into a shared scope: (a) identify ALL categories of identifiers that coexist in the scope (e.g., component imports, fill imports, slots imports, standard library imports; OR slot operator variables, constructor variables, route variables); (b) use a SINGLE disambiguation map/set that spans all categories — do NOT create separate per-category maps; (c) process categories in a defined order, with each category checking and updating the cumulative set; (d) hardcoded or pre-computed identifiers (e.g., `slots` for the slots package, slot operator variable names computed before constructor emission) must be inserted into the shared set BEFORE any subsequent dynamic disambiguation begins, so that later identifiers are disambiguated against the earlier ones; **(d.1) Mandatory enumeration of assembler-internal template variables:** When the shared scope is a function body (e.g. `func main()`), the shared set must include not only identifiers produced by dynamic emitter functions (slot operator vars, constructor vars) but also every variable that is hardcoded in the function's template — variables introduced by boilerplate-emitting functions like `writeDBSetup` (`dsn`, `db`, `err`), `writeRouteRegistration` (`mux`), `writeServerStart` (`addr`). These are not dynamic (they don't vary with input) but they occupy the same function scope and will collide with any dynamically-derived variable that produces the same name. **After seeding the shared set with any one category of pre-computed identifiers (e.g. slot operator vars), immediately grep ALL other emitter functions that write into the same scope for hardcoded `:=` or `var` declarations and seed every such identifier into the shared set.** A fix that seeds slot operator vars but omits assembler-internal template vars is an incomplete scope unification — both are pre-existing identifiers in the same function body that must be reserved before dynamic disambiguation runs. The audit is: for each emitter function that writes into the scope, extract every left-hand-side identifier from every `:=` or `var` statement, and confirm each one is in the shared set. Conditional emitters (e.g. `writeDBSetup` only runs when `hasDB`) produce identifiers that are conditionally in scope — seed them conditionally (only when the condition is true). **(d.2) Mandatory seeding of stdlib import names into import-alias disambiguation maps:** When the shared scope is an import block (e.g. the `import (...)` block in a generated `main.go`), the disambiguation maps used for non-stdlib imports (component aliases, fill aliases, slots alias) MUST be pre-seeded with the implicit package-name aliases of every stdlib import that the assembler conditionally or unconditionally writes. Stdlib imports written as bare strings (e.g. `"database/sql"`, `"log"`, `"fmt"`, `"net/http"`, `"os"`) introduce implicit aliases (`sql`, `log`, `fmt`, `http`, `os`) into the same import block. If the non-stdlib disambiguation map is not seeded with these names, a component or fill whose derived alias matches a stdlib name (e.g. component import `internal/sql` → base `"sql"`, or fill named `"log"`) will receive that alias from the disambiguation function (first use in the non-stdlib map), producing a duplicate/shadowing alias in the import block. The explicit alias shadows the implicit one: all stdlib-qualified references (`sql.Open(...)`, `log.Printf(...)`) in the generated function body now resolve to the wrong package. `parser.ParseFile` will not catch this because it only checks syntax, not import resolution. **After writing the stdlib import lines, immediately compute the set of implicit stdlib aliases (the last path element of each imported stdlib package — `sql` from `database/sql`, `http` from `net/http`, etc.) and insert each one into the shared non-stdlib disambiguation map before processing any slots, component, or fill imports.** Conditional stdlib imports (e.g. `"database/sql"` only when `hasDB`) produce aliases that are conditionally in scope — seed them conditionally. The audit is: for each stdlib import line the assembler writes, extract the implicit alias and confirm it is in the disambiguation map. **This is the import-block analog of (d.1), which covers function body scope. Both are instances of the same principle: pre-existing template-emitted identifiers must be seeded into disambiguation maps before dynamic entries are processed.** **(d.3) Mandatory seeding of non-stdlib import aliases into constructor variable disambiguation maps:** When the shared scope is a function body (e.g. `func main()`), constructor variable disambiguation maps must be seeded not only with assembler-internal template variables (d.1) and stdlib import aliases (covered by `assemblerInternalVars`), but also with ALL non-stdlib import aliases assigned during `writeMainImports` — component import aliases, fill import aliases, and the slots package alias (when present). These non-stdlib import aliases are package-level identifiers that are referenced by constructor expressions and route expressions AFTER the constructor variable declarations. A constructor whose `rawConstructorVarName` matches a non-stdlib import alias (e.g. `cache.NewStorage()` → variable `storage`, which shadows the `storage` import alias for `internal/storage`) creates a local variable that shadows the import from its declaration point onward. Constructors from later wirings that reference the shadowed import package via `storage.NewStore(db)` resolve to the local variable instead, and the `storage` import becomes unused — a Go compile error. `parser.ParseFile` does not catch this because unused imports are a semantic error. **The fix pattern:** `writeMainImports` must return (or populate via an out-parameter) the complete set of assigned non-stdlib import aliases (component aliases after disambiguation, fill aliases after disambiguation, and the slots alias if present). `writeConstructors` must seed its `varNames`/`varUsed` maps with this set, in addition to slot operator variable names and `assemblerInternalVars`. The seeding must happen BEFORE the constructor disambiguation loop begins. **The audit:** after any change to `writeMainImports` that adds a new import category or changes alias computation, verify that the returned alias set is still complete and that `writeConstructors` consumes it. After any change to `writeConstructors` that adds a new seeding source, verify that non-stdlib import aliases are still included. A fix that adds stdlib import aliases to the constructor var reservation (finding 16) but omits non-stdlib import aliases is an incomplete scope unification — both are import aliases in the same function scope, and both can be shadowed by constructor variables. (e) write tests with inputs from different categories that produce the same identifier (e.g., component path ending in `storage` + fill named `storage`, or a slot operator variable + a constructor variable with the same derived name) and verify the disambiguation produces distinct identifiers for each. **The principle: the scope boundary determines the disambiguation boundary. If two identifiers land in the same output scope (import block, function body, package namespace), they MUST be disambiguated against each other — regardless of which category they originate from or which emitter function produces them. Separate per-category maps are a scoping violation that guarantees cross-category collisions go undetected. This applies equally to import aliases in an import block AND to local variable names across multiple emitter functions writing into the same function body.**
   89. **Opaque string expressions referencing variables must be updated when those variables are renamed:** When generated code includes opaque string expressions that reference variable names (e.g., route registrations like `mux.HandleFunc("POST /users", userHandler.Create)` that reference the `userHandler` variable by name), and the code also applies disambiguation or renaming to those variable names (e.g., `userHandler` → `userHandler2` when two components produce the same base name), the opaque expressions MUST be updated to reference the post-disambiguation variable names. Failing to do so creates a dangling reference: the variable is declared as `userHandler2` but the route still references `userHandler`, binding to the WRONG handler. Three approaches can prevent this: (a) **structured route representations** — instead of opaque strings, use a struct with separate fields for the pattern, the variable name, and the method, so disambiguation can rewrite the variable reference; (b) **disambiguation before construction** — compute disambiguated names first, then construct the opaque expressions using the final names; (c) **post-disambiguation rewriting** — after disambiguation, scan all opaque expressions and replace variable references with their disambiguated forms. A test that only verifies constructor variable name disambiguation with constructors not referenced by routes (e.g., two colliding `NewStore` constructors that no route references) does NOT cover this failure mode — it leaves the route-reference breakage untested. After writing any code that: (1) produces opaque string expressions containing variable references, AND (2) applies disambiguation or renaming to those variables: (a) identify every opaque expression that contains a variable reference; (b) verify that disambiguation is applied to BOTH the variable declaration AND every reference to it in opaque expressions; (c) write a test where two handler constructors from different components produce the same base variable name (e.g., both produce `userHandler`) AND both have routes referencing that variable — verify the routes correctly reference the disambiguated names (`userHandler` and `userHandler2`). **The principle: disambiguation that renames a variable at its declaration point but not at its use sites is a dangling reference — the functional equivalent of renaming a function without updating its callers. If variables and their references are stored in separate data structures (e.g., constructors in one list and routes in another), the renaming MUST be applied to both structures or the references will break.**
   90. **Rename/tracking maps keyed by pre-disambiguation names must not silently overwrite entries:** When a disambiguation process tracks renames in a `map[string]string` keyed by the original (pre-disambiguation) name, and multiple inputs within the same scope share the same original name, only the last rename survives — all earlier entries for that key are silently overwritten. This is a data-structure-level bug: the map's cardinality (one value per key) does not match the input's cardinality (multiple entries per key). The consequences cascade: any post-disambiguation rewriting step (e.g. `applyVarRenames`) that consults the map applies the LAST rename to ALL references sharing the same original name — including references that intended to point at the FIRST constructor (which kept its original name and has no rename entry) or at an intermediate constructor (whose rename was overwritten). Item 13 covers silent map key overwrites in data-loading maps; item 89 covers propagating renames to opaque expressions. This item covers the gap: **the rename-tracking data structure itself is lossy when multiple inputs share the same pre-disambiguation key.** The fix requires one of: (a) **structured rename tracking** — instead of `map[string]string` (old→new), track renames per-entry using an index or unique identifier that distinguishes entries sharing the same base name (e.g. `map[int]string` keyed by constructor index, or a slice of `{OldName, NewName}` pairs); (b) **disambiguation before expression construction** — compute all disambiguated names first, then build constructor and route expressions using the final names, eliminating the need for a rename map entirely; (c) **expression-level variable binding** — instead of storing variable names as raw strings in route expressions, use a structured representation where the variable reference is a pointer or index into the constructor list, so disambiguation updates the reference automatically. After writing any code that tracks renames or name mappings in a map keyed by the original name: (a) determine whether multiple inputs can share the same original name (e.g. two constructors in the same wiring deriving the same `rawConstructorVarName`); (b) if yes, verify the map's value type and keying strategy can represent all renames without overwriting — a `map[string]string` CANNOT; (c) write a test where two constructors within the SAME wiring (not just across wirings) produce the same base variable name AND both have routes referencing their respective variables, and verify routes bind to the correct (distinct) handler variables after disambiguation. **The principle: a rename map keyed by the pre-disambiguation name has the same cardinality as the pre-disambiguation namespace — which is exactly the namespace that has collisions. The map must be keyed by something that is already unique (entry index, unique ID) to avoid replicating the collision it is trying to resolve.**
   91. **Input collections with composite keys must be validated for uniqueness across ALL key dimensions — AND for derived-identifier uniqueness:** When a generator or assembler receives a collection of items whose identity is defined by a composite key (multiple fields jointly forming a unique identifier — e.g. slot bindings identified by the triple `(Slot, Entity, OperatorType)`), the uniqueness validation MUST check the full composite key, not just individual dimensions. Item 55 covers uniqueness of single-key collections (e.g. expose blocks by entity name). Item 62 covers duplicate elements in flat sub-collections (e.g. duplicate operations in an operation list). This item covers the gap: **collections where uniqueness is defined by the combination of multiple fields, and duplication of the full composite key produces invalid output.** Concrete example: two slot bindings `{Slot: "before_create", Entity: "User", Gate: ["policy-a"]}` and `{Slot: "before_create", Entity: "User", Gate: ["policy-b"]}` share the same composite key `(before_create, User, Gate)`. The variable name derivation produces the same name for both (e.g. `beforeCreateUserGate`), resulting in duplicate `:=` declarations — a Go compile error. The lookup map (`buildSlotVarsByEntity`) appends the same variable name twice to the entity's injection list, producing constructor calls with duplicate arguments. Neither `go/parser.ParseFile` nor syntax checks catch this because it is a semantic error. After writing any code that processes a collection whose items have multi-field identity: (a) identify which fields jointly define uniqueness — ask "would two items with identical values for all these fields be meaningful, or would they be a configuration error?"; (b) before any processing, scan the collection for composite-key duplicates and return an error identifying the duplicate key and the conflicting entries (e.g. "duplicate slot binding: slot 'before_create' for entity 'User' with operator 'Gate' appears twice"); (c) build the composite key explicitly — e.g. `key := fmt.Sprintf("%s:%s:%s", sb.Slot, sb.Entity, operatorType)` — and check it against a seen-set; (d) write a test with two entries sharing the same composite key but differing in their payload (e.g. different fill lists for the same slot+entity+operator) and verify the generator returns a clear error. **Critically, composite-key uniqueness is necessary but NOT sufficient when the composite key undergoes a lossy transformation to derive an output identifier (per item 82).** When a derivation function (e.g. `slotVarName(slot, entity, suffix)`) applies a normalizing transformation (e.g. `snakeToCamel`) to one or more key dimensions, two composite keys that are DISTINCT in their raw form can produce the SAME derived identifier. For example, slot names `"before_create"` and `"before__create"` (double underscore) are distinct raw keys, but `snakeToCamel` strips underscores and skips empty segments, producing the same output `"beforeCreate"` for both — and therefore the same variable name `"beforeCreateUserGate"`. The composite-key uniqueness check passes (different raw slot names), but the generated code has duplicate `:=` declarations. **After implementing composite-key uniqueness validation, you MUST also compute the derived identifier for every entry (using the exact derivation function that will produce the emitted identifier) and check for collisions in the derived-identifier space.** If two distinct composite keys produce the same derived identifier, return an error identifying both raw keys and the colliding derived name (e.g. "slot bindings 'before_create' and 'before__create' for entity 'User' with operator 'Gate' produce the same variable name 'beforeCreateUserGate'"). Write a test with two entries whose raw composite keys differ only in a dimension that the normalizing transformation discards (e.g. underscore structure, case differences) and verify the generator returns a clear error. **The principle: uniqueness validation must operate on the natural key of the data AND on the derived identifiers that the data produces. When the natural key is composite, validating individual dimensions independently (e.g. checking slot names for uniqueness OR entity names for uniqueness, but not the combination) misses duplicates that are unique in each dimension but identical in their combination. When the derivation function is lossy (as most normalizing transformations are), validating only raw composite keys misses collisions that are distinct in the input space but identical in the output space. Both checks are mandatory — the composite key is the unit of identity for input validation, and the derived identifier is the unit of identity for output validation.**
   92. **Deduplication must propagate associated metadata to all deduplicated entries, not just the representative:** When a combiner or assembler deduplicates entries from multiple sources by a shared key (e.g., deduplicating component import paths via a `seen` map so each import line appears once), and the deduplication process computes metadata for the representative entry (e.g., a disambiguated import alias stored in `importRenames[wiringIndex]`), that metadata MUST be propagated to ALL entries that share the same key — not just the first one encountered. A common error pattern: a `seen` map guards against emitting duplicate import lines. When a second wiring declares the same import path, the `seen` check correctly skips the duplicate import line, but also skips storing the rename mapping for the second wiring's index. The second wiring's constructor and route expressions retain the original package name (e.g., `api.NewBaz()`), but the import alias for that package was disambiguated (e.g., `api2`) and stored only in the first wiring's rename map. The second wiring receives no entry in `importRenames`, so `writeConstructors` and `writeRouteRegistration` — which apply only the current wiring's renames — never rewrite the expressions. The generated code references alias `api` (which resolves to a different package) instead of `api2` (the intended package), producing either a compile error or a silent wrong-function call. **The fix pattern:** when a `seen`-map check determines an entry is a duplicate, the code must still propagate any computed metadata (rename mappings, alias assignments) from the representative entry to the duplicate entry. This can be done by: (a) storing a mapping from the deduplicated key to its computed metadata (e.g., `pathToRenames[fullPath] = map[string]string{base: alias}`), and applying that mapping to every wiring that declares the path — including the ones skipped by the `seen` check; or (b) processing ALL entries to compute metadata (populating `importRenames[i]` for every wiring `i`), and only using the `seen` map to control output emission (which import lines are actually written) — separating "emit this line" from "compute this mapping." After writing any deduplication logic that uses a `seen` map or equivalent: (a) identify ALL metadata or side-effects computed during the processing of the representative entry (rename maps, alias assignments, index associations); (b) verify that each piece of metadata is accessible to every entry that shares the deduplicated key, not just the first; (c) write a test where three or more wirings share the same import path, the path requires disambiguation (e.g., its base name collides with another import), and at least the second and third wirings have constructor or route expressions referencing the original package name — verify the generated code uses the disambiguated alias in ALL wirings' expressions, not just the first. **The principle: deduplication collapses N entries into 1 for output purposes (one import line), but the metadata computed for that output (the alias assignment, the rename mapping) must remain accessible to all N entries that depend on it. A `seen` map that skips both output AND metadata propagation conflates two orthogonal concerns — "should I emit this line?" and "does this wiring need a rename mapping?" — and silently drops the second whenever the first is false.**
   93. **Multiple rename passes on the same opaque string must not interfere — use structured representations or single-pass rewriting:** When post-disambiguation rewriting applies multiple sequential string-replacement passes to the same opaque expression (e.g., first import alias renames, then constructor variable renames on route expressions), and the rename maps from different passes contain entries keyed on the same original identifier, the passes WILL interfere: the first pass transforms a reference intended for the second pass, and the second pass can no longer find the original name to correct it. Items 69 and 89 each address individual rename propagation; item 70 addresses word-boundary safety within a single pass; this item covers the gap: **cross-pass interference when two rename maps overlap in their key space within the same opaque string.** Concrete example: a wiring has import rename `handler → handler2` (because `internal/other/handler` was disambiguated) and constructor variable rename `handler → handler3` (because the derived variable name `handler` collided with reserved aliases). A route expression `mux.HandleFunc("GET /", handler.Get)` is intended to reference constructor variable `handler3`. But the import rename runs first and transforms `handler.Get` → `handler2.Get` (treating it as a package reference). The constructor rename then looks for `handler.` in `handler2.Get` — no match. Final route: `handler2.Get`, referencing the import alias (package) instead of the constructor variable — either a compile error or a silent wrong-function call. **The fundamental issue:** opaque string expressions conflate package-qualified references (`pkg.Function`) and variable-method references (`variable.Method`) into the same syntactic form (`identifier.identifier`). Sequential string-based rename passes cannot distinguish between them. Three approaches prevent this: (a) **Structured route representations** — replace opaque route strings with a struct that separates the route pattern from typed references (e.g., `{Pattern: "GET /", VarRef: constructorIndex, Method: "Get"}`), so each rename pass operates on its own field without affecting the other; (b) **Single-pass rewriting with a unified rename map** — merge import alias renames and constructor variable renames into a single map with disambiguated keys (e.g., by tagging each entry with its category), and apply one pass that can distinguish reference types; (c) **Disambiguation before expression construction** — compute ALL final names (both import aliases and constructor variables) before constructing route expressions, so the expressions contain the final names from the start and no post-hoc rewriting is needed. Approach (c) is the strongest because it eliminates the need for any string-based rewriting of opaque expressions, which is inherently fragile (per item 60). After writing any code that applies multiple sequential rename/rewrite passes to the same string: (a) enumerate every pass and its key space — the set of original names each pass searches for; (b) determine whether any key appears in more than one pass's map — if so, the passes WILL interfere on any expression containing that key; (c) restructure to eliminate the interference using one of the three approaches above; (d) write a test where an identifier appears as a key in BOTH rename maps (e.g., a constructor variable name that equals a disambiguated import's base name) and verify the expression correctly references the intended target after all renames are applied. **The principle: sequential string replacement on an opaque expression is not composable — pass N can corrupt the input that pass N+1 expects. When two passes share a key space, the composition is guaranteed to be wrong for overlapping keys. The fix is not to reorder the passes (reordering just moves the corruption to the other pass) but to eliminate the ambiguity — either by making expressions structured so each pass touches only its own references, or by computing final names before expressions are constructed so no rewriting is needed.**
   94. **Constructor renames from reserved-name collisions must be excluded from route expression rewriting:** When a constructor variable name collides with a reserved identifier (assembler-internal variable like `mux`, `addr`, `db`, `dsn`, `err`, or a stdlib/non-stdlib import alias like `log`, `fmt`, `http`), the constructor is disambiguated (e.g., `mux` → `mux2`) and a rename entry `{OriginalVar: "mux", FinalVar: "mux2"}` is recorded. This rename MUST NOT be applied to route expressions, because the constructor was NEVER named `OriginalVar` in the generated code — it was directly emitted as `FinalVar`. The `OriginalVar` identifier in route expressions (e.g., `mux.HandleFunc(...)`) references the assembler's own variable (`mux := http.NewServeMux()`), not the constructor. Applying the rename corrupts `mux.HandleFunc(...)` into `mux2.HandleFunc(...)`, binding the route to the constructor variable instead of the HTTP mux — either a type error (if the constructor's return type lacks `HandleFunc`) or silently wrong behavior. This is distinct from item 89 (which says to propagate renames to routes) and item 93 (which covers multi-pass interference): here, the rename should NOT be propagated because the route's reference was never a constructor reference in the first place. The distinguishing property is that the `OriginalVar` was pre-reserved — it existed in the scope before the constructor was processed, meaning the constructor was immediately renamed at declaration and no route expression could ever have been constructed using `OriginalVar` as the constructor's name. After writing any code that applies constructor renames to route expressions: (a) filter out renames where `OriginalVar` matches a pre-reserved identifier (assembler-internal vars, stdlib import aliases, non-stdlib import aliases, slot operator vars) — these renames arose from pre-reservation, not from inter-constructor collision, so no route expression references the constructor by its `OriginalVar` name; (b) write a test where a constructor AND routes are in the SAME wiring, the constructor's derived name collides with an assembler-internal variable (e.g., `muxutil.NewMux()` with route `mux.HandleFunc("GET /health", healthHandler.Check)` in the same wiring), and verify the route still references `mux.HandleFunc` (the assembler's variable), not `mux2.HandleFunc`; (c) critically, tests for assembler-internal variable collisions MUST include same-wiring scenarios with routes — putting the colliding constructor and routes in different wirings avoids the failure mode entirely because the rename map is per-wiring. **The principle: a rename entry records that a constructor's INTENDED name was unavailable, not that the constructor was ever CALLED by that name. When the intended name was pre-reserved (not taken by another constructor), no expression in the codebase uses the intended name to reference this constructor — applying the rename to routes can only corrupt references to whatever DID use the reserved name. The safe rule: only apply renames to route expressions when the `OriginalVar` was taken by another constructor in the same scope, not when it was pre-reserved by a non-constructor identifier.**
   95. **Wiring metadata must be contract-tested against the code it describes — cross-generator integration compilation:** When a generator emits wiring metadata (e.g., `ConstructorEntities`, `NeedsDB`, constructor expressions, route expressions) that the assembler consumes to produce `main.go`, the generator's own output files (handler structs, constructor functions, interface definitions) MUST be type-compatible with the assembler's generated calls. The `gen.Wiring` struct is a contract between generators and the assembler: the generator declares "my constructors accept these parameters in this order" and the assembler trusts that declaration to emit constructor calls. If the generator declares `ConstructorEntities` (telling the assembler to inject slot operator arguments into handler constructors) but the generator's handler constructors do not accept those parameters, the assembled `main.go` passes N arguments to a function that accepts M < N — a Go type error. This class of bug is invisible to isolated tests: the assembler tests fabricate `gen.Wiring` values by hand (so they never see the generator's actual constructor signatures), and the generator tests validate handler files in isolation (so they never see the assembler's generated calls). **Only a test that compiles both outputs together can catch the mismatch.** Item 31 requires multi-file compilation within a single generator's output; this item extends that requirement ACROSS generators and the assembler. After writing or modifying any generator that emits `gen.Wiring` metadata: (a) for every field in the `gen.Wiring` struct that the generator populates (constructor expressions, routes, `ConstructorEntities`, `NeedsDB`, imports), trace how the assembler uses that field to generate code — identify every assumption the assembler makes about the generator's output files (constructor parameter counts, method signatures, struct field existence); (b) write an integration test that: (i) runs the generator to produce its output files AND its `gen.Wiring`, (ii) feeds the `gen.Wiring` (along with representative slot bindings if `ConstructorEntities` is populated) to the assembler to produce `main.go`, (iii) places ALL files (generator output + assembler output + slot interface files) in a temporary directory, (iv) runs `go build` on the directory and fails if compilation fails; (c) when adding a new field to `gen.Wiring` that changes the calling convention of generated constructors (e.g., `ConstructorEntities` adds slot operator parameters), update the generator's constructor signatures, struct fields, and method bodies IN THE SAME COMMIT — do not declare metadata that the generator's own code does not yet support; (d) specifically, when `ConstructorEntities` maps entity names to constructor indices, verify that: the constructor function at each index accepts slot operator interface parameters after its existing parameters, the handler struct has fields to store the injected slot operators, and the handler methods invoke the slot operators at the appropriate lifecycle points (gate before create, fan-out after entity changed, etc.). **The principle: wiring metadata is a promise. `ConstructorEntities` says "inject slot operators here" — if "here" cannot accept them, the promise is broken. Isolated unit tests cannot detect broken cross-module promises because each side fabricates the other's behavior. Integration compilation is the only mechanism that enforces the contract end-to-end. A generator that declares wiring metadata it does not support is worse than one that omits the metadata — the omission is visible (no slot operators wired), but the false declaration produces a type error that surfaces only at full compilation, after both modules have independently "passed" their tests.**
   96. **Generated slot/hook invocations must pass populated request objects, not zero-valued structs — with EVERY field enumerated:** When a generator emits code that calls a slot operator (gate, chain, fan-out) at a lifecycle point in a handler method, the slot request object MUST be populated with the data available in scope at the call site — not constructed as a zero-valued struct literal (e.g., `&slots.BeforeCreateRequest{}`). Slot operators exist so that fills can inspect and act on the entity being processed (authorization decisions, audit logging, notifications). A zero-valued request makes every fill non-functional: pointer fields are nil (causing nil-dereference panics when fills access nested fields like `req.Input.Fields["role"]`), and value fields carry no information. The handler has the decoded entity, caller context, and operation metadata in scope at the exact point the slot is invoked — all of this must be threaded into the request object's fields. After writing any generator that emits slot operator invocations: (a) for each `emitBeforeSlot` or `emitAfterSlot` call site, identify every variable in the handler method's scope at that point (decoded entity, request object, path parameters, etc.); (b) **enumerate EVERY field on the slot request type** (e.g., `BeforeCreateRequest` has `Input`, `Caller`, etc.) **and for each field, determine whether data to populate it is available in the handler's scope** — if the data is available, populate the field; if not, document why and ensure the field's zero value does not cause a downstream panic; (c) verify the generated slot request constructor populates its fields from these in-scope variables — at minimum, the entity's data fields AND the caller's identity/context (e.g., `Caller` populated from the HTTP request's authentication context); (d) write a test that generates a handler method with slot invocations and asserts that the generated request constructor references the in-scope entity variable (e.g., `&slots.BeforeCreateRequest{Input: &slots.CreateRequest{...}, Caller: &slots.Identity{...}}` populated from the decoded entity AND the caller context), not an empty struct; (e) if the slot request type has pointer-typed fields (e.g., `Input *CreateRequest`, `Caller *Identity`), verify the generated code initializes ALL such pointers — a nil pointer that fills unconditionally dereference is a guaranteed runtime panic; (f) **apply fix-class exhaustion (item 33) to request field population:** when a fix populates one field (e.g., `Input`), grep the request type definition for ALL other fields and verify each one is also populated or explicitly documented as intentionally omitted — a fix that populates `Input` but leaves `Caller` nil is an incomplete fix that will be caught in the next review round. The canonical spec fill example (`fills/admin-creation-policy/policy.go`) accesses BOTH `req.Input.Fields["role"]` AND `req.Caller.Role` — if either field is nil, the fill panics. Item 31 (compilation) and item 95 (cross-generator integration compilation) catch type errors but NOT runtime panics or semantic incorrectness — a handler that compiles perfectly but passes empty data to every fill is functionally inert. **The principle: a slot invocation with a zero-valued request is worse than no invocation at all — it gives the appearance of integration (the slot is "called") while providing none of the value (no data flows through it). Every field on a slot request type exists because a fill needs to read it; if the generator doesn't populate it, the fill cannot function. Populating SOME fields but not others is partial integration — equally broken, just harder to diagnose. **Critically, when the emission point is polymorphic (item 46) — i.e., the concrete request type varies by slot configuration — item 96's "enumerate every field" mandate must be applied PER CONCRETE TYPE, not once against an assumed-universal type. A fix that populates `Caller` on `BeforeCreateRequest` but emits the same `Caller` field for `ValidateRequest` (which has no `Caller` field) converts a runtime bug (nil panic) into a compile error (unknown field) — and simultaneously leaves `ValidateRequest`'s unique `entity` string field unpopulated. After populating any slot request field, check item 46: does the emission point serve multiple concrete types? If yes, verify the populated field exists on ALL of them, and verify each type's unique fields are also populated.**
   97. **Generated convenience constructors must nil-guard injected interface invocations for graceful degradation:** When a generator emits a convenience constructor (e.g., `NewRouter`) that does not receive slot operator parameters from the assembler and instead passes `nil` for interface-typed constructor arguments, the handler methods that invoke those interfaces MUST include nil guards (e.g., `if h.beforeCreateGate != nil { ... }`) so that the handler degrades gracefully to passthrough semantics when no fills are wired. A nil interface value is valid in Go — assigning `nil` to an interface field compiles — but calling a method on a nil interface panics at runtime. If `NewRouter` passes `nil` for all slot operator parameters, and handler methods unconditionally call `h.beforeCreateGate.Evaluate(...)`, every request to an entity with slot bindings panics. The spec's `default: passthrough` slot behavior requires that entities with declared slots work correctly even when no fills are provided — the slot mechanism should be additive (fills enhance behavior) not mandatory (missing fills crash the service). After writing any generator that emits both an assembler-facing constructor (accepting slot operators from `main.go`) and a convenience/self-contained constructor (hardcoding `nil` for slot operators): (a) for every handler method that invokes a slot operator field, verify the invocation is wrapped in a nil check: `if h.slotField != nil { result, err := h.slotField.Evaluate(...); ... }`; (b) verify that when the nil check skips the invocation, the handler continues with passthrough behavior (the operation proceeds as if no slot were configured); (c) write a test that constructs a handler via the convenience constructor (with nil slot operators) and calls each handler method — verify no panic occurs and the operation succeeds with passthrough semantics; (d) write a separate test that constructs a handler with non-nil slot operators and verifies they are actually invoked. **The invariant: if a constructor parameter can legally be nil (and `NewRouter` makes it nil), every method that uses that parameter must handle nil. A method that calls `.Evaluate()` on a potentially-nil interface without a nil check has a guaranteed panic on the nil path.**
   98. **Generated code consuming structured results must handle ALL independently significant fields — not just the error path:** When a generator emits code that receives a structured result type from a slot operator, middleware, or any hook-style invocation (e.g., `SlotResult` with fields `Ok`, `Halt`, `StatusCode`, `ErrorMessage`), the consuming code MUST handle EVERY field that can independently influence control flow — not just the primary success/failure field. A result type with N independent behavioral fields can represent 2^N distinct states; checking only one field collapses the state space and silently ignores the others. Concrete example: `SlotResult{Ok: true, Halt: true, StatusCode: 204}` is a valid state meaning "the chain succeeded but wants to halt further processing and return 204 (no-op)." If the consuming code only checks `!slotResult.Ok`, this state is treated as success and the handler continues executing the main operation — violating the spec's "Short-circuit halt stops chain and returns status" acceptance criterion. The halt works at the operator level (the chain stops calling subsequent fills), but NOT at the handler level (the handler ignores the halt signal and proceeds). After writing any generator that emits code consuming a structured result type: (a) enumerate EVERY field on the result type; (b) for each field, determine whether it can independently trigger a distinct behavioral outcome — ask "is there a valid state where this field's value changes the correct handler behavior while all other fields remain the same?"; (c) for each independently significant field, verify the generated code checks it and responds appropriately (e.g., `if slotResult.Halt { w.WriteHeader(slotResult.StatusCode); return }`); (d) write a test for each distinct behavioral state: success (`Ok: true, Halt: false`), rejection (`Ok: false`), AND halt (`Ok: true, Halt: true`) — verify each state produces the correct handler behavior (continue, error response, halt with status code respectively); (e) **order the checks correctly:** a halt check must come AFTER the error check but BEFORE the continuation path — `if !Ok { error } else if Halt { return status } else { continue }`. A check that only handles the error path and falls through to continuation on everything else silently ignores halt. **The principle: a structured result type is a multi-dimensional signal. Checking one dimension and ignoring the rest is the consumer-side analog of item 96's producer-side bug (populating one field and leaving others zero). Both are partial integration — the mechanism exists but only partially functions. Every field on a result type exists because it represents a distinct behavioral outcome; if the consumer doesn't check it, that outcome is silently dropped.**
   99. **Bidirectional set comparison for state diffs:** When computing differences between a previous state and a current state (e.g. old entities vs new entities, old files vs new files, old config keys vs new config keys), you MUST iterate BOTH collections — not just the current one. Iterating only the current collection detects additions and modifications but silently misses deletions. A deleted item that exists in the old state but not in the new state will never be visited, and the diff will report zero changes for it. After writing any diff/comparison function: (a) verify there is a loop over the current collection to detect additions and modifications; (b) verify there is a SEPARATE loop (or equivalent check) over the previous collection to detect items that are absent from the current collection — these are deletions; (c) write a test that removes an item entirely between two states and verify the diff reports the removal. The pattern is: `for item in new { check against old }` catches additions; `for item in old { check against new }` catches deletions. Both are required for a correct diff. A function named `computeChanges` that only detects additions is silently incomplete — it should be named `computeAdditions` or it should detect all change types\.
   100. **Read-once for hashing and parsing — no TOCTOU on external inputs:** When a function reads an external input (file, network response, stdin) for multiple purposes (e.g. hashing its content AND parsing its structure), it MUST read the input exactly once and reuse the in-memory bytes for all consumers. Reading the same external input multiple times creates a time-of-check-to-time-of-use (TOCTOU) race: if the input changes between reads, the hash corresponds to one version while the parsed structure corresponds to another. For files, this means the state record claims a hash that does not match the declaration that was actually compiled — breaking auditability. After writing any function that both hashes and parses the same input: (a) read the input once into a `[]byte`; (b) compute the hash from that byte slice; (c) pass the same byte slice to the parser (if the parser only accepts a file path, refactor it to accept `[]byte` or `io.Reader`); (d) write a test or verify by inspection that the hash and parse operate on identical bytes. The same principle applies to any dual-consumption of external input — checksumming + deserializing, validation + processing, logging + execution\. **The invariant: within a single operation, all consumers of an external input must see the same content.**
   101. **Doc comments must describe the function they are attached to — not a copy-pasted neighbor:** After writing or moving any doc comment, verify that the comment's subject matches the function, method, or type it is attached to. Copy-pasting a function along with its doc comment and then renaming the function without updating the comment produces a misleading annotation — the comment describes a different function's behavior, and readers will be misled about what the annotated function does. After writing any doc comment: (a) re-read the first sentence and verify it names or accurately describes the function/method/type immediately below it; (b) if the comment references a function name (e.g. "HasChanges returns true if..."), verify that name matches the actual function name; (c) if you copy-pasted a function, grep the comment for the original function's name and replace it. **The principle: a wrong doc comment is worse than no doc comment — no comment leaves the reader to read the code; a wrong comment actively misdirects them.**
   102. **Multiple code paths checking the same logical condition must use the same predicate:** When two or more code paths independently check whether a condition is true (e.g. "are there changes to apply?"), they MUST use the same predicate function or expression — not different subsets of the full condition. A common error pattern: one code path checks `HasFileChanges()` while another checks `HasFileChanges() || HasEntityChanges()`. These code paths will disagree when entity changes exist but file changes do not: one says "no changes" and skips processing, while the other would report changes. After writing any boolean check that gates a major code path (e.g. early return for "no changes", conditional display, skip-if-empty): (a) grep for other locations in the codebase that check the same logical condition under a different name or with a different expression; (b) if multiple sites exist, extract the predicate into a single function and use it everywhere — or at minimum verify that all expressions are logically equivalent; (c) write a test for the "disagreement" case: the state where the broader predicate is true but the narrower predicate is false (e.g. entity changes exist but file changes do not), and verify all code paths behave consistently. **The principle: two code paths that answer the same question differently will produce inconsistent user-visible behavior — one says "nothing to do" while the other would say "changes detected." Extract the question into one place.**
   82. **Collision detection must compare derived identifiers, not source names:** When a validation function detects naming collisions between entities (e.g., case-insensitive entity name uniqueness), the comparison MUST be performed on the DERIVED identifiers that actually collide in the output — not on an intermediate transformation of the source names. Item 61 requires normalized-form collision detection; this item specifies HOW that detection must be implemented. A common error pattern: the validation compares `strings.ToLower(entity.Name)` to detect "case-insensitive" collisions, but the actual collision risk is in derived identifiers like table names (produced by `toSnakeCase` + `pluralize`), Go type names, or route paths. Two entity names can differ in their lowercased form but produce identical table names — e.g., `UserProfile` and `User_Profile` both produce table name `user_profiles` via `toSnakeCase` + `pluralize`, but `strings.ToLower` produces `"userprofile"` vs `"user_profile"` (different). The validation passes, but the generated migration emits two `CREATE TABLE IF NOT EXISTS "user_profiles"` statements — the second silently succeeds due to `IF NOT EXISTS` but has different columns, and both entities' CRUD methods target the same table with mismatched column sets. After writing any collision-detection validation: (a) identify what the validation is ACTUALLY protecting — is it Go type names, SQL table names, route paths, or file paths? (b) apply the SAME transformation chain that produces the protected identifier (e.g., `tableName()` for SQL table collision detection, `toPascalCase()` for Go type collision detection); (c) do NOT substitute a simpler transformation (e.g., `strings.ToLower`) that approximates but does not match the actual derivation; (d) write a test with two entity names that are different under the simpler transformation but identical under the actual derivation (e.g., `UserProfile` and `User_Profile` for table name collision) and verify the validation catches it. **The principle: collision detection is only correct when it compares the exact outputs that would collide. Comparing a proxy (lowercased source name) instead of the actual derived identifier (table name) is a false-negative factory — it misses exactly the collisions it exists to prevent.**
   103. **Deterministic output from map iteration:** When iterating over a Go map to produce ANY form of output — user-visible text (plan summaries, change lists, error reports), generated code, specification documents, state files, or data structures that feed into ordered output — the iteration MUST produce deterministic, reproducible ordering. Go map iteration is explicitly non-deterministic per the language specification; `for k, v := range myMap` produces different orderings between runs. Non-deterministic output violates reproducibility ("identical inputs must produce identical outputs"), makes diffs noisy (reordering appears as a change when nothing changed), and causes flaky tests. After writing any code that iterates over a map to produce output or populates a slice from a map: (a) collect the map keys into a slice and sort them before iteration; (b) use a well-defined, stable sort criterion (e.g. lexicographic by name); (c) iterate the sorted keys, not the map directly; (d) when adding a new map-driven loop alongside existing loops in the same function that already sort before iterating, the new loop MUST follow the same pattern — inconsistency within the same function is a strong signal of a missed case. **The determinism requirement applies even when the output order "doesn't matter" for correctness** (e.g. entity change entries in a plan, deleted-file entries in a changeset). Non-deterministic output is a process smell that indicates the code path was not fully considered, and it makes plan output non-reproducible — two runs with identical inputs should produce identical plan text, character for character. After writing any diff or changeset function, verify that EVERY loop that populates the output iterates in a deterministic order — if some loops sort and others do not, the sorted loops demonstrate that the author understood the requirement but missed applying it to the unsorted loops\. **Mandatory audit step:** after writing or modifying ANY function that iterates a map, AND after fixing any `needs-revision` finding related to map iteration non-determinism, run `grep -n 'range ' <file>` on EVERY `.go` file you created or modified and examine EVERY `range` loop. For each loop, determine whether the variable being ranged over is a map (check its declaration or the return type of the function that produces it). For every map iteration found, verify that the keys are collected into a sorted slice before iteration. Do NOT stop at the function named in the finding — audit the entire file. This is an instance of item 33 (fix-class exhaustion) with pattern signature "unsorted iteration over a Go map." A file that has three functions iterating `reg.Components()` (a map) where one sorts and two do not is three instances of the same bug — fixing only the one named in the finding guarantees the other two will be found in the next review round\.
   104. **State reconciliation completeness across all tracked dimensions:** When a reconciler manages multiple categories of tracked state (e.g. files AND entities AND config values AND dependency versions), every diff algorithm within the reconciler MUST detect ALL change types (addition, modification, deletion) for EVERY category. Item 99 specifies the technique (bidirectional set comparison) for a single diff; item 33 (fix-class exhaustion) requires applying a fix across all code sites with the same pattern. This item applies both principles to the state model itself: when you implement or fix change detection for one state category, you MUST immediately enumerate ALL other state categories the reconciler tracks and verify each one supports all three change types. Concrete example: a reconciler that detects entity additions and entity deletions (item 99 applied) but only detects file additions (files in new state but not old) without detecting file orphans (files in old state but not new state) has a category-specific gap — the same class of bug (one-directional diff) at a different state dimension. After implementing or fixing any diff logic in a reconciler: (a) list every category of state the reconciler tracks (files, entities, config hashes, dependency versions, etc.); (b) for each category, verify the diff detects additions, modifications, AND deletions; (c) write a test for each category × change-type pair, with emphasis on the deletion case (the most commonly missed); (d) when fixing a deletion-detection bug for one category, apply item 33 (fix-class exhaustion) with the pattern signature "one-directional diff that only iterates the new collection" and grep every other diff function in the reconciler for the same pattern.
