A reconciler that correctly handles deletions for entities but not for files will leave orphaned files on disk that reference deleted types — breaking downstream compilation and violating "plan shows accurate changeset" requirements. **The principle: a reconciler's state model has N dimensions; each dimension needs three change types. That is 3×N coverage cells. Any cell that is missing is a silent data integrity gap. After adding or fixing any cell, audit all 3×N cells — not just the row or column you touched.**
   105. **Pipeline parameter consistency — no hardcoded defaults for parameterized concepts:** When functions in the same processing pipeline operate on the same configurable concept (e.g. output directory, base URL, registry path), and ANY function in the pipeline exposes that concept as a parameter, EVERY other function in the pipeline that references the same concept MUST either accept it as a parameter or derive it from the same configuration source. Item 49 covers partial parameterization within a single function or generator's output; this item extends it to parameter consistency ACROSS functions in a pipeline. A common error pattern: a public API function accepts a parameter (e.g. `Apply(plan, projectDir, outDir)`), but an upstream or downstream function in the same pipeline hardcodes the default for the same concept (e.g. `computePlan` using `filepath.Join(projectDir, "out", ...)` instead of accepting `outDir`). The pipeline works as long as callers pass the default, but silently produces wrong results when the parameter is overridden — the plan checks files in one directory while apply writes to another. After writing any function that accepts a configurable parameter: (a) trace the full pipeline — every function upstream (that prepares data for this function) and downstream (that acts on this function's output) that references the same concept; (b) grep for hardcoded occurrences of the parameter's default value in the pipeline (e.g. if `outDir` defaults to `"out"`, grep for the literal `"out"` in path construction calls across the pipeline); (c) replace every hardcoded occurrence with the parameterized form or thread the parameter through the call chain; (d) write a test that passes a non-default value for the parameter and verifies that ALL functions in the pipeline consistently use the non-default value — if any function still references the default, the parameterization is incomplete. **The principle: a parameter that is accepted by one function but hardcoded by another in the same pipeline is a ticking inconsistency. It works by coincidence (callers happen to use the default) and breaks by configuration (callers override the parameter). Item 49 asks "does this function use its own parameter everywhere?" This item asks "do OTHER functions in the same pipeline use the same concept consistently?"**
   106. **State and snapshot data structures must preserve all spec-required attributes for comparison and display:** When building an internal data structure (state record, snapshot, diff input) that captures entity or component state for later comparison or display, the structure MUST preserve every attribute that (a) the spec shows in example output for that feature, or (b) could change independently and constitute a meaningful state change. A common error pattern: the state structure stores only the identifying attribute (e.g. field names as `[]string`) while discarding qualifying attributes (e.g. field types, constraints, flags). This makes it impossible for downstream code to produce spec-conformant output or detect meaningful changes — the data was discarded upstream, and no amount of correct downstream code can recover it. Concrete example: if the spec shows plan output as `+ field: display_name (string)` (name + type), but the entity snapshot stores only `[]string` of field names, then (1) `FormatPlan` cannot display types because the type information does not exist in the snapshot, and (2) `computeEntityChanges` cannot detect type changes (e.g. `string` → `int32`) because two snapshots with identical field names but different types are indistinguishable. Both are spec violations caused by the same upstream data loss. After building any snapshot or state structure: (a) find every place the spec shows the structure's data being displayed, compared, or referenced; (b) list every attribute visible in the spec's example output or mentioned as a detectable change; (c) verify each attribute is present in the snapshot structure — if an attribute is missing, the feature cannot match the spec regardless of downstream correctness; (d) when the structure is used for change detection (diffing old vs new), verify the comparison examines all preserved attributes, not just identity fields. **The principle: a diff algorithm can only detect changes in attributes that the snapshot preserves. A snapshot that discards attributes is a ceiling on what the diff can detect — making the diff code correct but incomplete by construction. Item 2 covers lossy type mappings in config schemas; this item extends the same principle to internal state and snapshot data structures used for reconciliation and display.**
   107. **Configurable values must use established resolution mechanisms — no inline defaults that bypass resolution:** When the codebase provides a resolution mechanism for a configurable concept (e.g. `resolveComponentConfig` for component configuration values, a config lookup function for registry-defined defaults), every usage of that concept's value MUST go through the resolution mechanism — never hardcode the default value inline. A hardcoded default (e.g. `httpPort := 8080`) silently bypasses any user-configured override (e.g. `overrides: rest-api: port: 9090`) because the resolution mechanism — which applies the override — is never invoked. This is distinct from item 49 (partial parameterization within a generator's output) and item 105 (pipeline parameter consistency across functions): those cover parameters that are explicitly threaded through function signatures. This item covers cases where a resolution mechanism ALREADY EXISTS in the codebase and a specific code path bypasses it entirely by inlining the default instead of calling the resolver. The mechanism exists to centralize default-and-override logic; hardcoding the default elsewhere defeats this centralization. After writing any code that uses a configurable value with a known default: (a) search the codebase for an existing resolution function for that concept (e.g. grep for the config field name in resolver, config, or component-loading code); (b) if a resolution mechanism exists, call it instead of hardcoding the default; (c) if the resolution mechanism's output is already available in the current scope (e.g. via `ctx.ComponentConfig`), use the resolved value from that output; (d) write a test with a non-default override for the concept and verify the override takes effect everywhere the value is used — if any code path still uses the default, the bypass is the cause. **The litmus test: does the codebase already have a function or data structure that resolves this value? If yes, use it. A hardcoded default next to a resolution mechanism is a bug waiting for someone to configure an override and discover it is silently ignored.**
   108. **Struct literal completeness at integration boundaries — every field must be evaluated at every construction site:** Checklist item 1 requires that struct fields are assigned a non-zero value by "at least one code path." This is necessary but insufficient: a field that is assigned only in unit tests for the declaring package (e.g. `resolve_test.go`) but never in the production caller that constructs the struct (e.g. the reconciler building `ports.ResolveInput`) is dead from the production perspective — the feature works in isolation but is disconnected from the application by missing wiring at the integration boundary. After constructing any struct literal that is passed as input to a function in a different package or module: (a) open the struct type definition and enumerate EVERY field; (b) for each field, determine whether the calling context has access to data that should populate it — consult the spec for what the calling context should provide; (c) if the spec describes a feature that maps to a field on the struct (e.g. "service declaration can override port bindings" maps to `ServiceOverrides`), verify the caller extracts the relevant data from its own inputs and populates the field; (d) if a field is intentionally left at zero for this call site, add a comment explaining why (e.g. `// ServiceOverrides: nil — not applicable for initial validation pass`); (e) **do not count test-only assignments as production wiring.** After constructing any cross-package struct literal, grep for ALL assignments to each field of that struct type — if every assignment is in a `_test.go` file, the field is dead in production. The principle: item 1 asks "is this field ever assigned?" This item asks "is this field assigned at every integration boundary where it should be?" A field that is tested in isolation but never wired at the integration point is the most insidious form of dead code — all tests pass, the feature appears to work, but the production path silently omits it\.
   109. **Conditional validation guards must defensively cover the absent-value branch:** When a validation check is gated on a configuration value being present (e.g. `if comp.OutputNamespace != ""`), the absent-value branch (`else`, or the implicit fall-through when there is no `else`) MUST explicitly handle the unvalidated case — either by applying an alternative validation, by rejecting the configuration as incomplete, or by asserting a defensive invariant that guarantees the validation is unnecessary. A bare `if value != "" { validate(value) }` with no `else` branch creates a silent validation hole: the code implicitly assumes that when the value is absent, no validation is needed. This assumption may hold today (e.g. generators for components without a namespace currently return zero files), but it is not enforced — a future change that adds file output to a namespace-less component will silently bypass namespace validation with no error. After writing any conditional validation gate: (a) identify what the validation protects against (e.g. files written outside an allowed namespace); (b) determine whether the threat can exist when the guarding value is absent (e.g. can a component without a declared namespace produce files?); (c) if the threat can exist, add validation for the absent case (e.g. `if comp.OutputNamespace == "" && len(files) > 0 { return error "component X produced files but declares no output_namespace" }`); (d) if the threat provably cannot exist today, add a defensive assertion that will fail loudly if the assumption is violated in the future — do NOT silently skip; (e) write a test for the absent-value case with the threatening condition present (e.g. a component with no `output_namespace` that produces files) and verify the code either rejects it with a clear error or validates it correctly. **The principle: an `if` without an `else` on a validation path is an implicit claim that the `else` case is safe. Implicit safety claims must be made explicit — either via an alternative validation or a defensive assertion. "We don't validate because the situation can't arise" must be enforced, not assumed.**
   110. **Aggregated output paths from multiple producers must be validated for uniqueness before writing:** When multiple independent producers (generators, assemblers, slot processors, fill loaders) each contribute output files to a shared collection that will be written to disk, the aggregator MUST validate that no two entries share the same output file path before any file is written. A duplicate path means the later entry silently overwrites the earlier one in the output slice — one producer's output is lost with no error, no warning, and no diagnostic. This is the output-side analog of item 55 (input collection uniqueness) and the filesystem analog of item 59's import alias collision detection. Item 13 (no silent map key collisions) covers map-based storage, but file collections are typically slices — duplicates in a slice are not detected by the language, unlike maps where reinsertion is at least observable. The spec may provide authoring-time guarantees (e.g. "the archetype validates that no two component namespaces overlap"), but the compiler cannot rely on authoring-time validation — it must perform its own runtime check because (a) the archetype may be misconfigured, (b) components without declared namespaces (Finding 15) can write anywhere, and (c) assembler-produced shared files (e.g. `cmd/main.go`, `go.mod`) may collide with generator-produced files if a generator erroneously emits files at shared-file paths. After writing any code that appends files from multiple sources into a single output collection: (a) after all sources have contributed their files, build a map of file paths to their sources; (b) if two entries share a path, return an error identifying both sources and the colliding path (e.g. "generator 'rest-api' and assembler both produce 'cmd/main.go'"); (c) include ALL file sources in the check — generator output, shared/assembled files, slot-generated files, and fill files; (d) perform the check BEFORE writing any files to disk, so the user sees a single clear error rather than silently corrupted output; (e) write a test where two independent producers emit files at the same path and verify the aggregator returns a clear error rather than silently dropping one file's content. **The principle: a filesystem is a key-value store where file paths are keys. Writing two values to the same key without detection is a silent data loss bug. The same invariant that item 13 enforces for maps must be enforced for file path collections — uniqueness is not optional just because the storage medium changed from a map to a directory.**
   111. **State-based plan actions must verify on-disk presence before assuming file exists:** When a reconciler computes file actions by comparing generated content against tracked state (e.g. "hash in current state matches hash of newly generated content → unchanged"), the state comparison alone is insufficient — the file may no longer exist on disk despite being tracked in the state (manual deletion, failed prior apply, filesystem corruption). Every plan action that implies the file EXISTS on disk (e.g. `ActionUnchanged`, `ActionUpdate`) MUST verify on-disk presence via `os.Stat` or equivalent before assigning the action. If the file is tracked in state but missing from disk, the correct action is `ActionGenerate` (or a new `ActionRestore`), not `ActionUnchanged`. A plan that marks a missing file as "unchanged" violates "plan shows accurate changeset" — the changeset omits a file that needs to be written. When the plan shows no changes, the apply step is typically skipped entirely (e.g. `if !plan.HasChanges() { return }`), meaning the missing file is never recreated. After writing any plan computation that uses state hashes as a shortcut: (a) identify every code branch that assigns an action implying on-disk presence; (b) verify each branch includes an `os.Stat` check on the actual disk path; (c) write a test that deletes a generated file from disk between applies (without modifying service.yaml) and verifies the plan shows the file as needing regeneration, not "unchanged."
   112. **Module structure must support all import paths the assembler generates:** When a code generator produces both a module definition file (e.g. `go.mod`) and source files that import packages from specific paths, every import path generated MUST be resolvable relative to the module root (the directory containing `go.mod`). If the module root is placed in a subdirectory (e.g. `out/`), but import paths reference packages that exist at a DIFFERENT level of the directory tree (e.g. project-root `fills/`), the imports are structurally unresolvable — the Go toolchain looks for packages relative to the module root and has no mechanism (short of `replace` directives or multi-module workspaces) to find packages outside it. After writing any assembler that generates both a module definition and import statements: (a) enumerate every import path the assembler can generate; (b) for each import path, trace where the package would physically exist on disk relative to the module root; (c) verify the package location is under the module root — if it is not, either adjust the module root placement, generate appropriate `replace` directives, or restructure the project layout so all referenced packages are under the module root; (d) write a test that configures the feature generating cross-boundary imports (e.g. slot bindings with fills) and verifies the generated output compiles. **The principle: `go build` success requires every import to resolve. Import resolution follows module boundaries. If the assembler generates imports that cross module boundaries without bridging mechanisms, `go build` is structurally impossible — not due to missing code, but due to a topological impossibility in the module layout.**
   113. **Cross-generator invocation patterns must be derived from wiring metadata or verified against actual type definitions — not hardcoded:** When the assembler emits code that invokes a value produced by another generator (middleware wrapping, handler method calls, factory functions), the invocation pattern (function call vs method call, method name, argument signature) MUST be derived from structured wiring metadata or verified against the generator's actual type definitions — never hardcoded based on an assumption about the target type's interface. The assembler cannot see the generator's type definitions at generation time; when it hardcodes a method call like `.Middleware(mux)` on a middleware variable, it assumes the value is a struct with a `Middleware` method. If the generator actually returns `func(http.Handler) http.Handler` (a bare function type), the correct invocation is `middlewareVar(mux)` — function types have no methods, and `.Middleware()` produces a compile error: `authMiddleware.Middleware undefined (type func(http.Handler) http.Handler has no field or method Middleware)`. Syntax-only test validation (`parser.ParseFile`) will NOT catch this because it is a semantic type error — per item 31, only actual compilation detects type mismatches. After writing any assembler code that invokes a value produced by another generator: (a) read the generator's actual function signature that produces the value — identify the concrete return type (struct, interface, function type); (b) verify the emitted invocation pattern is valid for that concrete type — a method call (`.Method(args)`) requires the type to have that method, while a function call (`varName(args)`) requires the type to be callable; (c) if the invocation pattern varies by generator (some return structs with methods, others return function values), encode the calling convention in the wiring metadata (e.g. a `MiddlewareCallStyle` field or a `WrapExpression` template) so the assembler can adapt without hardcoding assumptions; (d) write an integration test per item 95 that compiles the assembler's output together with the generator's output, ensuring the emitted method/function calls type-check against the actual definitions — specifically, do NOT rely on `parser.ParseFile` for tests involving cross-generator invocations; (e) when the generator and assembler are modified independently, re-verify that the assembler's assumptions still hold against the generator's current type definitions. **The principle: a cross-generator invocation is a contract between the generator (which defines the type) and the assembler (which calls it). Hardcoding the calling convention in the assembler without consulting the generator's type definitions is a unilateral contract assumption — it will break silently whenever the generator's return type doesn't match the assumption. The test that "validates" the wrong invocation pattern by string-matching and syntax parsing is worse than no test — it asserts the bug is the intended behavior.**
   115. **Nil vs empty collection semantics for constraint/whitelist fields:** When a Go struct field uses a slice or map to represent a constraint set (whitelist, allowlist, compatibility list, `compatible_*`, `allowed_*`, `supported_*`), the code MUST distinguish between nil (field absent from the source data — meaning no constraint is applied, any value is acceptable) and empty (field explicitly declared as empty, e.g. `compatible_mixins: []` in YAML — meaning the constraint applies but allows nothing). In Go, YAML deserialization with `gopkg.in/yaml.v3` produces a nil slice when the field is absent from the YAML document, and a non-nil empty slice (`[]string{}`) when the field is explicitly `[]`. The guard `len(field) > 0` conflates these two semantically distinct states: both nil and empty slices have length 0, but nil means "no restriction" (the field was not declared, so no constraint exists) while empty means "nothing is allowed" (the field was declared with zero entries, so the whitelist permits nothing). The correct guard for "is this constraint declared?" is `field != nil`, not `len(field) > 0`. After writing any validation that checks a slice-typed constraint field: (a) determine the semantic of the nil case — does nil mean "unconstrained" or "error"? (b) determine the semantic of the empty case — does empty mean "nothing allowed" or same as nil? (c) if nil and empty have different meanings (which is the common case for whitelist/allowlist fields), use a nil check (`field != nil`) as the gate, not a length check (`len(field) > 0`); (d) write tests for BOTH the nil case (field absent from YAML — verify no constraint is applied) AND the empty case (field explicitly `[]` — verify the constraint rejects all values); (e) apply fix-class exhaustion (item 33) — after fixing one whitelist field, grep for ALL slice-typed constraint fields on the same type and verify each uses the correct nil-vs-empty guard. **The principle: in Go, nil and empty are distinct states for slices and maps. A whitelist that conflates "no whitelist exists" with "whitelist exists but is empty" has a security-grade semantic error — it converts an explicitly restrictive configuration into an unrestricted one. The guard `len(field) > 0` is correct for "does this list have items to process?" but WRONG for "was this constraint declared?" — use `field != nil` for the latter.**
   116. **Go `replace` directives only apply to external module dependencies — not intra-module packages:** When a generator produces `replace` directives in a `go.mod` file to redirect import paths to different disk locations, the generator MUST verify that the import paths being redirected are NOT sub-paths of the main module path. Go's module resolution treats any import path that begins with the main module's path prefix (e.g. `github.com/myorg/svc/fills/foo` when the module is `github.com/myorg/svc`) as an **intra-module package**, resolving it relative to the module root directory (`out/`). Go does NOT consult `replace` directives for intra-module packages — `replace` only applies to external module dependencies. A `replace` directive for an intra-module path (e.g. `replace github.com/myorg/svc/fills/foo => ../fills/foo`) is silently ignored, and the import fails with a package-not-found error. Additionally, per the Go module specification, "a replace directive alone does not add a module to the module graph" — every `replace` MUST have a corresponding `require` directive, or the replacement is never consulted even for external modules. After writing any generator that produces `replace` directives: (a) for each `replace` directive, check whether the source path is a sub-path of the main module path — if it is, the directive is inert and the approach must be restructured (e.g. place `go.mod` at the project root so fills are under the module root, use a `go.work` workspace file, or restructure the output layout); (b) for each `replace` directive, verify a corresponding `require` directive exists in the generated `go.mod`; (c) write a test that generates a `go.mod` with `replace` directives and actually runs `go build` (not `go/parser.ParseFile` — per item 31, syntax parsing does not catch module resolution failures) to verify the imports resolve; (d) do NOT treat `replace` as a universal escape hatch for module boundary problems — it has strict applicability constraints that must be understood before relying on it. **The principle: `replace` directives are a frequently misunderstood Go module feature. They redirect one MODULE to another MODULE — they do not redirect one PACKAGE within a module to a different disk location. When the import path shares the main module prefix, Go treats it as an intra-module package and looks for it under the module root, period. No `replace` directive can change this. A generator that emits `replace` directives for intra-module paths has a structurally broken module layout that no amount of `replace` entries can fix — the fix must address the root cause (module root placement or import path structure).**
   117. **Derived decision sets in code generation must be computed from observed facts — not assumed containment:** When code generation logic derives a decision set from one collection and uses it to affect behavior governed by a different collection, the assumed containment relationship between the two collections MUST be either (a) validated at the point of derivation, or (b) replaced by computing the decision from the actual intersection of the two collections. A common error pattern: an assembler builds a set of "injected" variable names from ALL entity-associated slot bindings (collection A = `slotVarsByEntity`), then uses this set to decide whether slot variables receive `_ =` suppression. The suppression decision depends on whether a matching constructor exists (collection B = constructors derived from the expose list). The code assumes A ⊆ B (every entity in slotVarsByEntity has a constructor), but this is only true when slot binding entities are a subset of exposed entities. When A ⊄ B, variables are created without suppression and without injection — producing unused variables and a compile error. The fix is to compute the `injected` set from the intersection of A and B (entities that have BOTH slot bindings AND constructors), not from A alone. After writing any code generation logic that derives a decision set from one collection: (a) identify every other collection whose behavior the decision set affects — e.g. if the decision set determines variable suppression, identify the collection that determines variable consumption (constructors); (b) verify the assumed containment relationship: is it guaranteed by upstream validation (item 51) that every key in the derived set exists in the consuming collection? If yes, cite the validation that enforces it. If no, either add the validation or compute the decision from the intersection; (c) write a test where the containment assumption is violated (e.g. a slot binding declares an entity not in the expose list) and verify the code either returns a clear validation error (if upstream validation is the fix) or correctly computes the decision from the intersection (if defensive computation is the fix) — not an unused-variable compile error in the generated output. **The principle: an implicit assumption that one set is contained in another is a latent bug. It works as long as the data happens to satisfy the assumption, and breaks silently — or with confusing downstream errors — when it doesn't. Either validate the assumption (fail early with a diagnostic) or eliminate it (compute from the intersection). Never assume containment between independently-derived collections without either proof or a check.**
   119. **New operations must inherit conditional-import obligations from every type they use:** When adding a new operation type (e.g. `patch`) to a generator, and the new operation produces structs or code that reference Go types requiring specific imports (e.g. `*time.Time` requires `"time"`, `json.RawMessage` requires `"encoding/json"`), the conditional-import flags (e.g. `needTime`, `needJSON`) that gate those imports MUST be updated to include the new operation. A common error pattern: the `needTime` flag checks `op == OpCreate || op == OpUpdate || op == OpUpsert` to determine if the `"time"` import is needed for computed timestamp zero-values, but a new `OpPatch` operation generates `*time.Time` pointer fields in its patch request struct for patchable timestamp fields — yet the flag does not include `OpPatch`. The handler file omits `import "time"`, producing `undefined: time` at compile time. The root cause is that the conditional-import logic enumerates operations by name rather than by the property that actually triggers the import need (i.e. "does this operation's generated code reference `time.Time`?"). **Mandatory audit step when adding ANY new operation:** (a) enumerate every conditional-import flag in the handler generator (`needTime`, `needJSON`, `needStrconv`, `needErrors`, `needFmt`, `needStrings`, `needUUID`, etc.); (b) for each flag, determine whether the new operation's generated code — including request/response structs, handler method body, and any helper code — references the type or package that the flag gates; (c) if yes, add the new operation to the flag's condition; (d) specifically, do NOT limit the analysis to "operations that clear computed fields" or "write operations" — the import need is determined by what code the operation EMITS, not by a conceptual category. A `patch` operation that generates `*time.Time` in its request struct needs `"time"` even though it is not a "write operation" in the same sense as `create`/`update`; (e) write a test with the new operation as the ONLY operation on an entity that has a field of each import-triggering type (timestamp, jsonb, etc.) and verify the generated handler file compiles — this catches any conditional-import flag that excludes the new operation. **The principle: conditional-import flags are predicates over operations. When the operation set grows, every predicate must be re-evaluated. An operation-type enumeration (`op == A || op == B || op == C`) is a closed set — adding operation D requires updating every enumeration. A property-based check ("does this operation emit code referencing time.Time?") would be more robust but harder to implement; the enumeration approach works if and only if every enumeration is audited when a new operation is added. This item makes that audit mandatory.**
   120. **Composite operations must inherit spec-contract obligations from their sub-behaviors:** When a new operation's handler implementation reuses or composes behavior from existing operations (e.g. `patch` performs a Get-then-merge-then-Save, reusing the Get sub-behavior from `read`), the new operation's specification contract (e.g. OpenAPI responses) MUST declare every response that any reused sub-behavior can produce. Items 37 and 39 cover shared semantics across operations and bidirectional spec/code consistency respectively, but neither specifically addresses **composite operations** — operations whose handler code includes control-flow paths inherited from other operations' sub-behaviors. A common error pattern: `read` correctly declares both `200` and `404` in its OpenAPI responses because the Get can fail with "not found." `patch` also performs a Get (to fetch the existing entity before merging), so the handler returns 404 via `NotFound()` when the entity does not exist. However, the `patch` OpenAPI spec only declares `200`, omitting `404`. The code-to-spec direction of item 39 is violated: the handler has an observable behavior (returning 404) that the spec does not declare. **Mandatory audit step when implementing any composite operation:** (a) list every sub-behavior the new operation performs — for `patch`: Get (from `read`), decode request body (from `create`/`update`), merge, Save (from `update`); (b) for each sub-behavior, find the existing operation that implements it and examine its OpenAPI response set; (c) for every non-success response declared by the reused operation (e.g. `404` on `read`, `400` on `create`), determine whether the same response is reachable in the composite operation's code path — if the composite operation calls Get and the Get can 404, then 404 is reachable; (d) include every reachable non-success response in the composite operation's OpenAPI declaration; (e) write a test that asserts the composite operation's OpenAPI response set is a superset of the union of its sub-behaviors' non-success responses — e.g. if the handler code path includes a Get, the spec must include `404`. **The principle: a composite operation inherits the failure modes of its sub-behaviors. If sub-behavior X can fail with response Y, and the composite operation includes X in its code path, then Y is reachable from the composite operation — the spec must declare it. Omitting a reachable failure response from the spec misleads API consumers: client code generators will not generate error-handling paths for undeclared responses, and API documentation will not mention the failure mode. The sub-behavior's responses are not optional additions — they are contractual obligations inherited through composition.**
   118. **Generated variable declarations must be topologically sorted by dependency — not emitted in input order:** When a code generator or assembler emits multiple variable declarations (`:=` statements) into a single function body, and those declarations reference each other (variable A's initializer references variable B), the emission order MUST respect the dependency graph — referenced variables must be declared before referencing variables. Go does not permit forward references in short variable declarations (`:=`): referencing a variable before its declaration is a compile error (`undefined: variableName`). This is the code-level analog of item 73 (DDL dependency ordering for `CREATE TABLE` statements). A common error pattern: the assembler emits constructor calls in the order components appear in the archetype's `components` list, without resolving inter-constructor dependencies. If component A's constructor references a variable produced by component B (e.g. `api.NewUserHandler(store)` where `store` comes from `storage.NewStore(db)`), but A appears before B in the component list, the generated code has a forward reference: `userHandler := api.NewUserHandler(store)` appears BEFORE `store := storage.NewStore(db)`. The archetype author controls component order for structural or conceptual reasons — the assembler must compute the correct declaration order independently based on actual variable dependencies. **Syntax-only validation (`parser.ParseFile`) will NOT catch forward variable references because forward references are syntactically valid Go — they are semantic errors caught only by compilation (per item 31).** After writing any code that emits multiple variable declarations into a single scope: (a) identify all dependencies between declarations — for each declaration, determine which variables its initializer expression references; (b) build a dependency graph from these references using structured metadata (e.g. `NeedsDB` on `gen.Wiring`, or a `Dependencies []string` field listing which wiring variables each constructor references) — do NOT infer dependencies by substring-matching on opaque constructor expressions (per item 60); (c) topologically sort the declarations so that each variable is declared before any declaration that references it; (d) detect cycles in the dependency graph and return a generation-time error identifying the cycle (e.g. "circular constructor dependency: component A requires variable from component B, which requires variable from component A"); (e) write a test where the input ordering places a dependent declaration BEFORE the declaration it depends on (e.g. list `rest-api` before `postgres-adapter` in the component list, where the rest-api constructor references the `store` variable produced by the postgres-adapter constructor), and verify the emitted code correctly reorders the declarations so that the generated output compiles; (f) write a test for a circular dependency and verify the generator returns a clear error at generation time. **The principle: input ordering (archetype component list, configuration file order) is not a dependency specification. Declaration ordering in generated code is a semantic constraint imposed by the target language. The generator must compute valid ordering from explicit dependency metadata, not inherit arbitrary ordering from the input. A test that validates declaration ordering using `parser.ParseFile` (item 31 violation) will pass for ANY ordering — including forward references — because syntax parsing does not perform name resolution. Only actual compilation or structured dependency analysis can verify correct ordering.**
   119. **Derived path computations used in non-filesystem contexts must be validated against target domain constraints:** When a function computes a relative path via `filepath.Rel`, `filepath.Join`, or similar path operations, and the result is used in a non-filesystem context (Go import paths, module paths, package declarations, URL templates), the computed value MUST be validated against the target domain's format constraints before use. Filesystem path operations can produce values (`.`, `..`, empty string) that are valid filesystem paths but structurally invalid in other contexts. For example, `filepath.Rel(base, target)` returns `"."` when `base == target` — a valid filesystem path meaning "current directory," but an invalid Go import path segment (`module/./internal/api` violates Go's import path specification). This class of bug arises specifically at API boundaries where public functions accept multiple path parameters that can have degenerate relationships (equality, containment, overlap). The CLI may hardcode safe values, but the public API permits the degenerate case. After writing any function that (a) computes a derived path from two or more input path parameters AND (b) uses the result in a non-filesystem context: (i) enumerate the degenerate parameter relationships — equality (`A == B`), containment (`A` is a subdirectory of `B` or vice versa), identity after resolution (`filepath.Clean(A) == filepath.Clean(B)`); (ii) for each degenerate case, compute the derived value and check whether it is valid in the target domain — e.g. does `filepath.Rel` produce `.` or `..`? Does `filepath.Base` produce `.` or `/`?; (iii) if the derived value is invalid for the target domain, return an error at the API boundary that identifies the conflicting parameters and the constraint violated (e.g. "OutDir must not equal ProjectDir: filepath.Rel produces '.' which is invalid in Go import paths"); (iv) write tests for each degenerate parameter relationship and verify the function returns a clear error rather than producing structurally invalid output. **The principle: filesystem path operations are defined over filesystem semantics. When their output feeds a different domain (Go modules, URLs, package names), the operation's edge cases (`.`, `..`, empty string, absolute paths) become domain violations. Validate derived values at the domain boundary — do not assume filesystem-valid means universally-valid.**
   120. **Generated declarations must be conditional on having at least one consumer in the generated output — including import statements:** When a generator or assembler emits a declaration into generated code — whether a variable declaration (`:=` or `var`) OR an import statement — and ALL code paths that reference the declared identifier are gated on a condition (e.g. `hasRoutes`, `hasDB`, `hasSlots`), the declaration itself MUST be gated on at least one consumer's condition. In Go, BOTH unused local variables (`declared and not used`) AND unused imports (`imported and not used`) are hard compile errors, so an unconditionally emitted declaration whose every consumer is conditional will fail to compile in any configuration where all consumers' conditions are false. **Import statements are subject to the same consumer-gating rule as variable declarations.** When an import emission function (e.g. `writeMainImports`) emits import lines for multiple categories (component imports, fill imports, slots package imports, stdlib imports), EACH category must be gated on the condition that ensures at least one code path in the generated output references that category's identifiers. If the fill imports serve only `writeSlotWiring`, and `writeSlotWiring` is gated on `hasSlots`, then fill import emission must also be gated on `hasSlots`. A function that correctly gates the slots package import on `hasSlots` but unconditionally emits fill imports has an INTERNAL inconsistency — two import categories serving the same conditional feature are gated differently, guaranteeing one produces unused imports when the feature is disabled. A common error pattern for variables: the assembler's `writeConstructors` unconditionally emits ALL constructor variables from all component wirings, but `writeRouteRegistration` and `writeServerStart` — the only consumers of variables like `authMiddleware` and `store` — are gated on `hasRoutes`. When `hasRoutes` is false (e.g. `expose: []`), the constructor variables are declared but never referenced. Similarly, a generator that unconditionally returns wiring with a constructor (e.g. jwt-auth always returns `auth.NewAuthMiddleware()`) contributes to this pattern by producing declarations regardless of whether any downstream code will use them. A common error pattern for imports: fill imports are emitted for every fill name in `SlotBindings`, but the slot wiring code that references fill aliases is gated on `hasSlots` (which requires BOTH non-empty `SlotBindings` AND non-empty `SlotsPackage`). When `SlotsPackage` is empty, `hasSlots` is false — fill aliases are imported but never referenced, and Go rejects the output with `imported and not used`. Item 38 mandates testing degenerate configurations including zero routes; this item mandates the structural invariant that such tests are designed to catch. After writing any code that emits declarations — variables OR imports — into generated output: (a) for each emitted declaration, identify EVERY code path in the generated output that references the declared identifier; (b) determine the condition (if any) that gates each consumer — e.g. route registration is gated on `hasRoutes`, DB setup is gated on `hasDB`, slot wiring is gated on `hasSlots`; (c) if ALL consumers are conditional, gate the declaration on the disjunction of the consumers' conditions (e.g. emit the declaration only `if hasRoutes || hasDB`), or on a single condition that implies at least one consumer exists; (d) write a test for each configuration where all consumers' conditions are false (e.g. `hasRoutes = false` AND `hasDB = false`, or `hasSlots = false` with non-empty `SlotBindings`) and verify the generated output compiles — per item 31, use actual compilation, not `parser.ParseFile`; (e) apply fix-class exhaustion (item 33): after identifying one unconditional declaration with conditional consumers, enumerate ALL declarations in the same emission function and verify each has at least one unconditional consumer or is itself conditional — this explicitly includes checking EVERY import category in an import emission function when one category is found to be ungated; (f) **when an import emission function has multiple import categories serving different features, verify INTERNAL consistency:** if category A (e.g. slots package import) is correctly gated on a condition, and category B (e.g. fill imports) serves the same conditional feature, category B MUST be gated on the same condition — otherwise the function contradicts itself. **The principle: a declaration is a promise that the declared identifier will be used. In Go, this promise is enforced by the compiler for BOTH variables AND imports. When the code that fulfills the promise (the consumer) is conditional, the promise (the declaration) must be conditional too — otherwise the promise is broken in configurations where the consumer is absent. Unconditional emission of declarations + conditional emission of consumers = guaranteed compile failure in the no-consumer configuration. This principle applies identically to `var x = ...` and `import "pkg/x"` — both introduce identifiers that Go requires to be used.**
   121. **Derived condition flags used by multiple generation-pipeline functions must originate from a single canonical computation:** When a generation pipeline computes a boolean flag or condition (e.g. `hasDB`, `hasRoutes`, `hasSlots`) and that flag is used by multiple independent functions (e.g. `writeMainImports` and `buildFillAliasMap` both use `hasDB` to seed stdlib alias reservation sets), ALL functions MUST receive the flag from the same canonical computation — not independently re-derive it using different logic. A common error pattern: one function computes `hasDB` by checking whether consumed constructors include a DB-dependent one (`effectiveHasDB` from `computeConsumedConstructors`), while another function computes `hasDB` by checking the raw wiring metadata (`needsDB(input)`). These two computations diverge when constructors are present but unconsumed (e.g. no routes). The divergence means the two functions seed different "reserved name" sets into their alias disambiguation maps, producing different aliases for the same fill name — the import block declares one alias while the code body references another, causing a compile error. Item 49 covers partial parameterization of configurable parameters; this item covers the case where the "parameter" is a derived condition that multiple functions need to agree on. After writing any function that computes a boolean condition to govern its behavior: (a) search the codebase for other functions that compute semantically equivalent conditions — grep for the flag name (e.g. `hasDB`, `needsDB`), the field it derives from (e.g. `NeedsDB`), and the concept it represents; (b) if another function already computes this condition, determine whether the two computations are equivalent for ALL configurations — not just the common case; (c) if they can diverge (e.g. one is unconditional while the other is consumption-aware), refactor so that the condition is computed once and passed to all consumers; (d) write a test where the two computations would diverge (e.g. NeedsDB components present but no routes, so `effectiveHasDB` is false while `needsDB` is true) and verify all generated output remains consistent — fill alias references match import declarations. **The principle: a derived flag is a single fact about the generation context. When multiple functions independently re-derive the same fact, they are each making an independent assertion about reality — and independent assertions can disagree. Disagreement between functions that must agree (e.g. import emission and variable reference emission) produces inconsistent output. Compute once, pass everywhere.**
   122. **Identifier reference detection in generated-code analysis must use word-boundary-aware matching — never raw substring matching:** When a function checks whether a generated expression, constructor call, or code fragment contains a reference to a specific identifier (variable name, function name, type name), the match MUST be word-boundary-aware. `strings.Contains(expr, name)` matches `name` as a substring of any larger word — `strings.Contains("datastore", "store")` returns `true` even though `datastore` is not a reference to the variable `store`. This produces false-positive matches that can cascade into incorrect analysis (e.g. marking a constructor as transitively consumed when it is not, or detecting a dependency that does not exist). If a word-boundary-aware identifier matching function already exists in the same package (e.g. `containsIdentRef`), ALL identifier-matching code paths MUST use it — including fallback paths, heuristic paths, and transitive-dependency detection. After writing any code that checks whether one string appears in another to detect identifier references: (a) search the file and package for existing word-boundary-aware matching functions (grep for `containsIdentRef`, `isIdentChar`, `unicode.IsLetter`, `regexp.MustCompile.*\\b`); (b) if one exists, use it; (c) if none exists, implement word-boundary checking that verifies the character before and after the match is not an identifier character (letter, digit, or underscore); (d) write tests with inputs where substring matching would false-positive but word-boundary matching would not (e.g. variable name "a" in expression "(dataA)", variable name "db" in expression "(handleDB)"). **The principle: an identifier reference is a lexical token, not a substring. `strings.Contains` operates at the character level — it cannot distinguish between the identifier `store` and the suffix of `datastore`. Every identifier-matching function in a code analysis pipeline must match at the identifier level. When the correct function already exists in the same file and is used by some code paths but not others, the non-using code path is a consistency bug — it will produce different results for the same inputs.**
   123. **Multi-phase analysis must operate in the same identifier namespace as the code it governs — analysis results do not survive renaming transformations:** When a generation pipeline has multiple phases — analysis (e.g. consumption tracking, dependency resolution) followed by transformation (e.g. variable name disambiguation) followed by emission — the analysis results must either (a) operate in the post-transformation namespace, or (b) be correctly remapped through the transformation. A common error pattern: consumption analysis indexes constructors by their pre-disambiguation base variable name (`varToEntries["store"]`). When a route references `store`, ALL constructors with base name `store` are marked consumed — including the second one that will be renamed to `store2` after disambiguation. After disambiguation, `store2` is marked as consumed (it inherits the consumption status of its base name `store`), but no generated code references `store2` — the consumption analysis was correct in the pre-disambiguation namespace but wrong in the post-disambiguation namespace. The emitted `store2 := ...` variable is declared but unused — a Go compile error. This is the identifier-namespace analog of item 117 (derived decision sets from mismatched collections): item 117 covers decision sets derived from one *collection* applied to a different collection; this item covers decision sets derived in one *namespace* applied after a namespace transformation. After writing any multi-phase pipeline where an analysis pass produces results consumed by a later emission pass, and an intermediate transformation modifies identifiers: (a) identify every identifier transformation between the analysis and emission phases (disambiguation, qualification, case conversion, aliasing); (b) for each analysis result (e.g. consumed set, dependency graph), determine whether it is indexed by pre-transformation or post-transformation identifiers; (c) if pre-transformation: verify the result is remapped through the transformation before emission uses it — e.g. after disambiguation, re-verify that each consumed constructor's post-disambiguation name is actually referenced by at least one consumer; (d) alternatively, restructure the pipeline so analysis runs after transformation, operating directly on the final identifiers; (e) write a test where two constructors share a pre-transformation base name (e.g. two wirings both produce `store`) and only one is actually consumed — verify the other is correctly identified as unconsumed after disambiguation and either suppressed or error-reported, not emitted as an unused variable. **The principle: consumption analysis answers the question "will this variable be referenced?" The answer depends on what the variable is called — and disambiguation changes what the variable is called. An analysis result computed for name X does not automatically hold for the renamed name X2. Either re-verify after renaming, or compute in the renamed namespace. Never assume analysis results survive a transformation that changes the identifiers the analysis was computed over.**
   124. **Conditional suppression of code emissions must propagate to all correlated supporting declarations — imports, type references, and upstream enablers:** When a generation pipeline introduces a conditional gate that suppresses a code emission (e.g. a `consumed` map that skips unconsumed constructor declarations), ALL declarations that exist solely to SUPPORT the now-conditional emission must also be gated on the same condition. The most common correlated supporting declaration is an **import**: an import statement provides the package namespace for the suppressed constructor call — when the constructor is suppressed, the import becomes the sole declaration referencing that package, and since it has no other consumers, it is unused. In Go, an unused import is a hard compile error (`imported and not used`). This class of bug is invisible to item 33 (fix-class exhaustion) because the import is not "the same pattern" as the constructor — it is a DIFFERENT kind of declaration that exists in a DEPENDENCY relationship with the constructor. You would not find it by grepping for "constructor emission gated on consumed" because the import emission code looks nothing like the constructor emission code — it is in a different function (`writeMainImports` vs `writeConstructors`), operates on different data (`Wiring.Imports` vs constructor expressions), and uses different mechanics (import alias disambiguation vs variable name formatting). Yet the two are inextricably linked: the import exists because the constructor needs its package. Item 120 covers "variable declarations must be conditional on having consumers" but speaks specifically about variable declarations (`:=` and `var`), not import declarations — imports are a distinct declaration type with distinct suppression mechanics (they cannot be gated with `if` in Go, they must be omitted from the import block entirely). After introducing any conditional gate that suppresses code emissions in a generated function body: (a) for EACH suppressed emission, identify EVERY declaration in the generated output that exists solely to support it — imports (provides the package for the constructor call), type references (provides the type for a variable declaration), variable declarations (provides a value consumed only by the suppressed code); (b) for each supporting declaration, determine whether it has any OTHER consumer in the generated output that is NOT also suppressed — if all consumers are suppressed, the supporting declaration must also be suppressed; (c) when the conditional gate is a map (e.g. `consumed map[string]bool`), the supporting-declaration emission function (e.g. `writeMainImports`) MUST receive and consult the same map — do not compute a separate condition from different data; (d) write a test for each configuration where the conditional gate suppresses ALL consumers of a supporting declaration (e.g. a wiring whose constructors are all unconsumed) and verify the generated output compiles via actual compilation (item 31), not `parser.ParseFile` — unused imports are semantic errors that syntax parsing cannot detect; (e) when the conditional gate is introduced as a FIX for a previous finding (e.g. adding a `consumed` map to fix unused variable findings), explicitly audit the ENTIRE generation pipeline for correlated supporting declarations at the time of the fix — do not wait for the verifier to find the import half of the bug in the next round. **The principle: a code emission and its supporting declarations form a dependency chain. Making one link in the chain conditional without making the others conditional breaks the chain — the supporting declaration is emitted, its only consumer is suppressed, and the declaration becomes orphaned. This is the declaration-dependency analog of item 33 (fix-class exhaustion): item 33 says "same bug, different site." This item says "same fix, incomplete propagation" — the fix correctly suppressed the downstream emission but left the upstream enabler unconditionally emitting, creating a new orphaned-declaration bug of a different kind than the one being fixed.**
   125. **Constraint attributes must be validated against their declared field type — not just for existence, but for applicability:** When the spec defines a finite set of constraint attributes and annotates which field types each applies to (e.g. "min_length, max_length, pattern (strings)" and "min, max (numerics)"), the validation function must enforce the constraint-to-type mapping, not just check that the field type itself is valid. A field like `{name: count, type: int32, min_length: 5}` contains a real field type (`int32`) and a real constraint (`min_length`), but the constraint is inapplicable to that type per the spec — this must be rejected. Similarly, type-exclusive attributes like `values` (enum-only), `to` (ref-only) must be rejected on non-matching types. After implementing any validation of user-supplied constraint attributes: (a) enumerate every constraint attribute the spec defines; (b) for each, identify which field types it applies to; (c) verify the validation code rejects the attribute when applied to a non-matching type; (d) write a negative test for each invalid combination (e.g. `min_length` on `int32`, `values` on `string`, `to` on `bool`, `min` on `string`). **The principle: validating that a field type is legal and validating that a constraint attribute is legal for that field type are two independent checks. Implementing one without the other accepts half-valid configurations — the type is right but the constraints are nonsensical for it. The spec's parenthetical annotations (e.g. "(strings)") are not commentary — they are type-restriction rules that must be enforced.**
   126. **Side-effect consistency across all consumers of a spec-mandated-observable value:** When the spec or ACs require a side effect (warning to stderr, log entry, metric emission, audit event) **whenever** a value is consumed (e.g. "a warning MUST be printed to stderr whenever STEGO_REGISTRY is used"), every code path that reads or uses that value must produce the specified side effect — not just the primary/canonical code path. A common error pattern: the canonical consumer function (e.g. `ResolveRegistry`) correctly produces the warning, but a separate helper (e.g. `initRegistryDir`) reads the same value (e.g. `os.Getenv("STEGO_REGISTRY")`) for a different code path and omits the side effect entirely. The spec requirement attaches to the **value**, not to a particular function — any code path that consumes the value inherits the obligation. After writing any code that reads a value with a spec-mandated side effect: (a) grep the entire codebase for every site that reads or consumes the same value (e.g. `grep -rn 'STEGO_REGISTRY' --include='*.go'`); (b) verify each site either produces the side effect itself or delegates to a function that does — a helper that reads the value and returns a result without the side effect is a bypass; (c) if adding a new consumer, either route it through the existing canonical function or explicitly add the side effect at the new site; (d) write a test for EACH consumer code path that verifies the side effect is produced (e.g. capture stderr and assert the warning message appears). When the spec says "whenever X is used," the word "whenever" means EVERY code path — not just the one you are currently implementing. **The principle: a spec-mandated side effect is a contract between the software and its user. A consumer that reads the triggering value without producing the side effect silently violates that contract. The most common failure pattern is creating a "lightweight" helper for a secondary code path (like `init`) that omits the warning present in the "full" function (like `ResolveRegistry`). The helper appears correct because it produces the right return value — but it is incorrect because it suppresses user-facing information the spec requires. After introducing any new consumer of a value with a spec-mandated side effect, the mandatory audit is: grep for ALL consumers and verify 100% coverage of the side effect.**
   127. **Error messages must include ALL contextual fields specified by acceptance criteria:** When an AC specifies the required content of error messages (e.g. "the error message identifies the URL, the ref, and the underlying git error"), every `fmt.Errorf`, `errors.New`, or error-construction call in the affected code path must include ALL specified fields — not a subset. A common error pattern: one error path in a function correctly includes all specified fields (serving as evidence the implementer read the AC), while adjacent error paths in the same function omit one or more fields. This inconsistency typically arises when error messages are written independently for each branch rather than derived from a common template or helper. After writing error-handling code in a function governed by an AC that specifies error content requirements: (a) re-read the AC that governs error messages and extract the explicit list of required fields; (b) enumerate every error return in the function (grep for `fmt.Errorf`, `errors.New`, `return.*err` within the function); (c) for each error return, list the contextual fields included in the message (e.g. URL, ref, git error); (d) compare each error return's field list against the AC's requirements — every error return must include ALL fields specified by the AC; (e) if a field is not available at a particular error site (e.g. the error occurs before `ref` is used), document why the omission is justified and ensure the message still provides maximum available context; (f) consider extracting a common error-formatting helper within the function to ensure consistency across branches (e.g. `registryError(url, ref, cause string) error`) — this makes it structurally difficult to omit a field. Apply item 33 (fix-class exhaustion): if one error path in a function has all specified fields and another does not, the inconsistency is the same class of bug repeated within the same function. **The principle: an error message with incomplete context forces the user to reproduce the failure and inspect intermediate state to determine what went wrong. When the AC explicitly lists required fields, the spec author already identified what the user needs. Omitting a field is not a style issue — it is a usability defect. The most insidious form is when SOME error paths include all fields (proving the implementer knew the requirement) while others do not (proving the requirement was not systematically applied). A common error-formatting helper eliminates this class of inconsistency by construction.**
   126. **Co-occurrence constraints between attributes must be enforced as a unit — never validate one half without the other:** When the spec defines two or more attributes as a mandatory pair or group (e.g. `computed: true, filled_by: <fill>` shown as a single line with both always present together, described as "computed/derived fields are read-only fields populated by a fill"), validation MUST enforce the co-occurrence: (a) the presence of either attribute without the other is an error, and (b) tests must cover both directions — attribute A without B, and attribute B without A. After implementing validation for any set of attributes the spec presents as a group: (a) identify every attribute group the spec shows together or describes with language implying they are a unit ("paired with", "populated by", "requires", "always accompanied by"); (b) for each group, write validation that checks all members are present when any member is present; (c) write negative tests for each possible partial presence (e.g. `computed: true` without `filled_by`, `filled_by: x` without `computed: true`). **The principle: the spec shows `computed: true, filled_by: <fill>` as a single concept. An implementation that accepts `computed: true` without `filled_by` has created a nonsensical state — a field declared as computed with no way to compute it. The spec's formatting (both on one line, both in one example) is the co-occurrence specification.**
   127. **Error subtype inspection must handle all branches — never check one subtype and silently ignore others:** When inspecting an error by subtype (e.g. `os.IsNotExist(err)`, `errors.Is(err, specificErr)`, type-switching on error types), every branch must be handled. The anti-pattern: `if os.IsNotExist(err) { report missing } // else: silently treat as success`. When `err` is non-nil but NOT the checked subtype (e.g. permission denied, I/O error), the else branch silently swallows the error, producing a false positive — the function reports "no problem" when it could not actually verify the condition. The correct pattern is: `if err != nil { if os.IsNotExist(err) { report missing } else { return err } }` — the unexpected error propagates rather than being silently absorbed. After writing any error-inspection code that checks for a specific error subtype: (a) identify every `if specificCheck(err)` or `switch err.(type)` block; (b) verify that the else/default branch either handles or propagates the error — never falls through silently; (c) write a test that triggers a non-matching error (e.g. a permission-denied scenario for code that checks `os.IsNotExist`) and verify it surfaces as an error, not a false success. **The principle: `os.IsNotExist(err)` answers a narrow question ("is this error specifically a not-found?"). A false answer does not mean "no error" — it means "a different error." Code that treats "not the error I expected" as "no error" has a logic inversion: the absence of a specific failure is not evidence of success when a different failure is present.**
   128. **Code comments asserting coverage by another function must be verified by reading that function — and available-sets must enumerate ALL contributing sources:** When writing a code comment that asserts a behavior is "already handled by," "covered by," or "included via" another function (e.g. "mixin slots are covered via collectComponentNames"), you MUST read that function and verify the assertion is factually correct before committing the comment. A comment that asserts coverage without verification is worse than no comment — it actively prevents the reader (and future maintainers) from noticing the gap, because the comment says "this is handled." Additionally, when building an "available" or "valid" set for validation purposes (e.g. "all available slot names," "all valid entity names," "all known component names"), you MUST enumerate ALL data sources that contribute items to that set — not just the primary or most obvious source. If a concept has multiple contributors (e.g. slots come from component `Slots` definitions AND from mixin `AddsSlots`), the available set must include items from ALL contributors. A set that includes only the primary source produces false positive validation errors for items contributed by the secondary source. After writing any code comment that references another function's behavior: (a) open the referenced function and read it; (b) verify the specific claim — if the comment says "X handles mixin slots," confirm that the function actually processes `AddsSlots`, not just `AddsComponents`; (c) if the assertion is wrong, either fix the code (add the missing handling) or fix the comment (remove the false assertion and add a TODO or implement the missing logic). After building any "available" or "valid" set used for validation: (a) identify the concept the set represents (e.g. "all valid slot names"); (b) enumerate every data structure in the codebase that can contribute items to that concept — grep for the concept's type or field name across all relevant structs (e.g. grep for `Slots` and `AddsSlots` to find all sources of slot definitions); (c) verify the set-building code includes items from ALL sources; (d) write a test where an item contributed by a secondary source (e.g. a mixin-added slot) is referenced, and verify it passes validation rather than producing a false "not found" error. **The principle: a code comment is a machine-unverifiable assertion. When it claims another function handles something, the only way to verify is to read that function. An unverified comment that claims coverage is the procedural root cause of silent validation gaps — it prevents the author, reviewers, and future maintainers from questioning whether the coverage actually exists. And an available-set that misses a contributing source will reject valid input — the user gets a validation error for something the framework is supposed to support.**
   129. **User-facing validation commands must be at least as comprehensive as downstream generators' validations of the same data:** When implementing a user-facing validation command (e.g. `stego validate`) that checks entity/field/constraint correctness, the validation must catch every error that ANY downstream generator would catch for the same data — plus configuration-level issues that span multiple generators. The validate command is the user's first line of defense: if it passes, the user believes their configuration is valid. If a generator subsequently rejects the configuration with an error, the user's trust in `validate` is broken, and the error message comes from a less user-friendly context (generation failure vs. validation diagnostic). Items 70 and 53 require generators to validate field-name references (e.g. `unique_composite` references to sibling fields, `scope` references to entity fields). If the generator validates these but the validate command does not, users discover the error only at generation time — later in the pipeline, with less context, and with a more confusing error message. After implementing any validation function in a validate command: (a) perform an EXHAUSTIVE audit of ALL generator validation functions — run `grep -rn 'func.*[Vv]alidate\|Validate.*(' --include='*.go'` across ALL generator and assembler packages to enumerate every validation function that exists in the generation pipeline; (b) for EACH generator-level validation function found — not just the ones that check "the same data" as the current task — determine whether the validate command should perform an equivalent check; (c) specifically, look for generator validation functions with names containing `Uniqueness`, `Duplicate`, `Validate`, `check`, and `verify` — these are the functions most likely to have validate-command counterparts (e.g. `validateExposeUniqueness` in `restapi/generator.go` must have a counterpart in the validate command's `validateExposeReferences`, and `validateOperationUniqueness` must have a counterpart in `validateExposeOps`); (d) if a generator validation has no equivalent in the validate command, add it — the validate command should be a superset; (e) write a test for each validation that exists in a generator but was missing from the validate command, using the same error-triggering input, and verify the validate command catches it. **The narrow-grep anti-pattern:** do NOT limit the audit to concept-specific greps like `grep -rn 'unique_composite'` — this finds validations for one concept but misses validations for other concepts (e.g. expose block uniqueness, operation uniqueness). The audit must be exhaustive: enumerate ALL validation functions in ALL generator packages, then assess each one. A concept-specific grep is useful for deep-diving into a specific validation, but the first pass must be broad. **The principle: the validate command and generators form a validation pipeline. The validate command runs first and should be the strictest filter — anything it passes should not be rejected downstream. When a generator catches an error that the validate command missed, the pipeline is inverted: the user gets a confusing generator error for something validate should have caught. After implementing validate, diff its checks against every generator's checks for the same data structures. Every generator-only check is a gap in validate.**
   130. **Validation functions for configuration objects must resolve references at EVERY granularity — entity names AND field names within entities:** When implementing a validation function for a configuration object that contains multiple reference-typed attributes (e.g. an expose block with `entity`, `parent`, `scope`, `upsert_key`), the validation MUST resolve ALL reference attributes — not just entity-name references. A common error pattern: the implementer validates that `entity` and `parent` resolve to defined entity names, but stops there — `scope` (a field-ref within the entity) and `upsert_key` (a list of field-refs within the entity) are left unchecked. The implementer validates the *coarse-grained* references (entity names) but misses the *fine-grained* references (field names within those entities). This creates a false sense of completeness: the validation function exists, it checks references, but it only checks references at one granularity level. Item 53 covers field-name references in generator configuration; item 129 says validate must be as comprehensive as generators. But when the validate command is the FIRST implementation to handle a configuration object, there is no upstream generator to diff against — the implementer must independently discover all reference attributes. After implementing any `validate*` function for a configuration object: (a) open the spec's definition of that configuration object (e.g. the spec section defining expose blocks); (b) enumerate EVERY attribute on the object; (c) classify each attribute by reference type: *entity-ref* (references an entity name — e.g. `entity`, `parent`), *field-ref* (references a field name within an entity — e.g. `scope`, `upsert_key`), *value* (a literal value — e.g. `operations`), *composite* (a list of references — e.g. `upsert_key: [field1, field2]`); (d) for each entity-ref, verify it resolves to a defined entity; (e) for each field-ref, verify it resolves to an actual field on the referenced entity — this requires first resolving the entity-ref to identify WHICH entity's field list to check, then checking the field name against that entity's fields; (f) for each composite field-ref (list of field names), validate EVERY element in the list, not just the list's existence; (g) write a negative test for each reference attribute with an unresolvable reference (e.g. `scope: nonexistent_field`, `upsert_key: [nonexistent_field]`) and verify the validation returns a clear error identifying the configuration object, the attribute, the unresolvable reference, and the entity's actual field list. **Mandatory audit step:** after implementing a `validate*` function, count the number of reference-typed attributes you validated and compare against the total number of reference-typed attributes on the configuration object per the spec. If the counts differ, you missed one. **The principle: a configuration object's reference attributes form a reference graph at multiple granularity levels. Validating only the top level (entity names) while ignoring the next level (field names within entities) is like validating that a file path's directory exists without checking that the file itself exists — the coarse check passes but the fine-grained reference is still broken. Every reference attribute, at every granularity, must be resolved.**
   131. **Lookup maps built from named collections must validate input uniqueness — in ALL code, not just generators:** When ANY function — validation, generator, reconciler, loader, or assembler — builds a lookup map from a collection of named items (e.g. entity names into `map[string]bool`, field names into a field lookup set), the function MUST validate that the input collection contains no duplicate names before or during map construction. Items 55 (collection-level uniqueness) and 76 (sub-element uniqueness within parent definitions) state this requirement using generator-specific language ("when a generator receives..."), but the same silent-overwrite risk exists in ALL code that indexes user-provided names into maps — including validation functions. A validation function that builds `entityNames[e.Name] = true` in a loop is silently overwriting on duplicate entity names; the validate command itself becomes complicit in hiding the domain error it should detect. **This applies at EVERY level of the data hierarchy:** (a) entity names within the service declaration (two entities named "Widget" silently collapse into one map entry — all cross-references become ambiguous and generated code produces duplicate type/table names), AND (b) field names within each entity (two fields named "label" silently collapse — `unique_composite`, `scope`, and `upsert_key` field-ref validations use the same map and cannot distinguish which field is intended, and generated Go structs and SQL tables require unique field/column names), AND (c) expose block entity names within the expose list (two expose blocks for entity "Widget" silently collapse in `map[string]bool` — different operation sets, scopes, or parent references are lost, and generators that validate uniqueness at generation time via `validateExposeUniqueness` catch the duplicate while the validate command passes, creating the inverted pipeline gap described in item 129), AND (d) operation names within each expose block's `operations` list (duplicate operations within `operations: [create, create, read]` produce duplicate handler method declarations causing Go compile errors, duplicate route registrations causing Go 1.22 ServeMux runtime panics, and duplicate OpenAPI operation entries with silent overwrites — generators catch this via `validateOperationUniqueness` but the validate command does not, repeating the same inverted pipeline gap). **Mandatory audit step — exhaustive hierarchy enumeration:** After implementing uniqueness validation for ANY one collection level in the service declaration, you MUST enumerate ALL named collections in the service declaration's Go type hierarchy — at minimum: entity names, field names within each entity, expose block entity names, operation names within each expose block, enum values within each enum field's `values` list, slot binding declarations keyed by their composite identity (slot name, entity, operator type), `upsert_key` entries within each expose block (duplicate field references produce `ON CONFLICT ("x", "x")` which PostgreSQL rejects at runtime), `unique_composite` entries within each field (duplicate entries produce redundant `UNIQUE` constraints), mixin names within the service declaration's `mixins` list (duplicate mixin names silently collapse — the same mixin's `AddsSlots` and
