`AddsComponents` are processed twice, with no user-visible error and no semantic benefit), and fill names within each slot binding operator list (`gate`, `chain`, `fan-out`) — and verify EACH one has equivalent uniqueness validation. Do NOT stop at the level named in the finding or the level you are currently fixing. A fix that adds duplicate entity detection (Finding 8) without also adding duplicate expose block detection is an incomplete hierarchy sweep — the same silent-overwrite pattern exists at every collection level, and fixing one without auditing the others guarantees the same finding will recur at the next unvalidated level. After implementing ANY function that builds a lookup map from a named collection — whether for validation, generation, reconciliation, or any other purpose: (a) before or during map insertion, check whether the key already exists (per item 13); (b) if a duplicate is found, return a domain-specific error identifying the collection type (entity, field, expose block, operation), the duplicate name, and the parent context (e.g. "entity 'Widget' is defined more than once" or "entity 'User': field 'label' is defined more than once" or "expose list contains duplicate expose blocks for entity 'Widget'" or "expose block for entity 'Widget': duplicate operation 'create'"); (c) apply this at EVERY level of the data hierarchy — entity names within the service declaration AND field names within each entity AND expose block entity names within the expose list AND operation names within each expose block AND slot binding declarations within the slot bindings list (keyed by the composite of slot name + entity + operator type — two bindings with the same slot and entity but different operators are distinct, but two bindings with identical slot, entity, and operator are duplicates that produce duplicate variable declarations in generated code) AND any other named sub-collection; (d) write tests with duplicate names at each level — including duplicate slot bindings with identical composite keys — and verify clear errors are returned. **The principle: items 13, 55, and 76 are not generator-specific principles — they are data-integrity principles whose checklist language happens to mention generators. Any code that indexes user-provided names into a map has the same silent-overwrite risk. Validation functions are especially susceptible because they are specifically designed to build lookup maps for cross-referencing — the very act of building the map is a uniqueness-validation opportunity that, if missed, makes the validation function complicit in the error rather than catching it. When you write `map[key] = value` in a loop over user data, you must ask: "what happens if two items have the same key?" If the answer is "the second silently replaces the first," you have a bug — regardless of whether the surrounding function is called `generate`, `validate`, `reconcile`, or anything else.**
   132. **Operation-attribute dependencies must be validated in BOTH directions — attribute requires operation AND operation requires attribute:** When a configuration object has an attribute whose semantics are bound to a specific operation (e.g. `upsert_key` only has meaning when `operations` includes `upsert`), the validation MUST enforce the dependency in both directions: (1) the attribute requires the operation — `upsert_key` without `upsert` is dead configuration with no consumer, and (2) the operation requires the attribute — `upsert` without `upsert_key` is an operation with no defined behavior for its distinguishing feature. This is distinct from attribute co-occurrence (item 126 — paired attributes that must both be present) and from field interaction consistency (item 79 — two fields that must agree on what entity they reference). This pattern covers attributes and operations that are **mutually dependent — each is semantically incomplete without the other**. The `upsert_key` attribute provides the natural-key fields for upsert's conflict resolution — without the `upsert` operation, the attribute has no consumer; without the `upsert_key`, the `upsert` operation has no conflict resolution strategy and generates code with an empty conflict key (e.g. `upsertKey := []string{}`). The spec shows `upsert_key` exclusively in the context of `operations: [list, upsert]` (spec.md:23-30), establishing the bidirectional dependency by example — the operation and attribute always appear together because neither functions without the other. After implementing validation for any configuration attribute or operation: (a) identify which operation(s) the attribute serves — does the attribute only have meaning when a specific operation is present? (b) if the attribute is operation-dependent, verify the validation checks that the prerequisite operation appears in the operations list; (c) **check the converse**: does the operation require the attribute to function? If the operation's distinguishing behavior depends on data provided by the attribute (e.g. upsert's conflict resolution depends on upsert_key's field list), verify the validation checks that the attribute is present when the operation is specified; (d) when either half of the dependency is missing, return a clear error identifying the attribute, the operation, and the configuration object (e.g. "expose block for entity 'Widget' specifies upsert_key but does not include 'upsert' in its operations list" or "expose block for entity 'Widget' includes 'upsert' operation but does not specify upsert_key — upsert requires a natural key for conflict resolution"); (e) write negative tests for BOTH directions — attribute without operation AND operation without attribute; (f) apply fix-class exhaustion (item 33): after implementing this check for one operation-attribute pair, enumerate ALL attributes and operations on the configuration object and ask which form bidirectional dependencies. **Mandatory audit step — full attribute enumeration:** After implementing or fixing any operation-attribute dependency check on a configuration object, you MUST open the spec's complete definition of that configuration object and produce a written enumeration of EVERY attribute on the object. For each attribute, determine: (i) is this attribute semantically dependent on a specific operation? (ii) if yes, is the dependency already validated? For expose blocks specifically, the spec defines at minimum: `entity`, `operations`, `parent`, `scope`, `upsert_key`, `concurrency`, and `path_prefix`. When fixing the `upsert_key ↔ upsert` dependency, this audit MUST check `concurrency` (which the spec shows exclusively paired with `upsert` — spec.md:23-30, `concurrency: optimistic # only update if generation is newer`) and any other attribute that the spec associates with a specific operation. Do NOT stop at the attribute named in the finding — enumerate exhaustively. A fix that validates `upsert_key ↔ upsert` but misses `concurrency ↔ upsert` in the same sweep is an incomplete fix-class exhaustion (item 33) at the configuration-object level. **The principle: when an attribute serves an operation, the dependency is almost always bidirectional — the attribute needs the operation to have a consumer, and the operation needs the attribute to have its defining input. Validating only one direction (attribute → operation, as in Finding 10) while missing the converse (operation → attribute, as in Finding 11) is a half-fix that leaves the complementary invalid state undetected. This follows the same bidirectional logic as item 126 (co-occurrence): just as `computed` without `filled_by` and `filled_by` without `computed` are both invalid, `upsert_key` without `upsert` and `upsert` without `upsert_key` are both invalid. When you implement one direction of a dependency check, always ask: "is the converse also required?" If the operation cannot function without the attribute, the answer is yes.**
   133. **Dependency validation must always consider both directions — when you validate A requires B, ask whether B also requires A:** When implementing any validation that enforces a dependency between two elements (attribute↔operation, attribute↔attribute, field↔constraint, operation↔configuration), the implementer MUST explicitly evaluate both directions of the dependency and implement checks for each direction that holds. This is a meta-principle that subsumes the bidirectional requirements in items 126 (attribute co-occurrence) and 132 (operation-attribute dependencies). The pattern recurs because dependencies are easy to see in one direction — the direction the finding or spec example highlights — and easy to miss in the other. Finding 10 revealed that `upsert_key` requires `upsert`; the fix implemented only that direction; Finding 11 then revealed the converse (`upsert` requires `upsert_key`). The second finding existed because the implementer saw the dependency as one-directional when it was bidirectional. After implementing any validation check that enforces a dependency A→B ("A requires B to be present/valid"): (a) explicitly ask: "does B also require A?" — can B function correctly without A? Does B produce meaningful behavior, valid generated code, or a complete configuration without A? (b) if B requires A, implement the converse validation check B→A in the same function, in the same commit — do not leave the converse for a future round; (c) write negative tests for both directions: A present without B, AND B present without A; (d) document both directions in comments: "upsert_key requires upsert (no consumer without it) and upsert requires upsert_key (no conflict resolution strategy without it)." **The principle: a dependency between two elements is a relationship, and relationships have two ends. When you grab one end and pull (validate one direction), the other end is still there. The cost of checking the converse at the time you implement the first direction is near zero — you are already looking at both elements, you already understand their relationship, and you already have the validation infrastructure in place. The cost of missing the converse is a full review round. Always check both ends.**
   114. **Validation against a declarative model must enumerate and check every model-defined constraint field:** When a task requires validation of user input against a declarative model (registry, archetype, schema, config), the implementation must enumerate EVERY constraint field on the validating model and implement a validation check for each one. A declarative model's fields represent the complete set of constraints — `compatible_mixins` on an archetype means "only these mixins may be used with this archetype," `required_components` means "these components must be present," `supported_versions` means "only these versions are accepted." Loading the model and using its data without enforcing its constraints is incomplete validation that silently accepts input the model was designed to reject. Items 30 and 41 cover exhaustive enumeration of enum values and schema properties respectively; this item extends the same principle to constraint fields on model types used for validation. After implementing any validation step described as "validate X against Y" or "validate X before Z": (a) open the Go type definition for Y (the validating model — e.g. the `Archetype` struct); (b) enumerate every field whose semantic is a constraint or restriction — look for fields named `compatible_*`, `required_*`, `allowed_*`, `supported_*`, `max_*`, `min_*`, or any field whose value restricts what configurations of X are valid; (c) for each constraint field, verify there is validation code that checks X against it — e.g. if `Archetype.CompatibleMixins` is non-empty and the service declares mixins, the validation must verify each declared mixin appears in `CompatibleMixins`; (d) if a constraint field has no corresponding validation code, implement the validation or document in a comment why the constraint does not apply in this context; (e) write a test for each constraint with an input that violates it (e.g. a service declaring `mixins: [incompatible-mixin]` where the archetype's `compatible_mixins` does not include it) and verify the validation returns a clear error identifying the constraint violation, the model that defines the constraint, and the input that violates it. **The principle: a declarative model is a specification in data form. Every constraint field on the model is an invariant that must be enforced at validation time. Skipping a constraint field means the model defines a rule that the code silently ignores — the model and the code disagree about what is valid, and invalid input passes through unchallenged. This is the model-level analog of item 6 (full task traceability): just as every stated requirement must trace to code, every model constraint must trace to a validation check.**
   134. **Constrained-value attributes must be validated against a valid-set map — not just structurally present:** When the spec defines an attribute whose value is constrained to a finite set of named modes (e.g. `concurrency: optimistic` where `optimistic` is the only defined mode, or `operations` constrained to `[create, read, update, delete, list, upsert]`), the validation function MUST check the attribute's value against an explicit valid-set map (e.g. `ValidConcurrencyModes`, `ValidOperations`, `ValidFieldTypes`) and reject unrecognized values with a clear error. Item 30 covers enum values in config schemas (YAML validation); this item covers the validation code itself — the `stego validate` command's Go validation functions. A common error pattern: the validation function checks that an attribute's value is non-empty (structural presence) or checks the attribute's interaction with other attributes (e.g. item 132's operation-attribute dependencies), but never checks whether the value itself is one of the spec-defined valid values. The result: `concurrency: pessimistic` passes validation despite `pessimistic` not being a defined concurrency mode, and the generated code silently degrades (the postgres-adapter's `Upsert` function only checks for `"optimistic"` — any other value is treated as no concurrency control). After implementing validation for any attribute whose spec definition constrains the value to a finite set: (a) identify the spec section that defines the valid values for the attribute; (b) create a `Valid*` map or set constant (e.g. `ValidConcurrencyModes = map[string]bool{"optimistic": true}`) alongside existing valid-set maps like `ValidFieldTypes` and `ValidOperations`; (c) in the validation function, check the attribute's value against the valid-set map and return a clear error listing the valid options when the value is unrecognized (e.g. "expose block for entity 'Widget' has invalid concurrency mode 'pessimistic' — valid modes: [optimistic]"); (d) write negative tests with invalid values for each constrained attribute. **Mandatory audit step:** after implementing a valid-set check for any one attribute, enumerate ALL attributes on the same configuration object and identify which ones have spec-defined value constraints. For each such attribute, verify a corresponding `Valid*` map exists and is checked. The pattern signature is: "the spec defines N valid values for attribute X, and the code validates operations against `ValidOperations` and field types against `ValidFieldTypes`, but does not validate X against a `ValidX` map." If the validation function checks some attributes' values but not others, the unchecked attributes are guaranteed future findings. **The principle: structural presence (the attribute has a value) and value validity (the value is one of the defined options) are independent checks. An attribute that is structurally present but contains an unrecognized value produces silently degraded behavior — the generated code compiles and runs but does not implement the intended semantic because the value does not match any handled case. Every constrained-value attribute needs both checks.**
   135. **Entity references that imply infrastructure dependencies must be validated against the infrastructure-providing scope — not just the existence scope:** When a configuration attribute references an entity by name, and the referenced entity must have its own infrastructure in the generated output (its own route, handler, expose block) for the reference to function correctly, the validation MUST check the reference against the scope that provides that infrastructure (e.g. the expose list) — not just against the scope that proves the entity exists (e.g. the entities list). The distinction matters because an entity can exist in the data model without being exposed via the API — a ref-target entity that is defined in `entities` but absent from `expose` has no route, no handler, and no parent-existence-verification endpoint. A common error pattern: the validation function checks that `parent: Cluster` resolves to a defined entity (correct — `Cluster` must exist in the entities list) but does not check that `Cluster` has its own expose block (also required — without an expose block for `Cluster`, nested routing cannot generate the parent's route segment, and `stego plan` or the rest-api generator fails with a downstream error). The user sees `stego validate` report "Validation passed" followed by `stego plan` failing — the validate command gave a false green signal. This gap is detectable by applying item 129 (validate must be as comprehensive as generators): the rest-api generator validates parent references against the expose list at `restapi/generator.go:validateParentReferences`. When the validate function does not perform this check, item 129 is violated. Additionally, this gap violates item 17 (behavioral parity): `validateSlotBindingEntitiesCollect` already validates slot binding entity references against the expose list — both `parent` and slot binding `entity` are entity references that must resolve to exposed entities for the generated code to function, but only the slot binding reference receives the expose-list check. After implementing any validation for an entity-name reference attribute: (a) ask: "does the referenced entity need its own infrastructure (expose block, route, handler) for this reference to function?" — if `parent: X` implies nested routing under X, then X needs its own expose block; (b) if infrastructure is required, validate the reference against the infrastructure-providing scope (expose list), not just the existence scope (entity list); (c) return a clear error when the reference resolves to an existing entity that lacks the required infrastructure (e.g. "expose block for entity 'NodePool' declares parent 'Cluster', but 'Cluster' is not in the expose list — parent entities must have their own expose block to generate the parent route and existence verification"); (d) apply item 17 (behavioral parity): enumerate ALL entity-reference attributes on the configuration object and verify each one that implies infrastructure dependencies receives the expose-list check — if slot binding entities are checked against the expose list, parent references must be too; (e) apply item 129 (validate ≥ generators): grep ALL generator packages for validation of the same reference attribute and verify the validate command performs an equivalent or stronger check; (f) write negative tests with an entity that exists in the entities list but is absent from the expose list, referenced as a `parent`, and verify the validation returns a clear error rather than deferring the failure to generation time. **The principle: entity references have two resolution requirements — existence (the entity is defined) and infrastructure (the entity has the generated support structures the reference depends on). Checking existence without checking infrastructure produces a validation that is half-complete — it catches typos (`parent: Clster`) but misses structural errors (`parent: Cluster` when Cluster has no expose block). The validate command must enforce both, because the user expects "validation passed" to mean "this configuration will generate successfully" — not just "all the names are spelled correctly."**
   136. **Context-dependent attribute validation must be exhausted across ALL configuration scopes — not just the scope where the pattern was first found:** When implementing validation that checks "attribute A is only meaningful within context B" (e.g., `upsert_key` requires the `upsert` operation on expose blocks, `concurrency` requires the `upsert` operation on expose blocks), this establishes a *pattern class*: "context-dependent attribute." Item 132 covers bidirectional operation-attribute dependencies within a single configuration scope. Item 33 covers fix-class exhaustion within a single scope (same pattern, different site). This item covers **cross-scope exhaustion**: the same structural pattern of "attribute that only has meaning with a specific operator/operation/mode" can appear in DIFFERENT configuration objects (expose blocks, slot bindings, entity fields). The pattern is not "upsert_key requires upsert" — that is one instance. The pattern is "attribute X has no semantic effect without context Y, so the validation must reject X when Y is absent." After implementing any attribute-context dependency check in one configuration scope: (a) enumerate ALL configuration object types in the schema (expose blocks, slot bindings, entity field definitions, and any future scope); (b) for each configuration object type, list every attribute and determine whether it is semantically tied to a specific operator, operation, or structural context — an attribute is context-dependent if the spec shows it exclusively in the context of a specific mode, if the implementation code only reads the attribute inside a conditional block gated on that mode, or if the attribute has no behavioral effect when the mode is absent; (c) for each identified dependency, verify validation enforces it; (d) write negative tests for each dependency. **Concrete cross-scope dependency pairs that must ALL be validated:** Expose blocks: (`upsert_key` → `upsert` operation), (`concurrency` → `upsert` operation). Slot bindings: (`short_circuit` → `chain` operator — the spec defines `short_circuit: true` exclusively in the context of chain steps at spec.md:56-66; the `ShortCircuit` field on the chain operator struct at operators.go:97 controls chain halt semantics; the assembler only reads `sb.ShortCircuit` inside the `if len(sb.Chain) > 0` block at assembler.go:917-921, meaning `short_circuit` is silently ignored on gate and fan-out bindings). A fix that validates upsert_key/concurrency dependencies on expose blocks but does not check short_circuit/chain on slot bindings has applied the pattern to one scope but not the other — a cross-scope fix-class exhaustion failure. **The principle: item 33 says "same bug, different site." Item 132 says "check both directions." This item says "same bug, different scope" — when a pattern class spans multiple configuration objects, exhaustion within one scope is necessary but not sufficient. The configuration schema is the exhaustion boundary, not the configuration object.**
   137. **Validation loops over required collections must check minimum cardinality — a `for range` over an empty list produces zero errors, not a validation pass:** When a validation function iterates a collection using `for range` and every iteration contributes to error detection (checking each element's validity, uniqueness, or existence), an empty collection produces zero loop iterations and therefore zero errors — the validation silently passes. If the spec requires the collection to contain at least one element (e.g. an expose block must have at least one operation, an enum field must have at least one value), the validation function MUST check collection length BEFORE or INSTEAD OF relying solely on the loop. The anti-pattern: `for _, op := range eb.Operations { validateOp(op) }` — when `eb.Operations` is empty or nil, this produces no errors, and the function returns "valid." But an expose block with no operations is semantically void — it exposes nothing. The correct pattern: check `len(collection) == 0` explicitly and return an error BEFORE the loop body, or as a separate check alongside the loop. Item 129 (validate ≥ generators) would catch this when a generator has a non-empty check (e.g. `validateExposeOperations` rejects empty operations lists), but this item catches it at a structural level: ANY `for range` loop that is the SOLE mechanism for producing validation errors on a collection is suspect if the collection can be empty. After writing any validation function that uses `for range` to validate elements of a user-provided collection: (a) ask: "does the spec require at least one element in this collection?" — check the spec's examples (do they always show at least one element?) and the semantic meaning (is an empty collection nonsensical?); (b) if yes, add an explicit `len(collection) == 0` check that returns a clear error (e.g. "expose block for entity 'Widget' has no operations — each expose block must have at least one operation"); (c) if the generator already rejects empty collections (audit per item 129), the validate function must too — users should not see "validation passed" followed by a generation failure for the same structural issue; (d) write a test with an empty collection for each validated list and verify a clear error is returned. **Mandatory audit step — exhaustive collection enumeration:** After implementing a minimum-cardinality check for any one collection, enumerate ALL user-provided collections that the validation function iterates and determine which ones have minimum cardinality requirements. At minimum: operations on an expose block (must have ≥1 — an expose block with no operations is semantically void), enum values on an enum field (must have ≥1 — an enum with no values is an empty type), operators on a slot binding (must have ≥1 of gate/chain/fan-out — a slot binding with no operator is semantically void, per item 139), and any future collection or attribute group where the spec's examples always show at least one element. Do NOT stop at the collection named in the finding. **The principle: a `for range` loop is a validation mechanism that scales to N elements but has a blind spot at N=0. When zero elements means "nothing to validate" and the function returns no errors, the zero case looks identical to the "all valid" case. But "nothing to validate" and "all valid" are not the same thing when the spec requires at least one element. The emptiness check is not inside the loop — it cannot be, because the loop body never executes. It must be a separate, explicit check.**
   138. **Reference-list attributes must validate intra-list uniqueness — duplicate references are always invalid:** When a configuration attribute contains a list of references to other named elements (e.g. `upsert_key: [field1, field2]` references field names on an expose block, `unique_composite: [field_a, field_b]` references sibling field names on an entity field), the validation function MUST check for duplicate entries within the list — not just that each entry resolves to a valid target. Items 62 and 131 cover uniqueness of primary definition collections (entity names, field names, operations, enum values). This item covers the gap: **reference lists** — lists whose elements are not definitions but pointers to definitions. A reference list with duplicate entries is always semantically invalid: `ON CONFLICT ("x", "x")` is rejected by PostgreSQL at runtime, `UNIQUE (label, label)` is semantically equivalent to `UNIQUE (label)` but expresses a different (wrong) intent, and in general a list of references that mentions the same target twice provides no additional information over mentioning it once — the duplicate is either a typo or a misunderstanding. The danger is that reference-list validation functions are typically structured as `for _, ref := range list { validate(ref) }` — checking each reference individually against an external set (e.g. "does this field name exist in the entity?") — and the per-element validation correctly confirms each reference exists but never asks "have I seen this reference before in this list?" The `for range` loop validates *downward* (element → target) but not *laterally* (element ↔ sibling elements). After implementing any validation that iterates a list of references to validate each element: (a) add a `seen` map alongside the per-element validation loop; (b) before or after validating each element's target, check whether the element is already in the `seen` map; (c) if a duplicate is found, return an error identifying the list attribute, the duplicate entry, and the parent context (e.g. "expose block for entity 'Widget': upsert_key contains duplicate field reference 'resource_type'" or "entity 'User': field 'email' has duplicate entry 'label' in unique_composite"); (d) apply fix-class exhaustion (item 33): after adding intra-list uniqueness to one reference-list attribute, enumerate ALL reference-list attributes in the configuration schema — at minimum: `upsert_key` (field-name references on expose blocks), `unique_composite` (field-name references on entity fields), `gate` fill lists (fill-name references on slot bindings), `chain` step lists (fill-name references on slot bindings), `fan-out` fill lists (fill-name references on slot bindings), and any future list-valued attribute whose elements reference named data-model entities — and verify each has equivalent intra-list uniqueness validation. **All three slot binding operator lists (`gate`, `chain`, `fan-out`) are structurally identical — each is a list of fill names on a slot binding — so intra-list uniqueness must be checked for all three, not just one.** **The structural anti-pattern:** a validation loop that calls `for _, ref := range list { if !targetSet[ref] { error } }` validates only *vertical* correctness (each reference points to a real target) while ignoring *horizontal* correctness (no two references point to the same target). Both are required. The existing items 62 and 131 use language focused on "named collections" and "sub-collection elements" — terminology that naturally evokes primary definitions (entity names, field names). Reference lists are easy to miss because their elements are not definitions but pointers — the implementer thinks "I validated each pointer resolves" and moves on without considering pointer-to-pointer relationships within the list. **The principle: per-element validation ("does this reference resolve?") and intra-list validation ("is this reference unique within this list?") are independent checks that must both be performed. A validation function that validates each element individually but never compares elements to each other has a structural blind spot at the list level — it can catch invalid references but not redundant ones. Every `for range` loop over a reference list needs two checks: "is this a valid reference?" and "have I seen this reference before?"**
   139. **Composite configuration objects with mutually optional but collectively required attributes must validate that at least one attribute is present:** When a configuration object has N optional sibling attributes where at least one must be present for the configuration to have any semantic effect (e.g. a slot binding has `gate`, `chain`, and `fan-out` — each is individually optional, but a slot binding with none of them is semantically void), the validation function MUST check that at least one attribute is populated. This is distinct from item 137 (minimum cardinality of a single collection — e.g. operations list must have ≥1 element) and from item 132 (bidirectional operation-attribute dependencies — e.g. `upsert_key` ↔ `upsert`). This item covers the pattern where a configuration object's **purpose** is defined by the presence of at least one of several sibling attributes, and the absence of ALL of them renders the configuration object meaningless. The anti-pattern: a slot binding `{slot: before_create, entity: Widget}` with no `gate`, `chain`, or `fan-out` passes all individual attribute validations (slot name is valid, entity is exposed, no `short_circuit` without `chain`), but the binding as a whole does nothing — it declares intent to bind a slot but wires no fills to it. The assembler's `writeSlotWiring` silently skips it (no `len(sb.Gate) > 0`, `len(sb.Chain) > 0`, or `len(sb.FanOut) > 0` branch matches), producing no generated code and no error. The configuration is dead weight that misleads the user into thinking the slot is wired. After implementing validation for any configuration object that has multiple optional sibling attributes: (a) ask: "can this configuration object have a meaningful effect with ALL of these attributes absent?" — if the answer is no, add an explicit check that at least one is present; (b) return a clear error identifying the configuration object and the set of attributes it requires at least one of (e.g. "slot binding for slot 'before_create' on entity 'Widget' has no operator — each slot binding must specify at least one of: gate, chain, fan-out"); (c) apply fix-class exhaustion (item 33): enumerate ALL configuration object types in the schema and identify which ones have the same "collectively required sibling attributes" pattern. **Mandatory audit step:** after implementing this check for any one configuration object type, enumerate ALL configuration object types (expose blocks, slot bindings, entity field definitions, mixin declarations) and ask: "does this object type have N optional attributes where at least one is required for the object to have meaning?" For slot bindings, the answer is yes: `gate`, `chain`, and `fan-out` are collectively required. For expose blocks, the answer is already covered by item 137 (operations list min cardinality). **The principle: item 137 catches the "empty single list" blind spot. This item catches the "empty multi-attribute" blind spot — a configuration object that passes all per-attribute and per-list validations individually but is collectively void because no attribute provides its defining behavior. Individual attribute validations are necessary but not sufficient — a wholeness check at the object level is also required. When implementing this check, also evaluate item 140 — if the attributes are mutually exclusive alternatives (not cumulative), "at least one" must be paired with "at most one" to enforce "exactly one."**
   140. **Mutually exclusive sibling attributes must be validated for exclusivity — at most one may be present:** When a configuration object has N optional sibling attributes that represent mutually exclusive alternatives (e.g. a slot binding has `gate`, `chain`, and `fan-out` — each is a distinct composition strategy with different semantics: AND-gate blocks on failure, chain pipes sequentially, fan-out broadcasts concurrently), the validation function MUST check that at most one attribute is populated. This is the complement of item 139 (at least one must be present): together they enforce "exactly one." Item 139 catches the lower bound (no operator → semantically void); this item catches the upper bound (multiple operators → conflicting semantics). The anti-pattern: the spec shows each slot binding with exactly one operator — `gate: [rbac-policy, admin-creation-policy]` on one binding, `fan-out: [user-change-notifier, audit-logger]` on another — establishing mutual exclusivity by example. The validation checks that at least one operator is present (item 139) but not that at most one is present. A slot binding `{slot: before_create, entity: Widget, gate: [rbac-policy], chain: [step-a]}` passes validation. The assembler's `writeSlotWiring` generates code for ALL present operators independently — producing both a gate variable and a chain variable. Both are injected into the handler constructor and the generated handler invokes both the AND-gate AND the sequential chain on the same slot invocation — conflating two semantically distinct composition strategies. This escapes all static checks (`stego validate`, `stego plan`, `go build`) because the generated code is syntactically valid — the semantic conflict only manifests as unexpected runtime behavior (a gate and chain both executing on the same slot hook). After implementing item 139's "at least one of N attributes" check on any configuration object: (a) immediately ask the follow-up question: "are these N attributes mutually exclusive alternatives, or can they be meaningfully combined?" — check the spec's examples (does any example show multiple attributes on the same object?) and the semantic descriptions (do the attributes describe distinct strategies?); (b) if the spec never shows multiple attributes on the same object AND the attributes describe semantically distinct strategies that cannot be meaningfully composed (e.g. "AND-all" vs. "sequential pipeline" vs. "concurrent broadcast"), they are mutually exclusive — add a check that counts the populated attributes and rejects when more than one is present; (c) return a clear error identifying the configuration object and the conflicting attributes (e.g. "slot binding for slot 'before_create' on entity 'Widget' has multiple operators (gate, chain) — each slot binding must specify exactly one operator: gate, chain, or fan-out"); (d) write negative tests for each pair of attributes appearing together on the same configuration object (gate+chain, gate+fan-out, chain+fan-out, gate+chain+fan-out); (e) apply fix-class exhaustion (item 33): after implementing this check for one configuration object type, enumerate ALL configuration object types in the schema and identify which ones have mutually exclusive sibling attributes. **The structural relationship between items 139 and 140:** item 139 checks the lower bound (≥1), this item checks the upper bound (≤1). When both apply to the same attribute set, they jointly enforce "exactly one." Implementing item 139 without considering item 140 is a half-fix — it rejects the empty case but accepts the conflicting case. **The principle: "at least one" and "at most one" are independent cardinality constraints. When the spec defines N alternatives and shows exactly one being used, both constraints apply. Implementing one without the other leaves the complementary invalid state undetected. Whenever you add an "at least one" check, ask: "should this also be 'exactly one'?" If the attributes are alternatives rather than cumulative options, the answer is yes.**
   141. **Paired range constraints must be validated for consistency — lower bound must not exceed upper bound:** When the spec defines paired constraints that form a range or interval on a field's valid values (e.g. `min_length`/`max_length` on string fields, `min`/`max` on numeric fields), the validation function MUST verify that the lower bound does not exceed the upper bound when both are present on the same field. A range where lower > upper is mathematically empty — no value can satisfy both constraints simultaneously. This produces contradictory generated artifacts that compile and deploy but fail at runtime or reject all input: the postgres-adapter generates contradictory CHECK constraints (e.g. `CHECK (length("label") >= 10)` AND `CHECK (length("label") <= 5)`) that no row can satisfy, and the rest-api generator produces contradictory OpenAPI schema properties (e.g. `minLength: 10, maxLength: 5`) that no client payload can match. The error escapes ALL static checks — `stego validate`, `stego plan`, `stego apply`, and `go build` all pass — and only surfaces at database runtime (every INSERT fails) or when an OpenAPI-aware client rejects the schema. This is distinct from constraint-type applicability (which validates that a constraint is valid for a field's type — e.g. `min_length` only on strings) and from constrained-value validation (item 134, which validates that an attribute's value is one of the spec-defined options). Those are per-constraint checks. This item covers **inter-constraint consistency** — the relationship between two constraints on the same field that together define a range. The anti-pattern: the validation treats each constraint independently — it confirms `min_length` is valid for strings (constraint-type check), confirms `max_length` is valid for strings (constraint-type check), confirms both values are non-negative, but never compares `min_length` against `max_length`. Each constraint passes individually; the pair fails collectively. After implementing validation for any constraint attribute that has a paired counterpart defining a range: (a) identify whether the constraint is one half of a range pair — look for attributes whose names share a semantic base with a min/max or lower/upper prefix (e.g. `min_length`/`max_length`, `min`/`max`, `min_items`/`max_items`); (b) when both halves of a pair are present on the same field, validate that the lower-bound value does not exceed the upper-bound value (`min_length <= max_length`, `min <= max`); (c) return a clear error identifying the field, the entity, and the contradictory values (e.g. "entity 'User': field 'label' has min_length (10) > max_length (5) — the constraint range is empty and no value can satisfy both bounds"); (d) apply fix-class exhaustion (item 33): enumerate ALL range-pair constraints in the spec's entity type system and verify each pair receives the consistency check — for the current spec, this means both `min_length`/`max_length` (strings) and `min`/`max` (numerics) must be checked; (e) write negative tests for each pair with lower > upper AND with lower == upper (boundary case — an equality constraint expressed as a range should be valid). **Mandatory audit step — exhaustive constraint-pair enumeration:** After implementing a range-consistency check for any one constraint pair, enumerate ALL constraint attributes defined in the spec's entity type system section and group them into pairs by semantic relationship. Every pair that defines a range must receive a consistency check. Do NOT stop at the pair named in the finding. **The structural anti-pattern this catches:** validation functions that enumerate constraint attributes and validate each one independently (type-applicability, value-validity, non-emptiness) without ever comparing two constraint values on the same field. Per-constraint validation is necessary but not sufficient — inter-constraint validation is also required. The pattern signature is: "the validation function has a per-field loop that checks each constraint attribute independently but never reads two constraint attributes from the same field in the same conditional expression." Any field with two or more constraints whose values interact (one bounds the other, one implies the other, one contradicts the other) needs an inter-constraint check. **The principle: constraints on the same field exist in a shared semantic space. They are not independent facts — they collectively describe the field's valid value set. A field with `min_length: 10` and `max_length: 5` has an empty valid value set, which is always an error (no default, no migration, no input can populate this field). Validating constraints individually catches "wrong type" and "wrong value" errors, but only validating constraints pairwise catches "impossible combination" errors. Both levels of validation are required.**
   142. **Constraint values with domain-specific syntax must be validated for syntactic validity — not just presence and type-applicability:** When a constraint attribute's value has intrinsic syntactic validity requirements beyond being present and being applied to the correct field type (e.g. `pattern` must be a syntactically valid regular expression, a JSON path must parse, a cron expression must be valid cron syntax), the validation function MUST verify that the value is well-formed at validation time. This is distinct from constrained-value validation (item 134, which checks values against a finite set of named modes like `ValidConcurrencyModes`) and from inter-constraint consistency (item 141, which checks paired constraints like min ≤ max). This item covers constraint values whose domain is NOT a finite enumerable set but HAS a well-defined syntax that can be validated programmatically. The anti-pattern: the validation function checks constraint-type applicability ("is `pattern` on a string field?" — yes, pass) and moves on without checking whether the pattern value itself is a valid regex. A field `{name: name, type: string, pattern: "["}` passes `stego validate`. The rest-api generator passes the malformed value into the OpenAPI schema's `pattern` field — JSON Schema validators and OpenAPI-aware clients reject it. The postgres-adapter generates `CHECK (name ~ '[')` — PostgreSQL rejects the migration at runtime with `ERROR: invalid regular expression`. The error escapes ALL static checks and only surfaces at deployment time or when downstream tooling rejects the schema. The precedent is already set: item 134 validates constraint values against finite sets (e.g. concurrency modes); item 141 validates inter-constraint relationships (e.g. min ≤ max). This item adds the third level: values whose validity is defined by a grammar or syntax, not a finite set or a numeric relationship. After implementing validation for any constraint attribute that accepts a free-form value (not drawn from a finite set): (a) ask: "does this value have a well-defined syntax or grammar that can be validated programmatically?" — regexes can be validated by `regexp.Compile`, JSON paths by a JSON path parser, cron expressions by a cron parser, SQL identifiers by naming rules, etc.; (b) if the value has a validatable syntax, call the appropriate validation function at validation time and return a clear error identifying the field, the entity, the constraint attribute, the invalid value, and the parse error (e.g. "entity 'User': field 'name' has invalid pattern constraint '[' — regexp parse error: missing closing ]: `[`"); (c) apply fix-class exhaustion (item 33): after adding syntactic validation for one constraint attribute, enumerate ALL constraint attributes in the spec's entity type system and identify which ones accept free-form values with validatable syntax — verify each receives equivalent validation; (d) write negative tests with syntactically invalid values for each constraint that has domain-specific syntax. **The three levels of constraint-value validation:** Level 1 (item 134): finite-set membership — the value must be one of N named options. Level 2 (item 141): inter-constraint consistency — paired values must satisfy a relationship (min ≤ max). Level 3 (this item): domain-specific syntactic validity — the value must be well-formed according to its domain's grammar. All three are independent checks. A validation function that performs Level 1 and Level 2 but skips Level 3 will catch "unknown concurrency mode" and "contradictory min/max" but miss "malformed regex" — the error escapes to runtime or downstream tooling. **The pattern signature:** a constraint attribute whose value is passed directly into generated code or spec output (e.g. into a SQL `CHECK` clause, an OpenAPI `pattern` field, a protobuf annotation) without any intermediate parsing or compilation step in the validation function. If the generated consumer will parse/compile the value, the validation function should do the same check earlier so the error is caught at `stego validate` time rather than at deployment time.
   144. **Error message recovery actions must reference implemented functionality:** When an error message suggests a recovery action for the user (e.g. "specify --component to disambiguate", "use --verbose for details", "run stego validate to check"), the suggested action MUST be functional — the flag, command, or workaround must actually exist and work as described. Item 24 covers factual accuracy of error messages about *runtime state* (e.g. "no component provides it" when providers exist). This item covers a distinct class: error messages that are accurate about the *problem* but prescribe a *recovery action* that doesn't work — the user follows the guidance, it fails, and they have no recourse. The error detection is correct; the error *resolution path* is broken. This is particularly insidious because the error message reads as authoritative — the user trusts the tool to know its own capabilities. A message that says "specify --component" implies `--component` exists; a message that says "run stego fix" implies `stego fix` is a command. When the suggestion is wrong, the user wastes time trying to follow it, then falls back to reading source code or filing a bug. After writing any error message that contains an imperative verb directed at the user (e.g. "specify", "use", "run", "pass", "set", "add"): (a) identify every flag, command, option, or feature the message references; (b) for each reference, verify it exists and is functional — for CLI flags, grep the FlagSet definitions in the same command function and confirm the flag is registered; for commands, verify the command is wired in the entrypoint; for configuration options, verify they are recognized by the config parser; (c) if the referenced functionality does not exist, either implement it before shipping the error message, or rewrite the message to suggest only actions that currently work (e.g. listing the matching items so the user can restructure their input, rather than suggesting a disambiguation flag that hasn't been built); (d) write a test that triggers the error and verifies the suggested recovery action is viable — e.g. if the message says "specify --component foo", test that `--component foo` is accepted by the flag parser and produces the expected behavior. **The principle: an error message is a contract with the user. The diagnostic part ("slot X is ambiguous") describes what went wrong. The prescriptive part ("specify --component to disambiguate") promises how to fix it. Both parts must be accurate. A diagnostic that is accurate but paired with an impossible prescription is worse than no suggestion at all — it wastes the user's time on a dead end. Item 24 validates the diagnostic; this item validates the prescription.**
   143. **Multi-source name lookups must detect ambiguity — never silently pick the first match:** When code searches across multiple independent collections (e.g. iterating all components in a registry to find which one owns a slot name, scanning all modules to find a provider of a capability, searching all packages to resolve a type name), and the search key is NOT guaranteed to be unique across the collections, the search MUST collect ALL matches and detect when more than one collection contains a match. A common error pattern: a `for range` loop iterates a map of collections, finds the first item matching the search key, and `break`s — returning a single result. Because Go map iteration order is non-deterministic, this produces different results across invocations when multiple collections contain the same key, creating a Heisenbug: the command works correctly in testing (where the map happens to iterate in a favorable order) but produces wrong results in production or on a different run. The spec may explicitly state that the search key can appear in multiple collections (e.g. "each component owns its own slot protos — no sharing between components — duplication is cheaper than coupling" implies the same slot name can appear in multiple components). Even when the spec does not explicitly state this, multi-source lookups must be defensive: the absence of a uniqueness guarantee means ambiguity is possible. After writing any code that searches across multiple independent collections for a name match: (a) replace the early-`break` pattern with a full scan that collects ALL matching sources into a slice; (b) after the scan, check `len(matches)`: if 0, return a "not found" error; if 1, proceed with the single match; if >1, return an error listing ALL matching sources so the user can disambiguate (e.g. "slot 'before_create' is defined by multiple components: [component-a, component-b] — specify --component to disambiguate"); (c) verify that the search function's return type supports reporting the ambiguity — a function that returns `(*T, error)` can report ambiguity via the error, but a function that returns just `*T` (nil for not found) cannot distinguish "not found" from "ambiguous"; (d) write tests for three cases: zero matches (not found), exactly one match (success), and two or more matches (ambiguity error). **The pattern signature:** a `for range` loop over a map (or any collection with non-deterministic iteration order) that contains an inner search and an early `break` or `return` on first match without counting total matches. Grep for `break` inside nested loops where the outer loop iterates a map and the inner loop searches for a name — each instance is a potential ambiguity bug. **The relationship to other items:** Item 13 covers duplicate keys on *insert* into a single map (write-side uniqueness). Item 22 covers degenerate inputs like self-references and cycles. This item covers *read-side ambiguity* — searching across multiple maps or collections where the same key can legitimately exist in more than one. Item 13 prevents duplicates within a single collection; this item detects duplicates *across* collections during lookup. **The principle: a search that returns "the first match" is only correct when at most one match can exist. When the search space spans multiple independent collections with no cross-collection uniqueness constraint, "first match" is an arbitrary selection — and when the iteration order is non-deterministic (Go maps), it is a non-deterministic arbitrary selection. The correct pattern is "collect all matches, then decide": zero matches is an error, one match is success, multiple matches is ambiguity. The cost of a full scan is negligible compared to the cost of a silently wrong result.**
   146. **Generated code that consumes middleware-populated context must extract the actual context value — never substitute a zero-value placeholder:** When a code generation pipeline produces BOTH a middleware component that writes structured data into the request context (e.g. `auth.NewAuthMiddleware()` stores an `Identity` via `context.WithValue`) AND a handler component that needs that data (e.g. a before-create gate fill that inspects `req.Caller.Role`), the generated handler MUST extract the value from the context using the middleware's published accessor (e.g. `auth.IdentityFromContext(r.Context())`) — not construct a zero-value placeholder (`&slots.Identity{}`). A zero-value placeholder compiles, avoids nil-dereference panics, and superficially looks correct — but it silently discards the authenticated caller's actual identity, causing every authorization check that depends on the caller's attributes (role, user ID, permissions) to see empty/zero values. This defeats the purpose of both the auth middleware and the gate fill. The pattern is especially insidious because: (a) the generated code is syntactically and type-correct; (b) `go build` and `go vet` both pass; (c) the fill's logic runs without errors — it simply makes decisions based on wrong (empty) data; (d) the bug is invisible in any test that doesn't assert on the *populated* Identity values flowing through the gate. **The generator has access to structured metadata about which components are wired** — it knows that auth middleware is present (the `Wiring` metadata includes middleware constructors) and it knows that the slot request type has a `Caller *Identity` field (the `HasCaller` metadata flag is already set). The gap is that the code emission for `HasCaller` emits a zero-value literal instead of an accessor call. After writing any generator that emits struct literals containing fields whose values should come from the request context (or any other cross-component data channel): (a) for each field in the emitted struct literal, determine whether the field's value is locally available (e.g. decoded from the request body — `Input` field) or must be extracted from a cross-component data channel (e.g. the request context — `Caller` field); (b) for fields that must be extracted from a cross-component channel, verify the generated code calls the appropriate accessor function (e.g. `auth.IdentityFromContext(r.Context())`) rather than constructing a zero-value or placeholder; (c) if the accessor function requires an import (e.g. the `auth` package), verify the import is included in the generated file; (d) write a test that verifies the generated handler code contains the accessor call, not a zero-value literal — grep the generated output for the accessor function name and for the zero-value pattern, and fail if the accessor is absent or the zero-value is present. **The principle: when two generated components communicate through a shared data channel (request context, shared state, message bus), the generator must wire BOTH sides — the writer AND the reader. Wiring only the writer (middleware stores identity in context) while leaving the reader as a placeholder (handler constructs zero-value Identity) is a half-wired pipeline. The data flows into the context and stops there — the handler ignores it and fabricates empty data instead. Item 1 (no dead code) catches unused definitions; this item catches unused data channels — the accessor function exists, the middleware populates the context, but the handler never calls the accessor. The data channel is wired at the write end but dead at the read end.**
   147. **Generated public API must not provide alternative entry points that bypass fill/slot wiring:** When a generator produces a public function (e.g. `NewRouter()`) alongside the primary wiring path (e.g. inline wiring in `main.go`), the public function MUST NOT silently pass `nil` for fill/slot operator parameters that the primary path correctly wires. A public function that accepts `(store Storage)` but internally calls `NewUserHandler(store, nil, nil)` — passing `nil` for the before-create gate and on-entity-changed fan-out — creates a bypass path that disables ALL security gates and audit hooks. The spec states: "An auditor reads main.go and sees every fill and every connection." A public alternative entry point that nullifies fills contradicts this auditability guarantee because: (a) a caller can use `NewRouter()` instead of the `main.go` wiring and silently lose all gate and fan-out protections; (b) the `nil` values are invisible in the function signature — the caller sees `NewRouter(auth, store)` and has no indication that fills are disabled; (c) the nil-guard pattern in handlers (`if h.beforeCreateGate != nil`) means the bypass is silent — no error, no log, no indication that the gate was skipped. **This is distinct from item 1 (dead code)** because `NewRouter` is a public function that IS callable — it is not dead. It is a live, public API surface that offers a worse (insecure) alternative to the correct wiring path. **There are two valid resolutions:** (a) **Remove the function entirely** — if `main.go`'s inline wiring is the only intended entry point, `NewRouter` is redundant and should not exist; (b) **Thread fills through the function signature** — change `NewRouter(auth, store)` to `NewRouter(auth, store, beforeCreateGate, onEntityChangedFanOut)` so that fills cannot be omitted by the caller. Resolution (a) is preferred when the function exists only as generator scaffolding; resolution (b) is appropriate when the function serves as a reusable API (e.g. for testing). After writing any generator that produces public functions alongside wiring code: (a) enumerate every public function the generator emits (exported functions — capitalized in Go); (b) for each public function, trace every constructor call inside it and verify that NO parameter is hardcoded to `nil` when the primary wiring path passes a non-nil value for the same parameter; (c) if a public function must exist, its signature must accept all fill/operator dependencies — callers must not be able to accidentally construct the dependency graph without fills; (d) write a test that verifies the generated `router.go` either does not contain a `NewRouter` function, or that any `NewRouter` function's signature includes parameters for all slot operators that the handler constructors accept. **The principle: every public entry point into the generated code is a potential bypass path. If the primary path wires fills correctly but a secondary path silently drops them, the security model has a hole that is invisible to auditors. A generated API surface must be fill-complete at every entry point — not just `main.go`.**
   148. **Code-generation templates that emit format strings must use correct escape levels for the target context:** When assembler or generator code constructs output strings that will be interpreted as format strings by the generated code's runtime (e.g. `log.Printf`, `fmt.Sprintf` in the generated output), the escape level of format verbs (`%s`, `%d`, etc.) MUST be correct for the string-construction method used AND the target interpretation context. A common error pattern: the assembler uses `buf.WriteString` (which does NOT interpret format verbs) to write a string that contains `%%s`. Since `WriteString` writes the literal bytes, `%%s` appears verbatim in the generated file. In the generated file, `log.Printf("starting server on %%s", addr)` interprets `%%` as an escaped literal `%`, producing the output `starting server on %s` — the `addr` argument is unused and `go vet` flags the call as having wrong argument count. The correct escape depends on the writing method: (a) **`buf.WriteString` / `buf.Write`**: these write literal bytes — use `%s` directly, since no format interpretation occurs at write time; (b) **`fmt.Fprintf(buf, ...)`**: this IS a format function — use `%%s` to produce a literal `%s` in the output, or `%s` with an argument if the value should be interpolated at generation time; (c) **Go template `{{printf}}`**: this is a format function — same rules as `Fprintf`. The error class is **escape-level mismatch**: the developer mentally models the wrong interpretation layer when choosing the escape level. After writing any generator or assembler code that emits text containing `%` characters: (a) identify the writing method (`WriteString`, `Fprintf`, `Write`, template rendering); (b) determine whether the writing method interprets format verbs; (c) determine whether the generated output will be interpreted as a format string at runtime (e.g. it appears inside a `log.Printf` or `fmt.Sprintf` call in the generated code); (d) choose the escape level based on the chain: if the writing method does NOT interpret (`WriteString`), use the escape level that the generated runtime needs — `%s` for a format verb, `%%` for a literal percent sign; if the writing method DOES interpret (`Fprintf`), add one escape level — `%%s` for a format verb in the output, `%%%%` for a literal percent sign in the output; (e) after writing any line that contains `%` in a string passed to a generation buffer, trace the character through both layers (generation-time writing method → generated-code runtime interpretation) and verify the final output is correct; (f) run `go vet ./...` on BOTH the generator/assembler source AND on representative generated output directories (e.g. `go vet ./examples/*/out/...`) to catch format-string mismatches at both layers. **Item 9 requires `go build ./...` and `go vet ./...` but does not explicitly require running these checks on generated output directories.** When the generated output is part of the repository (e.g. `examples/*/out/`), `go vet ./...` from the repo root may or may not include it depending on module structure. The self-verification must explicitly include: `cd examples/<name>/out && go vet ./...` for each example that produces generated output. **The pattern signature:** any `WriteString` or `Write` call whose string argument contains `%%` — this is suspicious because `WriteString` does not interpret `%`, so `%%` is written literally and is almost never what the developer intended. Grep the assembler and generator source for `%%` inside `WriteString` and `Write` calls and verify each instance is intentional. **The principle: every `%` character in a code-generation template passes through at least two interpretation layers — the generation-time writing method and the generated-code runtime. The correct escape level is determined by the product of these layers, not by either one in isolation. A developer who thinks "I need `%%` to produce `%`" is correct only when the writing method IS a format function. When the writing method is literal (`WriteString`), `%%` produces `%%` — which the generated runtime then interprets as a single `%`, consuming the `s` that follows as a (now-broken) format verb.**
   145. **User-provided optional parameters must be validated in ALL code paths — never silently ignored:** When a CLI command, API function, or configuration accepts an optional parameter (e.g. `--component` flag, `--format` option, `scope` override), and the parameter is provided by the user with an explicit value, the code MUST validate that value in EVERY code path — not just the code path that motivated the parameter's creation. A common error pattern: a parameter is added to solve a specific problem in one branch (e.g. `--component` added to disambiguate when multiple components define the same slot), and validation is only wired into that branch. When a different branch handles the non-ambiguous case, the parameter is silently discarded — the user explicitly specifies `--component nonexistent-component`, the command succeeds, and the `--component` value is completely ignored. The user receives no feedback that their intent was overridden. This is particularly insidious because: (a) the user believes they are controlling the command's behavior (they explicitly typed `--component foo`); (b) the command's output gives no indication that the flag was ignored; (c) the silent success means the error is invisible — the user discovers the problem only when the generated artifact references the wrong component. **The validation scope for an optional parameter is ALL code paths in the function, not just the motivating path.** When a user provides an explicit value for an optional parameter, the contract is: "use this value, or tell me it's wrong." The contract is NOT: "use this value if you happen to need it, otherwise throw it away." After adding any optional parameter (CLI flag, function argument, config field) to a command or function: (a) enumerate EVERY branch in the function that selects or computes the value the parameter is meant to influence (e.g. every branch that determines the owning component, every branch that selects an output format); (b) in EACH branch, verify the parameter's value is either used (overrides the default selection) or validated (checked against the available options and rejected if invalid); (c) if a branch does not need the parameter (e.g. only one component matches, so disambiguation is unnecessary), the branch must STILL validate the parameter value if it was provided — verify it is consistent with the branch's automatic selection and return an error if it conflicts (e.g. "slot 'before_create' is defined only by component 'rest-api', but --component specifies 'nonexistent-component' which does not define this slot"); (d) write tests for the parameter in EVERY branch — including the "unnecessary" branch where the automatic selection would suffice — with both valid and invalid parameter values; (e) specifically test with a parameter value that names something that does not exist at all (not just a value that exists but doesn't match the automatic selection) to verify the parameter is not simply discarded. **The pattern signature:** a conditional block `if len(matches) > 1 { validate(param) } else { ignore(param) }` — the parameter is validated only when the motivating condition (ambiguity) is true. The fix is to extract the validation outside the conditional, or to add validation to each branch independently. **The relationship to other items:** Item 24 covers error message accuracy about runtime state. Item 144 covers error messages that suggest non-functional recovery actions. This item covers the complementary case: the parameter IS functional (it was implemented per item 144's requirements) but its validation is branch-gated, so it only works in some code paths. Item 144 ensures the parameter exists; this item ensures the parameter is validated everywhere. **The principle: a user-provided value is user intent. Silently ignoring user intent is always a bug — it means the system did something different from what the user asked for, without informing them. The severity scales with the parameter's safety implications: a silently ignored `--component` produces wrong output; a silently ignored `--dry-run` produces destructive side effects. Every optional parameter must follow the rule: "if provided, validate everywhere; if invalid, reject with a clear error." No branch may silently discard a user-provided value.**
   149. **Rename-induced value domain audit — when a field's value convention changes, audit every downstream consumer:** When a task renames or replaces a struct field (e.g. `SlotDeclaration.Entity` → `SlotDeclaration.Collection`) and the new field carries values in a DIFFERENT naming convention than the old field (e.g. PascalCase entity names like `User` vs kebab-case collection names like `org-users`), every downstream consumer that uses the field's value to construct identifiers, paths, keys, or any format-sensitive output MUST be audited for compatibility with the new value convention. Items 4 and 59 cover type-name accuracy and input charset validation respectively, but neither addresses the case where a RENAME changes what values flow through existing code paths. The old code was correct for PascalCase values; the rename makes it incorrect for kebab-case values, and the code compiles without error because the Go type (`string`) didn't change — only the convention of the string's content changed. A common error pattern: `slotVarName()` concatenates the field value directly into a Go variable name. With PascalCase (`User`), this produces valid identifiers (`beforeCreateUserGate`). With kebab-case (`org-users`), this produces invalid identifiers (`beforeCreateorg-usersGate`) — hyphens are not valid in Go identifiers. The code compiles at the generator level (string concatenation doesn't validate identifier syntax), and the bug only surfaces when the generated code is compiled. After performing ANY field rename or replacement where the field's values follow a different naming convention than the old field: (a) identify the old field's value convention (PascalCase, snake_case, kebab-case, etc.) and the new field's value convention; (b) if the conventions differ, grep the ENTIRE codebase for every reference to the new field name (e.g. `grep -rn '\.Collection' --include='*.go'`) — this finds every consumer of the renamed field; (c) for each consumer, determine whether the consumer depends on the old value convention — look for: string concatenation into identifiers, use as map keys alongside convention-dependent values, interpolation into format strings, use in path construction, or passage to functions that assume a specific format (e.g. `toPascalCase` expects no hyphens); (d) for each consumer that depends on the old convention, add a sanitization step that converts the new convention to the format the consumer requires (e.g. strip hyphens and apply camelCase before concatenating into a Go identifier); (e) write a test with a value that is valid in the new convention but would produce invalid output in the old convention (e.g. a kebab-case name like `org-users`) and verify every consumer handles it correctly; (f) apply fix-class exhaustion (item 33) — after finding one consumer that breaks on the new convention, grep for ALL consumers and verify each is safe. **The principle: a field rename that changes the value domain is a semantic migration, not just a syntactic rename. The Go type system does not distinguish between `string` values in PascalCase and `string` values in kebab-case — both compile. The distinction lives in the conventions that downstream code implicitly depends on. When the convention changes, every consumer's implicit assumption must be re-evaluated. Item 4 ensures the struct field name is correct; this item ensures the struct field's VALUE CONVENTION is compatible with every consumer.**
   150. **Validation requirement traceability — match function logic, not just function names:** When a task lists specific validation rules (e.g. "Validate that fill declarations reference existing collection names"), tracing the requirement to a validation function with a relevant name (e.g. `validateFillsExist`) is NECESSARY but NOT SUFFICIENT. The implementer MUST open the function body and verify that the function's implementation logic performs the EXACT check described by the requirement — not just a check on the same general concept. Item 6 ("full task traceability") says "identify the exact line(s) of code that satisfy it," but does not explicitly require reading the function body to verify the logic matches the stated requirement. This item closes that gap. A common error pattern: the task says "validate that fill declarations reference existing collection names." The implementer finds `validateFillsExist`, sees it operates on fills, and considers the requirement satisfied. But `validateFillsExist` only checks that fill YAML files exist on disk — it does not parse the fill or check whether its `Collection` field references a defined collection. The function's NAME suggests it satisfies the requirement, but its LOGIC does not. After tracing any task requirement to a validation function: (a) open the function body and read its implementation; (b) identify the EXACT conditional or check that performs the described validation — e.g. for "validate that fill declarations reference existing collection names," look for code that reads the fill's `Collection` field and checks it against the set of defined collection names; (c) if no such check exists in the function, the requirement is NOT satisfied — either add the check to the existing function or create a new function; (d) for each validation requirement, write a test that provides input violating the requirement and verify the validation rejects it with a clear error. **Mandatory audit step for validation tasks:** when a task lists N validation requirements (e.g. under "Validation" in the description or in acceptance criteria), create a checklist of all N requirements. For each one: (i) identify the function that should implement it, (ii) read the function body, (iii) point to the exact conditional that performs the check, (iv) point to the test that exercises it. If any of these four steps fails for any requirement, the task is not complete. **The principle: function names are documentation, not implementation. A function named `validateFillsExist` is a CLAIM that it validates fill existence — the claim must be verified by reading the code. The implementation team is writing code to pass adversarial review; the verifier WILL read the function body. If the function name satisfies the requirement but the function body does not, the review will find it. Verify the logic, not the name.**
   151. **Semantic rename completeness — update string literals, error messages, cross-component API contracts, and internal names via data-flow tracing:** When a task renames a semantic concept (e.g. `Entity` → `Collection` on `SlotDeclaration` and `Fill` types), ALL references to the old concept name must be updated — not just struct field names and type references, but also string literals in error messages, log messages, diagnostic output, variable names used internally, comments, AND cross-component API contract fields (public struct fields, their godoc comments, and function names in OTHER packages that store or process the renamed field's value). Items 4 covers Go type/struct names. Item 33 covers fix-class exhaustion for bugs. Neither covers grepping for stale terminology after a conceptual rename, and neither covers tracing the renamed field's VALUE through downstream data flows into other structs. A common error pattern has two variants: **Variant A (same-package string literals):** after renaming `SlotDeclaration.Entity` to `SlotDeclaration.Collection`, error messages in the assembler still say `"for entity %q"` and internal variable/field names still use `entity` to hold a collection name. **Variant B (cross-component API contracts):** the renamed field's value flows into another package's struct field that still uses the old name — e.g., `SlotDeclaration.Collection` (formerly `.Entity`) is read by the generator, which stores the value in `Wiring.ConstructorEntities`. The `ConstructorEntities` field name and its godoc ("maps constructor index to the entity name it handles") still say "entity," but the field now stores collection names. Functions that build or consume this field (e.g. `buildSlotVarsByEntity`, variable `slotVarsByEntity`) also retain the old name. A new generator implementation reading the godoc would pass entity names instead of collection names, causing silent slot injection failures. Variant B is especially insidious because: (a) the stale field is in a DIFFERENT package than the renamed field, so it was not touched during the rename commit; (b) grepping for the old concept name returns hundreds of matches in a codebase where "entity" is a core concept, making it impractical to audit every hit; (c) the mismatch only manifests when a new consumer trusts the stale API documentation. **The fix for Variant B is data-flow tracing, not text search.** After performing ANY semantic rename: **(Phase 1 — data-flow tracing for cross-component contracts):** (a) find every site where the renamed field is READ — grep for `.Collection` (the new field name) or trace call sites of the type that contains it; (b) for each read site, follow the value through assignments, function arguments, and struct literal fields — where does the value end up being STORED in another struct, used as a map key, or passed as a named parameter?
