(c) for each downstream storage site (struct field, map variable, function name), verify the name reflects the new concept — if `Wiring.ConstructorEntities` stores collection names, it must be renamed to `ConstructorCollections`; if `buildSlotVarsByEntity` builds a map keyed by collection names, it must be renamed to `buildSlotVarsByCollection`; if the godoc says "entity name" but the field stores collection names, the godoc must be updated; (d) this data-flow trace is MORE RELIABLE than text-searching for the old concept name because it follows the actual value propagation rather than relying on naming conventions that may have hundreds of false positives. **(Phase 2 — text search for same-package references):** (e) grep for the old concept name in string literals — `grep -rn '".*entity.*"' --include='*.go'` — scoped to FILES THAT WERE MODIFIED during the rename or that import the modified packages; (f) for each match, determine whether the string references the renamed concept; (g) grep for the old concept name in variable and field names within the same scoped file set; (h) grep for the old concept name in comments that describe the renamed concept; (i) run the updated code and verify that error messages, log output, and diagnostic text consistently use the new terminology; (j) write a test that triggers each affected error path and assert the error message uses the new concept name. **Cross-component API contracts are the highest-priority audit targets** because they define the interface between independently-authored components — a stale field name in an API contract misleads every future consumer of that API, not just the current code. The principle: a semantic rename has four layers: (1) types and struct fields on the renamed type — the compiler enforces correctness; (2) cross-component API contracts — struct fields, godoc, and function names in OTHER packages that store the renamed field's value — the compiler does NOT enforce naming correctness, and data-flow tracing is required; (3) string literals and error messages — the compiler does NOT enforce correctness, so the implementer must grep; (4) internal names (variables, comments) — the compiler does not enforce correctness, and they create cognitive dissonance when they diverge from the public API. Layers 2, 3, and 4 are invisible to `go build`. Layer 2 requires data-flow tracing from the renamed field through its consumers. Layers 3 and 4 require text search. After every semantic rename, the checklist is: `go build` for layer 1, data-flow trace for layer 2, `grep` for layers 3 and 4, modified-function name audit for layer 5. **(Phase 3 — modified-function name audit):** After completing all rename modifications, use `git diff` to enumerate every function, method, and test function whose BODY was changed during the rename. For each such function, verify that the function's OWN NAME also reflects the new terminology. The common failure pattern: the implementer changes field accesses inside a function body (e.g. `sb.Entity` → `sb.Collection` inside `validateSlotBindingEntities`) but does not rename the function itself — the function body is correct but the function name is stale. This happens because renaming field references inside a function body is a straightforward text replacement, while renaming the function itself requires updating all call sites and test function names — a separate, more laborious step that is easy to defer and forget. Concretely: run `git diff --unified=0 | grep '^@@.*func '` to list all functions with changed bodies; for each, check whether the function name contains any old concept name from the enumeration checklist. If a function named `validateSlotBindingEntities` had its body modified to reference `.Collection`, the name must be updated to use "Collection" (e.g. `validateSlotCollectionsDefined`). Apply the same check to test function names, auditing EVERY component of compound test names — not just the function-name prefix. Test function names are compound identifiers with multiple segments separated by `_` (e.g. `TestFunctionName_SubcaseSuffix`). Each segment may independently contain stale terminology from a different old concept. Renaming the function-name segment to match the new function name is necessary but NOT sufficient — the subcase suffixes must ALSO be checked against the full old-concept enumeration. Concretely: if `TestValidateSlotBindingEntities_NonExposedCollectionRejected` tests a function that was renamed to `validateSlotCollectionsDefined`, the prefix must change to `TestValidateSlotCollectionsDefined_` AND the suffix `NonExposedCollectionRejected` must be audited — "NonExposed" is a concept from the old ExposeBlock model; the correct concept in the Collection model is "Undefined" (a collection name that doesn't exist in the `collections:` map), so the suffix must become `UndefinedCollectionRejected`. After renaming ANY test function, split the new name on `_` and check EACH segment against every old concept name in the enumeration checklist. A rename that updates one segment but leaves another stale is an incomplete rename — the next review round will find it. This is a fifth layer in the rename audit: (5) function and test names on functions the implementer already touched — the narrowest possible search scope with the highest hit rate, because any function whose body references the new concept while its name references the old concept is guaranteed stale. **Mandatory multi-rename enumeration:** When a task performs MULTIPLE concept renames (e.g. `ExposeBlock` → `Collection` AND `Entity` → `Collection` AND `expose:` → `collections:`), the implementer MUST enumerate ALL old concept names at the start of the audit and perform Phase 1 and Phase 2 INDEPENDENTLY for EACH old concept name. A common error pattern: the implementer applies the audit to one rename (e.g. grepping for "entity" in string literals) but skips others (e.g. does not grep for "expose block", "expose list", or "expose" in the same files). Each old concept name is a separate audit target — finding zero stale references for one name does not satisfy the audit for the others. Before marking ready-for-review, create an explicit checklist of ALL old concept names from the task description — every type name (e.g. `ExposeBlock`), field name (e.g. `Entity`, `Expose`), YAML key (e.g. `expose:`), and user-facing term (e.g. "expose block", "expose list") that was replaced — and next to each one, record the grep command used and the number of remaining references in string literals, error messages, and variable/function names. If any old concept name still appears in user-facing strings or API contract names, the rename is incomplete. The enumeration step is mandatory because multi-rename tasks create a combinatorial audit surface: the implementer will naturally focus on the rename that caused the most compiler errors (type/field renames) and overlook renames that only affect string literals (terminology in error messages). The string-literal renames are invisible to `go build` and will survive indefinitely unless explicitly grepped for. **Concrete example of the failure this prevents:** task-018 renames both `ExposeBlock` → `Collection` (type) and `SlotDeclaration.Entity` → `SlotDeclaration.Collection` (field). The implementer audits "entity" in error messages (per Phase 2) and updates the assembler. But the generator's validation functions contain 10 error messages referencing "expose block" and "expose list" — these survive five review rounds because the "expose" rename was never independently audited. The enumeration step would have produced a checklist entry for "expose block" and "expose list" alongside "entity," and the grep would have immediately revealed all 10 stale strings.
   152. **Single-value accessor methods on map-typed fields must be validated or made deterministic:** When a struct field has a map type (e.g. `Scope map[string]string`) and the struct provides accessor methods that iterate over the map to return a single element (e.g. `ScopeField()` returns the first key, `ParentEntity()` returns the first value), Go map iteration order is non-deterministic — for a map with more than one entry, these accessors return different results across invocations, producing a Heisenbug in any downstream code that depends on the returned value. This is distinct from item 80 (ambiguous match rejection in search functions) and item 143 (multi-source lookup ambiguity). Those items cover searching ACROSS collections for a match. This item covers accessor methods on a SINGLE map-typed field that assume the map has exactly one entry. The pattern is especially dangerous when the codebase requires deterministic output (e.g. "STEGO deterministically generates production-ready code") because the non-determinism is invisible in testing — a single-entry map (the common case) always returns the correct value, and multi-entry maps return a random correct answer that happens to pass most of the time. The bug surfaces intermittently in production when code generation produces different output across runs with the same input. **Three sources of the bug:** (a) **the type permits multi-entry maps** — `map[string]string` imposes no cardinality constraint; (b) **the accessor assumes single-entry** — it iterates and returns the first hit without checking whether there are more; (c) **no validation rejects multi-entry maps** — the validation layer checks each entry independently (e.g. validates each scope key resolves to a field and each scope value resolves to an entity) but does not check the map's cardinality. All three must be addressed for a complete fix, but (c) is the most important — if validation rejects multi-entry maps, the accessor's assumption is safe. After writing any accessor method that iterates over a map-typed field and returns a single element: (a) determine whether the map is DESIGNED to hold exactly one entry (the current accessor pattern) or multiple entries (future design intent); (b) if the map is designed for exactly one entry, add a validation rule that rejects maps with zero or more than one entry — e.g. "multi-field scopes are not yet supported; scope must contain exactly one entry" — this makes the accessor's assumption an enforced invariant rather than a silent assumption; (c) if the map is designed for multiple entries, the accessor pattern is wrong — either return a slice of all entries (and update all callers to handle multiple values), or require a key parameter so the caller specifies which entry it wants, or use a deterministic iteration order (sorted keys); (d) in EITHER case, the accessor method MUST NOT iterate a map and return the first hit without either validating the map has exactly one entry or sorting the keys — `for k := range m { return k }` is the canonical Go non-determinism footgun; (e) after writing any accessor on a map-typed field, grep for all callers of the accessor and verify that the caller's behavior is correct for all possible return values — if the caller uses the value to construct a file path, a route pattern, or a generated identifier, non-determinism in the accessor means non-determinism in the output; (f) write a test with a multi-entry map and verify that either (i) validation rejects it with a clear error, or (ii) the accessor returns a deterministic result (same value on repeated calls with the same input). **Mandatory audit step:** after writing or modifying ANY struct that has a map-typed field, enumerate all methods on that struct and check whether any method iterates the map and returns a single element. The pattern signature is: `for k, v := range s.MapField { return k (or v) }` — any method matching this pattern on a map that can have multiple entries is a non-determinism bug. **The principle: Go maps are unordered by design. An accessor that returns "the first element" of a map is returning "a random element" when the map has more than one entry. If the accessor's contract is "return THE scope field" (singular, definite), the map's cardinality must be validated to match — either by the accessor itself (return error if len > 1) or by upstream validation (reject multi-entry maps before they reach the accessor). An assumption about map cardinality that is not enforced by validation is a latent non-determinism bug.**
   153. **YAML key migration error exhaustiveness — reject deprecated keys in ALL schemas, not just the most prominent one:** When a task renames a struct field's yaml tag (changing the YAML key from one name to another, e.g. `yaml:"entity"` → `yaml:"collection"`), the implementer MUST enumerate ALL YAML schemas where the old key could appear and add explicit migration error detection in EACH schema's unmarshaling path. Items 149 and 151 cover downstream value consumers and semantic rename completeness, but neither addresses the YAML key name itself — the string in the yaml tag that determines which YAML key deserializes into the field. yaml.v3's default behavior silently discards unknown keys: renaming a yaml tag from `"entity"` to `"collection"` causes any YAML file still using `entity:` to silently lose the field's value (the struct field is populated with its zero value, e.g. empty string). If the codebase establishes a precedent of rejecting deprecated keys with migration errors (e.g. `expose:` → `collections:` in `ServiceDeclaration.UnmarshalYAML` produces a clear error), that precedent MUST be applied to ALL renamed keys across ALL YAML schemas — not just the top-level schema where the rename is most visible. A common error pattern: the task renames `SlotDeclaration.Entity` (yaml `"entity"`) to `SlotDeclaration.Collection` (yaml `"collection"`) AND `Fill.Entity` to `Fill.Collection`. The implementer adds a migration error for `expose:` → `collections:` in the top-level service declaration unmarshaler but does not add equivalent errors for `entity:` → `collection:` in slot declaration mappings or fill.yaml files. A user migrating from the old schema updates `expose:` → `collections:` (because the parser forces them to) but forgets to update `entity:` → `collection:` in slot declarations — the `entity:` key is silently ignored, `SlotDeclaration.Collection` is empty, and the slot binding is functionally dead. The same applies to fill.yaml files. After renaming ANY yaml tag: (a) list every YAML schema (top-level service declaration, nested object types like slot declarations, separate file types like fill.yaml, component.yaml, archetype.yaml) where the old key could appear — trace every struct type that had the renamed field and every YAML file format that deserializes into that struct type; (b) for each schema, add explicit detection of the old key in the `UnmarshalYAML` method (or add a custom unmarshaler if one doesn't exist) that returns a clear migration error identifying the old key, the new key, and the file context (e.g. "the 'entity' key in slot declarations has been renamed to 'collection' — please update your service.yaml"); (c) as a complementary defense, add validation that rejects the zero-value case for the renamed field when the zero value indicates a missed migration — e.g. if `Collection` is empty on a `SlotDeclaration` that has operators defined, the user likely forgot to rename `entity:` → `collection:`; (d) write tests for each schema that verify: (i) the old key is rejected with a helpful migration error, and (ii) the new key is accepted and correctly populates the field; (e) apply fix-class exhaustion (item 33): after adding migration error detection for the renamed key in one schema, enumerate ALL schemas and verify each has equivalent detection. **The principle: yaml.v3's silent key discarding is a migration hazard. When a YAML key is renamed, users who update some keys but not others receive no feedback — the old key silently becomes a no-op. If the codebase already rejects one deprecated key with a migration error, that establishes the contract that ALL deprecated keys will be similarly rejected. Applying migration error detection to only the most prominent schema leaves the less-visible schemas as silent migration traps. The precedent must be exhaustive, or it is misleading.**
   154. **Test fixture YAML key freshness — audit all fixtures after yaml tag renames:** When a struct field's yaml tag is renamed (e.g. `yaml:"entity"` → `yaml:"collection"`), ALL test fixtures that create YAML content for that struct type MUST be updated to use the new key. yaml.v3 silently ignores unknown keys, so test fixtures using the old key pass without error but produce structs with zero-value fields — the test exercises a fundamentally different code path than production code (empty field vs. populated field). This is distinct from item 5 (tests must use spec-complete examples, which covers structural completeness of test YAML) and item 153 (migration error detection in production schemas). This item covers the specific hazard of test fixtures that were correct before a rename but become silently stale after it. A common error pattern: a validation test creates a fill.yaml fixture with `entity: Widget` (the pre-rename key). After the yaml tag is renamed to `"collection"`, the YAML key `entity:` is silently ignored by yaml.v3, `Fill.Collection` is empty, and the test passes — but it now tests the empty-collection path instead of the populated-collection validation path. The test's name and intent suggest it covers fill-collection cross-validation, but it actually covers the degenerate case where no collection is bound. This masks the absence of real validation coverage: the 2 tests that correctly use `collection:` exercise the validation, while the 18 tests using the stale `entity:` key silently skip it. After renaming ANY yaml tag: (a) grep ALL test files for the old YAML key name scoped to the renamed struct's context — e.g. `grep -rn 'entity:' --include='*_test.go'` when renaming the `entity` key on `Fill` or `SlotDeclaration`; (b) for each match, determine whether it creates YAML content that deserializes into the renamed struct type; (c) update every matching fixture to use the new key with an appropriate value (e.g. `entity: Widget` → `collection: widgets` where `widgets` is a collection name defined in the test's service declaration); (d) after updating, verify that the updated fixtures exercise the validation paths that depend on the renamed field being populated — run the tests and confirm they still pass with the new key, and if any test now fails, it reveals a gap that was previously hidden; (e) apply fix-class exhaustion (item 33): after finding stale fixtures for one renamed key, grep for ALL yaml tags that were renamed in the same task and verify each one's test fixtures are updated. **Mandatory audit step:** after renaming any yaml tag, run `grep -rn '<old_key>:' --include='*_test.go'` from the repo root. Every match is a candidate for a stale fixture. The count of matches is the upper bound on the number of tests that may be silently exercising the wrong code path. If the count is high (e.g. 18 matches), the coverage gap is systemic — the validation added for the new field is tested by only the few fixtures that happened to be written after the rename, not by the majority that predate it. **The principle: a yaml tag rename is invisible to the test suite. Tests that were correct before the rename continue to compile, run, and pass — they just silently test a different scenario than their names suggest. The Go compiler cannot catch this because the YAML key is a string, not a type. The test framework cannot catch this because yaml.v3 does not error on unknown keys. The only defense is a manual grep audit after every yaml tag rename. This item makes that audit a mandatory step.**
   155. **Validation rules must be enforced on ALL code paths that consume the validated data, not just `stego validate`:** When a validation rule is added to `Validate()` in validate.go (the `stego validate` path), the implementer MUST verify that the same invariant is also enforced on the `Reconcile()` path (used by `stego plan` and `stego apply`). `Reconcile()` does NOT call `Validate()` — it parses service.yaml independently and passes the data directly to generators. A validation rule that only exists in `Validate()` is a best-effort advisory check, not an enforced invariant. If a generator or assembler depends on the invariant (e.g. `ScopeField()` assumes the scope map has at most one entry), the invariant MUST be enforced before the generator runs in ALL code paths — not just the one the user happens to invoke first. A common error pattern: the verifier identifies a non-determinism concern (e.g. multi-field scopes produce non-deterministic accessor results). The implementer adds a validation rule to `validateCollectionReferences()` in validate.go that rejects multi-field scopes. The verifier's review checks that the validation exists and has a test — but the test runs through `Validate()`, not through `Reconcile()`. A user running `stego plan` directly (without first running `stego validate`) bypasses the guard, and the generator produces non-deterministic output. After adding ANY validation rule that guards against data that would cause incorrect generator/assembler behavior: (a) determine whether the invariant is consumed by code in the `Reconcile()` path (generators, assembler, slot wiring); (b) if yes, add the same check to the `Reconcile()` path — either by calling the relevant validation function from within `Reconcile()` before generators run, or by adding the check to the generator's own validation functions; (c) write a test that exercises the invariant through the `Reconcile()` path (not just `Validate()`), confirming that invalid input is rejected with a clear error; (d) if the validation is purely advisory (e.g. style warnings that don't affect code generation correctness), it can remain in `Validate()` only — but document this explicitly. **The principle: `stego validate` and `stego plan` are independent entry points. Users are not required to run `validate` before `plan`. Any invariant that generators depend on must be enforced in BOTH paths, or the generator's correctness guarantee is conditional on the user's workflow — a fragile contract that will eventually be violated.**
   156. **When a one-to-one mapping becomes many-to-one, all identifiers must be updated to use the new disambiguating key:** When a task changes a data model from one-to-one (one expose block per entity, identified by entity name) to many-to-one (many collections per entity, identified by collection name), EVERY code path that uses the old identifier as a disambiguator must be updated to use the new identifier. The old identifier becomes a shared attribute, not a unique key — using it in diagnostics is ambiguous when two instances of the new concept reference the same old concept. Four categories of references must be audited: **(a) Error message format arguments:** `fmt.Errorf("collection for %q...", eb.Entity)` must become `fmt.Errorf("collection %q...", eb.Name)` — even when the error string text already uses correct terminology ("collection"), the format argument determines which identifier the user sees. A validation error that identifies the entity name is unactionable when two collections reference the same entity. **(b) Parameter names:** A function parameter named `entityName` that receives `eb.Name` (a collection name) is misleading — rename to `collectionName` and update the doc comment. **(c) Doc comments on functions whose semantics changed:** A doc comment saying "collects parameters for a specific entity" when the function now operates on collections is stale. A `Generate` function doc comment saying "one per exposed entity" when the implementation generates one per collection is stale. **(d) All string literals referencing the old concept** (already covered by item 151 — this item covers the cases item 151 misses: format arguments and parameter names). After implementing any task that changes a mapping cardinality (1:1 → many:1): (i) identify the old identifier (e.g. `eb.Entity`) and the new identifier (e.g. `eb.Name`); (ii) grep every `fmt.Errorf` and error-construction call in the modified module for the old identifier as a format argument — e.g. `grep -n 'eb\.Entity' generator.go` filtered to error-formatting calls — and verify each hit uses the correct identifier for disambiguation; (iii) for each hit, determine whether the error needs the old identifier (for factual accuracy about which entity is involved) or the new identifier (for actionable disambiguation of which collection to fix) — in validation functions, the answer is almost always the new identifier because the user needs to know which configuration entry to edit; (iv) grep for the old identifier in parameter names and doc comments (per item 151 Phases 2-3); (v) apply fix-class exhaustion (item 33): once one validation function is found to use the old identifier in its error, check ALL validation functions in the module — the pattern "format argument uses `eb.Entity` where `eb.Name` is correct" will repeat in every validation function that was ported from entity-based to collection-based without updating the error identifiers. Write a test with two instances of the new concept referencing the same old concept (e.g. two collections referencing entity "User" with different scopes, where one collection has a validation error) and verify the error message uniquely identifies the problematic instance by collection name — not by entity name. The test proves the error is actionable: the user can read the error, find the named collection in their `collections:` block, and fix it without guessing which of the N collections sharing that entity is the problem. **The principle: when a mapping changes from 1:1 to many:1, the old identifier that was coincidentally unique becomes genuinely ambiguous. An error that identifies a configuration item by its shared attribute ("entity User") instead of its unique identity ("collection org-users") is unactionable — the user cannot determine which entry to fix. Error messages must always use the finest-grained identity that uniquely identifies the item within its parent scope. This is the error-message analog of a database choosing the right primary key: when the cardinality changes, the key must change too.**
   157. **Post-migration terminology grep verification — mechanical enforcement of items 151 and 156:** After any task that migrates terminology (e.g., "expose" → "collection", "entity" → "collection on slot bindings"), run the following explicit grep audit before marking `ready-for-review`. **This audit MUST be re-run after EVERY `needs-revision` cycle, not just the initial task completion.** When a `needs-revision` finding identifies stale terminology and you fix it, the fix itself may be incomplete — fixing one naming component of a construct (e.g. a parameter name) while leaving other components stale (e.g. the function name, the struct name, the test name) is a partial fix that guarantees another review round. Re-running this full audit after each fix cycle is the only way to catch partial fixes before the verifier does. This item prescribes a PROCEDURE; items 151 and 156 describe the PRINCIPLE. The principle can be partially applied; this grep is a single exhaustive check that catches anything the phased analysis missed. **(a) Enumerate every old term** that the task replaces — type names, field names, YAML keys, and user-facing nouns (e.g. for an expose→collection migration: `Expose`, `expose`, `entity` on slot bindings). **(b) For each old term, run `grep -rn '<old_term>' --include='*.go'` scoped to every file modified in the task** (use `git diff --name-only` to get the file list). **(c) For each hit, classify it using TYPE-AWARE analysis:** (i) function or method name containing the old term — rename to use the new term; (ii) `fmt.Errorf` or error-construction call using the old identifier as a format argument (e.g. `eb.Entity` where `eb.Name` is correct) — update the format argument; (iii) comment or doc comment referencing the old concept — update the text; (iv) variable or parameter name using the old term — rename; (v) **struct or type name** using the old term for a construct that now operates on the new concept — rename (e.g. `entitySlotParam` → `collectionSlotParam` when the struct describes a slot parameter scoped to a collection, not an entity); (vi) **test function name** containing the old term — rename to use the new term (e.g. `TestGenerate_EmptyExpose` → `TestGenerate_EmptyCollections`, `TestEntityBasePath_*` → `TestCollectionBasePath_*`); (vii) legitimate reference to the old concept in a different context (e.g. the entity type system, which is NOT being renamed) — skip. **TYPE-AWARE classification is mandatory for category (vii):** when deciding whether a hit is "legitimate," check the TYPE SIGNATURE of the parameter, variable, or function — if a parameter is typed as `[]types.Collection` but named `expose`, it is unambiguously stale regardless of whether "expose" is a legitimate concept elsewhere in the codebase. A function that accepts `types.Collection` as its first argument but is named `entityBasePath` is using the old terminology for the new concept. The type signature resolves ambiguity that the name alone cannot. **(d) After updating all hits, re-run the same grep and verify zero hits remain in categories (i)–(vi).** **(e) Naming-unit completeness check:** After fixing ANY naming issue on one component of a code construct, verify ALL naming components of that construct are also updated. A "naming unit" is the set of identifiers that describe a single code construct: function name + parameter names + return type names + doc comment + local variable names + associated struct/type names + test function names. When a review finding identifies a stale parameter name within a function and you rename the parameter, you MUST also check: Is the function name itself stale? Is the struct type used by the function stale? Are the test names for this function stale? A fix that renames `entityName` to `collectionName` inside `collectEntitySlotParams` but does not rename the function to `collectCollectionSlotParams` or the struct `entitySlotParam` to `collectionSlotParam` is an incomplete naming-unit fix — the very next review round will find the remaining components. The cost of checking all components is seconds; the cost of missing one is a review round.** This catches the specific failure modes of Round 3 review findings: 9 functions with `expose` parameter names for `[]types.Collection` parameters, struct `entitySlotParam` that was partially fixed (parameter renamed in Round 1 but struct/function name left stale), test names like `TestGenerate_EmptyExpose` and `TestEntityBasePath_*` that use deprecated terminology, and function names like `entityBasePath` that accept `types.Collection` but are named after the old concept. The cost of this grep is minutes; the cost of missing a stale reference is a review round\.
   158. **Acceptance criteria test enumeration — mechanical enforcement of items 6 and 11:** Before marking a task `ready-for-review`, enumerate every numbered acceptance criterion in the task file. For each criterion: (a) write down the name of the test function(s) that exercise it; (b) if no test function exists, write one. This is a concrete procedure for item 6's "every requirement must be traceable to code" and item 11's "write at least one test that exercises the feature end-to-end." The failure mode this prevents: a criterion like "`stego fill create` accepts `--collection` flag" (acceptance criterion #7) is implemented in the CLI but has no test that passes `--collection` or asserts the `Collection` field is populated. The implementation is complete but unverified — the verifier will find it. After enumeration, verify: every criterion has at least one test function name next to it, and every test function actually exercises the criterion's specific surface (the flag, the output format, the error message). A criterion that names a specific CLI flag, API parameter, or behavioral output is satisfied ONLY when a test exercises that exact surface — implementing without testing leaves the criterion unverified\.
   159. **Multi-entry-point validation test coverage — explicit testing supplement to item 155:** When validation logic is reachable through multiple public entry points with different error semantics (e.g., `Validate()` returns `ValidationResult.Errors` while `Reconcile()` returns a hard `error` that aborts plan generation), each entry point MUST have its own test — even when the underlying validation function is shared. A test through `Validate()` proves the validation function works; it does NOT prove that `Reconcile()` calls it, handles its return value correctly, or aborts processing on failure. Item 155 covers enforcement (the validation must exist in both paths); this item covers testing (both paths must have independent test coverage). After adding validation logic to any function: (a) enumerate every public function that transitively calls it (at minimum: `Validate()` and `Reconcile()`); (b) for each public function, verify a test exercises the validation through that entry point; (c) the test must assert on the entry point's specific error semantics — a `Validate()` test asserts on `ValidationResult.Errors` content, while a `Reconcile()` test asserts that the returned `error` is non-nil and that no partial plan was produced. The failure mode this prevents: language validation is added to `Reconcile()` at `reconciler.go:128-135` and tested only through `Validate()` in `validate_test.go`. The `Reconcile()` path is untested — if the validation call is accidentally removed or the error is swallowed, no test catches it\.
   160. **Needs-revision fix scope must encompass the full class, not just the cited instances — especially for terminology:** When a `needs-revision` review cites N instances of a defect class (e.g. "4 validation functions use `eb.Entity` where `eb.Name` is correct"), the fix MUST address the ENTIRE CLASS across the codebase, not just the N cited instances. Item 33 (fix-class exhaustion) already states this principle, but terminology-migration defects have a specific failure pattern that item 33's examples do not cover: the implementor fixes the cited instances, considers the finding addressed, and does not re-run the item 157 grep audit. The result is that each review round finds a NEW subset of the same class, producing a multi-round whack-a-mole. **The mandatory procedure for terminology findings in `needs-revision`:** (a) Read the finding and identify the defect class — not the specific instances, but the PATTERN (e.g. "parameter named after old concept but typed as new concept," "function named after old concept but operating on new concept," "test named after old concept"). (b) Run the item 157 grep audit for the old term(s) mentioned in the finding, scoped to ALL files modified in the task — not just the files cited in the finding. (c) Fix ALL hits that match the defect class — not just the ones the reviewer cited. The reviewer is sampling, not enumerating; the grep IS enumerating. (d) After fixing, re-run the grep and confirm zero hits remain for the defect class. **The failure mode this prevents:** Round 1 finds 4 error messages using `eb.Entity`, the implementor fixes those 4. Round 2 finds 1 more error message and 1 function name — the implementor fixes those 2. Round 3 finds 9 parameter names, 2 struct/function names, and 4 test names — all the same class ("stale old-concept terminology on migrated constructs") that a single grep would have caught in Round 1. Each round the implementor applies spot fixes to the cited instances without running the systematic sweep that would eliminate the entire class. Three review rounds and 20+ findings could have been one round with zero terminology findings if the first fix had been accompanied by a full grep. **The principle: a review finding is a sample from a population. The fix must address the population, not the sample. The reviewer's job is to find ANY instance of a defect class; the implementor's job is to eliminate ALL instances. When the defect class is "stale terminology in modified files," `grep` is the tool that converts a sample into a census. Run it.**
   161. **User-facing documentation must be updated when user-facing concepts, formats, or commands change:** When a task changes user-facing concepts (YAML configuration keys, CLI commands, conceptual nouns, examples), ALL user-facing documentation files (README.md, guides, tutorials, example service declarations) MUST be updated to reflect the new concepts. Items 6 and 158 require tracing every acceptance criterion to code and tests — but neither requires tracing to documentation. Documentation drift is a distinct failure mode: the code correctly implements the new concept, tests pass, but the README instructs users to use the old format — and the parser rejects the old format with a clear error. The user's first interaction with the project fails. **This is especially critical when the parser actively rejects the old format** (e.g. `'expose' is no longer supported; use 'collections' instead`). When the migration is destructive (the old format is a hard error), documentation using the old format is not merely stale — it is actively harmful. After implementing any task that changes user-facing concepts: (a) identify every user-facing concept that changed — YAML keys (e.g. `expose:` → `collections:`), CLI flags (e.g. new `--collection` flag), conceptual nouns (e.g. "Five nouns" → "Six nouns"), configuration examples; (b) grep all documentation files (README.md, docs/, examples/, any `.md` files outside specs/) for the old concept terms; (c) update every hit: examples must use the new format, concept descriptions must include the new nouns, command examples must use the new flags; (d) for concept inventories (e.g. "Five nouns: ..."), verify the count and list are exhaustive against the current spec; (e) run `stego validate` on every example service declaration in the documentation to verify the documented examples are valid according to the current parser. **The mandatory grep list for documentation:** after any task that renames or replaces a user-facing concept, run `grep -rn '<old_concept>' README.md docs/ examples/ --include='*.md' --include='*.yaml' --include='*.yml'` and update every hit. **The principle: documentation is the user's entry point to the project. Code-level correctness that is contradicted by documentation-level guidance produces a worse user experience than having no documentation at all — the user follows the guide, gets an error, and loses trust in both the documentation and the tool. When a migration actively rejects the old format, the documentation MUST lead with the new format. This is the documentation analog of item 33 (fix-class exhaustion): when a concept changes in code, the "same class" includes every reference to that concept in documentation.**
   162. **Terminology grep scope must be project-wide for migration tasks, not limited to task-modified files:** Item 157 scopes its grep audit to "every file modified in the task" (determined by `git diff --name-only`). This scope is insufficient for terminology migration tasks where the old terminology may exist in files that the task did NOT modify — files written by prior tasks that used the old terminology and were never touched by the migration task. The failure mode: a test file (`assembler_test.go`) written during a prior task contains comments using the old terminology ("no expose → no wiring"). The migration task does not modify this file (it modifies `assembler.go` but the test file's code still works because it tests behavior, not terminology). The old comment survives the migration because item 157's grep scope excludes unmodified files. **The fix:** for tasks that explicitly migrate terminology (as identified by task descriptions containing phrases like "update all...to work with named collections instead of expose blocks," "rename expose/entity terminology to collection throughout," or any task whose description says to update terminology across the codebase), item 157's grep audit scope MUST be expanded to the ENTIRE module (e.g. `grep -rn '<old_term>' --include='*.go'` from the repository root), not just task-modified files. The rationale: a terminology migration task's scope is "all references to the old term in the codebase," which is not bounded by the set of files the task happened to modify in Go source code. A comment in an unmodified test file is still a stale reference. **Additionally, the scope must include non-Go files:** when the old terminology appears in documentation (README.md, specs, guides), YAML examples, or any other file type, those are also stale references that belong to the migration task's scope. Run `grep -rn '<old_term>' --include='*.go' --include='*.md' --include='*.yaml' --include='*.yml'` from the repository root. **The audit cost** for a project-wide grep is seconds. The cost of a missed reference is a review round. **The principle: a terminology migration task has implicit scope — "all references to the old term." The explicit scope (files modified by the task) is a strict subset of the implicit scope. When item 157 uses `git diff --name-only` to determine scope, it optimizes for non-migration tasks where the old term is unlikely to appear in unmodified files. For migration tasks, this optimization produces false negatives. The scope selection must match the task type: non-migration tasks use modified-file scope; migration tasks use project-wide scope.**
   164. **Checked-in generated output must be regenerated when generators change:** When a task modifies code generators (output format, file naming, routing, wiring, identifiers, struct names, operation IDs, or any aspect of what the generator produces), ALL checked-in generated-output directories (e.g. `examples/*/out/`) MUST be regenerated by running the tool against the example inputs and replacing the old output. Checked-in generated output serves as the MVP demonstration and the first artifact a user encounters after the Quick Start — if it was produced by a prior version of the generator, it contradicts the current tool's behavior and misleads users who compare their own output against the example. Item 161 covers documentation text (README, guides, concept descriptions); this item covers **generated artifacts checked into the repository as reference examples**. The failure mode is distinct: documentation text can be manually edited to match the new concepts, but generated output must be PRODUCED by the current generator — manual editing is insufficient because the output must be identical to what the tool actually generates. After implementing any task that modifies a code generator's output: (a) enumerate every directory in the repository that contains checked-in generated output (grep for `out/` directories under `examples/`, or any directory documented as containing reference output); (b) for each such directory, run the tool (`stego apply` or equivalent) against the example's input (`service.yaml`) using the current generators; (c) replace the entire contents of the output directory with the freshly generated output; (d) verify the regenerated output reflects the changes made by the task — handler file names, struct names, route patterns, slot wiring, OpenAPI operation IDs, and all other generator-produced identifiers must match the current generator's behavior; (e) if the example's `service.yaml` itself needs updating to use new configuration syntax (as covered by item 161), update the input FIRST, then regenerate the output. **The mandatory check:** after any generator modification, run `ls examples/*/out/ 2>/dev/null` (or equivalent) to find checked-in output directories. If any exist, they MUST be regenerated. A generator change without a corresponding output regeneration guarantees the example output will be caught as stale in the next review round. **The principle: checked-in generated output is a snapshot of the generator's behavior at a point in time. When the generator changes, the snapshot is stale by definition — it shows what the tool USED to produce, not what it produces now. Unlike documentation text (which describes behavior), generated output IS behavior — it must be produced by the current tool, not maintained by hand. The cost of regeneration is one command; the cost of stale output is user confusion and a review finding.**
   163. **Acceptance-criteria tests must assert on the LITERAL values in the AC — not on implementation-derived values:** Item 158 requires enumerating every acceptance criterion and writing a test for each. This item adds a constraint on the test's assertions: when an AC specifies a LITERAL value (a specific path like `/organizations/{org_id}/users`, a specific error message, a specific output format), the test MUST assert on that EXACT literal value — not on whatever value the implementation happens to produce. The failure mode: AC #2 says `org-users` with `scope: { org_id: Organization }` → `/organizations/{org_id}/users`, with the path parameter `{org_id}` matching the scope field name. The implementation derives the parameter name from the parent entity name (`{organization_id}` instead of `{org_id}`). The test asserts on `r.PathValue("organization_id")` — matching the implementation rather than the spec. The test passes, the implementation appears correct, but both disagree with the AC. **The mandatory procedure:** when writing a test for an AC that contains a literal example value: (a) copy the EXACT literal value from the AC into the test's expected-value constant (e.g. `expected := "/organizations/{org_id}/users"`); (b) assert the implementation's output matches the AC's literal, not the other way around; (c) if the implementation produces a different value, the implementation is wrong — fix the implementation, not the test. A test that is derived FROM the implementation rather than FROM the spec is a tautology — it can only confirm the implementation does what it does, not that it does what it should. **The principle: an acceptance criterion with a literal example is a specification, not a suggestion. The AC says `{org_id}` — not `{organization_id}`, not `{org_identifier}`, not "a parameter derived from the parent entity name." When the AC is specific, the test must be equally specific, and the specificity must trace to the AC, not to the code. The test is a bridge between the spec and the implementation — if the test is built from the implementation's side, it cannot detect disagreements with the spec's side. Build tests from the spec side.**
   165. **Generated interface contracts must exactly match spec-defined method signatures — names, parameters, AND behavioral semantics:** When the spec or task defines an interface contract with named methods and their signatures (e.g. a DAO layer with `Create(ctx, entity)`, `Get(ctx, id)`, `Replace(ctx, entity)`, `Delete(ctx, id)`, `List(ctx, listArgs)`, `Upsert(ctx, entity, upsertKey, concurrency)`, `Exists(ctx, id)`), the generated interface MUST match on THREE dimensions: **(a) Method names** must be verbatim from the spec — `Get` not `Read`, `Replace` not `Update`. Item 4 covers type and method naming but historically has been applied more rigorously to type names than to method names on generated interfaces. This item makes the method-name requirement explicit for generated API contracts. **(b) Method parameters** must include every parameter the spec defines, with appropriate Go types. If the spec shows `ctx` as the first parameter, the generated method MUST accept `context.Context` — this is not optional. Context propagation is required for observability (OpenTelemetry trace propagation), cancellation, and timeout enforcement. Omitting `context.Context` from a database access layer makes it impossible to wire tracing, cancellation, or request-scoped values through the data path. **(c) Behavioral semantics** specified alongside the method signature must be implemented, not just the method signature itself. If the spec says `List(ctx, listArgs) — g2.Offset(offset).Limit(limit).Find(&list) with pagination`, the implementation must support pagination parameters (offset, limit) — a `List` that returns all records without pagination violates the spec even though its name is correct. After writing any generator that produces interface methods or DAO functions: (a) copy the EXACT method list from the spec or task into a verification checklist; (b) for each method, verify the generated name matches verbatim; (c) for each method, verify every parameter from the spec is present with the correct Go type (e.g. `ctx` → `context.Context`, `listArgs` → a struct with pagination fields); (d) for each method, verify any behavioral semantics described alongside the signature are implemented (e.g. pagination, soft delete, upsert conflict handling); (e) write a test for each behavioral semantic — e.g. a test that calls `List` with offset and limit and verifies the results are bounded. **The principle: a generated interface is a contract. A contract that uses different names, omits parameters, or drops behavioral guarantees is a different contract — even if it "does the same thing." The spec's method names are chosen deliberately to match the archetype's conventions (e.g. rh-trex/hyperfleet-api uses `Get`/`Replace`, not `Read`/`Update`). Renaming them breaks interoperability with existing code that depends on the convention.**
   166. **Generated resource acquisition must have corresponding lifecycle management (open → close):** When a generator produces code that acquires a resource (database connection, file handle, network socket, gRPC connection, HTTP client, message queue consumer), the generated code MUST include the corresponding cleanup mechanism — typically `defer resource.Close()` or a shutdown hook. A resource that is opened but never closed is a resource leak that degrades the generated application over time. **For database connections specifically:** when the generated code opens a connection via an ORM (e.g. `gorm.Open(...)`) or `sql.Open(...)`, it MUST retrieve the underlying closeable resource and defer its closure. For GORM, the pattern is `sqlDB, _ := db.DB(); defer sqlDB.Close()`. For raw `sql.DB`, the pattern is `defer db.Close()`. **When parallel code paths exist for the same resource type (e.g. a raw SQL setup function AND a GORM setup function), verify BOTH paths include cleanup** — it is a common error to add cleanup to the original path but omit it when writing a new path for a different backend. After writing any generator that produces resource-acquisition code: (a) grep the generated output for every `Open`, `Dial`, `Connect`, `NewClient`, or similar acquisition call; (b) for each acquisition, verify a corresponding `Close`, `Disconnect`, or `Shutdown` exists — either via `defer` in the same function or via a returned cleanup function or shutdown hook; (c) when adding a new acquisition path alongside an existing one (e.g. adding GORM alongside raw SQL), diff the two paths and verify the new path includes every lifecycle step the original path has; (d) write a test that verifies the generated code includes the cleanup call — either by asserting on the generated source text or by running the generated code in a test and verifying the resource is released. **The principle: resource acquisition without cleanup is a generator bug, not a user concern. The user trusts that generated code follows best practices — a generated application that leaks database connections on every restart erodes that trust. Every `Open` needs a `Close`; verify this mechanically, not by inspection.**
   167. **Generated infrastructure must be invoked in the application lifecycle — registration without invocation is dead code:** When a generator produces infrastructure code (migration registration, middleware registration, service registration, health check registration, background worker registration) alongside the corresponding invocation mechanism, the generated application MUST actually INVOKE the infrastructure at the appropriate lifecycle point. A common failure pattern: the generator produces both the registration infrastructure (e.g. `Register("001_initial", func(g2 *gorm.DB) error { ... })` and `Migrate(db)` function) AND the init-time registration calls (`func init() { Register(...) }`), but the wiring or assembler never emits a call to `Migrate(db)` in the generated `main.go`. The registration infrastructure is generated but orphaned — migrations are registered but never run, health checks are registered but never served, middleware is defined but never mounted. This is a subtle variant of checklist item 1 (dead code): the infrastructure functions ARE referenced (by `init()` and by each other), so they pass a naive "is it referenced?" check. But the entry point that activates the entire chain (`Migrate(db)` called from `main`) is missing — the infrastructure is inert. After writing any generator that produces infrastructure with a registration pattern: (a) identify the invocation function that activates the registered infrastructure (e.g. `Migrate(db)` for migrations, `router.Use(middleware)` for middleware, `healthServer.Register(check)` for health checks); (b) verify the wiring, assembler, or `main.go` generator emits a call to that invocation function at the correct lifecycle point — for migrations, this is after database connection setup and before application constructors; for middleware, this is before route registration; for health checks, this is before server start; (c) write a test that generates a complete application with the infrastructure enabled and verifies the invocation call appears in the generated `main.go` or equivalent entry point; (d) trace the lifecycle: setup → invoke infrastructure → construct application → serve. Every step must be present. If any step is missing, the downstream steps are unreachable. **The principle: registration is half of a two-part contract. `Register(...)` says "here is what to do." `Migrate(db)` says "do it now." Without the invocation, the registration is a promise without fulfillment — the infrastructure is defined but inert, and the generated application silently skips a critical lifecycle step (like running migrations). The generator must produce BOTH halves of the contract and wire them together.**
   168. **Framework-derived identifiers must not be re-implemented by the generator — defer to the framework or avoid the dependency:** When a generator targets a specific framework (e.g. GORM, Django ORM, Rails ActiveRecord, SQLAlchemy) that derives identifiers from user-provided names using its own internal logic (e.g. GORM uses `jinzhu/inflection` for table name pluralization, Rails uses `ActiveSupport::Inflector`), the generator MUST NOT re-implement the framework's derivation logic with a different algorithm. If the generated code depends on matching a framework-derived identifier (e.g. a SQL expression references a table name that the framework auto-created), the generator must either: **(a)** use the framework's own function or library to compute the identifier at generation time — import the same dependency the framework uses and call the same function; **(b)** configure the framework to use a generator-controlled name (e.g. GORM's `TableName()` method override, Django's `db_table` Meta option) so the generator is authoritative and the framework follows, not the other way around; or **(c)** avoid referencing the framework-derived identifier entirely by using a form that doesn't depend on it (e.g. unqualified column names in SQL `WHERE` clauses where the context makes the table unambiguous — in PostgreSQL's `ON CONFLICT ... DO UPDATE SET ... WHERE` clause, the unqualified column name correctly refers to the existing row without needing the table name). A generator that re-implements pluralization with a naive algorithm (e.g. `name + "s"`, or even a rule-based pluralizer that handles common suffixes but not irregular forms) will diverge from the framework's pluralization for irregular English words. For example, a naive pluralizer produces `persons`, `childs`, `gooses`, `addresss`, `statuss`, while `jinzhu/inflection` produces `people`, `children`, `geese`, `addresses`, `statuses`. The divergence produces SQL expressions that reference table names the framework never created — a runtime error that surfaces only for entities with irregular plural forms, making it intermittent and hard to reproduce. Item 63 (linguistically correct derived names) requires correct pluralization; this item goes further by requiring that when a framework is responsible for the derivation, the generator defers to the framework rather than competing with it. The issue is not just correctness of the pluralization, but *consistency* with the authoritative source. Even a "correct" pluralizer that happens to agree with the framework today may diverge when the framework updates its rules. After writing any generator that targets a framework with its own identifier-derivation logic: (a) identify every point where the generated code references a framework-derived identifier (table names, column names, association names, route patterns); (b) for each reference, determine whether the identifier was derived by the framework or by the generator — if the framework derived it, the generator must not independently re-derive it; (c) apply one of the three strategies above (use framework's function, override framework's naming, or avoid the reference); (d) write a test with an entity whose name has an irregular plural form (e.g. `Person`, `Child`, `Goose`, `Status`, `Address`) and verify the generated code either matches the framework's table name or avoids referencing it. **The principle: when two systems independently derive the same identifier from the same input using different algorithms, they will eventually disagree. The disagreement produces a latent runtime error that only surfaces for inputs where the algorithms diverge (irregular forms). The fix is not to make the algorithms agree — it is to ensure only ONE system derives the identifier, and the other either uses the derivation directly or avoids depending on it. In the generator-framework relationship, the framework is authoritative because it creates the actual database objects; the generator must follow or sidestep, never compete.**
   169. **New wiring expression categories must receive all existing expression-processing treatments — audit on addition:** When adding a new expression category to the wiring metadata (e.g. `PostDBCalls` alongside existing `Constructors` and `Routes`), the implementer MUST audit every processing step the assembler applies to existing expression categories and determine whether the new category requires the same treatment. Expression categories in the wiring metadata are opaque strings that reference package-qualified identifiers (e.g. `storage.Migrate(db)`). The assembler applies several processing steps to these strings before emitting them into generated code: **(a)** import alias disambiguation — when two imports share the same `path.Base`, one is renamed (e.g. `storage` → `storage2`), and all expressions referencing the original alias must be updated (item 69); **(b)** constructor variable renaming — when derived variable names collide, they are disambiguated, and route expressions that reference those variables must be updated (item 89); **(c)** word-boundary-safe replacement — identifier renames must match at word boundaries, not substrings (item 70). When a new expression category is added without receiving these treatments, it becomes a latent compilation failure: the expression references an import alias that was renamed during disambiguation, producing a reference to a non-existent or wrong package in the generated code. The failure is invisible in simple tests (where no disambiguation occurs) and only surfaces when two components share a `path.Base`, triggering alias renaming — at which point the new expression category references the pre-disambiguation alias while all other expressions use the post-disambiguation alias. **The mandatory audit procedure when adding a new wiring expression field:** (a) grep the assembler for every function that processes existing expression categories — search for `replaceAliasInExpr`, `applyVarRenames`, `ImportRenames`, `importRenames`, and any function that takes a `Wiring` or constructor/route expression as input and transforms it; (b) for each processing function found, determine whether the new expression category is subject to the same transformations — if the new expression references package-qualified identifiers (i.e. contains `package.Function(...)` syntax), it IS subject to import alias renames; if it references constructor variable names, it IS subject to variable renames; (c) add the new expression category to each applicable processing function — e.g. pass `imports.ImportRenames` into the function that emits the new expression and call `replaceAliasInExpr` on each expression before emission; (d) write a test where the new expression's package alias is disambiguated (e.g. two components with the same `path.Base` import, where one produces the new expression) and verify the generated code uses the disambiguated alias in the new expression. **This is the new-field analog of item 33 (fix-class exhaustion):** item 33 says "same bug, different site"; this item says "same treatment, new category." When you add a new expression to the wiring, it inherits all the obligations of existing expressions — it is a package-qualified opaque string that lives in a context where aliases may be renamed. Every processing step that existing expressions receive exists because opaque strings in that context need it. The new expression is in the same context and needs the same steps. **The heuristic:** after adding any new field to `gen.Wiring` or the assembler's input, search the assembler for every `for ... range wirings` loop that processes existing wiring fields and ask: "should this loop also process my new field?" If the answer is "maybe," the answer is "yes." **The principle: wiring expression processing steps exist because opaque strings containing package-qualified identifiers require post-processing when the package aliases they reference are subject to disambiguation. This requirement is inherent to the expression's nature (an opaque string with package references), not to its specific purpose (constructor vs. route vs. post-DB call). Any new opaque string added to the wiring metadata inherits the same requirement by virtue of being the same kind of data. The audit must happen at addition time — not after a reviewer discovers the omission.**
   171. **Framework auto-behaviors tied to naming conventions must be explicitly configured when spec names diverge:** When generated code targets a framework that provides auto-behaviors triggered by field naming conventions (e.g. GORM auto-populates `CreatedAt` and `UpdatedAt` timestamps, Django auto-sets `created_at`/`updated_at`, Rails timestamps convention), and the spec defines fields with the SAME semantic purpose but DIFFERENT names (e.g. `CreatedTime` instead of `CreatedAt`), the generator MUST add explicit configuration to enable the auto-behavior for the non-conventional names. Framework auto-behaviors are typically convention-based — they activate when a field has a specific name and silently do nothing when the name doesn't match. This silence is the danger: the generated code compiles, the struct looks correct, the field exists with the right type, but the framework never populates it. Every record gets zero-value timestamps (`0001-01-01T00:00:00Z` for `time.Time`) with no error, no warning, and no indication that the feature is not working. **For GORM specifically:** `CreatedAt` triggers `autoCreateTime` behavior, `UpdatedAt` triggers `autoUpdateTime` behavior. If the spec names are `CreatedTime` and `UpdatedTime`, the generator must add `gorm:"autoCreateTime"` and `gorm:"autoUpdateTime"` struct tags respectively — or set the fields explicitly in the store methods before the GORM write call. **The general pattern:** (a) identify every framework auto-behavior the generated code relies on (auto-timestamps, auto-increment, soft-delete, default values, validation hooks); (b) for each auto-behavior, determine the naming convention that activates it — read the framework's documentation, not just the spec; (c) compare the spec's field names against the framework's expected names; (d) when names diverge, add explicit configuration (struct tags, annotations, method overrides) to enable the auto-behavior for the spec's names; (e) write a test that verifies the auto-behavior is activated — e.g., create a record and assert that the timestamp fields are populated with non-zero values. **The audit trigger:** whenever the spec says "following the [framework] pattern" but defines field names that differ from the framework's conventions, EVERY auto-behavior the framework provides must be checked for convention dependency. The spec saying "following the GORM pattern" with non-GORM field names is a contradiction that the implementer must resolve by adding explicit configuration — not by assuming the spec names magically trigger the framework's conventions. **The principle: framework auto-behaviors are opt-in by convention. A field that looks like a timestamp but doesn't have the conventional name is NOT opted in. The framework doesn't know the spec's intentions — it only knows its own conventions. When the spec and the framework disagree on names, the generator must bridge the gap with explicit configuration. Silence from the framework (no error, no population) is the symptom of a missing bridge, not evidence that the feature is working.**
   172. **Attribute-to-annotation mapping functions must exhaustively cover ALL IR attributes in their domain — enumerate at the function boundary:** When a generator contains a function whose purpose is to map data-model attributes to framework-specific annotations (e.g. `buildGormTag` maps `types.Field` attributes to GORM struct tags, `fieldToOpenAPISchema` maps field constraints to OpenAPI properties, `columnDefinition` maps field attributes to SQL column modifiers), the function MUST handle EVERY attribute on the input type that falls within its domain — not just the subset the implementer happened to think of. Items 35 and 66 state this principle at the generator level ("enumerate ALL field-level attributes," "grep the generator source for each responsible field name"). This item makes the mandate concrete and mechanical at the FUNCTION level: the mapping function is the narrowest scope where exhaustiveness can be verified, and it is the scope where violations are easiest to detect and prevent. **The mandatory audit procedure when writing or modifying an attribute-to-annotation mapping function:** (a) open the definition of the input type (e.g. `types.Field` in `types/types.go`); (b) list EVERY field on the input type that represents a constraint, attribute, or behavioral flag (e.g. `Unique`, `UniqueComposite`, `Optional`, `Computed`, `MinLength`, `MaxLength`, `Pattern`, `Default`, `Min`, `Max`, `Values`, `To`, `Immutable`); (c) for each field, determine whether it is within the mapping function's domain — a GORM tag builder is responsible for `Unique` (→ `uniqueIndex`), `UniqueComposite` (→ `uniqueIndex:composite_name`), `MaxLength` (→ `size:N`), `MinLength` (→ `check:length(col) >= N`), `Optional` (→ nullable), etc.; (d) verify the mapping function contains a code path for each in-domain attribute — if an attribute is not referenced, the feature it represents is silently dropped from the generated output; (e) when adding a NEW mapping function for a different framework (e.g. adding GORM tags alongside existing SQL column definitions), diff the new function's attribute coverage against the old function's — every attribute the old function handles should be handled by the new function, possibly with different output but with equivalent coverage; (f) write a test with an entity that populates EVERY in-domain attribute and verify the mapping function's output reflects ALL of them. **The specific failure patterns this prevents:** (i) `buildGormTag` handles `f.Unique` (→ `gorm:"uniqueIndex"`) but ignores `f.UniqueComposite` (→ should emit `gorm:"uniqueIndex:composite_<name>"` on each participating field); (ii) `buildGormTag` handles `f.MaxLength` (→ `gorm:"size:N"`) but ignores `f.MinLength` (→ should emit `gorm:"check:length(column) >= N"` via GORM's CHECK constraint tag); (iii) a new OpenAPI schema mapper handles `f.MaxLength` (→ `maxLength: N`) but ignores `f.Pattern` (→ `pattern: <regex>`). In each case, the function handles SOME attributes of the input type but not ALL — the unhandled attributes are silently dropped. **When migrating from one backend to another (e.g. raw SQL → GORM):** the migration creates a new mapping function that replaces an existing one. The new function must cover at least the same attributes as the old function — otherwise the migration silently degrades features. After writing any replacement mapping function: (a) open the old function and list every attribute it handles; (b) verify the new function handles each one — the output format will differ (SQL `CHECK` constraint vs GORM `check:` tag) but the INPUT coverage must be equivalent; (c) write a test that exercises each attribute through the new function and verify the output. **The principle: a mapping function is a commitment to translate ALL attributes in its domain from one representation to another. A function that maps 8 of 10 attributes is not "almost complete" — it is silently dropping 2 features. The missing mappings produce generated code that compiles and runs but lacks constraints the spec requires, making the generated application less safe than the spec intends. The function boundary is the EASIEST place to audit for completeness because the input type is known, the output format is known, and the gap can be identified by diffing the input type's fields against the function's code paths.**
   170. **Generated API handlers must use spec-defined external parameters, not internal implementation parameters:** When the spec defines API-level query parameters with specific names, defaults, constraints, and conversion logic (e.g. `page` and `size` with defaults `page=1, size=100`, maximum `size=65500`, and conversion `offset = (page-1)*size, limit = size`), the generated handler MUST parse the spec-defined parameter names from the HTTP request and apply the spec-defined conversions internally — not expose the underlying implementation-level parameters (e.g. `offset`/`limit`) directly to the API consumer. Exposing internal parameters violates the API contract in three ways: **(a)** the parameter names don't match the spec, so clients using the spec (or the generated OpenAPI document) as documentation will construct requests with wrong parameter names and get default behavior instead of the pagination they requested — a silent failure; **(b)** the defaults and constraints the spec defines (e.g. `page` defaults to 1, `size` defaults to 100, `size` clamped to 65500) are not applied, leaving the API unbounded — a client that omits pagination parameters gets every row; **(c)** the conversion logic is pushed to the API consumer, who must compute `offset = (page-1)*size` themselves — breaking the abstraction boundary between the API contract and the storage implementation. This is the query-parameter-specific instantiation of item 39 (bidirectional spec/code consistency). Item 39's Pass 2 (code→spec) would catch this: the handler reads `offset` and `limit` from query parameters, but the spec declares `page` and `size` — a code→spec mismatch. However, the failure mode is specific enough and common enough to warrant its own checklist item because it involves a TRANSFORMATION between the API-level parameters and the storage-level parameters, not just a naming difference. The handler is the translation layer — it accepts the API's vocabulary (`page`, `size`) and converts to the storage's vocabulary (`offset`, `limit`). Omitting this translation layer conflates two abstraction levels. After writing any generator that emits HTTP handler code for operations with query parameters: (a) re-read the spec section that defines the operation's query parameters — specifically look for parameter names, default values, maximum/minimum constraints, and conversion formulas; (b) verify the generated handler parses the EXACT parameter names the spec defines (e.g. `r.URL.Query().Get("page")`, not `r.URL.Query().Get("offset")`); (c) verify the handler applies all spec-defined defaults when parameters are absent (e.g. `page` defaults to 1, `size` defaults to 100); (d) verify the handler applies all spec-defined constraints (e.g. `size` clamped to max 65500, `page` minimum 1); (e) verify the handler performs the spec-defined conversion before passing values to the storage layer (e.g. `offset = (page-1)*size`); (f) write a test that asserts the generated handler code parses the spec-defined parameter names (not implementation-level names), includes default-value logic, includes clamping logic, and includes the conversion formula; (g) apply item 39 bidirectionally: verify the generated OpenAPI spec declares `page` and `size` as query parameters with the spec-defined defaults and constraints, AND verify the handler implements those exact parameters — not different ones. **The principle: the HTTP handler is a boundary between two abstraction levels — the API consumer's vocabulary (page, size) and the storage layer's vocabulary (offset, limit). The spec defines the API-level vocabulary precisely because it is the public contract. When the handler exposes the storage-level vocabulary directly, it leaks an implementation detail that cannot be changed without breaking the API. The handler must translate — accepting the public vocabulary, applying the public contract's constraints, and converting to the private vocabulary internally. This is the same principle as item 49 (configurable parameters must be used everywhere), applied to the API boundary: the spec-defined parameters are the "configuration," and the handler must use them, not bypass them with hardcoded internals.**
   173. **Computed restriction sets must actually constrain the framework API calls they govern — an unused restriction is a silent spec violation:** When a generated method computes a restriction set — a whitelist of columns to write (`writeCols`), a blacklist of fields to exclude (`computedFields`), a set of allowed operations, or any derived subset that limits what a downstream API call should affect — the API call MUST consume that restriction set via the framework's column/field selection mechanism. Computing a restriction set and then calling an unrestricted API nullifies the restriction: the code "knows" which columns should be written but ignores that knowledge. **The canonical failure pattern:** a Replace/Update method computes `writeCols` (non-computed columns eligible for update), then calls `db.Save(&entity)` instead of `db.Model(&entity).Select(writeCols).Updates(&entity)`. GORM's `Save` performs a full-column update of ALL fields — including computed fields that were intentionally zeroed. The restriction set exists as a local variable, the spec says "computed fields excluded from update," but the API call overwrites computed columns with zeros because `Save` ignores column restrictions. The data was correct (computed fields zeroed), the restriction was correct (`writeCols` lists only writable columns), but the API call was wrong (full update instead of selective update) — and the restriction set became dead code. **This applies beyond GORM to any framework with both "write everything" and "write selectively" API variants:** Django's `model.save()` vs `model.save(update_fields=[...])`, SQLAlchemy's `session.merge(obj)` vs `session.query(Model).filter_by(id=id).update({...})`, raw SQL's `UPDATE SET col1=$1, col2=$2` (selective) vs constructing a SET clause from ALL struct fields (unrestricted). After writing any generated method that computes a restriction set for a write operation: (a) identify the framework API call that performs the write (e.g. `db.Save`, `db.Create`, `db.Updates`); (b) determine whether the API call respects column restrictions by default — `Save` does NOT (it writes all columns), `Updates` does when combined with `Select`; (c) if the API call is unrestricted, replace it with the framework's selective-write API and pass the restriction set (e.g. `db.Model(&entity).Select(writeCols).Updates(&entity)`); (d) verify by reading the framework's documentation — do not assume the API name implies selective behavior; (e) grep the method for every local variable that was computed as a restriction set (look for patterns like `writeCols`, `updateCols`, `selectFields`, or any slice/map built by filtering the entity's field list) and verify each one is consumed by an API call or loop — a restriction set with zero consumers is dead code whose purpose has been defeated; (f) write a test that verifies computed field values are preserved through a Replace/Update cycle — create an entity, populate its computed fields via a direct DB write (simulating a fill), then call Replace via the generated code and verify the computed fields retain their values, not zeros. **The audit trigger:** whenever a method contains BOTH a restriction-set computation AND a framework write call, ask: "does this API call actually use this restriction set?" If the answer is "no," either the API call is wrong or the restriction set is dead code. There is no valid third option — a restriction set exists to restrict, and a write call exists to write. If they don't connect, the restriction is theatrical. **The principle: a restriction set that is computed but not consumed is worse than no restriction at all. Without the restriction set, the implementer might notice the gap — "I should restrict this write." With the restriction set present as a local variable, the implementer (and reviewer) may believe the restriction is enforced because the variable exists — a false sense of safety. The variable's presence masks the bug. Every restriction set must trace to an API call that consumes it; every write API call must be verified against the framework's documentation to confirm it respects restrictions. `Save` means "save everything" in every major ORM — it is never the correct API for a selective update.**
   174. **Input-accepting persistence operations must share ALL data-sanitization steps — diff method bodies pairwise as a mandatory audit:** When a generator emits multiple persistence methods that accept user-provided entity data as input (Create, Replace/Update, Upsert, Patch, Import, BulkCreate — any method that takes untrusted data and writes it to the database), EVERY data-sanitization step applied to one method MUST be evaluated for applicability to all others.
