This is a specialization of item 37 (shared semantics across operations) with a mandatory mechanical audit procedure designed to prevent the specific failure mode where upsert-specific complexity (ON CONFLICT, concurrency, write columns) dominates the implementer's attention and causes shared-with-Create semantics (computed field clearing, auto-field population, input validation) to be overlooked. **The mandatory pairwise diff procedure:** after writing ALL input-accepting persistence methods, create a written enumeration of the data-sanitization steps in each method: (a) for each method, list every step between "receive entity data" and "call the framework write API" — e.g. `Create` has: unmarshal → clear computed fields → set auto-populated fields → validate → db.Create; `Upsert` has: unmarshal → validate upsert key → build ON CONFLICT → db.Clauses(onConflict).Create; (b) **diff each pair of methods** — specifically, compare Create vs Upsert, Create vs Replace, Upsert vs Replace; (c) for every step present in one method but absent from another, justify the difference with a specific spec reason — "Create clears computed fields but Upsert does not because..." If no spec reason exists, the missing step is a bug; (d) pay special attention to the Upsert method: Upsert is a CREATE-on-insert and UPDATE-on-conflict hybrid — it inherits sanitization requirements from BOTH Create (on the insert path) and Replace (on the update path). The insert path must clear computed fields (like Create does), and the update path must restrict to writable columns (like Replace should). If Upsert has neither, it has TWO bugs — one from each parent operation. **Specific data-sanitization steps that MUST be cross-verified across ALL input-accepting methods:** (i) **computed field clearing** — any operation that accepts user input can receive values for computed fields; the operation must zero these before the write to prevent user-provided values from overwriting fill-managed data; (ii) **auto-populated field setting** — if Create sets `CreatedTime` or `UpdatedTime`, Upsert's insert path must too; if Replace sets `UpdatedTime`, Upsert's update path must too; (iii) **parent ref assignment from URL path** — if Create pins the parent ref from the URL after body decode (item 37), Upsert and Replace must too; (iv) **input validation** — if Create validates required fields, Upsert and Replace must too. Item 37 states this principle but relies on the implementer to voluntarily enumerate steps. The present item makes the audit mechanical: list the steps, diff pairwise, justify every difference. The diff is an artifact that can be reviewed — "did you diff Create vs Upsert?" is verifiable, while "did you consider shared semantics?" is not. **The principle: Upsert is the most dangerous operation for missed sanitization because its complexity (ON CONFLICT clause, concurrency handling, write column selection) creates a cognitive load that crowds out the simpler but equally critical steps (clearing computed fields) that it shares with Create. The implementer correctly focuses on what makes Upsert unique and overlooks what makes it similar to Create. The pairwise diff is a forcing function that makes the similarities visible — you cannot diff two method bodies without seeing that one has a step the other lacks. The cost of the diff is five minutes; the cost of the missed step is a review round. Always diff.**
   175. **Generated query methods must implement ALL spec-defined query mechanics — not just the result-fetching step:** When the spec defines a query method's behavior as a multi-step process (e.g. "Count total matching records first (SELECT COUNT(*)), then fetch page via OFFSET/LIMIT"), the generator must produce code for EVERY step, not just the final fetch. The failure mode: the spec defines List pagination as a two-step process — (1) count total matching records, (2) fetch the page — because the response envelope requires a `total` field alongside the paginated `items`. The implementer focuses on the visible output (the items list) and implements only the fetch step. The count step is omitted because the method's return type (`(any, error)`) has no slot for a total count. The omission cascades: without a count from the store, the handler cannot produce the spec-required pagination envelope, so the handler returns a bare array instead of `{kind, page, size, total, items}`. **The mandatory check for query methods:** after writing any generator that produces a query/list/search method: (a) re-read the spec section that defines the method's query mechanics — look for multi-step descriptions ("count first, then fetch," "validate orderBy fields, then apply ordering"); (b) verify every step is implemented in the generated code; (c) verify the method's return type can carry all data the spec requires in the response — if the spec requires `total` in the response, the store method must return a total count alongside the results; (d) trace the data path from the store's return value to the handler's response body and verify every spec-required response field can be populated. **The principle: a query method's implementation must match the spec's query mechanics, not just the spec's method signature. A `List` method with correct name, correct parameters, and correct pagination-via-OFFSET/LIMIT is still incomplete if the spec says "count total first" and the implementation doesn't count. The return type is a contract — if it can't carry a total count, the total is architecturally impossible, not just unimplemented. Fix the return type first, then implement the count.**
   176. **Response envelope fields must be populated from actual response data, not from request parameters:** When a spec defines a response envelope with fields that describe properties of the response payload (e.g. `size` defined as "the actual number of items returned (may be less than requested on the last page)"), the value for each such field MUST be derived from the actual response data — not echoed from the corresponding request parameter. This is a distinct failure mode from item 39 (spec/code mismatch) and item 170 (request parameter naming): even when the handler correctly parses spec-defined request parameters and the response field names match the spec, the VALUES assigned to response fields can still be wrong if they are sourced from the request instead of the response. **The classification rule:** each response envelope field falls into exactly one of two categories based on the spec's field description: **(a) Request-derived fields** — fields whose spec description references what the client requested (e.g. `page` described as "the requested page number," `kind` described as "the resource type"). These correctly echo the request parameter or a constant. **(b) Response-derived fields** — fields whose spec description references a property of the actual result (e.g. `size` described as "actual number of items returned," `total` described as "total count of matching records across all pages"). These MUST be computed from the response data after the query completes. The spec's field description is the source of truth for classification. If the description says "actual," "returned," "in the response," or "matching records," the field is response-derived. If the description says "requested," "specified," or "the [parameter] value," the field is request-derived. **The common failure pattern:** a pagination response has both `size` (requested page size, from query parameter) and `size` (actual items returned). When the spec defines `size` as the ACTUAL count, the implementer uses the query parameter value because the variable is already available, the names match, and on all pages except the last one the values are identical — making the bug invisible in most tests. The bug surfaces only on the last page, when fewer items remain than the requested page size. **The mandatory check for response envelope construction:** after writing any generator that produces response envelope code: (a) enumerate every field in the response envelope; (b) for each field, read the spec's field description and classify it as request-derived or response-derived; (c) for response-derived fields, verify the value is computed from the actual query result — e.g. `size` should be the length of the items array/slice, `total` should come from a COUNT query, not from a request parameter; (d) for response-derived fields whose value is a count or length, verify the computation uses the correct Go mechanism for the type at hand — if the items variable is typed as `any` (because the store returns a generic type), use `reflect.ValueOf(items).Len()` or add a typed length helper; (e) write a test that specifically exercises the last-page boundary condition: set up data where `total` is not evenly divisible by `size` (e.g. 103 items with `size=100`), request the last page (`page=2`), and assert that the response's `size` field equals the actual item count (3), NOT the requested page size (100). **The principle: a response envelope is a description of the response, not a mirror of the request. When a field describes a property of the response payload ("actual number of items returned"), its value must come from the response. Echoing the request parameter produces a correct value on most pages (where the actual count equals the requested size) but an incorrect value on the boundary page — the exact place where the distinction matters most. The spec's parenthetical "(may be less than requested on the last page)" exists precisely because this is a known confusion point. The test for last-page behavior is not optional — it is the ONLY test that distinguishes correct from incorrect `size` population.**
   177. **Spec-defined auto-population behaviors on generator-injected implicit fields must be explicitly implemented when the framework provides no equivalent:** When the spec defines a behavioral contract on a generator-injected implicit field (e.g. "id -- auto-generated UUID, assigned on create" on the Meta struct's `ID` field), and the target framework provides NO auto-behavior for that contract (unlike timestamps, where GORM has `autoCreateTime`/`autoUpdateTime`), the generator MUST produce code that explicitly implements the behavior. Item 171 covers the case where the framework HAS an auto-behavior but the spec's naming diverges from the convention — the fix is explicit configuration (struct tags, annotations). This item covers the complementary case: the framework has NO auto-behavior at all for the spec-required contract, so the generator must implement the behavior itself (e.g. a `BeforeCreate` GORM hook, a pre-write assignment in the store method, or inline code in the handler). The failure mode is subtle: the implementer checks whether the framework auto-populates the field, discovers it does not, and moves on — treating "the framework doesn't do this automatically" as "this doesn't need to happen." But the spec says it DOES need to happen; the framework's silence means the generator is responsible, not that the requirement is waived. **The mandatory audit procedure for generator-injected implicit fields:** after writing any generator that injects implicit fields into generated structs (e.g. `Meta` with `ID`, `CreatedTime`, `UpdatedTime`, `DeletedAt`): (a) enumerate EVERY implicit field the generator injects — grep the generator for struct field emissions that are NOT derived from the entity's declared fields (hardcoded fields like `ID string`, `CreatedTime time.Time`); (b) for each implicit field, read the spec's behavioral contract — the spec says what the field IS and what behavior it REQUIRES (e.g. "auto-generated UUID, assigned on create"); (c) for each behavioral contract, determine whether the target framework provides an auto-behavior that fulfills it — check the framework's documentation for auto-population hooks, naming conventions, or configuration options; (d) if the framework provides an auto-behavior: verify it is activated (per item 171 — add explicit configuration if naming diverges); (e) **if the framework provides NO auto-behavior:** the generator MUST produce explicit code that implements the contract — e.g. for auto-UUID, generate a `BeforeCreate` hook method on the Meta struct that assigns `m.ID = uuid.New().String()` when `m.ID` is empty, or generate inline ID assignment in the `emitCreateMethod` and `emitUpsertMethod`; add required dependencies to the generated `go.mod` (e.g. `github.com/google/uuid`); (f) apply item 174 (pairwise diff of input-accepting methods): if the auto-population applies to Create, verify it also applies to Upsert's insert path — both methods accept new entity data and both must auto-populate the ID; (g) write a test that creates an entity without providing an ID in the request body and verifies the persisted record has a non-empty, valid UUID as its ID. **The relationship between items 57, 171, and 177:** item 57 validates that user-declared field names don't collide with generator-injected implicit field names (structural concern). Item 171 ensures framework auto-behaviors are activated when spec field names diverge from framework conventions (configuration concern). Item 177 ensures behavioral contracts are fulfilled when the framework has NO auto-behavior at all (implementation concern). Together, they form a complete coverage chain for generator-injected implicit fields: collision prevention (57) → convention bridging (171) → behavior implementation (177). **The principle: when the spec says a field is "auto-generated" or "auto-populated," SOMEONE must generate/populate it. The framework is the first candidate — check its auto-behaviors and naming conventions (item 171). If the framework provides nothing, the generator is the fallback — it must produce explicit code. The worst outcome is assuming the requirement is met when neither the framework nor the generator implements it. The field exists in the struct, it has the right type, but its value is always empty/zero — a silent spec violation that compiles, passes basic tests, and produces records with missing identity data in production.**
   178. **Spec-provided input→output examples must be used as golden test cases:** When the spec or task description provides a concrete input→output example for a transformation, derivation, or computation (e.g. `hyperfleet-api` → `HYPERFLEET` for error code prefix derivation, or an example JSON showing specific field values for a specific input), tests MUST use those exact example inputs and assert those exact example outputs. The spec example IS the specification of correctness — if the implementation produces different output for the same input, the implementation is wrong, not the example. A test that uses the spec's example input but asserts a different output has encoded a bug into the test suite — the test validates the implementation against itself rather than against the spec, making it impossible for the test to catch implementation-vs-spec divergence. After writing any function that transforms, derives, or computes a value: (a) search the task description, spec excerpt, and acceptance criteria for concrete examples of that transformation's input and expected output; (b) for each example found, write a test case using the exact input value and asserting the exact output value from the spec; (c) if the test fails, fix the implementation — do NOT "fix" the expected value to match your code; (d) when a function's doc comment provides an example (e.g. `// deriveErrorPrefix converts "hyperfleet-api" to "HYPERFLEET"`), the test MUST include that exact example as a test case — a doc comment that states one transformation while the test asserts a different result is a contradiction that a spec-example test would catch immediately. **The failure mode this prevents:** the implementer writes a function, writes a doc comment with the correct example from the spec, writes the implementation with a subtle bug, then writes a test that asserts the buggy output instead of the spec-provided output. The doc comment, the spec, and the task all agree on the correct answer — but the test agrees with the wrong implementation. The spec-example test is the bridge between the spec's examples and the code's behavior. Without it, the implementation and its test can agree with each other while both disagreeing with the spec. **The principle: a test derived from the implementation is a tautology — it can only confirm the code does what it does, never that it does what it should. A test derived from the spec is a specification check — it confirms the code matches the authoritative definition. When the spec provides concrete examples, those examples are the most valuable test cases because they were written by the spec author to illustrate correct behavior. Use them verbatim.**
   179. **Serialization and output-construction functions must populate ALL fields they are responsible for — no partial population:** When a function constructs or populates a struct that will be serialized to output (JSON response body, error response, API payload, log entry, generated code struct literal), the function MUST set ALL fields on that struct that the spec or task description says should carry data at that point — not just a subset. A function that populates 5 of 7 fields is a partial implementation: the two unpopulated fields serialize as their zero values (empty string, 0, nil), silently omitting data the spec requires. This is distinct from item 1 (which catches struct fields that are NEVER assigned ANYWHERE in the codebase) — this item catches fields that ARE defined and that a specific function is responsible for populating, but that the function skips. The gap between "field exists on the struct" and "field is populated at runtime" is where this class of bug lives. After writing any function that constructs a struct for serialization or output: (a) open the struct type definition and enumerate EVERY field; (b) for each field, determine whether this function is the intended populator — consult the task description for behavioral requirements like "populates X", "sets Y from Z", "derives W from context"; (c) for each field this function should populate, verify code exists within the function that assigns a non-zero value to that field; (d) if a field is intentionally left unpopulated by this function (e.g. it is populated by the caller), add a comment explaining why; (e) when the task description uses behavioral verbs ("populates trace_id from OpenTelemetry span context when available"), the verb directly identifies both the function responsible and the field to be populated — tracing the requirement to the struct field definition alone is insufficient; the trace MUST reach code that performs the assignment within the responsible function. **The pairwise audit for sibling fields:** when a function populates multiple sibling fields on the same struct (e.g. `handleError` sets `Instance` from request path AND `Timestamp` from current time), the presence of populated siblings is a strong signal that ALL siblings in the same category (runtime-derived fields) should also be populated. After populating any field in a struct, enumerate all other fields on the struct, identify which ones share the same population pattern (runtime-derived, context-extracted, user-provided), and verify each is populated. A function that populates `Instance` and `Timestamp` but skips `TraceID` — when all three are runtime-derived fields specified in the same task description — has a partial-population bug that the sibling audit would catch. **The principle: a struct field that is defined and populated by SOME function but not by the function responsible for it is the most insidious form of incomplete implementation. The field exists (passes item 1's "is it defined?" check), the struct is instantiated (passes item 1's "is it referenced?" check), but the specific function that should fill the field doesn't — the field silently serializes as zero. The spec says "populates trace_id"; the struct has `TraceID`; but `handleError` never sets it. Every link in the chain exists except the actual assignment. After writing any output-construction function, the mandatory check is: for each field on the output struct, does THIS function set it? Not "does any function set it?" — DOES THIS FUNCTION set it?**
   180. **All construction of a typed error/struct with constructor functions must use those constructors — no inline struct literals that bypass field-population invariants:** When a type has designated constructor functions that populate all required fields (e.g. `NotFound()`, `BadRequest()`, `InternalError()` for `ServiceError` — each setting `Type`, `Title`, `Status`, `Detail`, AND `Code`), every code path that creates an instance of that type MUST use one of the constructors — never an inline struct literal (`&ServiceError{Type: "...", Title: "...", Status: sc, Detail: msg}`). An inline literal bypasses the field-population invariants that the constructors enforce: the implementer must manually populate every field, and any omitted field silently serializes as its zero value. This is distinct from item 1 (which checks that a field is populated by "at least one code path" in the entire codebase) and from item 96 (which checks that slot request structs are fully populated). This item covers the specific case where constructors exist AND correctly populate all fields, but a separate code path bypasses them by constructing the struct inline — creating a partial instance that is missing fields the constructors would have set. The most dangerous omission is fields that carry structured metadata (like error codes in `{PREFIX}-{CATEGORY}-{NUMBER}` format): the constructor computes the code from the status, but inline construction skips the computation, producing a response with an empty `code` field that violates the spec's "all error responses carry structured error codes" requirement. After writing any constructor function for a type: (a) grep the entire codebase for inline construction of that type — search for `&TypeName{` and `TypeName{` outside of the constructor definitions themselves; (b) for each inline construction site found, either replace it with a call to the appropriate constructor, or verify that every field the constructors populate is also populated in the inline literal; (c) when the inline construction site needs to derive values dynamically (e.g. mapping a status code to an error category for the `Code` field), extract a helper function or extend an existing constructor rather than duplicating the derivation logic inline; (d) write a test that exercises the inline construction code path and asserts that ALL fields (including `Code`) are non-empty in the serialized output. **The principle: constructors exist to centralize field-population logic. An inline struct literal that bypasses a constructor is a declaration that the implementer knows all the fields and will populate them correctly — but unlike the constructor, the inline literal has no tests, no doc comment listing the fields, and no mechanical enforcement. When a field is added to the type or a population rule changes, the constructors are updated but the inline literals are not — they silently degrade. The rule is simple: if constructors exist for a type, use them. If no constructor fits the use case, write a new constructor — do not construct inline.**
   181. **Storage/IO error handling must distinguish between domain errors (not found, conflict) and infrastructure errors (connection failure, timeout) — never classify all errors from a single call as one category:** When a generated handler calls a storage method that can return multiple categories of errors (e.g. `store.Get()` can return "entity not found" OR "database connection failed"), the error handling MUST distinguish between these categories and use the appropriate error constructor for each — never collapse all errors from the call into a single category. The most dangerous collapse is treating all `store.Get()` errors as "not found" (`NotFound()`): a database timeout, connection refused, or serialization error produces a 404 response with code `{PREFIX}-NTF-001` instead of a 500 response with code `{PREFIX}-INT-001`. The client receives "Not Found" and stops retrying — when the entity actually exists and the failure is transient. This is a specialization of item 21 (distinct error messages for distinct failure modes) and item 86 (error propagation, not collapse). Item 21 covers the general principle; item 86 covers collapsing errors into boolean returns. This item covers the specific pattern of misclassifying an infrastructure error as a domain error via the wrong error constructor. The pattern is particularly common in Read/Get handlers because the pre-typed-error code used `http.Error(w, err.Error(), http.StatusNotFound)` for all errors — and the migration to typed error constructors mechanically swaps `http.Error` for `NotFound()` without re-evaluating the error classification. After writing any generated handler method that calls a storage/IO method and uses a typed error constructor: (a) enumerate every error category the storage method can return — at minimum: "entity not found" (domain error, 404) and "infrastructure failure" (internal error, 500); (b) verify the generated code distinguishes between these categories — the standard pattern is: check for a sentinel or typed "not found" error (e.g. `errors.Is(err, storage.ErrNotFound)` or `errors.As(err, &NotFoundError{})`) and use `NotFound()` only for that specific case; use `InternalError()` for all other errors; (c) apply item 33 (fix-class exhaustion): after fixing the error classification in one handler method (e.g. Read), grep ALL handler methods for the same pattern — any `NotFound()` call that is not guarded by a not-found-specific error check is the same class of bug in a different method; (d) if the storage interface does not currently distinguish error categories (e.g. all errors are generic `error`), add a sentinel error (`var ErrNotFound = errors.New("not found")`) to the storage layer and return it from Get/Read when the entity does not exist — this enables correct classification at the handler level. **The principle: an error constructor is a classification decision. `NotFound()` says "the entity does not exist." `InternalError()` says "something went wrong internally." When a storage call fails, the handler MUST know WHY it failed before choosing a constructor. Using `NotFound()` as the default for all storage errors is a misclassification that produces incorrect HTTP status codes, misleading error messages, and wrong retry behavior. The handler must inspect the error, not assume the category.**
   183. **Mechanical spec/code sync audit — grep-based enforcement of item 39 for query parameters and response schemas:** Item 39 requires bidirectional consistency between generated runtime code and generated specification documents. This item prescribes a MECHANICAL PROCEDURE for the two most commonly missed sync points. **Query parameter sync:** after writing or modifying any handler code that reads query parameters (via `r.URL.Query().Get()`, `r.FormValue()`, or similar), run the following audit: (a) grep the generated handler code for EVERY `r.URL.Query()` and `r.FormValue()` call; (b) extract the parameter name from each call; (c) for each parameter name, grep the OpenAPI generation code for that parameter name in the `parameters` array construction; (d) if a handler-parsed parameter has no corresponding OpenAPI parameter declaration, add it to the OpenAPI spec — the handler reads it, so the spec must declare it. **Response schema sync:** after adding or modifying any response format (e.g. switching from bare array to envelope object, adding metadata fields to response bodies), run the following audit: (a) identify the handler's response body structure — what does `json.NewEncoder(w).Encode(...)` or `json.Marshal(...)` actually write?; (b) compare that structure against the OpenAPI response schema for the same operation; (c) if the handler writes an object with fields `{kind, page, size, total, items}` but the spec describes `type: array`, the spec is wrong — update it to match the actual response shape; (d) this audit MUST be performed for EVERY configuration variant that changes response format (e.g. `response_format: envelope` vs `bare`) — test each variant's OpenAPI output against its handler output. **The trigger:** this audit fires whenever you (i) add a new `r.URL.Query().Get()` call, (ii) add a new `r.FormValue()` call, (iii) change the structure of a `json.Encode` response, or (iv) add a conditional response format (like envelope wrapping). **The principle: item 39 describes WHAT to check; this item describes HOW to check it mechanically. The most common failure mode is not ignorance of item 39 but failure to execute its audit after incremental changes — the implementer adds a query parameter to the handler and forgets to add it to the spec, or changes the response shape and forgets to update the schema. The grep-based procedure makes the audit a concrete step that can be executed in seconds, not a principle that requires re-reading a 1000-word checklist item.**
   182. **Generated runtime URL/href construction must resolve all route template parameters — no `{param}` literals in runtime strings:** When a generator constructs code that builds URLs, hrefs, or paths at runtime for inclusion in response bodies (e.g. the `href` field in a response envelope, `Location` headers, HATEOAS links), the generated code MUST substitute every route template parameter (`{param_name}`) with the actual runtime value extracted from the request (e.g. `r.PathValue("param_name")`). Route template syntax (`{param}`) is for route REGISTRATION with Go's ServeMux — it tells the router which URL segments are variable. This syntax must NEVER appear as a literal string in runtime response construction, because the router has already parsed the actual values and they are available via `r.PathValue()`. A common error pattern: the generator computes a `hrefBase` from the route template (e.g. `/organizations/{org_id}/users`) and reuses it in the presenter function call: `presentEntity(user, "User", user.ID, "/organizations/{org_id}/users/"+user.ID)`. The `{org_id}` is a Go string literal — it is never resolved, producing the invalid href `/organizations/{org_id}/users/abc123` instead of `/organizations/actual-org-id/users/abc123`. This bug is invisible for flat (non-scoped) entities because their hrefs have no parent path parameters — only scoped/nested entities expose the pattern. After writing any generator that emits code constructing response URLs or hrefs: (a) grep the generated output for `{` followed by a word and `}` inside string literals used for href/URL construction — any match is a route template placeholder that was not resolved; (b) for each route template parameter in a scoped entity's path, verify the generated code calls `r.PathValue("paramName")` to extract the actual value and concatenates it into the href string; (c) verify the parameter name passed to `r.PathValue()` matches the parameter name in the route registration pattern — mismatches cause `PathValue` to silently return `""`; (d) write a test for EVERY nesting level: flat entities (no parent parameters), one-level scoped entities (one parent parameter), and multi-level scoped entities (multiple parent parameters in the ancestor chain) — assert that the generated href code contains `r.PathValue()` calls for each ancestor's path parameter and does NOT contain `{param}` template literals; (e) apply item 33 (fix-class exhaustion): after finding unresolved template parameters in one handler method (e.g. Create), grep ALL handler methods that construct hrefs (Create, Read, Update, Upsert) for the same pattern — any method that passes the route template to the presenter instead of substituting actual values has the same bug. **The principle: route template syntax and runtime URL construction are two distinct contexts. The template tells the router "this segment is variable." The runtime construction tells the client "here is the actual value." Conflating the two produces URLs with unresolved placeholders — syntactically valid strings that are semantically broken. The generator has access to both the template (for route registration) and `r.PathValue()` (for runtime extraction) — it must use each in its correct context.**
   184. **Conditional features must be applied exhaustively across ALL operations in BOTH runtime code AND specification documents:** When a generator applies conditional behavior gated on a configuration flag (e.g. `response_format: envelope`, `auth: enabled`) that changes the shape of generated output, the condition MUST be applied consistently to EVERY generated artifact that describes or produces that output — runtime code AND specification documents, across ALL operations\. A common failure mode: a conditional feature is correctly applied to one operation type's response schema (e.g. list gets an envelope wrapper with `kind`, `page`, `size`, `total`, `items`) but not to other operations' schemas (e.g. create, read, update, upsert continue to reference the bare entity schema `$ref: #/components/schemas/Entity`)\. The spec then accurately describes the list response but misrepresents single-resource responses — clients see `id`, `kind`, `href` metadata fields in actual responses but the OpenAPI spec claims the response is the bare entity type, so client code generators never parse those fields\. After implementing any conditional feature that changes response or request shapes: (a) grep the generator for EVERY occurrence of the configuration flag/variable (e.g. `envelope`, `responseFormat`); (b) for each occurrence, identify whether it modifies runtime code, specification output, or both; (c) if the flag modifies runtime code for N operations, verify the specification output is ALSO modified for all N operations — not just a subset; (d) for each operation, verify the spec schema matches the actual response structure the handler emits under the condition; (e) write a test that enables the conditional feature and validates the generated specification document for EVERY operation type, not just the one you implemented first\. A conditional feature is incomplete if it transforms the runtime behavior but not the contract specification — the API becomes undocumented\. **Concrete example:** if `response_format: envelope` causes handlers to call `presentEntity()` which wraps single-resource responses with `id`, `kind`, `href` metadata, the OpenAPI response schema for create, read, update, and upsert must describe this wrapper object — not reference the bare entity schema\. The list response schema was correctly updated to an envelope object; applying the same conditional logic to single-resource response schemas is the same feature applied consistently\. **Second concrete example — nested schema references within envelope structures:** when the list response envelope structure (`kind`, `page`, `size`, `total`, `items`) is correctly described in the OpenAPI spec, the `items` array's element schema must ALSO reflect any conditional transformation applied to entities\. If list handlers run each item through `presentEntity()` (adding `id`, `kind`, `href` metadata), the items element schema must use the presented entity schema (e.g\., `allOf` composition with envelope metadata) — not the bare entity schema (`$ref: #/components/schemas/Entity`)\. A correct envelope wrapper around incorrect inner element schemas is a partial fix — the outer structure matches but the inner content does not\. When fixing a schema reference at one level (single-resource responses → envelope schema), apply item 33 fix-class exhaustion to ALL schema references that describe presented entities — including list item element schemas within envelope structures\. The pattern signature is: "OpenAPI schema reference to bare entity where the generated handler code applies `presentEntity()` to the same data\." Grep for every `Ref:` or `$ref` to entity schemas in the OpenAPI generation code and verify each one uses the envelope-aware variant when `response_format: envelope` is enabled\.
   185. **Generated runtime validation must be symmetric across all query parameters accepting the same input category:** When a generator emits handler code that validates one query parameter's values against a known set (e.g., `orderBy` field names validated against a `validFields` map), EVERY other query parameter in the same handler that accepts the same category of user input (e.g., `fields` also accepting field names) MUST be validated against the same set using equivalent logic. A common error pattern: the generator builds a `validFields` map and uses it to reject invalid `orderBy` entries with a 400 response, but passes `fields` values directly to the storage layer's SQL SELECT clause without any validation check. This creates an asymmetric security posture — one parameter is protected against invalid or malicious field names while the other reaches the same SQL query builder unguarded. The asymmetry is especially insidious when a code comment states the validation intent (e.g., "id is always included even if not listed") without corresponding executable code — per item 6's "code comments are not implementation evidence" clause, the comment creates a false sense of completeness while the unvalidated parameter reaches SQL. The spec or task description grouping parameters together (e.g., "List query parameters parsed and validated: page, size, orderBy, fields") implies uniform treatment — the word "validated" applies equally to ALL parameters in the group, not just the ones the implementer happened to validate first. After writing any generator that emits runtime input validation in generated handler code: (a) identify every validation set built during handler generation (e.g., `validFields` map, `validOperations` set); (b) enumerate ALL parameters in the generated handler that accept the same category of user input as the validated parameter (field names, entity names, enum values, etc.); (c) for each validation you implement for one parameter, verify every other parameter in the same category receives equivalent validation against the same set; (d) specifically, when building a validation set like `validFields`, grep the ENTIRE generated handler function for other uses of user-provided values that reference the same domain (field names reaching SQL, entity names reaching lookups) and verify each one is checked against the set; (e) apply item 33 (fix-class exhaustion): the pattern signature is "user-provided field name reaching storage/SQL layer without validation against validFields" — grep the ENTIRE handler for this pattern, not just the parameter you are currently implementing; (f) write a test that passes an invalid value to EACH parameter accepting field names and verify each one is rejected with a 400 response identifying the invalid value. **The principle: a validation set that guards one gate while another gate to the same destination is unguarded provides only the illusion of security. When two parameters carry the same type of data to the same downstream consumer (SQL query builder), they must pass through the same validation. The `validFields` map exists because unvalidated field names in SQL are dangerous — that danger applies equally to every parameter that accepts field names, not just the first one the implementer chose to validate.**
   186. **Output-subsetting features must be verified at the final serialization stage — input-side parsing and storage pass-through are not evidence of response-side implementation:** When a task or spec describes a feature that controls which data appears in the response (e.g. "sparse fieldset selection", "field projection", "partial responses", "field masking"), the implementation MUST include code that operates on the FINAL output representation (the response map, struct, or serialized body) to include only the selected data and exclude everything else. A common error pattern: the feature's input side is fully implemented — the query parameter is parsed, validated against entity fields, injected with required defaults (e.g. `id` always included), and passed to the storage layer via an options struct — but the output side is never implemented. The response construction function (e.g. `presentEntity()`) processes the full entity without consulting the field selection, causing non-selected fields to appear in the response with their Go zero values (empty strings, 0, false, null) instead of being absent. This creates a convincing illusion of completeness: the input pipeline is wired end-to-end (parsing → validation → storage), the storage layer may even use the field list for query optimization (`SELECT` only requested columns), but the serialized response still contains every field because the presentation/serialization step never filters the map. The illusion is strengthened when tests assert on the PRESENCE of expected fields without asserting on the ABSENCE of non-selected fields — such tests pass whether or not the output filtering exists. **The mandatory audit for output-subsetting features:** after implementing any feature described as "selection", "projection", "filtering", "sparse", or "partial" with respect to response content: (a) identify the exact function that constructs the response body (the function that builds the map, struct, or JSON that `json.NewEncoder(w).Encode(...)` serializes); (b) within that function, verify there is executable code (not a comment, not a variable name, not a TODO) that reads the field selection parameter and removes or excludes non-selected entries from the response representation; (c) if no such code exists, the feature's output side is unimplemented regardless of how complete the input side is; (d) write tests that assert on the ABSENCE of non-selected data: for sparse fieldsets, request `?fields=email`, deserialize the response JSON into a `map[string]any`, and assert that keys like `role` and `org_id` do NOT exist in the map — not that they exist with zero values, but that the keys are entirely absent; (e) apply item 37 (shared semantics across operations): if the field selection applies to list responses, verify it also applies to single-resource responses that support the same parameter — the filtering logic should be shared or symmetrically implemented. **The three-stage completeness check for subsetting features:** Stage 1 (input): the parameter is parsed and validated — this is necessary but not sufficient. Stage 2 (storage): the parameter reaches the data layer — this may optimize queries but does not guarantee response filtering. Stage 3 (output): the response representation is filtered to contain only selected fields — this is where the feature's user-visible behavior lives. All three stages must be implemented. Stages 1 and 2 without Stage 3 produce responses that look like the feature doesn't exist. **The principle: a feature that filters output has TWO implementation halves — input-side (parsing and validation) and output-side (response transformation). Completing the input-side creates a convincing but false sense of done-ness because all the visible infrastructure (parameters, validation, storage integration) is in place. The output-side work — often a single map-filtering loop in the response construction function — is where the feature's user-visible behavior actually lives. Tests that assert "expected fields are present" do not catch this gap; only tests that assert "non-selected fields are absent" can verify the output-side implementation.**
   187. **Generated type representations must match spec-literal examples exactly — do not substitute "semantically equivalent" alternatives:** When the spec provides literal type signatures or code examples showing specific type wrapping (e.g. `*json.RawMessage`, `*[]byte`, `*time.Time`), the generated code MUST use those exact type representations. Do not "optimize" by substituting alternatives based on implementation reasoning such as "reference types don't need pointer wrapping because nil already means not-set." The spec chose the literal form deliberately — alternatives that appear equivalent often have semantic differences the spec author accounted for. **The canonical failure pattern:** the spec says "pointer fields for only the listed fields (`*string`, `*int32`, `*json.RawMessage`, etc.)" — the `*json.RawMessage` is an explicit example, not a suggestion. The implementation reasons that `json.RawMessage` (a `[]byte` alias) is already a reference type, so bare `json.RawMessage` is "equivalent" to `*json.RawMessage` for nil-means-not-provided semantics. This reasoning is wrong: with bare `json.RawMessage`, a JSON payload containing `{"field": null}` produces `json.RawMessage("null")` — a non-nil byte slice containing the literal string "null" — which the `!= nil` guard treats as "provided" and applies to the entity. With the spec's `*json.RawMessage`, both an absent key and an explicit JSON null produce a nil pointer, treating both as "not provided." The "optimization" silently changes the API contract for null handling. **Item 163 catches this pattern in tests** ("assert on LITERAL values in the AC, not implementation-derived values"); this item extends the same principle to the implementation itself: when the spec shows a literal type, the code must emit that literal type. The spec is the source of truth for type representations — implementation-level reasoning about "equivalent" alternatives is not a valid basis for deviation. **The mandatory audit:** after writing any generator that produces type declarations based on spec-provided examples: (a) copy every literal type example from the spec into a checklist (e.g. `*string`, `*int32`, `*json.RawMessage`); (b) for each example, verify the generated code uses that EXACT type representation — not a "simplified" or "optimized" variant; (c) if the implementation uses a different type representation for any example, the implementation is wrong unless the spec explicitly permits the alternative; (d) pay special attention to reference types (`[]byte`, `json.RawMessage`, slices, maps, interfaces) — these are the types most likely to be "optimized" away from pointer wrapping, and they are precisely the types where pointer vs. bare semantics diverge for JSON null handling. **The principle: a spec that shows `*json.RawMessage` has already made the design decision. The implementer's job is to implement that decision, not to second-guess it with "but reference types don't need pointers." The spec author may have chosen the pointer form for null-vs-absent disambiguation, for API consistency across all field types, or for future extensibility — the reason doesn't matter. What matters is that the spec is explicit and the implementation must match. When the spec shows a literal, it IS the requirement — not a starting point for optimization.**
   189. **GoModRequires module paths must match the import paths used in generated code — major version suffixes are distinct modules:** When a generator declares Go module dependencies via `GoModRequires` (or any equivalent mechanism that populates `go.mod`), the module path in the `GoModRequires` map MUST exactly match the module path used in the generated code's `import` statements, including major version suffixes. In Go modules, `github.com/foo/bar` and `github.com/foo/bar/v5` are DISTINCT modules — they have different module paths, different version ranges, and different entries in `go.mod`. A `GoModRequires` entry for `github.com/foo/bar v0.3.2` does NOT satisfy an import of `github.com/foo/bar/v5/pkg/thing` — the generated `go.mod` will list the wrong module, and `go build` will fail with an unresolved import. This error is invisible to syntax checks, `go/format.Source`, and `go/parser.ParseFile` — it only surfaces during actual dependency resolution. After writing any generator that both declares `GoModRequires` entries AND emits import statements in generated code: (a) for each entry in `GoModRequires`, grep the generated code for ALL import paths that reference the same base repository; (b) verify that the module path in `GoModRequires` matches the import path up to and including any `/vN` major version suffix — if the import uses `/v5/pkg/...`, the `GoModRequires` key must be `github.com/.../v5`, not `github.com/...`; (c) verify the version string is valid for the major version suffix — a `/v5` module requires a `v5.x.x` version tag, not `v0.3.2`; (d) write a test that extracts both the `GoModRequires` map and all import paths from the generated files, and asserts that every import path's module prefix (the path up to and including `/vN`) appears as a key in `GoModRequires` with a version compatible with that major version. **The principle: Go's module system enforces a strict identity between the module path in `go.mod` and the import path prefix in source files. A mismatch between these two is not a warning or a style issue — it is a hard build failure. The generator knows both the module path (it declares `GoModRequires`) and the import paths (it emits them) — it is the only actor that can ensure they agree. Relying on downstream `go build` to catch the mismatch wastes a full build cycle on an error that is trivially detectable at generation time.**
   190. **Security and correctness mechanisms must be verified to actually function — claimed properties require proof:** When generated code or a generator comment claims that a mechanism provides a specific security or correctness property (e.g. "parameterized queries via squirrel for SQL injection prevention," "input sanitization via encoding/html," "CSRF protection via token validation"), the implementation MUST be verified to actually deliver that property — not just call the library. A common failure pattern: the code calls a security-relevant library function (e.g. `sq.Expr(where).ToSql()`) and comments describe the intended security property ("Use squirrel to parameterize the WHERE clause for SQL injection prevention"), but the actual call is a no-op for the claimed purpose — `sq.Expr(rawString)` wraps a string with no `?` placeholders and returns it unchanged with nil args, providing zero parameterization. The squirrel dependency adds weight and false confidence but delivers no security benefit; the actual SQL injection posture depends entirely on the upstream library's internal quoting, which is undocumented and unverified. This class of bug is especially dangerous because it passes all functional tests (queries execute correctly) and all code reviews that check "is a parameterization library used?" without checking "does the parameterization actually happen?" After writing any code that claims a security or correctness property via a library call: (a) trace the actual data flow through the library function — what goes in, what comes out, and specifically where the claimed transformation (parameterization, escaping, validation, sanitization) occurs; (b) for parameterization specifically: verify that the output contains `?` (or `$N`) placeholders and that the args slice is non-empty for any input containing user-provided values — if args is always nil/empty, the "parameterization" is not happening; (c) write a test that exercises the claimed property with adversarial input (e.g. a search expression containing `'; DROP TABLE users; --`) and asserts on the OUTPUT of the security mechanism — not just that the function ran without error, but that the output has the claimed property (placeholders instead of literals, escaped characters instead of raw ones, rejected input instead of passed-through input); (d) if the library function does not actually perform the claimed transformation for the given usage pattern, either restructure the code to use the library correctly (e.g. build the query using squirrel's builder API from the start, not by wrapping a pre-built string) or replace the mechanism with one that works. **The principle: calling a security library is not the same as being secure. The claim "parameterized queries via squirrel" is a promise to every reader of the code — and to the users whose data it protects — that squirrel is the mechanism providing parameterization. If squirrel's `Expr()` function does not parameterize the input (because the input has no placeholders to parameterize), the promise is false. False security claims are worse than no claims at all — they prevent the real vulnerability from being investigated because everyone believes it is already handled. Every security mechanism must be verified end-to-end: input with adversarial content → mechanism → output with the claimed property. If the output does not have the property, the mechanism does not work, regardless of which library is called.**
   191. **Generated error responses must classify errors by origin — client input errors are 4xx, infrastructure failures are 5xx:** When a generator emits HTTP handler code that receives errors from downstream layers (store, search engine, validator), the handler MUST classify errors by their origin and return the appropriate HTTP status code class — 400-level for client input errors (malformed queries, unknown fields, invalid syntax, constraint violations) and 500-level for infrastructure failures (database errors, timeouts, connection failures). A common failure pattern: the handler catches all errors from the store layer with a blanket `handleError(w, r, InternalError(err.Error()))`, which returns 500 for every error regardless of cause. When the store wraps a client-input error (e.g. `fmt.Errorf("search error: %w", parseErr)`) and the handler treats it as an internal error, the user receives 500 Internal Server Error for a typo in their search query — the API appears broken when in fact the input was simply invalid. This violates HTTP semantics (the server did not malfunction — the client sent bad input) and makes client-side error handling impossible (clients cannot distinguish "retry later" from "fix your request"). The fix requires the generator to establish an error classification boundary between presentation and storage/application layers. Three approaches: (a) **Typed errors** — define error types (e.g. `ValidationError`, `NotFoundError`) that the store returns for client-input failures, and have the handler inspect the error type via `errors.As()` to select the appropriate HTTP status; (b) **Error classification at the handler layer** — perform input validation (parsing, field validation) in the handler before calling the store, so client-input errors are caught and returned as 400 before any store call; (c) **Error code/tag on the error** — attach a classification tag to errors at their origin (e.g. `search.Parse` returns errors tagged as `ErrKindValidation`) and have the handler map tags to HTTP status codes. After writing any generator that produces HTTP handlers calling downstream layers: (a) enumerate every error that the downstream layer can return and classify each as "caused by client input" or "caused by infrastructure failure"; (b) verify the handler maps each classification to the correct HTTP status code — 400 for validation/parse errors, 404 for not-found, 409 for conflicts, 500 for infrastructure; (c) specifically, search/filter expression parse errors, unknown field errors, and constraint-violation errors are ALWAYS client-input errors (400), never internal errors (500); (d) write tests for each error classification: pass a malformed search expression and assert 400, pass an unknown field name and assert 400, simulate a database error and assert 500 — verify the status codes are correct for each. **The principle: HTTP status codes are the API's error classification contract with the client. A server that returns 500 for bad user input is lying — it tells the client "I broke" when the truth is "you broke." This mismatch is not cosmetic; it determines whether clients retry (5xx → retry, which re-sends the same bad request) or fix their input (4xx → correct the request). The generator knows which errors originate from user input validation (it emits the validation code) and which originate from infrastructure (database calls) — it must emit error handling that preserves this distinction through to the HTTP response.**
   188. **New generator methods must audit and update `handlerScopeIdentifiers` for every hardcoded local they introduce:** When adding a new generator method (e.g. `generatePatchMethod`, `generateUpsertMethod`, or any function that emits a new handler method body via `fmt.Fprintf`), you MUST audit every hardcoded local variable name the method introduces and add each to `handlerScopeIdentifiers` BEFORE marking the task complete. Item 48 covers the collision-set concept and its mandatory audit step, but item 48's triggers fire when you are "writing a generator function that derives local variable names from user input" or "modifying the collision set" — neither trigger fires when you add a new generator method that introduces hardcoded locals without touching the collision set. This gap allows new locals to silently enter the generated scope unguarded. **The specific trigger for this item:** any time you write or modify a function whose name matches `generate*Method` or that emits `func (h *...Handler) MethodName(...)` via `Fprintf`, perform the following audit: (a) grep the new function's body for every `Fprintf` call that emits `:=` or `var ` declarations — extract every left-hand-side identifier (e.g. `existing` from `existing, err := h.store.Get(...)`, `patch` from `var patch PatchReqType`); (b) check each extracted identifier against `handlerScopeIdentifiers` — if absent, add it; (c) do NOT assume that identifiers shared with other methods (e.g. `id`, `err`) are already covered — verify by inspection; (d) write a test with an entity whose `strings.ToLower(name)` matches each new identifier (e.g. entity "Existing" for local `existing`, entity "Patch" for local `patch`) and verify `safeVarName` appends `_` to avoid the collision. **Why this is distinct from item 48:** item 48's audit step is triggered by modifying the collision set — it ensures completeness WHEN you are already touching the set. This item ensures you TOUCH the set in the first place when adding a new method. The failure mode is not "incomplete audit of the set" but "never auditing the set because no trigger fired." **The principle: `handlerScopeIdentifiers` is a maintenance contract — every generator method that introduces a hardcoded local must pay into it. A new method that introduces locals without updating the set is a silent scope violation that will surface as a compile error only when a user happens to name an entity after one of the unguarded locals.**
   192. **Every intermediate I/O result in generated methods must have its error checked — no silent fallthrough on unchecked errors:** When a generator emits a method body that performs MULTIPLE I/O operations (database queries, HTTP calls, file reads) in sequence, EVERY operation's error return MUST be checked before the method proceeds to use the operation's result. Item 86 covers method-level return signatures (ensuring the method itself returns `error`); this item covers the distinct gap: **within a method that correctly returns `error`, individual intermediate I/O calls whose error is not captured or checked.** A common error pattern in ORM-based code: `s.db.WithContext(ctx).Model(&v).Where(...).Count(&result)` returns a `*gorm.DB` whose `.Error` field may be non-nil (connection lost, invalid query, permission denied), but the generator emits only the call without capturing or checking the return. The `result` variable retains its zero value (`0` for `int64`), and the method proceeds as if the query succeeded — silently producing incorrect behavior based on a default value rather than actual database state. This is insidious because: (a) the method compiles and passes functional tests (the error path is never exercised in happy-path tests); (b) the method's own return signature correctly includes `error` (satisfying item 86), masking the fact that this particular error is never propagated through it; (c) the zero value is often a plausible result (e.g. count=0 means "no matching rows"), so the incorrect behavior looks correct until a real database failure occurs. **After writing any generator that emits a method performing multiple database or I/O operations:** (a) enumerate EVERY I/O call in the generated method body — for GORM, this includes `s.db.*.Create()`, `s.db.*.Find()`, `s.db.*.Count()`, `s.db.*.Delete()`, `s.db.*.Save()`, and any chained query method that terminates in a database round-trip; (b) for each call, verify the generated code captures the return value and checks for errors — for GORM, this means either `result := s.db....; if result.Error != nil { return ..., result.Error }` or equivalent; (c) grep the generated method body for any I/O call whose return value is NOT assigned to a variable — these are the unchecked calls; (d) write a test for EACH intermediate I/O call that verifies the generated code includes error checking for it — do not rely on happy-path tests alone, as they never exercise the error branch of an unchecked call. **The principle: a method that returns `error` but silently swallows an intermediate I/O error has a false error contract — the caller believes errors will be propagated, but this particular error is silently absorbed into a zero value. The zero value then cascades into incorrect downstream behavior (wrong status codes, wrong return values, wrong side effects) that is extremely difficult to diagnose because the root cause (a database error) was discarded. Every I/O call in a generated method is a potential error source; every error source must be checked. An unchecked I/O call is not "missing error handling" — it is a silent data corruption vector where infrastructure failures masquerade as legitimate domain results.**
   193. **Multi-step database operations requiring atomicity must use transactions or single-statement alternatives — no TOCTOU between queries:** When a generator emits a method that performs a pre-check query (e.g. COUNT, SELECT, EXISTS) followed by a mutation (INSERT, UPDATE, DELETE) whose behavior or return-value semantics depend on the pre-check result, the pre-check and mutation MUST be atomic — either wrapped in a database transaction with appropriate isolation, or replaced by a single atomic statement that combines both operations. A common error pattern: an upsert method first queries `COUNT(*) WHERE natural_key = ?` to determine whether the row exists (setting `existingCount`), then performs `INSERT ... ON CONFLICT DO UPDATE` as a separate query. Between these two queries, a concurrent operation can INSERT a row matching the natural key, causing the COUNT to return 0 ("does not exist") while the subsequent INSERT hits ON CONFLICT and updates instead of inserting. The `created` return value reports `true` (because `existingCount == 0`) when the actual database operation was an update — producing an HTTP 201 instead of the correct 200. The reverse race is also possible: a concurrent DELETE between COUNT and INSERT causes `created` to report `false` when the actual operation was an insert. The consequences are: (a) **incorrect HTTP status codes** — 201 vs 200 conveys create-vs-update semantics to the client, and the wrong code breaks client-side caching, audit logging, and idempotency tracking; (b) **data integrity signals lost** — systems monitoring for "new entity created" events receive false signals; (c) **optimistic concurrency undermined** — if the pre-check is used to gate a WHERE condition on the mutation, the race can cause the condition to be evaluated against stale state. **Three fix patterns for generated database methods:** (a) **Single atomic statement** — use PostgreSQL's `RETURNING` clause or `xmax` system column to determine insert-vs-update atomically within the same INSERT...ON CONFLICT statement (e.g. `INSERT ... ON CONFLICT DO UPDATE ... RETURNING (xmax = 0) AS created` — when `xmax = 0`, the row was inserted; otherwise it was updated); (b) **Transaction wrapping** — wrap the pre-check and mutation in a `BEGIN ... COMMIT` block with `SERIALIZABLE` or `REPEATABLE READ` isolation to prevent concurrent modifications between the two queries; (c) **Eliminate the pre-check** — restructure the method so the insert-vs-update distinction is determined from the mutation's own result (e.g. `RowsAffected` combined with conflict clause behavior), removing the need for a separate COUNT query entirely. **Framework-default isolation trap:** ORM frameworks (e.g. GORM) default to the database's default isolation level when `Transaction()` is called without explicit options. For PostgreSQL, the default is Read Committed, which does NOT prevent the TOCTOU race — each statement within the transaction sees the latest committed data, not a snapshot. Wrapping a pre-check and mutation in a GORM `Transaction()` without specifying isolation is a false fix that adds transactional overhead without atomicity guarantees. When using GORM's `Transaction()`, you MUST pass `&sql.TxOptions{Isolation: sql.LevelSerializable}` as the second argument: `s.db.WithContext(ctx).Transaction(func(tx *gorm.DB) error { ... }, &sql.TxOptions{Isolation: sql.LevelSerializable})`. After generating any GORM `Transaction()` call, grep the generated code for `Transaction(` and verify EVERY call site includes the `sql.TxOptions` argument with the required isolation level — a `Transaction(` call without `TxOptions` is always a bug when used for atomicity of pre-check + mutation patterns. **After writing any generator that emits a method performing a query followed by a mutation where the mutation's behavior or return-value interpretation depends on the query result:** (a) identify the temporal gap between the query and the mutation — is there any window where a concurrent operation could change the queried state before the mutation executes? (b) if yes, the method has a TOCTOU (time-of-check-to-time-of-use) race — restructure using one of the three fix patterns above; (c) write a test or code review comment that explicitly describes the concurrency model — "this method assumes serialized access" or "this method is safe under concurrent upserts because [mechanism]"; (d) apply item 33 (fix-class exhaustion): after identifying a TOCTOU in one method, grep the entire generator for other methods that perform a query-then-mutate pattern (e.g. check-then-create, read-then-update, count-then-insert) and verify each is atomic or explicitly documented as serialized-access-only. **The principle: a pre-check query and a dependent mutation are a read-write pair. Without transactional isolation or a single-statement alternative, they execute as two independent operations — the database provides no guarantee that the state observed by the read is still true when the write executes. This is the database analog of item 100 (read-once for hashing and parsing — no TOCTOU on external inputs): both require that the data consumed by multiple operations is observed exactly once. The database solution is transactions or atomic statements; the filesystem solution is read-once-into-memory. The failure mode is the same: the second consumer sees different data than the first, and the resulting behavior is based on a stale observation.**
   194. **Spec-defined derivation rules must be mechanically verified against actual inputs:** When the spec defines a deterministic transformation rule that derives a value from another value (e.g. "the error code prefix is derived from the service name: uppercased, hyphens removed: `hyperfleet-api` → `HYPERFLEET`"), the implementation MUST apply the stated transformation to the ACTUAL input value and verify the output matches what is used in the generated code. Do not approximate, abbreviate, or "make sense of" the input — apply the rule literally, character by character. Concrete example: if the rule is "uppercased, hyphens removed" and the service name is `user-management`, the output is `USERMANAGEMENT` — not `USER`, not `USERMGMT`, not `UM`. The implementer may feel the derived value looks odd or verbose, but the spec's rule is authoritative — if the output is undesirable, the service name should be changed, not the rule. **After implementing any code that uses a spec-derived value:** (a) identify EVERY derivation rule the spec defines for the domain concept (error codes, path segments, type names, module names); (b) write down the actual input value from the service declaration; (c) apply the spec's stated transformation step by step and write down the expected output; (d) grep the generated code for the expected output AND for any plausible abbreviation or shorthand — if the generated code uses a value that differs from the mechanically derived result, it is wrong; (e) write a test that applies the derivation rule to the service name and asserts the result matches the value used in generated code. **The principle: a derivation rule is a function. The implementer's job is to call the function with the actual input and use the actual output — not to intuit what the output "should" be. Human intuition about what looks right is the #1 source of derivation errors.**
   195. **Generated SQL conflict operations must have corresponding schema constraints:** When a generator emits SQL that relies on a database constraint — specifically `ON CONFLICT (col1, col2, ...)`, `INSERT ... ON DUPLICATE KEY`, or any upsert/merge operation that references a unique constraint by column set — the generator MUST also emit the corresponding unique index or unique constraint on the referenced columns. PostgreSQL's `ON CONFLICT` clause requires a unique index or unique constraint on the conflict target columns; without one, the operation fails at runtime with `there is no unique or exclusion constraint matching the ON CONFLICT specification`. For GORM-based models, this means the struct must carry the appropriate `gorm:"uniqueIndex"` tags (for single-column constraints) or `gorm:"uniqueIndex:idx_name"` tags (for composite constraints) so that `AutoMigrate` creates the index. **After writing any generator that emits upsert or ON CONFLICT logic:** (a) identify every conflict target column set in the generated SQL or ORM clause (e.g. `clause.OnConflict{Columns: []clause.Column{{Name: "org_id"}, {Name: "key"}}}`); (b) verify the corresponding GORM model struct has a unique index covering exactly those columns — for composite keys, each column must carry the same `uniqueIndex:idx_name` tag; (c) if the conflict columns come from the service declaration's `upsert_key` field, the generator must mechanically translate `upsert_key` columns into GORM `uniqueIndex` tags on the model struct at generation time — do not rely on the service author to manually add the constraint; (d) write a test that inspects the generated model struct's tags and asserts the unique index exists for the declared `upsert_key`. **The principle: `ON CONFLICT (columns)` is a promise to the database that a unique constraint exists on those columns. The generator makes the promise (by emitting the SQL) and must keep it (by emitting the constraint). A promise without the backing constraint is a runtime error that only surfaces when the upsert path is exercised — compile checks, syntax checks, and happy-path create tests will not catch it.**
   196. **Optional field representation must be consistent across ALL struct types for the same entity field:** When an entity field is declared `optional: true`, EVERY Go struct type that represents or carries that field — GORM model, API request struct, API response struct, patch request struct — MUST use a pointer type (`*string`, `*int32`, `*bool`, etc.) to distinguish "absent/null" from "zero value." If the GORM model uses `*string` but the API request struct uses `string`, the absent→zero-value mapping is silently broken: a client that omits the field sends no JSON key, `json.Decoder` produces the zero value (`""`), and the database stores an empty string instead of NULL. The optional semantics are violated even though each struct individually "works." **This extends item 28 (field semantics must be consistent across all consumers) with a specific, mechanically verifiable rule:** after writing any struct that contains a field corresponding to an `optional: true` entity field, verify the field's Go type is a pointer. The check is: (a) enumerate every entity field with `optional: true`; (b) for each such field, grep all generated struct types for a field mapped to that entity field; (c) verify every occurrence uses a pointer type; (d) if any occurrence uses a value type, it is a bug — fix it. **The principle: `optional` is a semantic property of the data, not of a particular struct. It must be preserved at every layer of the stack. A pointer in the model but a value in the API struct creates a lossy boundary where the optional distinction is silently destroyed.**
   197. **Derivation rules must produce consistent results across ALL instances — not just the first:** When a spec defines a rule for deriving one value from another (e.g. "collection paths are derived from the entity name, pluralized and lowercased"), and the generated output contains MULTIPLE instances of that derivation (multiple collections, multiple entities, multiple error codes), EVERY instance must be produced by the SAME derivation logic applied to the SAME input type. A common error pattern: the derivation works correctly for the first instance (because the implementer manually reasoned about it), but a later instance uses a different input (e.g. the collection name instead of the entity name) or applies a different transformation (e.g. concatenation instead of the entity-name-based rule), producing an inconsistent result. **After implementing any derivation rule that applies to multiple instances:** (a) enumerate ALL instances in the generated output where the rule applies (all collection paths, all error codes, all type names); (b) for each instance, trace the actual derivation path — what input was used, what transformation was applied; (c) verify that every instance uses the same input type and the same transformation — if one collection derives its path from the entity name and another derives its path from the collection key, the derivation is inconsistent; (d) write a test with at least two instances that would produce DIFFERENT results if the wrong input were used (e.g. collection key `org-settings` with entity `OrgSetting` — the entity-derived path is `orgsettings` or `org-settings`, but a correctly scoped path would strip the scope prefix and use `settings`). **The principle: a derivation rule is a function. If two instances of the same concept use different functions, one of them is wrong. The first instance is usually right (it was implemented with the spec open); the Nth instance is where errors creep in because the implementer generalizes from memory rather than re-reading the spec.**
   198. **New mutation operations must inherit ALL shared semantics from existing mutation operations — slot invocations, fan-outs, and event notifications:** When a new operation type is added to a generator or handler (e.g. `patch`, `upsert`), and that operation mutates entity state, it MUST invoke every slot, fan-out, event notification, and post-mutation hook that the existing mutation operations (create, update, delete) invoke — unless the spec explicitly exempts the new operation. This extends item 37 (shared semantics across operations) with a specific **trigger for new operations:** item 37's pairwise-diff audit can be overlooked when the new operation is conceptually different from existing ones (e.g. "patch is partial, so maybe it doesn't count as a full entity change"). The spec does not make this distinction — a partial update IS an entity change. **The specific trigger for this item:** any time you add a new `generate*Method` function or a new handler method that performs a database mutation (Create, Replace, Delete, Upsert, or any method that changes stored state): (a) enumerate every post-mutation action in ALL existing mutation handlers — slot invocations (e.g. `h.onEntityChangedFanOut.Evaluate(...)`), event emissions, cache invalidations, audit log calls; (b) for each post-mutation action, determine whether the new operation should also perform it — the default answer is YES unless the spec explicitly says otherwise; (c) verify the new handler includes each applicable action; (d) if you decide to omit an action, document the specific spec sentence that exempts the new operation from it. **Concrete example from this codebase:** if Create, Update, and Delete handlers all call `h.onEntityChangedFanOut.Evaluate(...)` after mutating a User entity, the Patch handler MUST also call it — a partial update is still an entity change. **The principle: entity-change notifications exist to maintain system invariants (audit logs, downstream caches, event streams). A mutation operation that omits the notification creates a silent gap where the system's state changes but observers are not informed. The only valid reason to omit a notification is an explicit spec exemption — "it felt different" is not a valid reason.**
   199. **Response values reflecting database state must be derived from the database result, not from pre-computed inputs:** When a handler generates or computes a value (e.g. a UUID for a new record's ID) before a database operation, and the database operation may or may not use that value (e.g. an upsert where `ON CONFLICT` preserves the existing row's ID), the HTTP response MUST return the value that is ACTUALLY stored in the database after the operation — not the pre-computed value. A common error pattern in upsert handlers: the handler generates `entity.ID = uuid.New().String()` before the upsert, the database hits `ON CONFLICT` and performs an UPDATE (preserving the existing row's ID, not the newly generated one), but the handler returns `entity.ID` (the phantom UUID) in the response body and `href`.
