The client receives an ID that does not exist in the database. **Three fix patterns:** (a) **Read-after-write** — after the upsert, read the actual record back from the database by its natural key and return the read result; (b) **RETURNING clause** — use PostgreSQL's `RETURNING *` (or GORM's `Clauses(clause.Returning{})`) to get the actual row values as stored after the upsert, including the real ID; (c) **ID assignment from result** — after the upsert, extract the actual ID from the database result and overwrite the pre-computed value before building the response. **After writing any handler that pre-computes values before a database operation whose outcome is conditional (upsert, conditional insert, merge):** (a) enumerate every field in the response that was pre-computed before the database call; (b) for each such field, determine whether the database operation always uses the pre-computed value (insert) or may preserve an existing value (upsert on conflict) — if the latter, the response must derive the field from the database result, not the pre-computed input; (c) pay special attention to identity fields (`id`, `href`, `self` links) — these are the fields clients use to locate the resource, and a phantom ID breaks all subsequent operations on the resource; (d) write a test that performs an upsert against an existing row and asserts the response's `id` and `href` match the original row's values, not a newly generated UUID. **The principle: the response is a claim about the state of the world after the operation. If the operation may or may not have used the handler's pre-computed values, the response must reflect what actually happened — not what the handler hoped would happen. A response that returns a phantom ID is a lie to the client, and lies about identity are the most damaging kind because every subsequent operation uses the ID.**
   200. **Generated storage layer methods must translate framework-specific errors into domain-specific sentinel errors at the storage boundary:** When a generator produces storage/DAO methods that wrap a framework (e.g. GORM, SQLAlchemy, ActiveRecord), EVERY framework-specific error type that has a domain-level semantic equivalent MUST be translated into a domain-specific sentinel error at the storage layer boundary — before the error reaches the handler. The handler's error classification (item 181) depends on receiving classifiable errors; if the store returns raw framework errors, the handler cannot distinguish domain outcomes from infrastructure failures because it has no knowledge of framework-specific error types and MUST NOT import framework packages. The canonical failure: GORM's `gorm.ErrRecordNotFound` is a domain error meaning "the entity does not exist" — the store MUST translate it to a domain sentinel (e.g. `var ErrNotFound = errors.New("entity not found")`) that the handler can check via `errors.Is()`. Without this translation, the handler's `errors.Is(err, ErrNotFound)` check never matches (because `gorm.ErrRecordNotFound` is a completely different error value), and ALL not-found GETs fall through to the `InternalError` branch, returning HTTP 500 instead of 404. **The same principle applies to ALL framework-specific errors with domain equivalents:** unique constraint violations → `ErrConflict`, foreign key violations → `ErrBadRequest`, optimistic lock failures → `ErrConflict`. **This is the store-side complement to item 181 (handler-side error classification):** item 181 ensures the handler uses the right error constructor for each error category; this item ensures the store provides errors the handler can actually classify. After writing any generator that produces storage methods wrapping a framework: (a) enumerate every framework-specific error type the framework can return from each CRUD operation — for GORM: `gorm.ErrRecordNotFound` (Get/First), unique constraint violations (Create/Save), foreign key violations (Create/Update), `gorm.ErrDuplicatedKey` (Create); (b) for each framework error, determine the domain-level semantic — "not found," "conflict," "bad reference," "internal"; (c) define domain sentinel errors in the storage package (e.g. `var ErrNotFound`, `var ErrConflict`) and document which framework errors each one wraps; (d) in every store method, check for framework-specific errors and return the corresponding domain sentinel — the standard pattern is `if errors.Is(err, gorm.ErrRecordNotFound) { return nil, ErrNotFound }`; (e) verify the handler imports ONLY the storage package's sentinel errors, never the framework package directly — the handler must not know or care whether the store uses GORM, raw SQL, or an in-memory map; (f) write a test for each store method that verifies the correct domain sentinel is returned for each framework error condition — do not rely on handler-level tests alone, because the translation must be verified at the store boundary independently. **Mandatory exhaustiveness audit:** after implementing error translation for one store method (e.g. `Get` → `ErrNotFound`), apply item 33 (fix-class exhaustion) with pattern signature "framework error returned without domain translation." Grep EVERY store method for direct returns of framework errors (e.g. `return result.Error` without any preceding `errors.Is` check) and verify each one either translates the error or documents why translation is unnecessary (e.g. the error is genuinely an infrastructure failure with no domain equivalent). **The principle: the storage layer is the boundary between framework-specific and domain-specific error semantics. Errors that cross this boundary untranslated are framework leaks — they force upstream code to import the framework, understand its error types, and handle its idiosyncrasies. The store's contract with the handler is defined by domain sentinels, not framework internals. Every framework error with a domain equivalent must be translated at the boundary; every framework error without a domain equivalent should be wrapped with context and returned as a generic error for the handler's `InternalError` path.**
   201. **Generated code must never perform unguarded type assertions, and cross-package type assertions must reference the correct package's types:** When generated code performs a type assertion (`v.(Type)`) on a value returned by a function in a different package, two independent checks must be verified: **(a) Guard the assertion.** An unguarded type assertion (`user := existing.(User)`) panics at runtime when the concrete type does not match the asserted type — crashing the server on every request that reaches that code path. ALL type assertions in generated code MUST use the two-value form: `user, ok := existing.(User); if !ok { return InternalError("unexpected type") }`. **(b) Assert the correct package's type.** Two different named types in different packages — even with identical field sets — are distinct types in Go. A type assertion from one to the other ALWAYS fails: `existing.(api.User)` panics when `existing` is `storage.User`, and vice versa. The asserted type MUST come from the package that actually produced the value. If `store.Get()` returns a value declared in the `storage` package, the assertion must use `storage.User`, not `api.User`. The failure mode is catastrophic: an unguarded cross-package type assertion is a guaranteed runtime panic on every request — it is not an edge case or a race condition, it is structurally impossible for the assertion to succeed. `go build` and `go vet` do not catch this because the type assertion is syntactically valid; the mismatch is only detectable by tracing the concrete type through the interface boundary. After writing any generated code that performs a type assertion: (a) verify the assertion uses the two-value form (`v, ok := ...`); (b) trace the value being asserted to its origin — what function returned it, and what concrete type does that function declare in its return? For interface returns (e.g. `Get() (any, error)`), what concrete type does the implementation actually return? (c) verify the asserted type matches the origin type exactly — same package path, same type name; (d) if the handler needs to work with a different package's type (e.g. needs `api.User` but received `storage.User`), perform an explicit field-by-field conversion or use a conversion function — never assert across package boundaries; (e) when a store method returns `any` or `interface{}` and the handler must recover the concrete type, consider whether the interface should instead return the concrete type directly (eliminating the need for type assertions entirely) or whether the handler should work with the value through an interface rather than a concrete type. **Apply item 33 (fix-class exhaustion):** after finding one unguarded or cross-package type assertion, grep ALL generated handler code for the pattern `\.(Type)` (single-value type assertion syntax — no `ok` on the left) and verify every instance is guarded AND uses the correct package's type. **The principle: a type assertion is a runtime contract — "I know this value's concrete type." An unguarded assertion that is wrong crashes the program; a guarded assertion that is wrong returns an error. Cross-package assertions are almost always wrong because the value was produced by a different package using its own types. The generator has full knowledge of both the store's return type and the handler's working type — it must either align them or convert between them explicitly. A type assertion should never be the mechanism for crossing a package boundary.**
   202. **URL-path-derived fields must be excluded from request body schema requirements:** When a field's authoritative value is derived from the URL path (scope fields like `org_id` on scoped collections, parent reference fields, entity identity `id`), the field MUST NOT appear in the `required` array of the request body's OpenAPI schema — and the validation middleware must not reject requests that omit these fields from the body. The handler derives these fields from `r.PathValue()` and overwrites any body-provided value (per item 37), so requiring them in the body serves no purpose and creates two failure modes: **(a) Spurious validation failures:** when a validation middleware checks the request body against the OpenAPI schema BEFORE the handler runs, and the schema lists a URL-derived field as `required`, a client that correctly relies on the URL to provide the value (as scoped routing intends) receives a 400 validation error before the handler ever sees the request. The client is forced to redundantly include the field in the body. **(b) Silent data divergence:** when the body DOES include the URL-derived field and its value differs from the URL's value, the handler silently overwrites the body value with the URL value — the client's stated intent is discarded without notice. Consider returning a 400 error when body and URL values diverge for the same field, or at minimum, document that the URL value takes precedence. After writing any generator that produces BOTH OpenAPI schemas AND scoped/nested route handlers: (a) enumerate every field whose value is derived from the URL path — this includes scope fields (e.g. `org_id` from `{org_id}` path parameter), parent reference fields, and the entity's own identity field when server-generated; (b) verify each such field is EXCLUDED from the body schema's `required` array — the field may appear in `properties` (to document its existence) but must not be required; (c) verify the validation middleware does not reject requests that omit URL-derived fields from the body — test by sending a POST/PUT/PATCH request body WITHOUT the scope/parent field and confirming the handler processes it successfully (filling the field from the URL); (d) apply this check to ALL operations that accept request bodies on scoped routes — create, update, upsert, and patch; (e) write a test for each scoped collection that sends a request body WITHOUT the scope field and asserts the request succeeds with the scope field populated from the URL path parameter. **The principle: the request body schema describes what the CLIENT must provide in the body. URL-derived fields are provided by the URL, not the body — requiring them in the body conflates two input channels. The OpenAPI spec is a contract with API consumers and code generators; a required body field that the handler ignores (overwriting with the URL value) is a misleading contract. The schema must reflect the handler's actual requirements: what does the CLIENT need to put in the body for the request to succeed? URL-derived fields are not in that set.**
   203. **Generated error handling must exhaustively map ALL spec-defined error categories to their corresponding HTTP status codes — including database constraint violations:** When the spec defines an error handling table with N categories mapped to HTTP status codes (e.g. NTF → 404 Not Found, CNF → 409 Conflict, VAL → 400 Validation Error, INT → 500 Internal Server Error), EVERY generated handler must provide error-handling branches for ALL categories reachable from its store calls — not just the "not found" and "internal error" cases. A common error pattern: the spec defines a `Conflict()` error constructor for 409 responses, `errors.go` generates it, but create/update handlers treat ALL store mutation errors as `InternalError(err.Error())`. When a unique constraint violation occurs (a domain-level conflict, not an infrastructure failure), the handler returns 500 with the raw database error message exposed to the client — wrong status code AND information leakage. **The mandatory audit for handler error classification:** after writing any handler method that calls a store mutation (Create, Replace, Upsert, Patch): (a) enumerate every error category the spec defines in its error handling table; (b) for each store call, determine which error categories are reachable — at minimum: unique constraint violations → Conflict (409), foreign key violations → BadRequest (400), not-found on update/patch → NotFound (404), validation failures → ValidationError (400); (c) verify the handler checks for each reachable error category using domain sentinel errors from the store (per item 200) — the standard pattern is `if errors.Is(err, storage.ErrConflict) { Conflict("resource already exists") } else if errors.Is(err, storage.ErrNotFound) { NotFound(...) } else { InternalError("internal error") }`; (d) verify the handler NEVER exposes raw database error messages to the client — use sanitized messages like "resource already exists" instead of `pq: duplicate key value violates unique constraint "users_email_key""`; exposing constraint names, table names, or SQL fragments is an information disclosure vulnerability; (e) apply item 33 (fix-class exhaustion) with pattern signature "store error passed directly to InternalError without classification" — grep ALL handler methods for `InternalError(err.Error())` immediately following a store call and verify each one is preceded by checks for domain-specific error categories; (f) write tests that trigger each error category: insert a duplicate record and assert 409 (not 500), update a non-existent record and assert 404 (not 500), violate a foreign key and assert 400 (not 500). **The relationship between items 181, 200, and 203:** item 200 ensures the store translates framework errors into domain sentinels. Item 181 establishes the principle of distinguishing domain errors from infrastructure errors at the handler level. This item provides the MECHANICAL AUDIT PROCEDURE for exhaustively mapping every spec-defined error category in every handler method — the specific gap that allowed create handlers to return 500 for unique constraint violations despite the existence of a `Conflict()` constructor. Item 181 says "distinguish error categories"; this item says "enumerate the spec's error table, verify each category is handled, grep for the catch-all anti-pattern." **The principle: error handling is not a default — it is an enumeration. The spec defines N error categories; each handler must provide branches for the subset of N that are reachable from its operations. A handler with only two branches (domain error X and catch-all InternalError) has N-1 missing branches. The catch-all is necessary for truly unexpected errors, but it must be the LAST resort after all reachable domain categories are checked — not the ONLY handler for store errors. Every `InternalError(err.Error())` immediately after a store call is suspicious: is the store error really always an infrastructure failure, or could it be a constraint violation, a not-found, or a validation error?**
   204. **Fix code is new code — it must be tested, traced, and audited like any other new code path:** When addressing a `needs-revision` finding, the fix introduces NEW code paths (new branches, recovery logic, fallback paths, re-fetch queries). These new code paths are subject to ALL checklist items — not just the one that triggered the finding. Three specific obligations: **(a) Write a test that exercises the exact fix code path and proves the fix works.** A fix without a test that exercises it is unverified. If the fix adds a recovery path for upsert-on-existing-record, write a test that performs an upsert against an existing record and asserts the response contains the original record's values (not pre-computed values). If the test cannot be made to exercise the fix path, the fix path may be unreachable — which means the fix does not work. **(b) Apply ALL relevant checklist items to the fix code itself, especially items describing the SAME class of bug being fixed.** If you are fixing a cross-package type assertion (item 201), verify your fix code does not contain another cross-package type assertion. If you are fixing a missing error translation (item 200), verify your fix code translates errors correctly. The fix code is the code MOST LIKELY to contain the same class of bug because the implementer is focused on the specific finding, not on the general pattern. **(c) Trace every new expression in the fix through its type chain.** If the fix adds a type assertion, trace the value to its origin and verify the asserted type matches. If the fix adds a function call, verify the return type is handled correctly. A "fix" that adds a list-and-scan recovery path using a cross-package type assertion that always returns `ok == false` is not a fix — it is dead code that masks the continued presence of the original bug. The recovery path was never exercised because the type assertion structurally cannot succeed, and the response still contains phantom data. **Concrete anti-pattern from this codebase:** Round 1 found a phantom UUID in upsert responses. The "fix" added a recovery path that calls `store.List`, then asserts the result as `[]api.OrgSetting` — but `store.List` returns `[]storage.OrgSetting` (a different package's type). The assertion always fails (`ok == false`), the recovery path never executes, and the handler falls through to return the same phantom UUID. This fix was accepted because no test exercised the upsert-on-existing-record path. A single test — "upsert existing record, assert response ID matches original" — would have caught the failure immediately. **The principle: a fix is a hypothesis — "this change will correct the behavior." A test is the experiment that confirms or refutes the hypothesis. A fix without a confirming test is an unverified hypothesis. When the hypothesis is wrong (the fix contains the same class of bug), only the test can reveal it.**
   205. **Store mutation methods targeting specific entities must translate zero-rows-affected into ErrNotFound — ORM silent success on empty WHERE match is not application success:** When a store's Replace, Update, or Delete method targets a specific entity by identity (e.g. `db.Model(&User{}).Where("id = ?", id).Updates(&v)`), and the underlying ORM returns nil error with zero rows affected (the mutation "succeeded" against zero rows), the store MUST return `ErrNotFound`. ORMs like GORM treat a no-op UPDATE or DELETE as success — the SQL executed without error, it just matched nothing. But at the application level, "the entity I asked you to update does not exist" is a domain error, not a success. Without this translation, the handler receives nil error for a non-existent entity and proceeds to: (a) return HTTP 200 with the request body presented as a persisted entity — the response is fabricated; (b) fire post-mutation side effects (fan-outs, event notifications, audit log entries) for a mutation that never occurred — producing false-positive notifications downstream; (c) construct envelope metadata (`id`, `kind`, `href`) pointing to an entity that does not exist. **The mechanical check for every store mutation method:** after every `db.Updates(...)`, `db.Save(...)`, or `db.Delete(...)` call that targets a specific entity by identity: (a) check `result.RowsAffected == 0` — if true, return `ErrNotFound`; (b) this check MUST occur before returning nil error to the handler; (c) write a test for each store mutation method that calls it with a non-existent ID and asserts the method returns `ErrNotFound`, not nil. **Contrast with methods that do NOT need this check:** `Create` always inserts a new row (RowsAffected is always 1 on success); `List` returns an empty slice for no matches (which is a valid result, not an error). The check is specific to methods that target a single entity by identity and whose semantics require that entity to exist. **The Patch handler vs. Update handler asymmetry that exposed this bug:** the Patch handler correctly calls `store.Get` before `store.Replace`, receiving `ErrNotFound` when the entity doesn't exist and returning 404. The Update handler skips the existence check and calls `store.Replace` directly — which silently "succeeds" and returns 200. Both are "modify existing entity" operations and must have the same existence-verification behavior (per item 37's shared-semantics principle). The store-level zero-rows check eliminates this asymmetry by making `Replace` itself return `ErrNotFound` when the target doesn't exist — neither handler needs an explicit pre-check because the store enforces the invariant. **The principle: "zero rows affected" on a targeted mutation is the ORM's way of saying "the entity you asked me to mutate does not exist." The store must translate this ORM-level signal into a domain-level error. A nil error from the ORM does not mean the application-level operation succeeded — it means the SQL executed without syntax or constraint errors. Whether the operation achieved its intended effect (mutating a specific entity) is a separate question that only `RowsAffected` can answer.** **Mandatory grep-and-count audit — this step cannot be skipped:** After modifying ANY Store mutation method to add a `RowsAffected` check, immediately verify completeness by enumerating all entity-targeting mutation methods (`Replace`, `Delete`, and `Upsert` for every entity type) and confirming each contains a `RowsAffected == 0` guard. The grep pattern is `RowsAffected` — every entity-targeting mutation method must appear in the results. If a method is absent, the fix is incomplete. Do NOT mark ready-for-review until every mutation method is accounted for. **This audit was added because a fix to `Store.Replace` (added in response to Round 3 findings) was applied without extending to `Store.Delete` — despite this item explicitly listing Delete as a method requiring the check, and despite item 33 requiring fix-class exhaustion. The implementation team fixed the specific method named in the finding but did not grep for sibling methods with the identical pattern. A fix to one mutation method that omits the identical fix from sibling mutation methods is the most common form of incomplete fix in this codebase. When you fix Replace, immediately check Delete and Upsert. When you fix Delete, immediately check Replace and Upsert. Always.**
   206. **Error-detection helpers in the store layer must be called in EVERY mutation method where the error can occur — not just the method where the bug was first discovered:** When a store layer defines a helper function for detecting and translating a specific class of framework error (e.g. `isUniqueConstraintError()` for detecting PostgreSQL unique constraint violations), that helper MUST be called in EVERY store method that performs a database mutation which could trigger that error — not just the method where the bug was initially found and fixed. A common error pattern: `Create` correctly calls `isUniqueConstraintError()` and returns `ErrConflict`, but `Replace` (which also performs a write that can trigger a unique constraint violation when updating a field with a unique index) returns the raw GORM error directly. The helper exists, the translation pattern is established, but it is only applied to one method. **The mandatory audit when a store layer has error-detection helpers:** after implementing or reviewing ANY store error translation: (a) enumerate every error-detection helper in the store (`isUniqueConstraintError`, `isForeignKeyError`, etc.); (b) for each helper, enumerate every store method that performs a database operation which could trigger the error the helper detects — at minimum: `Create` (INSERT → unique violations, FK violations), `Replace`/`Update` (UPDATE → unique violations on changed unique fields, FK violations), `Upsert` (INSERT OR UPDATE → unique violations, FK violations), `Delete` (DELETE → FK violations from dependent tables); (c) verify each applicable method calls the helper and returns the corresponding domain sentinel; (d) a helper that is called in `Create` but not in `Replace` is an incomplete application — the same SQL constraint violation can occur in both methods. **Concrete example from this codebase:** `store.go` defines `isUniqueConstraintError()` and calls it in Create methods (lines 43-44 for Organization, 59-60 for User, 75-76 for OrgSetting), returning `ErrConflict`. The Replace methods (lines 123-161) do NOT call `isUniqueConstraintError()` — they return `result.Error` directly. A PUT or PATCH that changes a User's `email` to a value held by another User triggers a PostgreSQL unique constraint violation in `Replace`, which passes through as a raw GORM error to the handler's `InternalError` branch — returning HTTP 500 instead of 409. **The grep-based audit:** after implementing error translation in any store method, run: `grep -n 'isUniqueConstraintError\|isForeignKeyError\|errors.Is.*gorm' store.go` and verify every mutation method appears in the results. If a mutation method is absent, it is missing error translation. Then run: `grep -n 'return.*result\.Error\|return.*err$' store.go` and verify every direct framework error return is preceded by a domain-error check. Any `return result.Error` without a preceding `isUniqueConstraintError` check is a candidate for missing translation. **The principle: an error-detection helper is a reusable translation. If it is only used once, it is not reusable — it is a point fix. The helper's existence implies the error class can occur in multiple contexts. After creating or finding a helper, the immediate next step is to audit every context where the error can occur and wire the helper in. A helper that exists but is only called in one method is a process failure — the generalization was identified (the helper was created) but the application was not completed (the helper was not wired into all applicable methods).**
   207. **Values serialized into string-typed containers must use format-appropriate serialization — never `fmt.Sprintf("%v", ...)` on pointer or byte-slice-backed types:** When populating a `map[string]string`, a string field, or any string-typed interface from a Go struct field, the value MUST be serialized using format-appropriate methods — not `fmt.Sprintf("%v", ...)`. The `%v` verb on pointer types produces Go debug output that is unusable by downstream consumers: (a) `*json.RawMessage` (which is `*[]byte`) prints the raw byte-slice representation — e.g. `[123 34 107 101 121 34 58 34 118 97 108 34 125]` — instead of the JSON string `{"key":"val"}`; (b) `*string` prints `<nil>` when nil instead of an empty string or an absent key; (c) `*int`, `*float64`, `*bool` print `<nil>` when nil. Any fill or downstream consumer that accesses a string map value populated via `fmt.Sprintf("%v", pointerField)` receives debug output, not data. **Required serialization patterns by Go type:** (a) `string` (non-pointer) → use directly; (b) `*string` → if nil, omit the key or use `""`; if non-nil, dereference: `*v`; (c) `json.RawMessage` → `string(v)` to get the JSON text; (d) `*json.RawMessage` → if nil, omit the key; if non-nil, `string(*v)`; (e) `*int`, `*int32`, `*int64` → if nil, omit the key; if non-nil, `strconv.FormatInt(int64(*v), 10)` or `fmt.Sprintf("%d", *v)` (dereference first); (f) `*float32`, `*float64` → if nil, omit; if non-nil, `strconv.FormatFloat(float64(*v), 'f', -1, 64)`; (g) `*bool` → if nil, omit; if non-nil, `strconv.FormatBool(*v)`. **After writing any code that populates a `map[string]string` from struct fields:** (a) enumerate every field being serialized and note its Go type; (b) for each pointer type, verify the serialization handles nil explicitly and dereferences before formatting; (c) for each byte-slice-backed type (`json.RawMessage`, `[]byte`), verify the serialization converts via `string()`, not `%v`; (d) grep for the anti-pattern: `fmt.Sprintf("%v",` followed by a field name — if the field is a pointer type or `json.RawMessage`, this is ALWAYS wrong; (e) write a test that populates the map from a struct with (i) nil pointer fields, (ii) non-nil pointer fields, and (iii) `json.RawMessage` fields containing valid JSON, then asserts each map value is a human-readable, format-appropriate string — not Go debug output like `[123 34 ...]` or `<nil>`. **Apply item 33 (fix-class exhaustion):** after finding one `fmt.Sprintf("%v", pointerField)` call, grep ALL handler code for the pattern `Sprintf("%v",` and verify every occurrence uses a non-pointer, non-byte-slice type. The pattern signature is "fmt.Sprintf %v on a type whose %v representation is not its meaningful string form." This includes not just pointer types but also `[]byte`, `json.RawMessage`, `time.Time` (whose `%v` format may not match the desired serialization format like RFC 3339), and any custom type whose `String()` method is absent or produces debug output. **The principle: `fmt.Sprintf("%v", ...)` is a debugging tool, not a serialization tool. It produces the Go internal representation of a value, which is useful for log output but meaningless to API consumers, fills, or any code that expects to parse the string as data. When a `map[string]string` is part of a public contract (like slot request Fields), every value must be a meaningful string that the consumer can parse or display — not a debug dump of the Go runtime's internal memory layout.**
   208. **Cross-package type assertions on `any`/`interface{}` values are structurally invalid — use type-safe conversion patterns instead:** When a function receives a value typed as `any`, `interface{}`, or a struct field typed as `any` (e.g. `ListResult.Items interface{}`), and the value was produced by a different package (e.g. a storage layer returning `[]storage.Entity` inside an `any`-typed field), a type assertion against the CONSUMER package's type (e.g. `items.([]api.Entity)`) ALWAYS fails at runtime — the concrete type is `[]storage.Entity`, not `[]api.Entity`, regardless of how similar the struct fields look. These are distinct named types in distinct packages; Go's type system does not perform structural equivalence for named types. The assertion evaluates to `ok == false` on every call, the recovery branch never executes, and the code silently falls through to the default path — which typically returns stale or fabricated data. **This bug is the single most recurring defect in this codebase.** It was first found in Round 1 (phantom UUID in upsert response), "fixed" in Round 3 with a recovery path that used the SAME broken pattern (cross-package type assertion), and found again in Round 5 still unfixed. The recovery path that was supposed to fix the bug contained the identical bug — it was dead code from the moment it was written. **The mandatory protocol when working with `any`/`interface{}` values from another package:** (a) NEVER write a type assertion `value.(LocalType)` when the value originates from a different package — this is structurally guaranteed to fail; (b) identify the CONCRETE type the producer package returns (e.g. `[]storage.OrgSetting`) — read the producer function's source code, do not guess; (c) to convert between packages' types, use one of these type-safe patterns: (i) JSON marshal/unmarshal round-trip — `json.Marshal(storageValue)` then `json.Unmarshal(bytes, &apiValue)` — this is the pattern the Patch handler uses correctly; (ii) explicit field-by-field mapping in a conversion function; (iii) assert against the PRODUCER's type (`items.([]storage.OrgSetting)`) and then convert each element to the consumer's type; (d) after writing ANY type assertion in handler code, trace the value to its origin: what function produced it, in what package, returning what concrete type? If the producing package differs from the consuming package, the assertion is wrong — replace it with a type-safe conversion; (e) write a test that exercises the exact code path containing the type assertion and asserts on the output values — a test that exercises the assertion path will immediately reveal `ok == false` if the types don't match. **The grep-based audit:** after writing any type assertion `.(` in handler code, run `grep -n '\\.(' handler_*.go` and for each match, verify the asserted type's package matches the concrete value's origin package. Any mismatch is a guaranteed runtime failure. **The principle: a type assertion is a runtime claim about the concrete type of an interface value. When the interface value crosses a package boundary, the concrete type belongs to the producing package. Asserting the consuming package's type is asserting a type that the value cannot possibly be. This is not an edge case — it is a structural impossibility. No amount of similar field names or identical field layouts changes this: `storage.Entity` and `api.Entity` are different types, period. The correct approach is type-safe conversion, not type assertion.**
   209. **When a store method gains a new error sentinel from a fix, ALL handler callers must be re-audited for the new error — item 203's audit is triggered by item 205/206 fixes:** When a fix to a store method adds a new domain sentinel error return (e.g. adding `ErrNotFound` when `RowsAffected == 0` per item 205, or adding `ErrConflict` for unique constraint violations per item 206), EVERY handler method that calls the modified store method MUST be re-audited to determine whether it handles the new error. This is the handler-side counterpart to items 205 and 206: those items ensure the store returns the correct domain error; this item ensures the handler RESPONDS to that error with the correct HTTP status code. A store fix without a corresponding handler fix converts a silent bug (handler returns 200 for non-existent entity) into a different silent bug (handler returns 500 because `ErrNotFound` falls through to `InternalError`). The bug class changes but the user-visible symptom remains wrong. **The mechanical trigger:** whenever you apply item 205 (add RowsAffected→ErrNotFound to a store method) or item 206 (add isUniqueConstraintError→ErrConflict to a store method), IMMEDIATELY perform item 203's audit on every handler that calls that store method. Concretely: (a) grep all handler files for the store method name (e.g. `store.Replace`, `store.Delete`); (b) for each handler call site, enumerate the error branches — does the handler check `errors.Is(err, ErrNotFound)`? Does it check `errors.Is(err, ErrConflict)`? (c) if the handler only has a catch-all `InternalError` after the store call, add the missing error branch. **The pairwise audit between operations:** Item 37 (shared semantics) requires that operations sharing the same store call handle errors the same way. When one operation correctly handles `ErrNotFound` (e.g. Patch handler calls `store.Get` and returns 404) but a sibling operation calling the same store method does NOT (e.g. Update handler calls `store.Replace` and falls through to 500), the inconsistency is a guaranteed finding. After adding any error handling to any handler method, diff it against every other handler method that calls the same store method and verify they handle the same set of errors. **Concrete example from this codebase:** Round 3 fixed `Store.Replace` to return `ErrNotFound` when `RowsAffected == 0`. The Update handler calls `store.Replace` but only checks `errors.Is(err, ErrConflict)` — `ErrNotFound` falls through to `InternalError("internal error")`, returning HTTP 500 instead of the spec-defined 404. The Patch handler, which also calls `store.Replace` (indirectly, after `store.Get`), correctly handles `ErrNotFound` because it checks before calling Replace. The asymmetry between Update and Patch — two operations that modify the same entity type — should have been caught by item 37's pairwise diff. **The principle: a store-level fix and its handler-level counterpart are two halves of a single semantic fix. The store translates ORM signals into domain errors; the handler translates domain errors into HTTP responses. Fixing one layer without the other produces a half-fix: the domain error is correctly generated but incorrectly classified at the HTTP boundary. Items 205/206 are triggers for item 203's audit — they are not standalone fixes. Every store-error addition MUST be accompanied by a handler-error audit.**
   210. **Example fills demonstrating a spec feature must exercise every distinct behavioral state — not just the primary path:** When a task requires "demonstrating" or "exercising" a spec feature via example configuration and fills (e.g. "at least one slot with `chain` operator and `short_circuit: true` to demonstrate halt semantics"), each distinct behavioral state of the feature must be reachable by at least one example fill. A feature whose structured result type has N independently significant outcome states (per item 98's analysis) requires N fills — each producing a different state — so that every code path in the generated handler is exercised. A fill that technically sets a flag but in a combination where the flag is masked by a higher-priority check does NOT demonstrate the flag. Concrete example: a chain with `short_circuit: true` has three distinct behavioral states: (a) success-continue (`Ok: true, Halt: false`), (b) rejection (`Ok: false`) which stops the chain on error, and (c) success-halt (`Ok: true, Halt: true, StatusCode: 204`) which stops the chain and returns a status code with no body. A fill that returns `Ok: false, Halt: true` on invalid input does NOT demonstrate halt semantics — the `!result.Ok` check fires before `result.Halt` is inspected, making `Halt: true` redundant. The handler's halt code path (which returns the status code with an empty body for the `Ok: true, Halt: true` case) is dead code — it compiles but is never reached. The spec's canonical halt example is a step like `discard-stale-generation` that succeeds but stops the pipeline early (`Ok: true, Halt: true, StatusCode: 204` — "this is fine, but we're done"). No fill in the example exercises this pattern, so the feature appears implemented but is never demonstrated. **The mandatory audit when a task requires demonstrating a feature:** (a) identify the feature's structured result type and enumerate every independently significant outcome state (per item 98); (b) for each state, verify at least one example fill produces EXACTLY that state under a normal input scenario — not a state where the desired flag is set but masked by a higher-priority field; (c) trace from each fill's return value through the operator and handler to confirm the intended code path is actually reached — do not assume that setting a flag means the flag is inspected; (d) if a result type has fields that interact hierarchically (e.g. `Ok` is checked before `Halt`), enumerate the CHECK ORDER and verify fills produce states that reach each subsequent check — a fill must pass ALL prior checks to demonstrate a downstream field; (e) for the halt state specifically: write a fill that returns `Ok: true, Halt: true` with an appropriate `StatusCode` (e.g. 204), demonstrating a step that succeeds but intentionally stops the pipeline — this is semantically distinct from rejection (`Ok: false`) which stops the pipeline due to failure. **The relationship between items 98, 1, and 210:** item 98 ensures the HANDLER CODE checks all result fields — it is about the consumer. Item 1 ensures there is no dead code — but a handler code path that checks `result.Halt` passes item 1's "is it referenced?" test even if no fill ever triggers it. Item 210 ensures the EXAMPLE FILLS produce all states — it is about the producer. Together, items 98 and 210 form a bidirectional coverage guarantee: the handler handles all states (98), and the example exercises all states (210). Without 210, the handler code for a state may be dead code that happens to pass a grep-based "is it referenced?" check because the conditional branch exists but is never entered. **The principle: demonstrating a feature means proving it works end-to-end — from fill return value through operator logic through handler response. A feature with N behavioral states requires N proofs. A fill that returns a masked flag provides zero proof for that flag's behavioral path. When the task says "demonstrate halt semantics," the deliverable is not code that handles halt — it is an example where halt actually happens and the handler responds correctly. Code without an exercising example is an assertion without evidence.**
   211. **Scoped collection handlers must enforce scope for ALL operations — not just List — by verifying the target entity belongs to the URL path's scope before performing the operation:** When a collection declares `scope: { field: ParentEntity }`, the spec defines the collection as "a scoped, operation-constrained access pattern over an entity" (spec.md:17). The List handler correctly enforces scope by passing `scopeField` and `scopeValue` to the store, filtering results to only entities within the specified scope. However, individual-resource operations (Read, Update, Patch, Delete) must ALSO enforce scope — the target entity must belong to the scope specified in the URL path. Without scope enforcement on individual operations, the scoped URL is decorative: any entity of the correct type can be accessed, modified, or deleted through any scope's URL, as long as the caller knows the entity's ID. This creates multiple failure modes: **(a) Cross-scope data leakage:** Read returns entities regardless of which scope they belong to — `GET /parents/{parentB}/children/{child_in_parentA}` returns the child even though it belongs to a different parent. **(b) Silent scope reassignment:** Update (PUT) sets the scope field from the URL path (`entity.ScopeField = r.PathValue("scope_param")`) then saves — silently moving the entity from one scope to another without any existence check, permission check, or audit trail. **(c) Inconsistent scope mutation between PUT and PATCH:** PUT overwrites the scope field with the URL's value (moving the entity), while PATCH preserves the original scope field (since it reads the existing entity and only modifies declared patchable fields). Two operations on the same entity through the same scoped endpoint produce opposite scope behaviors. **(d) Cross-scope deletion:** Delete removes entities by ID regardless of scope membership — `DELETE /parents/{parentB}/children/{child_in_parentA}` deletes the child and fires post-mutation side effects (fan-outs, audit logs) as if the operation occurred within parentB's scope. **The root cause is structural:** the `Storage` interface defines `Get(entity, id)`, `Replace(entity, id, value)`, and `Delete(entity, id)` with no scope parameters, while `List` has `scopeField` and `scopeValue`. Handlers cannot enforce scope on individual operations because the storage contract does not support it. **The fix requires changes at two layers:** (a) **Storage interface:** add scope parameters to `Get`, `Replace`, and `Delete` — e.g. `Get(ctx, entity, id, scopeField, scopeValue)` — so the store can add `WHERE id = ? AND scope_field = ?` to individual queries; (b) **Handler:** pass the URL path's scope value to the store for every operation, not just List. An alternative minimal fix: after `store.Get`, verify the returned entity's scope field matches the URL's scope value before proceeding — `if returnedEntity.ScopeField != r.PathValue("scope_param") { return NotFound(...) }`. **The `checkAncestors` method is necessary but insufficient:** it verifies the parent entity exists, which prevents operations against non-existent scopes. But it does not verify the target child entity belongs to that parent's scope. Parent existence is a prerequisite for scope enforcement, not a substitute for it. **The principle: a scoped URL path is a claim — "this resource exists within this scope." Every operation through a scoped endpoint must validate this claim. If the claim is not validated, the scope is not enforced, and the URL hierarchy is misleading. A scoped collection that only scopes List but not Read/Update/Delete provides a false sense of containment — the scope boundary is permeable for any operation that targets a specific entity by ID.**
   212. **Conditional code paths within the same operation must preserve response-observable parity — check implementations must not alter the response object:** When a generator introduces a conditional branch within a single operation's handler (e.g., scoped vs. unscoped Read, authenticated vs. unauthenticated, parent-entity vs. parentless), the branch MUST preserve the client-observable response shape (field set, field types, included metadata) identically to the non-conditional branch for all aspects NOT explicitly governed by the condition. The condition adds behavior (e.g., a scope check that may return 404); it must not subtract behavior (e.g., dropping metadata fields from the response). A common error pattern: the conditional branch needs to inspect a single field on the value (e.g., verify the scope field matches the URL's scope parameter) and introduces a type conversion (JSON marshal → unmarshal into a typed struct) to extract that field. The type conversion has the side-effect of discarding fields present on the original storage-layer value but absent from the target API struct — storage metadata like `created_time`, `updated_time` is lost. The original (non-conditional) code path returns the raw `store.Get()` result directly, preserving all fields. The conditional path returns the re-typed value, missing metadata. A client reading the same entity through a scoped URL receives a different field set than through an unscoped URL — an inconsistency that propagates through both bare and envelope response modes. **The fix pattern:** when a conditional branch needs to inspect a field on an `any`-typed value without altering the response, use a technique that extracts the needed field WITHOUT transforming the response object: (a) **`map[string]any` extraction** — unmarshal the value into `map[string]any` for the field lookup, then encode the ORIGINAL `existing` value (not the map) for the response; (b) **JSON field extraction via `json.Marshal` + partial unmarshal** — marshal the original value to JSON bytes, unmarshal only the needed field into a small struct or map, perform the check, then encode the original value for the response; (c) **Reflection** — use `reflect.ValueOf(existing)` to read the field by name without type conversion. All three approaches leave the original value untouched for the response. **The mandatory audit when adding a conditional branch within an operation handler:** (a) identify the response expression in BOTH branches — what variable is passed to the response encoder or presenter function? (b) if the conditional branch passes a DIFFERENT variable (or a re-typed copy of the original), trace both variables' types and field sets — are they identical? If not, fields visible in one response but not the other are a parity violation; (c) when both branches should produce identical responses, verify both pass the SAME variable (or equivalent value) to the response encoder; (d) write a test that generates handler code for a scoped entity, extracts the response-construction code from both branches (scoped and unscoped), and asserts both encode the same variable — or at minimum, asserts both responses for the same entity contain identical field sets regardless of which URL path was used. **The relationship to other items:** item 37 covers shared semantics across DIFFERENT operations (create vs. update vs. patch). This item covers shared output semantics across CONDITIONAL VARIANTS of the SAME operation (scoped Read vs. unscoped Read). Item 39 covers spec/code consistency for a single code path. This item covers code/code consistency between two branches of the same code path. Item 208 covers cross-package type assertions on `any` values — the JSON roundtrip approach described in the finding is often used to AVOID a direct type assertion, but it introduces the field-loss side-effect this item addresses. **The principle: a conditional branch exists to ADD a check, not to TRANSFORM the response. When the check requires inspecting a field on the value, the inspection must be non-destructive — extract the data you need for the check without altering the data you return to the client. A scope check that changes the response shape has conflated two concerns (access control and response construction) into a single code path, and the access-control concern has corrupted the response-construction concern. Separate them: check scope using a read-only technique, then return the original value unchanged.**
   213. **Import-tracking flags must be re-audited whenever a new standard library call is added to a conditional handler path in a generator:** When a generator uses boolean flags (e.g., `needJSON`, `needFmt`, `needLog`) to conditionally include `import` statements based on which operations or features are enabled, adding a NEW standard library call to ANY generated code path REQUIRES re-evaluating the flag-setting logic for ALL configurations that can reach the new code. A common error pattern: a cross-cutting feature addition (e.g., scope verification) introduces `json.Marshal` and `json.Unmarshal` calls into a handler method (e.g., the Delete handler) whose operation type (`OpDelete`) does not set `needJSON = true`, because the original handler did not use JSON. The new code compiles when tested alongside other operations (which independently set `needJSON`), but a configuration with ONLY that operation (e.g., a scoped delete-only collection) generates code referencing `json.Marshal`/`json.Unmarshal` without importing `encoding/json` — a hard compile error. The test for the new feature uses `strings.Contains` to verify the scope verification code is present but does not compile the generated output, so the missing import passes the test suite. **The mechanical audit when adding any standard library call to generated handler code:** (a) identify which import flag gates the package being used (e.g., `encoding/json` gated by `needJSON`, `fmt` gated by `needFmt`); (b) trace the flag-setting logic and enumerate ALL conditions under which the flag is currently set; (c) determine whether the new code path is reachable under a configuration where the flag is NOT set — specifically, can the operation type, scope setting, or feature combination that triggers the new code path occur WITHOUT any of the existing flag-setting conditions? If so, update the flag-setting logic to include the new condition (e.g., `if op != types.OpDelete || isScoped { needJSON = true }`); (d) apply item 38: write or update a degenerate-configuration test that exercises the new code path IN ISOLATION (e.g., scoped collection with only `[OpDelete]`) and verify the generated output compiles — do NOT rely on `strings.Contains` alone (per item 31, use actual compilation); (e) apply item 33 (fix-class exhaustion): after identifying that a cross-cutting feature adds a package dependency, check ALL operations modified by the feature and verify the import flag accounts for each one. **The pattern signature for grep-based audit:** search the generator for all import-flag-setting assignments (e.g., `needJSON = true`, `needFmt = true`) and for every conditionally-emitted standard library call (`json.Marshal`, `json.Unmarshal`, `fmt.Sprintf`, `fmt.Fprintf`, `log.Printf`). For each emitted call, trace backward to verify at least one flag-setting condition is guaranteed to be true for every configuration that reaches the call. If a call is reachable without any flag-setting condition, the import will be missing for that configuration. **The key insight:** import-tracking flags encode "which operations need which packages" at the time the flags were written. When a cross-cutting feature (like scope verification) adds package dependencies to operations that previously did not need them, the flag logic is stale — it reflects the pre-feature dependency graph and must be updated to reflect the post-feature graph. This is particularly dangerous for features that modify ALL operations uniformly (scope checks added to Read, Update, Patch, AND Delete), because the feature author tests with a multi-operation entity (where at least one other operation sets the flag) and never encounters the missing-import bug — it only manifests in degenerate configurations where the newly-dependent operation is the ONLY operation.
   214. **Acceptance criteria containing literal shell commands must be executed verbatim as self-verification:** When acceptance criteria specify exact shell commands (e.g. "`cd out && go build` compiles the generated service"), those commands MUST be executed literally as part of self-verification — verbatim, in the exact working directory, with the exact arguments. A command that achieves the same compilation goal from a different working directory or with different arguments (e.g. `go build ./out/cmd/` from the project root, or `cd out && go build ./...` instead of `cd out && go build`) may succeed where the AC command fails, masking structural issues like a missing `go.mod` in the expected location, missing Go files at the expected directory root, or an incorrect module layout. Item 9 specifies a generic compile check (`go build ./...`); this item requires ADDITIONALLY running the EXACT commands stated in the AC text. After reading the task's acceptance criteria, extract every shell command or command sequence and run each one literally as written. If the command fails, the AC is not satisfied — regardless of whether an adapted version of the command succeeds. If the exact command cannot work due to architectural constraints (e.g. the module root must encompass directories outside the specified build directory), this is itself a finding that must be surfaced and resolved — not silently worked around by substituting a different command. **The principle: the AC command is the specification. It defines not just the intent ("the code compiles") but the structural precondition ("the code compiles from THIS directory with THIS command"). An adapted command that passes is not evidence the AC is satisfied — it is evidence the adapter works. Only the literal command proves the AC.**
   215. **Error format consistency across all architectural layers — handlers, middleware, and infrastructure:** When the spec mandates a specific error response format for all errors (e.g. RFC 9457 Problem Details with `application/problem+json`), ALL code paths that produce HTTP error responses MUST use that format — not just handler-level error returns. This includes: (a) authentication/authorization middleware rejecting unauthenticated or unauthorized requests (401, 403); (b) request validation middleware rejecting malformed input (400); (c) rate limiting or quota enforcement middleware (429); (d) any infrastructure-level error path (503, 502). A common pattern that violates this: auth middleware in a separate package uses `http.Error(w, "message", statusCode)`, which sets `Content-Type: text/plain` and returns a bare string body, while all handler errors use `application/problem+json` with structured RFC 9457 fields. The same service returns different error formats depending on which layer rejects the request — clients cannot parse errors consistently. After implementing error handling, enumerate EVERY code path that can produce an HTTP error response by grepping for `http.Error`, `w.WriteHeader`, and direct response writes in ALL packages — not just handler packages. For each error-producing code path, verify it produces the spec-mandated error format. If a middleware package cannot import the handler's error-formatting utilities (e.g. they are in a different package with circular import risks), either refactor the error utilities into a shared package accessible to both handlers and middleware, or replicate the format faithfully in the middleware package. Do not fall back to `text/plain` as the path of least resistance. Write a test or perform manual audit that traces every possible HTTP error status code the service can return, identifies which package/layer produces it, and verifies the response body format is consistent across all of them. **The principle: an error response format is a service-level contract, not a per-layer contract. Clients parse error responses without knowing which internal layer produced them — if handler errors are JSON and middleware errors are plain text, the client needs two parsers and no way to predict which one to use. The spec says "RFC 9457 for all error responses" — the word "all" includes middleware.**
   216. **Type-to-schema mapping must preserve the source type's full value space — never narrow it:** When mapping spec-defined types to generated specification schemas (e.g. stego types to OpenAPI schema types), the generated schema MUST accept the same value space as the source type — not a narrower subset. If the spec's type system defines a type as accepting a broad value space (e.g. `jsonb` representing any valid JSON value — objects, arrays, strings, numbers, booleans, null), the generated OpenAPI schema must also accept any valid JSON value. Mapping `jsonb` to `{"type": "object"}` rejects valid arrays (`["a", "b"]`), strings (`"hello"`), numbers (`42`), and booleans (`true`) — the schema is narrower than the type it represents. The correct mapping for a type that accepts any JSON value is an empty schema `{}` (which accepts any JSON value per OpenAPI/JSON Schema rules) or a schema with no `type` constraint. When the validation middleware validates request bodies against the generated OpenAPI schema, a narrowed schema causes the middleware to reject valid input before the handler ever sees it — the client sends a valid `jsonb` value, and the middleware returns 400. Item 35 covers exhaustive handling of field attributes in generated specs; this item extends that principle to the TYPE MAPPING itself — the `type` field in the generated schema must reflect the source type's complete semantics, not just its most common usage pattern. After writing any type-mapping function for generated specs: (a) enumerate every spec type; (b) for each type, determine the full value space the spec defines for it; (c) verify the generated schema accepts exactly that value space — neither broader (allowing values the spec disallows) nor narrower (rejecting values the spec allows); (d) pay particular attention to polymorphic or union types (`jsonb`, `any`, `mixed`) whose value space spans multiple JSON Schema types — these MUST map to schemas without a `type` constraint, or use a union schema (`oneOf`, `anyOf`); (e) write a test for each broad-value-space type verifying the generated schema does not include a restrictive `type` constraint. **The principle: a type-to-schema mapping is a translation, not an approximation. The schema is a promise to API consumers about what values are accepted. A schema that narrows the source type's value space breaks that promise — valid data is rejected. The most common error pattern is mapping a "any JSON" type to `"type": "object"` because objects are the most frequent usage, but the type explicitly allows all JSON value types. Map what the type IS, not what it's usually used for.**
   217. **Fixes to generated output must be applied in the generator — never hand-edit files under `out/` or any directory carrying a `DO NOT EDIT` header:** When a review finding identifies a defect in generated output (e.g. missing type conversions in handler files, incorrect response shapes, wrong imports), the fix MUST be applied in the generator that produces the output (`internal/generator/*/generator.go`) — never directly in the generated files under `out/` or any other generated-output directory. Generated files carry a `// Code generated by stego. DO NOT EDIT.` header (spec.md:161) precisely because they are the OUTPUT of a deterministic pipeline. Hand-editing generated output creates three compounding failures: **(a) Non-reproducibility:** running `stego apply` overwrites the hand-edits with the generator's canonical output, reverting the fix. The task's own AC sequence (`stego validate && stego plan && stego apply`) becomes the mechanism that un-fixes the bug. **(b) False AC satisfaction:** the committed example output appears correct (the hand-edits address the finding), but the AC commands produce DIFFERENT output — the example is not reproducible from the declared inputs. AC #2 (`stego apply` succeeds) and AC #3 (`cd out && go build` compiles) may both independently pass, but together they cannot be satisfied: running apply reverts the edits, and the reverted output may not compile or may exhibit the original bug. **(c) Root cause persistence:** the generator still produces the defective output. Every future service compiled with `stego apply` will have the same bug — the fix only exists in the hand-edited example, not in the tool itself. **The mandatory self-verification after fixing any finding in generated output:** (a) identify the generator function responsible for the defective output — grep for the template text or variable names from the affected output file in the generator source; (b) apply the fix in the generator; (c) delete the example's `out/` directory; (d) run `stego apply` from the example directory to regenerate all output from scratch; (e) verify the regenerated output includes the fix — the fix must appear in the new output WITHOUT any hand-editing; (f) run the full AC command sequence (`stego validate && stego plan && stego apply && cd out && go build`) and verify all steps pass on the regenerated (not hand-edited) output; (g) commit the regenerated output alongside the generator change. If any step fails, the generator fix is incomplete — iterate on the generator, not on the output. **The grep-based audit for hand-edits in generated output:** after any `needs-revision` fix cycle, run `diff <(cd examples/user-management && stego apply --dry-run) out/` or equivalent to verify the committed output matches what the generator produces. Any divergence is a hand-edit that will be reverted by `stego apply`. **Item 164 (regenerate checked-in output when generators change) is the COMPLEMENT of this item:** item 164 says "when you change a generator, regenerate the output." This item says "when you need to change the output, change the generator." Together they enforce the invariant: committed generated output is ALWAYS the product of the current generator applied to the current inputs — never hand-edited, never stale. **The principle: the generator is the single source of truth for generated output. A hand-edit to generated output is a fork — the committed file diverges from the generator's canonical output, creating two versions of "what the tool produces" that will inevitably conflict. The `DO NOT EDIT` header is not a suggestion — it is a contract. If the output needs to change, the generator must change. If the generator cannot produce the needed output, that is a generator bug to be fixed in the generator, not a content bug to be patched in the output.**
   218. **Plan output accuracy must be verified by comparing plan predictions against actual apply results:** When a task's acceptance criteria include `stego plan` (e.g. "stego plan — shows expected changeset"), the plan output MUST be verified for accuracy by comparing its predictions against the actual changes `stego apply` produces. Running `stego plan` and confirming it "succeeds" (exits without error) does NOT satisfy an AC that says the plan "shows expected changeset" — the AC requires the plan to ACCURATELY REPORT what will change. A plan that reports "0 to generate, 2 to update, 0 to delete, 20 unchanged" when `stego apply` actually modifies 6 files has failed the AC even though it exited successfully. **The mandatory verification procedure:** (a) run `stego plan` and record the reported file counts (generate, update, delete, unchanged); (b) take a snapshot of the current `out/` directory state (e.g. `cp -r out/ out-before/`); (c) run `stego apply`; (d) compare the post-apply `out/` against the snapshot to determine the actual number of changed files (`diff -rq out-before/ out/ | wc -l`); (e) verify the plan's reported counts match the actual counts — specifically, files the plan reported as "unchanged" must actually be unchanged (identical content before and after apply), and files that actually changed must have been reported in the plan's "to update" or "to generate" count; (f) if the counts diverge, there is a bug in stego's state tracking or plan computation — this is a stego-internal issue that must be filed and fixed, not silently accepted. The implementation team should not mark a task as `ready-for-review` if the plan output contradicts reality, even if `stego apply` and `go build` both succeed. **When is this a stego bug vs. a task bug?** If the plan under-reports changes because the state file (`.stego/state.yaml`) records checksums of previously hand-edited files rather than the checksums the generator would produce, the root cause is hand-editing (item 217) and stale state. Regenerating from scratch (deleting `out/` and `.stego/` then running `stego apply`) will reset state to match the generator's canonical output, and subsequent `stego plan` runs will be accurate. If the plan still under-reports after a clean regeneration, the bug is in stego's plan computation and must be reported. **The principle: `stego plan` is a preview mechanism — it exists so users can see what `stego apply` will do before it does it. A plan that does not accurately predict the apply is a broken preview. The user trusts the plan to be truthful: "unchanged" means the file will not be modified. If the plan says "20 unchanged" but apply modifies 4 of those 20, the plan actively lied about 4 files. Verifying plan accuracy is not optional — it is the plan feature's core acceptance criterion.**
   219. **Error handling parity within the same operation pattern across different generator methods — identical code patterns must handle errors identically:** When a generator introduces the same code pattern (e.g. JSON marshal/unmarshal for storage→API type conversion) in multiple operation methods (e.g. Read and List), the error handling for that pattern must be consistent across ALL methods that use it. If the Read handler's type-conversion code checks `json.Marshal` and `json.Unmarshal` errors and returns `InternalError` on failure, the List handler's type-conversion code — which performs the identical operation for the identical purpose — must also check those errors. A silent error discard (`itemData, _ := json.Marshal(item)` and `json.Unmarshal(itemData, &apiItem)` with no error check) in the List handler, while the Read handler checks the same errors, is an inconsistency that signals one of two problems: either (a) the errors CAN occur and the List handler silently produces zero-valued entities on failure, or (b) the errors CANNOT occur and the Read handler's error checks are dead code. In either case, the two methods disagree about whether the operation can fail, and the generated code is internally inconsistent. **The audit when adding a code pattern to a generator method:** (a) grep the generator for the same pattern in other methods — `json.Marshal`, `json.Unmarshal`, `store.Get`, `store.Replace`, etc.; (b) for each occurrence, compare the error handling — does one check errors while another discards them? (c) if the pattern appears in multiple methods, standardize the error handling: either all check errors or none do (with a code comment explaining why the operation cannot fail); (d) pay particular attention to patterns added in fix cycles — a fix that adds error-checked code to one method but error-discarded code to another introduces an inconsistency that didn't exist before the fix.
   220. **Generated code must never discard error return values from fallible standard library functions:** When a generator emits code that calls a standard library function known to return an error (e.g. `json.Marshal`, `json.Unmarshal`, `json.NewDecoder(...).Decode`, `io.ReadAll`, `strconv.Atoi`, `fmt.Fprintf` to a network writer), the generated code MUST capture and handle the error — never discard it via blank identifier (`result, _ := fn()`) or uncaptured return value (`fn(args)` when `fn` returns `(T, error)` or `error`). Item 219 addresses the parity concern (comparing error handling across methods that share a pattern); this item establishes the **baseline rule**: there should be no discarded errors to compare against, because NO generated code path should discard errors from fallible functions — regardless of whether another method exists to compare against. The blank identifier on an error return in generated code is particularly dangerous because: (a) it creates a silent failure mode where downstream code receives zero values on error with no indication of the failure — e.g. `itemData, _ := json.Marshal(item)` produces `nil` on failure, and `json.Unmarshal(nil, &apiItem)` silently fails, leaving `apiItem` zero-valued with empty fields and no ID; (b) unlike application code where a developer might intentionally discard an error with full context about why it cannot fail, generated code runs across ALL service configurations — a marshal that "cannot fail" for one entity shape may fail for another with different field types or nesting patterns; (c) the error discard appears in a string template (`fmt.Fprintf(buf, "..., _ := ...")`) which makes it invisible to Go linters like `errcheck` that would catch the same pattern in direct code. **The audit after writing any `Fprintf` that emits a function call into generated code:** (a) check whether the emitted function returns an error — consult the standard library documentation if unsure; (b) if it does, the emitted code must capture the error and handle it (e.g. check `err != nil` and return an appropriate error response); (c) after completing a generator method, grep the method's `Fprintf` calls for the patterns `, _ :=`, `_ :=`, and bare function calls without return capture, cross-referencing each against the function's return signature; (d) the heuristic "this function cannot fail for our use case" is NOT justification for discarding — generated code must be defensive because the generator cannot predict all entity shapes it will process. **The principle: in generated code, every fallible function call is a potential failure point across the entire configuration space. Discarding an error is a bet that the function will never fail for ANY input — a bet the generator cannot make because it does not control the inputs.**
   221. **Wiring expressions must thread ALL spec-defined runtime configuration sources — env vars and builder methods must not be baked at generation time:** When a spec defines runtime configuration sources (environment variables, config files, feature flags) that the generated `main.go` must read at startup, the generator's `Wiring` must ensure those sources are consumed at runtime — not baked into the generated code at generation time. A common error pattern: the spec's "Environment Variables" section defines `FOO_URL` as a runtime override for a config default, the spec's "Wiring" section says "main.go reads FOO_URL from environment," but the generator reads the config default at generation time and hardcodes it into the constructor (e.g. `NewHandler().Build()` with the URL baked into the struct literal), never emitting code that calls `os.Getenv("FOO_URL")`. The generated code compiles and works with the default, but the runtime override is silently impossible — the env var has no effect. Similarly, when a builder pattern offers methods like `WithKeysFile(...)` and `WithKeysURL(...)` but the wiring constructor is hardcoded as `NewHandler().Build()` with no chaining, the builder methods are dead code — structurally present but unreachable. This is a specific instance of item 1 (dead feature scaffolding) combined with item 6 (full task traceability), but neither item explicitly flags the **generation-time vs. runtime boundary** as the locus of the bug. **The mandatory audit after implementing any component with runtime configuration:** (a) read the spec's "Environment Variables" section (or equivalent) and list EVERY env var the spec defines; (b) read the spec's "Wiring" section and list every runtime configuration action it describes (e.g. "main.go reads X, Y, Z from environment"); (c) for each env var and wiring action, grep the generator's output (including the wiring constructor expression) for `os.Getenv("VAR_NAME")` or the equivalent runtime-reading mechanism — if the grep returns zero hits, the env var is not wired; (d) for each builder method the generator emits (e.g. `WithKeysURL`, `WithKeysFile`), verify the wiring constructor expression chains it (e.g. `NewHandler().WithKeysURL(os.Getenv("FOO_URL")).Build()`) — a builder method that exists in the generated code but is never called in the wiring is dead feature scaffolding; (e) verify the wiring correctly implements the spec's priority logic (e.g. "file overrides URL if set" means the wiring must conditionally chain `.WithKeysFile(...)` only when the env var is non-empty); (f) write a test that verifies the wiring constructor expression contains all expected env var reads and builder method chains. **The key distinction:** component config fields (e.g. `jwk_cert_url` with a default value in `component.yaml`) provide generation-time defaults. Environment variables provide runtime overrides. The generator must thread BOTH: the config default goes into the generated struct literal or `NewHandler()` call, and the env var override goes into the wiring chain that wraps or reconfigures the default at startup. Baking only the config default and ignoring the env var override means the generated service cannot be reconfigured without regenerating — which violates the 12-factor principle the spec's env var section exists to support. **The principle: if the spec defines an environment variable, the generated code must read it at runtime. If the spec defines a builder method, the wiring must call it. A generator that emits runtime configuration mechanisms (builder methods, env var names) but never wires them is producing dead feature scaffolding at the architectural level — the feature exists in the generated library code but is unreachable from the application entry point.**
   222. **Generated concurrent code must hold locks for the entire check-then-act sequence — no TOCTOU races on mutex-protected state:** When a generator emits code that uses `sync.Mutex` or `sync.RWMutex` to protect shared state, every check-then-act sequence on that state MUST hold the lock continuously from the check through the act — releasing the lock between the check and the act creates a Time-of-Check-to-Time-of-Use (TOCTOU) race. A common error pattern: a cooldown check acquires `Lock()`, reads `lastRefresh`, determines the cooldown has expired, calls `Unlock()`, then calls a refresh function that re-acquires `Lock()` later to update `lastRefresh`. Between the `Unlock()` and the re-acquisition of `Lock()` inside the refresh function, any number of concurrent goroutines can also pass the cooldown check and initiate parallel operations — exactly the "storm" the cooldown is designed to prevent. The spec says "A 30-second cooldown prevents refresh storms," but the TOCTOU gap means N concurrent goroutines with unknown `kid` values all pass the cooldown check simultaneously and all call refresh in parallel. **The mandatory audit for generated concurrent code:** (a) identify every `Lock()`/`Unlock()` pair in the generated code; (b) for each pair, determine what state the lock protects (e.g. `lastRefresh`, `keys` map); (c) trace every read of the protected state and verify the read occurs while the lock is held; (d) for every conditional branch that reads protected state and then acts on it (check-then-act), verify the lock is held continuously from the read through the completion of the act — the `Unlock()` must come AFTER the act, not between the check and the act; (e) specifically for cooldown/rate-limiting patterns: the `lastRefresh = time.Now()` update (or equivalent timestamp/counter update) MUST occur within the same critical section as the `time.Since(lastRefresh)` check — if the check and update are in different critical sections, concurrent goroutines can interleave between them; (f) for the pattern where a helper function (e.g. `refreshKeys()`) needs to run under the same lock as the caller (e.g. `tryRefresh()`), either: (i) have the caller hold the lock and pass it through (the helper assumes the lock is already held and does NOT re-acquire it), or (ii) combine the check and act into a single function that acquires the lock once, checks, acts, and releases; (g) write a test (or trace manually) that demonstrates the race: given N goroutines calling `tryRefresh()` simultaneously at t=0 (when `lastRefresh` is stale), verify that exactly 1 refresh occurs, not N. If the lock is released between check and act, all N goroutines pass the check. **The principle: a mutex protects a critical section, not individual statements. Acquiring a lock, reading state, releasing the lock, and then acting on the stale read is equivalent to reading without the lock at all — the state can change between the read and the act. The entire check-then-act must be atomic with respect to the lock. When the spec says "cooldown prevents storms," the implementation must make it impossible for multiple actors to pass the cooldown check simultaneously — and that requires the lock to span the entire sequence from check through state update.**
