   223. **Generator must consume every config field declared in component.yaml — no silent config discards:** When a component's `component.yaml` declares config fields (e.g. `jwk_cert_url`, `jwk_cert_file`, `public_paths`), the generator MUST read and act on EVERY declared config field from `ctx.ComponentConfig`. A config field that is declared in the component schema but never read by the generator is dead metadata at the config level — service authors can set the field in their service declaration, but the value is silently discarded with no effect on the generated output. This is distinct from item 66 (which covers struct fields populated but never read by downstream consumers) and item 221 (which covers env var overrides not wired at runtime): this item covers the **component config → generator consumption** path specifically. Item 66 addresses data model types consumed by generators; item 221 addresses runtime env var wiring. Neither explicitly mandates that every field declared in the component's YAML schema is consumed by the generator that owns that component. **The mandatory audit after implementing any component generator:** (a) open the component's `component.yaml` and list every field under `config:`; (b) for each config field, grep the generator source for `ComponentConfig` accesses that read that field name (e.g. `ctx.ComponentConfig["jwk_cert_file"]`); (c) if a config field is not read, the generator is silently discarding user-provided configuration — either wire it into the generated code (baked as a generation-time default, threaded through a builder method, or emitted as a runtime configuration source) or remove the field from `component.yaml` if it is truly unused; (d) verify that config fields with equivalent semantics are handled consistently — if `jwk_cert_url` is read from `ComponentConfig` and baked into a builder method default, then `jwk_cert_file` (which has the same semantic role for a different config source) must receive the same treatment; (e) write a test that sets a non-default value for EVERY config field and verifies the generated output reflects each value. **The principle: a component's config schema is a contract with service authors — every declared field is a promise that the value will have an effect. A field that is declared but never consumed is a broken promise. The generator is the sole consumer of `ComponentConfig`; if the generator doesn't read a field, no one does.**
   224. **Co-specified configuration parameters must be processed at the same lifecycle stage — no mixed startup/per-request handling:** When the spec groups multiple configuration parameters together in a single section (e.g. "Environment Variables: `JWK_CERT_URL`, `JWK_CERT_FILE`, `AUTH_ENABLED`") and describes them as being read at the same lifecycle stage (e.g. "main.go reads X, Y, and Z from environment"), the generated code MUST process ALL of them at that same lifecycle stage. A common error pattern: two of three env vars are read once at startup in a `Build()` or constructor function, but the third is read per-request inside a middleware closure via `os.Getenv(...)`. This creates three classes of inconsistency: (a) **behavioral asymmetry** — changing the first two env vars requires a restart (because they are read at startup), but changing the third takes effect immediately (because it is read per-request); the spec does not distinguish between them, so the user cannot predict which behavior applies; (b) **resource waste** — when the per-request check controls a feature gate (e.g. `AUTH_ENABLED=false` should make the middleware a no-op passthrough), but the startup-time resource initialization (e.g. a background key refresh goroutine) has already been launched unconditionally, the "disabled" state still consumes resources; (c) **semantic mismatch with the spec's language** — the spec says the middleware is "a passthrough" when disabled, implying a structural decision at startup (return a no-op middleware), not a per-request conditional branch (check the env var on every request and skip auth if false). **The mandatory audit after implementing configuration handling for a set of related parameters:** (a) read the spec section that defines the configuration parameters and note the lifecycle stage it describes (startup, per-request, hot-reload, etc.); (b) enumerate every parameter in that section and identify WHERE in the generated code each one is read — constructor/`Build()` (startup), middleware closure (per-request), or config watcher (hot-reload); (c) if any parameter is read at a different lifecycle stage than the spec describes or than its siblings in the same section, it is a lifecycle inconsistency — move it to the correct stage; (d) specifically for feature gates that control whether infrastructure is initialized (e.g. `AUTH_ENABLED` controls whether a key refresh goroutine starts): the gate MUST be checked at startup before the infrastructure is created, and when the gate is false, the infrastructure must not be created at all — not created and then conditionally bypassed per-request; (e) write a test that verifies all parameters in the group are read at the same lifecycle stage (e.g. all are read in `Build()`, none are read inside the middleware closure). **The principle: when the spec groups configuration parameters together and describes them as being read at the same stage, that grouping is intentional — it defines a uniform lifecycle contract. Mixed lifecycle handling creates unpredictable behavior for operators: some config changes require a restart, others take effect immediately, and the spec gives no indication of which is which. Uniform lifecycle handling makes the operational contract clear and predictable.**
   225. **Verification tasks must evidence each content assertion by inspecting actual generated output — tool success is not content confirmation:** When a task includes numbered verification steps that assert specific content in generated output files (e.g. "Verify generated `out/main.go` contains: Environment variable reading (JWK_CERT_URL, JWK_CERT_FILE, AUTH_ENABLED)"), the engineer MUST confirm each assertion by reading or grepping the actual generated file — not by inferring correctness from the fact that the generation tool exited successfully. `stego apply` succeeding means the generator ran without errors; it does NOT mean the generated code's internal structure matches the spec's Wiring section. A common error pattern: the engineer runs `stego apply`, sees it succeed, runs `go build ./...`, sees it compile, and marks all verification steps as satisfied — without ever opening the generated `main.go` to check whether it actually contains `os.Getenv("JWK_CERT_URL")`. The code compiles because the env vars ARE read somewhere (inside `Build()` in `middleware.go`), but the spec says they must be read in `main.go` — a structural requirement that compilation cannot verify. **The mandatory audit for every verification step in a task:** (a) for each verification step that says "verify file X contains Y," open file X and grep for Y — do not skip this because the build succeeded; (b) for structural assertions (e.g. "main.go reads env vars," "middleware.go contains builder pattern"), verify the assertion is true in the SPECIFIC file named, not just true somewhere in the generated output — the spec's Wiring section assigns responsibilities to specific files for architectural reasons (main.go owns configuration, middleware.go owns request processing); (c) for each verification step, record the evidence: the specific file, the specific lines that satisfy the assertion — if you cannot point to lines in the named file, the step is not satisfied regardless of whether the behavior exists elsewhere; (d) when the spec's Wiring section says "main.go reads X from environment," that is a structural contract — the env var read MUST appear in main.go's source, not delegated inside a library function that main.go calls; `os.Getenv("X")` must be lexically present in main.go or in the wiring expression the assembler emits into main.go. **The principle: a verification step is a content assertion about a specific artifact, not a behavioral assertion about the system. "Verify main.go contains X" means X is in main.go — not "X happens somewhere when main.go runs." Compilation proves syntactic validity; verification steps prove structural compliance with the spec. They are different claims and require different evidence. Running `go build` and marking verification steps as passed is like running `go test` and marking code review as done — the tool checks one dimension while the verification step asserts on another.**
   226. **Spec-provided input→output examples are mandatory test cases — algorithm correctness is measured by example reproduction, not plausible behavior:** When the spec defines a transformation (e.g. entity name → URL path segment, type name → Go type, field name → column name) and provides concrete input→output examples alongside or instead of an algorithmic description, EVERY example MUST be encoded as a test case with the spec's exact input and the spec's exact expected output. The implementation team MUST NOT substitute algorithmically plausible outputs for the spec's stated outputs. A common error pattern: the spec describes a transformation in general terms ("pluralized, lowercased") and provides three examples (`Cluster` → `clusters`, `NodePool` → `nodepools`, `AdapterStatus` → `statuses`). The engineer implements an algorithm that matches the general description and tests it against inputs that happen to work — but never tests the algorithm against ALL of the spec's concrete examples. The algorithm produces `adapterstatuses` for `AdapterStatus`, which seems plausible given the description "pluralized, lowercased" — but the spec explicitly shows the output as `statuses`, which implies the algorithm requires PascalCase word-splitting before pluralization of the last word. The test encodes the algorithm's incorrect output as the expected value instead of the spec's stated output. **The mandatory audit after implementing any transformation with spec-provided examples:** (a) re-read the spec section that defines the transformation and extract EVERY input→output example — these examples exist in comments (e.g. `# path derived: /clusters/{cluster_id}/statuses`), in prose (e.g. "entity `AdapterStatus` → `statuses`"), and in acceptance criteria; (b) for each example, write a test case with the spec's EXACT input and the spec's EXACT expected output — do not compute the expected output from your algorithm and then use that as the test expectation; (c) run the tests — if any fail, your algorithm is wrong, even if it seems like a reasonable interpretation of the general description; the spec's examples are authoritative and override any general description; (d) when the spec's examples reveal that the general description is ambiguous or insufficient (e.g. the description says "lowercased, pluralized" but the examples show word-splitting behavior), the examples define the required behavior — if your algorithm cannot reproduce the examples, change the algorithm, not the test expectations; (e) if two spec examples contradict each other or the general description is irreconcilable with the examples, flag the ambiguity for the spec author — do not silently resolve it by choosing the interpretation your algorithm happens to implement. **Additionally, when a transformation is tested, the test table MUST include ALL spec examples, not a subset.** A test table that includes `Cluster` → `clusters` and `NodePool` → `nodepools` but omits `AdapterStatus` → `statuses` provides false confidence — the two passing tests suggest the algorithm is correct when it is actually wrong for the omitted case. The spec's examples are chosen to illustrate edge cases and boundary conditions; omitting any of them defeats their purpose. After writing any test table for a spec-defined transformation, enumerate every example in the spec and verify each one appears in the test table with the spec's exact expected output. **The principle: spec examples are acceptance tests, not illustrations. They define the contract between the spec and the implementation. An algorithm that matches the general description but fails a concrete example is wrong — the example is more specific than the description and takes precedence. Tests that encode algorithmically-derived expectations instead of spec-stated expectations cannot catch this class of error because they validate the algorithm against itself.**
   227. **Map iteration feeding ordered output must sort keys first — Go map iteration is non-deterministic:** When iterating a Go `map` and the iteration order affects any observable output — appending to a slice, emitting generated code, writing file content, constructing ordered data structures, or producing user-visible messages — the map keys MUST be sorted before iteration. Go's `map` type has intentionally randomized iteration order (per the Go specification: "The iteration order over maps is not specified and is not guaranteed to be the same from one iteration to the next"). Code that ranges over a map and appends results to a slice, emits lines to a buffer, or builds an ordered list produces non-deterministic output: the same input yields different output across runs. This causes three classes of problem: (a) **test flakiness** — tests that assert on ordered output (slice contents, generated file content, error messages listing multiple items) pass or fail depending on map iteration luck; (b) **spurious diffs** — generated files that should be identical across runs differ only in ordering, triggering false-positive drift detection and confusing `git diff`; (c) **debugging difficulty** — non-deterministic ordering makes it impossible to reproduce issues reliably or compare outputs across runs. A pre-existing codebase that iterates only slices and deterministic sources is REGRESSED by introducing a map iteration that feeds into an ordered output — the non-determinism is new and is a defect introduced by the change. **The mandatory audit after writing any `for ... range` loop over a map:** (a) determine whether the loop body produces ordered output — does it `append` to a slice, `Fprintf` to a buffer, write to a file, build an error message, or feed into any downstream consumer that preserves insertion order? (b) if yes, extract the map keys into a slice, sort the slice, and iterate the sorted slice instead of the map directly; (c) the standard pattern is: `keys := make([]string, 0, len(m)); for k := range m { keys = append(keys, k) }; sort.Strings(keys); for _, k := range keys { v := m[k]; ... }`; (d) apply item 33 (fix-class exhaustion): after finding one map-iteration-order bug, grep the entire module for ALL `for .* range` loops over map types and verify each one either (i) feeds into an unordered consumer (another map, a set, a boolean check) where order is irrelevant, or (ii) sorts keys before iterating; (e) write a test that runs the function multiple times and asserts the output is identical across runs — non-deterministic iteration will eventually produce a different order, failing the test. For the specific case of maps derived from user-facing configuration (e.g. `svcDecl.Overrides` which is `map[string]any`), sorting is especially critical because the user sees the output (component ordering, error messages) and expects it to be stable and predictable. **The principle: Go maps are intentionally non-deterministic. Any code path from a map iteration to an ordered output is a determinism bug. The fix is always the same: sort keys first. There are no exceptions — if the output is ordered, the input iteration must be ordered. Assuming "it doesn't matter" or "there's usually only one key" is not a justification; the code must be correct for all valid inputs, including maps with multiple keys whose iteration order varies.**
   229. **Validation logic that applies to ALL inputs must not be nested inside a conditional that covers only a SUBSET of inputs:** When a validation check applies to every instance of a concept (e.g. "every override component must provide the overridden port"), the check MUST execute for ALL instances — not just those that happen to enter a specific conditional branch. A common error pattern: validation is placed inside an `if !exists` block (e.g. "if the override component is not yet in the active set, load it and validate it"), which means the validation runs only for newly loaded instances and is skipped entirely when the instance is already present. The already-present instance may not satisfy the invariant (e.g. a component already in the active set that does NOT provide the overridden port), and the missing validation allows the invalid state to proceed uncaught. The downstream error IS caught later by a different phase (e.g. binding resolution), but: (a) the error message attributes the problem to the wrong cause (blaming the requiring component rather than identifying the invalid override), (b) the archetype default has been unnecessarily removed from the active set by the time the error surfaces, and (c) the user cannot distinguish "I referenced a wrong component" from "the component doesn't provide this port" because the error lost its override context. **The mandatory audit when placing validation inside a conditional block:** (a) ask: "does this validation apply to ALL instances of the concept, or only to instances that enter this branch?" — if the validation protects an invariant of the concept itself (e.g. "override components must provide the overridden port"), it applies to ALL instances; (b) if the validation applies universally, move it OUTSIDE the conditional or DUPLICATE it so both branches validate — specifically, if a block validates newly loaded instances, add the same validation for pre-existing instances AFTER the conditional; (c) trace the consequences of skipping the validation: what state does the code enter when an invalid pre-existing instance passes without the check? Does the invalid state cause a downstream error with a misleading message? Does it corrupt shared state (e.g. deleting the archetype default from the active set before confirming the override is valid)?; (d) write a test for the pre-existing-instance case that provides an input which IS in the active set but does NOT satisfy the invariant the validation checks, and verify the error is caught with a message identifying the actual problem (the invalid override, not the downstream consumer); (e) apply item 33 (fix-class exhaustion): after finding one validation nested inside a subset-conditional, grep the function for all other validations and verify each one executes for the full input space, not just a branch. **The structural heuristic:** when you see the pattern `if !exists { load; validate; store } else { /* no validation */ }`, ask: "is the validation tied to the loading, or to the concept?" If the validation is "component must provide port X," it is tied to the concept of overriding port X — NOT to the act of loading. The loading is a side effect of the component being absent; the validation is a precondition of the override being valid. Conflating the two means absent-and-invalid is caught but present-and-invalid is not. **The principle: a validation check that runs only inside a conditional branch implicitly assumes that inputs entering the other branch are already valid. This assumption must be verified — it is NOT safe by default. A component being "already in the active set" means it was loaded for a different purpose (the archetype included it); it does NOT mean it provides the port being overridden. Presence is not validity. When validation is tied to a concept (all overrides must be valid), it must execute for all instances of that concept — not just the instances that happen to need loading.**
   230. **Integration tests for callback/loader/strategy parameters must exercise the callback through the actual caller — not just in unit isolation:** When a function accepts a callback, loader, strategy, or any injectable behavior (e.g. `ComponentLoader func(string) *Component` on `ResolveInput`), and the function's caller (e.g. the reconciler) is responsible for providing the real implementation of that callback, tests MUST include an integration test that exercises the FULL path: caller → function → callback → callback side effects. A unit test that directly populates the callback in a test fixture (e.g. setting `ComponentLoader` in `resolve_test.go`) proves the function uses the callback correctly in isolation. But it does NOT prove that the caller (a) provides the callback, (b) provides a correct implementation, or (c) handles the callback's side effects (e.g. the modified active component set returned by the resolver). The integration test must construct the CALLER's input (e.g. a service declaration with overrides referencing components NOT in the archetype's component list), invoke the CALLER (e.g. `Reconcile()`), and verify the END-TO-END behavior (e.g. the override component was loaded from the registry, the archetype default was excluded, and downstream generators received the correct active component set). **The mandatory audit when a function acquires a new callback/loader parameter:** (a) identify every caller of the function — grep for the function name across the codebase; (b) for each caller, determine whether a test exists that exercises the caller with an input that triggers the callback — not an input where the callback is unnecessary (e.g. the override component is already pre-loaded in the active set); (c) if no such test exists, write one — the test must use an input that REQUIRES the callback to fire (e.g. an override referencing a component NOT in the archetype's component list), and the test must assert on the end-to-end result (e.g. the override component appears in the generated output, the archetype default does not); (d) when acceptance criteria specify end-to-end behavior through the caller (e.g. "stego plan and stego validate work on the example where auth-provider is overridden to rh-sso-auth"), the integration test is a DIRECT requirement of the AC — the unit test covers the mechanism, the integration test covers the contract. **The distinction from existing checklist items:** item 11 covers end-to-end tests for behavioral requirements in general. Item 95 covers cross-generator wiring contracts via compilation. Item 108 covers struct literal completeness at integration boundaries. None of these specifically addresses the callback/loader pattern where the unit test exercises the callback in isolation but the integration test (through the caller) is missing — leaving the caller→callback wiring untested. The gap is especially dangerous when the caller's test pre-loads the data the callback would provide (e.g. adding the override component to `baselineComponents` in the reconciler test), making the callback path unreachable in tests while appearing to test the override feature. **The principle: a callback parameter creates a contract between the function and its caller. The function promises to call the callback under certain conditions; the caller promises to provide a correct implementation. Unit tests verify the function's half of the contract; integration tests verify the caller's half. Without the integration test, the caller could pass `nil` for the callback, pre-load data that makes the callback unnecessary, or provide a callback that returns wrong results — and all unit tests would still pass. The integration test is the only mechanism that verifies both halves of the contract together.**
   148. **Removal hygiene — all secondary references to a removed item must be cleaned up:** When removing any item from a set, list, map, conditional block, or import list (e.g. removing `"fmt"` from the conditional stdlib alias list because the code path that used `fmt.Sprintf` was replaced), you MUST grep the entire file (and closely related files) for ALL other references to the removed item and clean up every one. Secondary references include: (a) **data structure entries** — entries in maps, sets, slices, or arrays that reference the removed item (e.g. a `covered` map that still lists `"fmt": true` after `fmt` was removed from the conditional alias block — this is not dead code, it actively changes runtime behavior by blocking the fallback path for that alias); (b) **comments that enumerate the removed item** — comments listing items in a set (e.g. `"stdlib import alias (log, fmt, http, os, sql)"`) become factually wrong when one item is removed, misleading future readers and reviewers into believing the item is still relevant; (c) **documentation, docstrings, and variable name lists** that reference the removed item; (d) **test assertions** that check for the presence or behavior of the removed item; (e) **conditional checks** that gate behavior on the removed item's existence (e.g. `if name == "fmt"` branches that are now unreachable). **The mandatory audit after ANY removal:** (a) identify the canonical name of the removed item (e.g. `"fmt"`, `Port`, `writeOldFunction`); (b) grep the entire file for that name: `grep -n 'fmt' assembler.go` — examine EVERY hit, not just the ones in the function you're modifying; (c) for each hit, determine whether it is a reference to the removed item or an unrelated use of the same string — map entries, comment enumerations, and conditional checks are almost always references that must be updated; (d) extend the grep to closely related files if the removed item is referenced cross-file (e.g. removing a struct field requires grepping for the field name in test files and other consumers); (e) verify that every removed reference is either deleted (if the reference is now dead) or updated (if the reference is part of a live enumeration that must remain accurate). **The distinction from item 33 (fix-class exhaustion):** item 33 asks "where else does this same bug pattern appear?" This item asks "what else references the specific thing I just removed?" Item 33 is about pattern-level thinking (find analogous sites); this item is about instance-level thinking (find all mentions of a concrete name). Both are required: item 33 finds sibling bugs, this item finds orphaned references to a removed item. **The distinction from item 1 (no dead code):** item 1 catches unused *definitions* — types, functions, and variables that nothing references. This item catches *references* to something that was removed — map entries, comment text, and conditional branches that reference an item that no longer exists or participates in the system. Item 1's grep finds "is anything pointing at this definition?" This item's grep finds "what is this definition pointing at — and is it still valid?" **Concrete example from task-038:** removing `"fmt"` from the conditional stdlib alias list required removing `"fmt": true` from the `covered` map (a data-structure residue that blocked the fallback alias path) and removing `fmt` from the comment listing stdlib import aliases (a documentation residue that misled readers). Both were missed because the implementer correctly removed the primary code path but did not grep for `"fmt"` across the rest of the function to find secondary references. **The principle: a removal is not complete until every reference to the removed item is also removed or updated. The primary removal (deleting the code that adds the item) is the obvious step. The secondary removals (cleaning up maps, comments, conditionals, and documentation that mention the item) are the ones that get missed — because they are in different parts of the file, they look unrelated, and they don't cause immediate compilation errors. But they cause behavioral bugs (map entries change runtime behavior) and documentation rot (comments mislead readers). After any removal, grep for the removed item's name across the entire file and clean up every hit. **This item applies equally to replacements, not just removals.** When a value is replaced (e.g. `fmt` replaced by `os` in test cases, or a hardcoded port replaced by an env var read), the OLD value is effectively removed — every comment, test description, and enumeration that mentioned the old value must be updated to reflect the new value. A replacement that updates the code but not the surrounding comments or test descriptions is a partial removal — the old value persists in documentation while the code has moved on. **Additionally, the mandatory audit must extend backward from removed consumers to their data sources.** When removing code that CONSUMES a value defined in a config schema, registry YAML file, or external data source (e.g. removing reconciler code that reads `port` from `component.yaml`), the data source entry is now dead — it is declared but never consumed. Grep not just the implementation file and test file, but also the registry and config schema files that supplied data to the removed code path. The pattern signature is: you removed a `ComponentConfig["field"]` read, a config resolution call, or a struct field access that was populated from a YAML config entry — trace backward to the YAML file that declared the config entry and remove or update it. A config schema entry whose only consumer has been removed is dead metadata that misleads service authors into thinking the field has an effect (cross-reference item 223: "every declared field is a promise that the value will have an effect").**
   228. **Error struct literals must populate all fields consumed by the Error() method with meaningful values — no zero-valued format fragments:** When constructing an error value using a struct type that has an `Error()` method (or any formatting method that produces user-facing messages), EVERY field that the method interpolates into the formatted output MUST be populated with a meaningful, non-zero value — or the formatting method must handle the zero-value case gracefully. A struct literal like `InvalidBinding{Component: "", Port: "auth-provider", Reason: "..."}` where the `Error()` method unconditionally formats `Component` via `fmt.Sprintf("invalid binding for port %q required by %q: %s", ib.Port, ib.Component, ib.Reason)` produces `required by ""` — a meaningless fragment that confuses users and provides no diagnostic value. This pattern arises when an error type designed for one phase (e.g. binding resolution, where `Component` is always the component whose `requires` port is being resolved) is reused in a different phase (e.g. override validation, where the error is port-level and no single component is the "requirer"). The zero-valued field signals a mismatch between the error type's field set and the new code path's semantics. **The mandatory audit after constructing any error struct literal:** (a) read the `Error()` method (or `String()`, `Format()`, or any method that produces user-facing output) and identify every field it interpolates — follow every `%s`, `%q`, `%d`, `%v` placeholder to the corresponding field access; (b) for each interpolated field, verify your struct literal assigns a meaningful, non-zero value — empty strings (`""`), zero integers (`0`), and nil pointers each produce meaningless output fragments (`""`, `0`, `<nil>`) when interpolated; (c) if a field has no applicable value for your error scenario, you have three options: (i) adapt the `Error()` method to handle the zero-value case with conditional formatting (e.g. omit the `required by` clause when `Component` is empty), (ii) introduce a distinct error type for the new scenario whose fields match its semantics, or (iii) populate the field with a descriptive context string that makes the message meaningful even though the original semantic doesn't apply (e.g. `Component: "service override"` when the error is about a port-level override rather than a specific component's binding); (d) after constructing the literal, mentally evaluate the full formatted output string by substituting your field values — read the result as a user would and ask: "Does every fragment of this message help the user diagnose the problem?" If any fragment is empty, zero, or nil, the message is malformed; (e) apply item 33 (fix-class exhaustion): after finding one zero-valued error field, grep for ALL construction sites of the same error type and verify each one populates all interpolated fields meaningfully. **The principle: every error message a user sees must be fully formed and actionable. A zero-valued interpolation fragment (`""`, `0`, `<nil>`) in a formatted error message is a signal that the error type's design does not match the code path that constructs it — the fix must reconcile the two, not leave the mismatch as a meaningless fragment in the user's terminal.**
   231. **Every entry in a reserved-identifier or collision set must have a corresponding test case:** When adding a new entry to any reserved-identifier set, collision-prevention set, or internal-variable reservation set (e.g. `assemblerInternalVars`, `reservedTypeNames`, `handlerScopeIdentifiers`, `reservedFieldNames`), you MUST add a corresponding test case in the existing collision test that exercises disambiguation for that entry. Items 45, 47, 48, and 71 require writing collision tests when CREATING the sets. This item covers the incremental case: **adding a new entry to an existing set.** A set entry without a test is an untested guard — a regression that removes the entry from the set (or misspells it, or changes its type from the exact form used in disambiguation) will go undetected. The existing collision test (e.g. `TestAssemble_ConstructorCollidesWithAssemblerInternalVars`) exercises specific entries and proves the disambiguation mechanism works for those entries; a new entry that is never tested could be silently ineffective. **The mandatory audit after adding ANY entry to a reserved-identifier or collision set:** (a) identify the test function that exercises the set (grep for the set's variable name in `*_test.go` files); (b) read the test's table-driven cases and verify the new entry has a corresponding test case — e.g. if you added `"port"` to `assemblerInternalVars`, there must be a test case with a constructor that derives the variable name `port` (e.g. `networking.NewPort()`), verifying the generated code disambiguates it (e.g. `port2 := networking.NewPort()`); (c) if no test case exists for the new entry, add one; (d) when adding multiple entries in the same commit (e.g. adding both `port` and `addr`), add a test case for EACH entry — do not assume coverage of one entry implies coverage of another. **The principle: a reserved-identifier set is a safety mechanism. Each entry in the set is a specific claim: "this identifier will collide with generated code and must be disambiguated." Every safety claim must be independently tested. An untested entry in a collision set is an unverified safety claim — it may be correct, but without a test, a future change that invalidates the claim (removing the entry, changing the disambiguation logic, renaming the template variable) will not be caught. The cost of adding one test case per entry is trivial; the cost of an untested regression is a compile error in generated code that the test suite should have prevented.**
   232. **Validation/preprocessing scope must match input scope, not mechanism scope:** When a validation or preprocessing step is implemented for request bodies (e.g. `kind` field validation, field sanitization, input normalization), the step MUST be applied to EVERY handler that accepts a request body — not just the handlers that share a particular implementation mechanism. A common error pattern: a new validation (e.g. reading the raw body with `io.ReadAll` to inspect a field before struct decode) is implemented in create and upsert handlers, and a mechanism variable (e.g. `needsIO = hasCreate || hasUpsert`) gates the prerequisite infrastructure (e.g. raw body reading). The update handler, which also accepts a request body, is excluded from the mechanism variable — and therefore silently excluded from the validation. The engineer framed the question as "which operations need this mechanism?" instead of "which operations accept input that this validation applies to?" The mechanism is an implementation detail; the validation is a semantic requirement. **The mandatory audit after implementing any validation or preprocessing step on request bodies:** (a) identify the semantic scope of the validation — does it validate a property of the request body itself (e.g. a `kind` field)? If so, it applies to ALL operations that accept a request body, including create, update, upsert, and patch; (b) enumerate every handler that accepts a request body (POST, PUT for both create-or-replace and update, PATCH — and any custom methods that decode a body); (c) verify each handler applies the validation — if a handler uses a different decode mechanism (e.g. `json.NewDecoder` instead of `io.ReadAll`), adapt the mechanism for that handler rather than skipping the validation; (d) do NOT gate validations on mechanism variables — gate them on semantic variables (e.g. `acceptsBody = hasCreate || hasUpdate || hasUpsert`) or apply them unconditionally in every body-decoding code path; (e) after introducing any mechanism variable that controls which operations get a behavior, enumerate the full set of operations that SHOULD have the behavior and verify the variable includes all of them. **The distinction from item 37:** item 37 asks you to diff operation methods pairwise and identify shared behavioral steps (field assignments, response transformations, store calls). This item addresses a specific failure mode where item 37's audit passes because the validation step does not appear in the update method at all — there is nothing to diff. The engineer never added it to the update method because the mechanism variable excluded update, so the pairwise diff shows "create has kind validation, update does not" and the engineer concludes the difference is justified because `needsIO` doesn't include update. The error is upstream of the pairwise diff: the mechanism variable's scope was wrong. This item catches the error at the point where the mechanism variable is defined, before the pairwise diff even runs. **Concrete example from task-039:** `needsIO` was defined as `hasCreate || hasUpsert`, gating `io.ReadAll` + `emitKindValidation`. The update handler used `json.NewDecoder(r.Body).Decode()` and had no kind validation. The spec says "The `kind` field in the request body (if present) is validated against the entity name" with no handler restriction — kind validation is a property of the request body, not a property of a specific operation. The semantic scope is "all body-accepting operations"; the mechanism scope was "create and upsert." The mismatch left update unvalidated. **The principle: validation is a semantic requirement derived from the spec. Mechanism is an implementation technique. When a validation is skipped because a handler doesn't share the mechanism, the root cause is confusing "needs this mechanism" with "needs this validation." The fix is to separate the two: decide which operations need the validation (semantic question), then ensure each operation gets it (mechanism question).**
   233. **Full-entity writes after field clearing must not lose unrestored fields:** When a handler clears or zeros a set of fields on an entity struct and then persists the entity via a full-entity write (e.g. GORM's `Save()` which writes ALL columns, or any `Replace`/`Put`-style store method), EVERY cleared field must be accounted for before the write: either (a) restored to its existing database value by reading the current record first, (b) set to a new server-derived value by the populate/set logic after the clear, or (c) intentionally zeroed per the spec with an explicit comment citing the spec justification. A common error pattern: an update handler calls a "clear server-managed fields" function that zeros ALL server-managed fields (including create-time fields like `created_by`, `created_time`, and existing counters like `generation`), then a "populate server-managed fields for update" function only restores update-time fields (`updated_by`, `updated_time`) and increments `generation`. But `generation` is incremented from the cleared zero value (producing `1` on every update), not from the existing database value. And `created_by`/`created_time` are never restored — they remain at their zero values (`""` and `time.Time{}`). Since `store.Replace` uses `gorm.Save(entity)` which writes all columns (the spec says "`Replace(ctx, entity)` -- `g2.Save(entity)`"), the original `created_by`, `created_time`, and correct `generation` are permanently overwritten with zero values on every update. **The mandatory audit after writing any handler that modifies fields and calls a full-entity write:** (a) identify every field that is cleared, zeroed, or left at its struct zero value after body decode — this includes fields explicitly zeroed by a "clear" function AND fields that are simply not present in the request body and therefore default to zero after `json.Decode`; (b) for each such field, trace forward to the persist call and ask: "will this field be written to storage?" — for full-entity writes (`Save`, `Replace`), the answer is YES for every field on the struct; (c) for each field that will be written as zero, determine whether zeroing is correct per the spec: create-time metadata (`created_by`, `created_time`) must NEVER be zeroed by an update — these values were set at creation and must be preserved; version counters (`generation`) must be incremented from their EXISTING database value, not from zero; computed fields should remain as-is from the database; (d) if a field should preserve its existing value, the handler must either: read the existing record before the write and copy the preserved fields from it, OR use a partial update (update only specific columns) instead of a full-entity write, OR exclude the field from the clear step entirely; (e) enumerate every "clear" or "zero" utility function called by the handler and diff its field set against the "populate" function that follows — create a two-column list: left column is "fields cleared," right column is "fields restored" — any field in the left column without a corresponding entry in the right column is a data loss bug when followed by a full-entity write; (f) for counter fields like `generation`, verify that the increment reads from the existing database value, not from the struct's current (potentially cleared) value — `entity.Generation++` after a clear produces `1`, not `existingGeneration + 1`. **The distinction from item 37:** item 37 catches cases where a behavioral step (like clearing computed fields) is present in some operations but missing from others. This item catches cases where a behavioral step is present but INCOMPLETE — the clear step runs in the update handler, but the corresponding restore step doesn't cover all cleared fields. Item 37's pairwise diff would show "create clears fields, update clears fields" and conclude they match — but the bug is inside the clear-then-populate sequence, not in whether the sequence exists. **The principle: a full-entity write is a destructive operation — it overwrites EVERY column, including fields the handler didn't intend to change. A "clear then populate" pattern is safe ONLY if the populate step is exhaustive over the clear step's field set. Any gap between "fields cleared" and "fields restored" is a data loss window that the full-entity write makes permanent. The engineer must prove — by exhaustive enumeration — that every cleared field has a corresponding restoration, or that the write excludes the cleared field. "I only meant to clear the update-time fields" is not a defense when the clear function zeros all server-managed fields and the write persists all of them.**
   234. **Deserialization failure during input validation must be treated as invalid input, not absent input:** When validation code deserializes or parses a raw input value into a typed variable (e.g. `json.Unmarshal(rawBytes, &stringVar)`) before checking it against an expected value, a deserialization failure means the value is PRESENT but has an UNEXPECTED TYPE — it does NOT mean the value is absent. The condition `if err == nil && typedVar != expected { reject }` silently skips validation when the value has the wrong JSON type: `err != nil` makes the entire conjunction false, and the request proceeds as if the field were not present. But the field IS present — it is just not the expected type. The spec says "if present and wrong, return 400" — a value that is present but not deserializable into the expected type IS "present and wrong." A JSON `kind` field with value `42`, `true`, `[1]`, or `{"x":1}` is present in the request body; it is not a string and therefore cannot possibly match the expected entity name; it is "present and wrong" and must be rejected. **The correct condition structure is:** first check whether the raw value exists (i.e. the key is present in the JSON object). If it IS present, then attempt to deserialize. If deserialization fails (wrong type), reject — the value is present but invalid. If deserialization succeeds, check the value — if it doesn't match, reject. The condition should be `if err != nil || typedVar != expected { reject }` (given that the field's presence was already established), NOT `if err == nil && typedVar != expected { reject }`. The former treats parse failure as invalid; the latter treats parse failure as absent. **The mandatory audit after writing any validation that parses before checking:** (a) identify the raw-value-exists check (e.g. `kindRaw, hasKind := jsonMap["kind"]` or `if kindRaw != nil`) — this establishes presence; (b) identify the parse step (e.g. `json.Unmarshal(kindRaw, &kind)`) — this converts the raw value to a typed value; (c) identify the value check (e.g. `kind != entityName`) — this validates the parsed value against the expected value; (d) verify that when the parse step FAILS, the code path leads to rejection, NOT to acceptance — the value was present (established in step a) but could not be parsed into the expected type, which means it cannot possibly match the expected value; (e) specifically, verify that the error from the parse step is NOT used as a condition to SKIP the value check — `if err == nil && ...` is the bug pattern; `if err != nil { reject } else if value != expected { reject }` or `if err != nil || value != expected { reject }` is the correct pattern; (f) write tests that submit the field with each JSON type that is NOT the expected type — for a string field, test with: number (`42`), boolean (`true`), array (`[1]`), object (`{"x":1}`), and null (`null`) — and verify each one produces a 400 rejection, not silent acceptance. **The distinction from item 232:** item 232 catches validation steps that are missing from certain handlers (scope mismatch between "which operations get the validation" and "which operations need it"). This item catches validation steps that ARE present in all handlers but have a logic error in the validation condition itself — the validation runs, but its condition is structured so that a class of invalid inputs bypasses the check. Item 232's audit would confirm "kind validation is present in create, update, and upsert" and pass, but the validation itself silently accepts non-string values in all three handlers. **The distinction from item 11:** item 11 requires end-to-end tests that exercise the feature. A test that submits `{"kind": "WrongEntity"}` and expects 400 satisfies item 11 but does not catch this bug — the test uses a valid JSON type (string) with an invalid value. The bug only manifests with invalid JSON types. This item requires testing with inputs of the WRONG TYPE, not just the wrong value within the right type. **The principle: deserialization is not validation — it is type conversion. A deserialization failure means the value failed type conversion, which is a STRONGER form of invalidity than a value mismatch. A value that cannot even be converted to the expected type cannot possibly satisfy the value check — treating the conversion failure as "field absent" is a logic error that creates a type-based bypass of the validation. Any input of any JSON type other than the expected one sails through the check unchallenged. The engineer must distinguish three states: (1) field absent → accept (no validation needed), (2) field present, correct type, correct value → accept, (3) field present, wrong type OR wrong value → reject. The bug pattern collapses states 1 and 3 into a single "accept" path.**
   235. **Typed struct decoding silently discards keys not in the struct — body-level validation for non-struct keys requires raw body interception:** When a handler method validates a JSON key that is NOT a field on the decode-target struct (e.g. validating `kind` against the entity name, where `kind` is not a field on `PatchRequest`), the handler MUST read the raw request body first (`io.ReadAll`), perform the key-level validation on the raw JSON, and then decode the typed struct from the buffered bytes. Go's `json.Decoder` and `json.Unmarshal` silently discard unknown keys by default (unless `DisallowUnknownFields` is set, which is not appropriate for forward-compatible APIs). A handler that decodes directly into a typed struct (`json.NewDecoder(r.Body).Decode(&patch)`) will silently discard any `kind` field in the body — the validation cannot run because the data it needs to inspect was consumed and discarded by the decode step. This is a **structural incompatibility**, not a simple oversight: the validation and the decode step cannot share the same `r.Body` read because (a) `r.Body` is a one-shot reader (reading it for validation consumes it for decode), and (b) the decode step's type system silently drops the key the validation needs. The handler must buffer the body, inspect the buffer for the validation key, and then decode the struct from the buffer. **The mandatory audit after implementing any body-level validation that targets a key NOT present on all decode-target structs:** (a) list EVERY handler method that accepts a request body — create, update, upsert, AND patch (and any future operations that accept a body); (b) for each handler, identify the decode-target struct (e.g. `Entity` for create/update/upsert, `PatchRequest` for patch); (c) for each decode-target struct, check whether the validated key (e.g. `kind`) is a field on that struct; (d) if the key is NOT a field on the struct, the handler MUST be refactored to use the `io.ReadAll` + raw inspection + buffered decode pattern — direct struct decoding will silently discard the key and make the validation unreachable; (e) do NOT assume that a handler "doesn't need" the validation because its decode struct doesn't include the validated key — the decode struct's field set determines what data the handler PROCESSES, not what data the handler VALIDATES; validation happens BEFORE processing and inspects the raw input, not the processed struct. **The distinction from item 232:** item 232 catches the scope mismatch ("which operations need this validation?") and correctly identifies that ALL body-accepting operations need it, including patch. This item catches the MECHANISM mismatch that occurs even after item 232's scope audit passes — the implementation agent correctly identifies that patch needs kind validation, but incorrectly concludes that the existing decode path handles it (because the decode path "processes the body"). The decode path processes the body into a TYPED struct that does not include `kind` — the key is silently dropped, and the validation never fires. Item 232 says "check patch"; this item explains WHY patch's existing mechanism cannot implement the validation and WHAT must change. **Concrete example from task-039:** after item 232 was added (R1 process revision), the implementation team fixed kind validation for update and upsert but still missed patch. The patch handler used `json.NewDecoder(r.Body).Decode(&patch)` into a `PatchRequest` struct containing only patchable fields — no `kind` field. A client sending `{"kind": "WrongEntity", "spec": {...}}` to PATCH had the `kind` key silently discarded by Go's decoder, and the request was accepted without returning 400. The fix required refactoring the patch handler to: read body with `io.ReadAll`, call `emitKindValidation` on the raw bytes, then decode `PatchRequest` from the buffered bytes using `json.NewDecoder(bytes.NewReader(bodyBytes))`. **The principle: Go's JSON decoder is a typed deserializer, not a raw parser. It maps JSON keys to struct fields and silently discards everything else. When a validation targets a key that is not a struct field, the decoder is the wrong tool — it is structurally incapable of preserving the data the validation needs. The raw body must be intercepted before the decoder runs. A handler that validates a non-struct key MUST use the raw-body interception pattern, regardless of what decode mechanism it used before the validation was added. The pattern signature to grep for: any handler that uses `json.NewDecoder(r.Body).Decode` or `json.Unmarshal` into a typed struct AND has a sibling handler that performs raw-body validation on a key not present in that struct — the first handler is missing the validation.**
   236. **Complete removal/replacement audit — all references to removed concepts must be found and updated:** When a task removes, replaces, or deprecates a code path, feature, identifier, or import (e.g. removing `"fmt"` from the generation pipeline, replacing a hardcoded port with an environment variable lookup, removing a config parameter from a component), ALL references to the removed concept across the entire repository must be found and either updated or removed. This extends item 33 (fix-class exhaustion for bugs) to feature changes: just as a bug fix must search for the same bug pattern elsewhere, a removal must search for all references to the removed thing. A removal is not complete when the primary code path is changed — it is complete when zero stale references remain anywhere in the repository. **The mandatory audit protocol after any removal or replacement:** (a) identify every identifier, string literal, config key, and concept being removed or replaced (e.g. `"fmt"`, `Port`, `8080` as a hardcoded port, a YAML property name); (b) grep the ENTIRE repository for each identifier — not just the files you modified in this task; (c) for each match, determine whether the reference is stale (describes or depends on the old behavior) or still valid (references the concept in an unrelated context); (d) update or remove every stale reference. **Stale reference categories, ordered by risk:** **(1) Auxiliary data structures** — entries in coverage maps, alias tables, reserved-name sets, tracking maps, or any data structure that enumerates the concepts being processed. These are the highest risk because they are functionally distant from the primary code change, they compile and pass tests even when stale, and they mislead maintainers into thinking the removed concept is still active. Concrete example from task-038: removing `"fmt"` from the import generation pipeline left a stale `"fmt"` entry in the `stdlibAliases()` `covered` map — the map still tracked `"fmt"` as a covered import even though `"fmt"` was no longer emitted. **(2) Config files** — YAML, JSON, or other config properties that defined parameters for the removed feature. Dead config properties tell users and downstream tools that a feature is configurable when it is not. Concrete example from task-038: replacing the hardcoded port with `os.Getenv("PORT")` obsoleted the `port` config property in `rest-api/component.yaml`, but the property was left in place — a user setting `port: 9090` in their component config would see no effect, with no error or warning. **(3) Comments and docstrings** — descriptions of how the old code worked, why the old approach was used, or what the old behavior was. Stale comments mislead readers into thinking the described behavior still applies. This includes comments in production code AND in test code. Concrete example from task-038: a comment in `constructorRename.PreReserved` still described the old `fmt.Sprintf`-based port formatting, and a test comment in `TestAssemble_StdlibImportAliasShadowing` still referenced `"fmt"` behavior that no longer applied. **(4) Test assertions** — test cases that assert on the old behavior or use the removed concept in setup. These may still pass if the assertions are broad enough, but they test something that no longer exists and create false confidence. **After completing any removal, build a two-column audit:** left column is "identifiers/concepts removed," right column is "all grep matches found, with disposition (removed/updated/still-valid)." Every match must have a disposition. If any match lacks a disposition, the removal is incomplete. **The distinction from item 1 (dead code):** item 1 catches types and functions that are defined but never referenced. This item catches the inverse: references that still exist but whose target has been removed or replaced. A stale map entry, a dead config property, and a stale comment are all references to something that no longer exists — they are not "dead code" in the sense of item 1 (they are not definitions), but they are dead references that item 1 does not cover. **The distinction from item 33 (fix-class exhaustion):** item 33 searches for the same *bug pattern* in other code sites after fixing a bug. This item searches for the same *concept* in all reference sites after removing the concept. The trigger for item 33 is a bug; the trigger for this item is a removal or replacement. Both require a repository-wide grep, but they search for different things: item 33 greps for the pattern signature of the bug; this item greps for the literal identifiers being removed.
   237. **Struct field changes require a codebase-wide fixture audit — ALL testdata files deserializing into the modified struct must be updated:** When you add, rename, or remove a field on any struct that is populated via YAML/JSON deserialization (identified by struct tags like `yaml:"fieldname"` or `json:"fieldname"`), you MUST find and update EVERY testdata/fixture file across the entire codebase that deserializes into that struct type — not just the fixture files in the package you are modifying. A struct that is used by multiple packages (e.g. `types.Convention` used by both `parser` and `registry`) will have separate testdata copies in each package's `testdata/` directory. Updating one copy while missing another leaves a test exercising stale fixture data that lacks the new field — the test passes (Go's YAML/JSON unmarshaling silently ignores missing keys, defaulting to zero values), but it provides no coverage for the new field through that code path. **The mandatory audit after modifying any deserialized struct field:** (a) identify the struct you modified and its YAML/JSON tag for the new/changed field (e.g. `CORS string \x60yaml:"cors"\x60` on `Convention`); (b) identify the parent YAML key that contains this struct — for nested structs, this is the key whose value deserializes into the parent struct (e.g. `conventions:` for `Convention`); (c) grep ALL `testdata/` directories and registry fixture directories for YAML/JSON files containing the parent key: `grep -r "conventions:" --include="*.yaml" internal/ registry/`; (d) for each matching fixture file, verify the new field is present with a representative value — if missing, add it; (e) for each test that uses the updated fixture, verify there is an assertion that the new field is correctly populated after deserialization (e.g. `assert Convention.CORS == "enabled"`); (f) do NOT rely on the test suite catching the gap — Go's YAML/JSON decoders treat missing keys as zero values without error, so a fixture missing the new field will deserialize successfully with the field at its zero value, and any test that doesn't explicitly assert on the new field will pass silently. **The distinction from item 5 (spec-complete examples):** item 5 requires that test YAML includes all sub-fields shown in the spec. This item addresses a different failure mode: the test YAML is complete within its own package but a COPY of the same fixture in a different package was not updated. Item 5 audits one fixture for completeness; this item audits the codebase for completeness of fixture INSTANCES. Both are required. **The distinction from item 33 (fix-class exhaustion):** item 33 searches for the same bug pattern at different code sites after a bug is found. This item is a proactive audit during initial implementation — it searches for all instances of the same fixture data, not the same bug pattern. The trigger for item 33 is a discovered bug; the trigger for this item is a struct field change. **Concrete example from task-046:** adding `CORS string \x60yaml:"cors"\x60` to `Convention` required updating `registry/archetypes/rest-crud/archetype.yaml` (live registry), `internal/registry/testdata/registry/archetypes/rest-crud/archetype.yaml` (registry testdata), AND `internal/parser/testdata/archetype.yaml` (parser testdata). The first two were updated; the third was missed because it lives in a different package (`parser` vs `registry`). The parser test (`TestParseArchetype`) consequently had no assertion that `Conventions.CORS` was correctly parsed, and no test failure signaled the gap. **The principle: a struct field addition is a schema change. Every fixture that conforms to the old schema must be updated to conform to the new schema. Unlike a database migration, Go's YAML/JSON decoders do not error on schema drift — they silently accept the old schema by zero-valuing missing fields. This means the test suite cannot catch missing fixture updates unless every test explicitly asserts on every field. The fixture audit is the safety net: find every fixture instance, update it, and add assertions for the new field. A struct field that is tested in one package's fixture but not another's is a coverage gap that will only be discovered by the verifier — or worse, in production.**
   238. **Pre-existing test assertions must not be weakened or relaxed:** When modifying a test function to add new assertions or adapt to new behavior, you MUST NOT weaken, relax, or broaden pre-existing assertions that are unrelated to the change. A common error pattern: a test function asserts `strings.Contains(output, "Size    int")` (verifying both the field name and its type together), and while adding a new assertion for a new field, the implementation agent gratuitously changes the existing assertion to `strings.Contains(output, "Size")` — which now passes if the word "Size" appears anywhere in the output (e.g. in a comment, a different field, or a function name), losing the type verification entirely. The generator output for the existing fields did not change, so the original exact-match assertions would still pass — there is no justification for weakening them. **The mandatory audit before marking ready-for-review:** (a) for every test function you modified, run `git diff` on that function and examine EVERY changed assertion line; (b) for each changed assertion, determine whether the change was REQUIRED by the task (e.g. the generator output actually changed for that field) or GRATUITOUS (the generator output for that field is unchanged); (c) if the change was gratuitous, revert it — the original assertion is strictly stronger and the task does not require weakening it; (d) specifically watch for these weakening patterns: exact substring match (`"FieldName    Type"`) replaced with partial match (`"FieldName"`); `assert.Equal` replaced with `assert.Contains`; multi-field contiguous string check broken into individual single-word checks; regex match replaced with substring match; assertion on specific value replaced with assertion on non-nil/non-empty. **The principle: existing assertions are evidence of prior verification. They encode specific knowledge about the expected output that was validated when the assertion was written. Weakening an assertion discards that knowledge — it makes the test pass for a broader class of outputs, including incorrect ones. The task asked you to ADD behavior, not to reduce confidence in EXISTING behavior. If a pre-existing assertion fails after your change, investigate why — either your change broke something (a bug), or the assertion needs to be UPDATED to match the new correct output (an adaptation). Neither case justifies making the assertion less precise. Weakening is never the right response to a failing assertion; updating to match the new expected output is. And if the assertion still passes, there is zero reason to touch it at all. Concrete example from task-053: four assertions in `TestGenerate_StorageInterfaceListOptionsAndResult` for `Page`, `Size`, `OrderBy`, and `Fields` were relaxed from `strings.Contains(router, "Page    int")` to `strings.Contains(router, "Page")` while adding a new `ImplicitFilters` assertion. The generator output for these four fields was unchanged — the original assertions would still pass. The weakened `Size` check now passes if "Size" appears anywhere in the file, losing the type assertion entirely.**
5. Mark the task as `ready-for-review`\.
6. Commit your work, using conventional commits, and author: &quot;Implementation &lt;implementation@redhat\.com&gt;&quot;
7. List the commits you added to the task.
8. CRITICAL: Call \``kill $PPID\`` this will transfer control over to the implementation team, who will work on a task\.
